# 南华真经循本

*The Southern Florescence True Classic: A Return to the Root*

**Text Title:** *The Southern Florescence True Classic: A Return to the Root*
Author: Luo Miandao of the Ming dynasty
Thirty fascicles
Source edition: *Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era*, *Dongshen* Section, *Jade Instructions* Category

**Zhuangzi**
Narrated by Luo Miandao of Luling Bamboo Peak

Zhuangzi was a man of Song, named Zhou, styled Zixiu. Born in Meng County of Suiyang, he once served as an officer in the Meng Lacquer Garden. His learning spanned all fields, yet its essence returned to the words of Laozi. Thus, he composed a work of over one hundred thousand characters, largely employing parables. His language flowed boundlessly, following his own inclinations, so that even kings and great ministers could not contain him.

King Wei of Chu, hearing of Zhuang Zhou’s worthiness, dispatched envoys with generous gifts to welcome him, offering the position of chief minister. Zhou laughed and said to the messengers: "A thousand gold pieces are a weighty profit; the post of minister is a lofty rank. Have you alone not seen the sacrificial ox at the suburban rites? Reared for years, clad in embroidered robes, and led into the grand ancestral temple—at that moment, if it wished to be a lone piglet, could it do so? Begone swiftly, and do not defile me. I would rather wander and play in the filth, free and joyful, than be fettered by rulers. He never entered official service, fulfilling his own aspirations to the end."

The Tang dynasty posthumously enfeoffed him as the Perfected of Southern Florescence, and his book was titled *The Southern Florescence True Classic*.

**Explanation of the Title in *The Southern Florescence True Classic: A Return to the Root***

Though Zhuangzi’s writings are vast, uncanny, and strange, drifting beyond the Six Classics, they resemble heaven and earth, sun and moon—possessing constant principles and movements—yet wind and clouds open and close, spirits and ghosts transform, all of which cannot be lacking. Scholars of past and present have always marveled at them, yet their phrasing and diction belong to the language of the late Zhou, beyond the full comprehension of later ages.

Nevertheless, some passages remain verifiable. For instance, the questions of *Zheng* and *Huo* to the market supervisor regarding the measurement of hides correspond to the *Grand Archery* officers *Zheng* and *Huo* (see *The Book of Etiquette and Ceremonial*). The explanation of the ox with a white forehead, the pig with an upturned snout, and the man with hemorrhoids—none fit for the river—refers to the ancient Son of Heaven’s springtime expiatory sacrifices (see *The Han Treatise on Suburban Sacrifices*). *Tangzi* was the son of the *Chief of the Hall*, just as the legitimate son of the Zhou royal clan was called *Gate Master*. *Yitai* (Righteous Terrace) is *Yitai* (Ceremonial Terrace). Zheng Sinong said: "In old texts, *yi* (ceremony) was written as *yi* (righteousness)." The *doujian* (neck and shoulder) appears in *The Artificers’ Record*. The carpenter’s marking of the sounding stone’s hollows and numbers refers to *gu* (inspection), where *doujian* is the *gu* character. Such examples are numerous.

Without ancient learning, scholars cannot grasp these allusions. Many commentators either expound with empty discourse, drag in Chan Buddhist terms, or forcibly attach Confucian orthodox principles—most of which miss the original text’s intent.

Slowly I remark: The marvelous subtleties of this text are peerless—yet who truly recognizes its wonder? Across the desolate span of eighteen hundred years, the author’s intent has remained pent-up and unexpressed, while plagiarists’ misapplications have only multiplied errors. Is this not a lamentable omission in the annals of cursive script?

Fortunately, I, Mian Dao, retired early to leisure, entrusting my aspirations to purity and emptiness. Thus, I found time to comb through a few passages with my humble insights, setting brush to paper until, unawares, a volume took shape. I dare hope this effort follows the original intent, and so I title it *Following the Root of the Zhuangzi*.

*Following the Root of the Southern Florescence True Scripture*, Scroll One
By Lu Ling’s Bamboo Peak, Luo Mian Dao
Collated by Disciple Peng Xiang

**Inner Chapters: Free and Easy Wandering**
To wander freely without constraint—this is called *xiaoyaoyou* (free and easy wandering). Zhuangzi sought to inspire those who would study the Dao, and thus began with this title.

The Inner, Outer, and Miscellaneous Chapters are like sequential collections, with no fundamental difference in meaning. According to the *Han Arts and Letters*, the *Zhuangzi* originally comprised fifty-two chapters. Guo Xiang had already discerned that thirteen parts were cunningly spurious, leaving the extant thirty-three. Su Shi of the Eastern Slope later excised *Yielding the Throne*, *Robber Zhi*, *Discoursing on the Sword*, and *The Old Fisherman*, merging *Liezi* with the end of *Giving Away the Throne* into a single chapter—his judgment was precise. Yet I still deem *Refining the Will* and *Mending the Nature* superficial and inauthentic, and thus propose fixing the total at twenty-six chapters.

The Inner Chapters all establish their titles first, and the text’s meaning does not stray from them. The Outer and Miscellaneous Chapters merely extract the opening characters for their names, for the Inner Chapters’ intent suffices, while the Outer and Miscellaneous Chapters merely elaborate upon it.

**In the northern darkness there is a fish, and his name is Kun.**
The vastness of Kun—who knows how many thousand *li* he spans? He transforms into a bird, and his name is Peng. The back of Peng—who knows how many thousand *li* it stretches? Roused to flight, his wings hang like clouds suspended from the sky. This bird, when the seas surge, will journey to the southern darkness. The southern darkness is the Celestial Pond.

The northern and southern darknesses are not merely the northern and southern seas, but the polar extremes of the sea, so distant and obscure that they are called *ming* (darkness). Kun, according to the *Erya*, is the general term for all fish roe, hence the *Nei Ze*’s "roe sauce" is read as *kun*. The *Lu Language* also states: "In fish prohibitions, *kun* and *fu* both refer to fish roe." Zhuangzi takes the tiniest and makes it vast—this is the beginning of his playful absurdity. Peng does not appear in the classics. The *Island Barbarians’ Records* notes: "In the layered lands of Kunlun, there is often a great Peng whose flight blots out the sun and can devour camels. A man once gathered a fallen Peng wing and cut its quill into a water bucket." Kun’s size is said to span "who knows how many thousand *li*," and Peng’s back likewise—truly, the greater the substance, the greater its transformation.

"Roused to flight" means mustering its fury to take off. "When the seas surge, it will journey to the southern darkness": *yun* (surge) means *dong* (movement). When typhoons arise, the sea’s *qi* stirs, and the bird migrates to avoid them. The *Lu Language* states: "The *yuanju* bird halted outside the eastern gate of Lu for three days." Zhan Qin remarked: "This year, the sea must be bracing for disaster." For the birds and beasts of great rivers all know to evade calamity. That year, the seas were beset by great winds, and the winter was unseasonably warm—this is the event in question.

The Celestial Pond is where the sea meets the sky, like the "pond" in "bathing in the Salty Pond." A *chi* (pond) is where fish and birds alight; Peng’s alighting place is this pond. It is called the southern darkness, the Celestial Pond. Elsewhere it says: "North of the barren lands, there is a dark sea, the Celestial Pond." For the term *minghai* (dark sea) still does not fully convey its remoteness, so it is further specified as the Celestial Pond, revealing it as the place where the sea meets the sky. This shows Peng’s flight from the northernmost sea to the southernmost, spanning such vast distances.

The opening speaks of Kun transforming into Peng, able to soar high and migrate far, drawing an analogy to humans transforming into sages or spirits, achieving the ultimate and attaining *xiaoyaoyou*. The word *hua* (transformation) appears at the outset; at first glance, it seems unremarkable, but upon closer reading, one realizes its indispensability.

**The *Qi Xie* is a record of anomalies.**
Its words state: "When Peng journeys to the southern darkness, it strikes the water for three thousand *li*, ascends on the whirlwind to ninety thousand *li*, and departs with the six-month *xi* (breath)." The *Qi Xie* is a book of Qi proverbs. Mencius said: "The words of Qi’s rustics," so it is fitting that Qi customs would produce such proverbial sayings. The *Qi Xie*’s words are proverbial. Zhuangzi, though himself full of strange tales, calls the *Qi Xie* a "record of anomalies"—this is another instance of his playful absurdity.

*Ji* (strike) means to beat. As Peng’s *qi* and momentum take flight, waves surge to a height of three thousand *li*. *Bo* (ascend) means to follow the wind’s circular motion. *Fuyao* (whirlwind) is a cyclone. The wind’s force lifts it in a spiraling ascent—hence the phrase "the branches of the sea-bound whirlwind" also draws from this meaning. "Ninety thousand *li*" refers to the distance from the northern darkness to the sky; from the Central Lands to the zenith, the distance would be even greater. According to the *Kao Ling Yao*: "The celestial sphere spans 365¼ degrees, each degree measuring 2,932 *li* and 1,461/348 *li*. The circumference is 1,071,000 *li*. Taking the ratio of circumference to diameter as three to one, the diameter is 357,000 *li*—this is the diameter of the twenty-eight constellations’ circuit. Beyond the twenty-eight constellations, above, below, east, and west each extend 15,000 *li*, forming the four polar extremes, called the *sibiao* (four limits). Within the four limits and including the constellations, the total diameter is 387,000 *li*. The exact center of the sky is thus 193,500 *li*."

The earth lies at the center, with a thickness of thirty thousand *li*. At the spring equinox, the earth is precisely at the center. From this point, it gradually descends, and by the summer solstice, it has sunk fifteen thousand *li*, so that its upper edge aligns with the midpoint of the sky. After the summer solstice, the earth gradually rises again, and by the autumn equinox, it returns to the exact center of the sky. From there, it continues to ascend, and by the winter solstice, it has risen fifteen thousand *li*, so that its lower edge aligns with the midpoint of the sky. After the winter solstice, the earth gradually descends once more, perpetually ascending and descending within the span of thirty thousand *li*. The theory of the *siyou* (four movements) originally derives from the *Zhou Bei* text; whether the scholars of the armillary sphere acknowledge its validity remains uncertain, but their calculations of celestial degrees and terrestrial distances appear reliable. Furthermore, Zheng Xuan’s commentary on the *Rites of Zhou* employs the *gougu* method to measure the shadow of a gnomon, yielding 81,394 *li*, 30 *bu*, 5 *chi*, and 3 *cun* with 6 *fen*—this being the distance from the point directly beneath the sun to the earth, which is half the diameter of the sky. As for the phrase "after six months of rest," it refers to the roc’s migration from the Northern Darkness to the Southern Darkness, traversing half the circumference of the sky, hence requiring only half a year of rest before pausing, demonstrating that the roc’s flight aligns with celestial measurements. The preceding passage speaks only of the distance of the roc’s migration; this section further proves the height of its flight. The initial statement of "ninety thousand *li*" is placed here, with the explanation following thereafter.

"Wild horses," "dust," and "living creatures blown by the breath"—is the deep blue of the sky its true color? Or is it so distant that it has no limit? When viewed from above, it appears the same. *Xi* (息) means *qi*. "Wild horses" and "dust" are metaphors for the drifting *qi*. Zhang Zai of Hengqu said: "The *qi* surges and swirls through the Great Void, ascending, descending, and soaring without cease. This is what the *Yi* calls *kunyi*, and what Zhuangzi refers to as 'living creatures blown by the breath'—is this not the 'wild horses'?" Zhu Xi of Hui’an remarked: "The Way of Qian forms the male, and the Way of Kun forms the female—this is the tumult of drifting *qi*." *Cangcang* (苍苍) is the color seen from afar. *Yi* (已) means "to cease." This merely explains the phrase "ninety thousand *li*." It warns against mistaking the drifting *qi*’s deep blue for the sky’s ultimate boundary, thereby doubting the existence of ninety thousand *li*. Thus, it clarifies that what appears in the Great Void like "wild horses" and "dust" is the creative power of nature, which uses this *qi* to animate living things. Humans, confined within this *qi*, remain unaware of it, perceiving only a distant, deep blue. Now, when we look up, is that deep blue the sky’s true color? Or is there still an infinite expanse beyond? Since the blue extends endlessly upward, the roc can ascend ninety thousand *li*. From above, looking down, the same deep blue appears—this is the realm of the tangible earth. It ceases here; no further ascent is possible. For the blue above is not like the blue below, which cannot be traversed. Du Meng of the Han said: "The sky has no substance; when gazed upon, it is boundlessly high and distant, beyond the limits of human vision. Thus, it appears deep blue, which aligns with this passage." The phrase "then it is so" (*ze yi yi*) is often misread as "and that is all" (*er yi yi*), disrupting the coherence of the preceding and following text.

As for the transition—where passages often begin with phrases like "furthermore" (*qie fu*) or "allow me to explain" (*qing shi yan zhi*), recognizing this mechanism ensures the text remains unbroken—

If water is not deep, it lacks the strength to bear a great vessel. Pour a cup of water onto a level floor, and a mustard seed may serve as a boat; place a cup there, and it will stick fast, for the water is shallow and the vessel too large. If wind is not deep, it lacks the strength to bear great wings. Thus, at ninety thousand *li*, the wind lies beneath, and only then can one ride the wind. With the azure sky at one’s back and no obstacle to block the way, only then can one set course for the south.

*Aotang* (坳堂) refers to a depression in the floor of a hall. "A mustard seed as a boat" means the seed floats like a vessel. *Pei* (培) means "to accumulate." The "azure sky" is not the deep blue seen from below; only at ninety thousand *li* does the true azure appear. The preceding text states that deep blue is not the sky’s true color, so it is clear that azure is the true color. The Six Classics never speak of the "azure sky," only the "deep-blue sky," for they describe only what is visible. This further explains why the roc must fly to ninety thousand *li*: it requires the vast power of the wind to migrate so far. Water is likened to wind, and the vessel to the roc. If water is not deep, it cannot bear a great vessel; if wind is not deep, it cannot bear great wings. Thus, at ninety thousand *li*, the wind lies beneath, its force vast and abundant, and with the azure sky at one’s back, the celestial path is open and unobstructed. Only by harnessing this wind can the roc set course for the south. *Nai jin* (乃今) refers to the moment of migration. The following passage, "and then it heads for the Southern Darkness," describes the completion of the journey.

This section describes the marvelous transformations of the *kun*-roc, citing the *Qi Xie* to prove its flight of ninety thousand *li*, and the passage on "wild horses" to clarify the principle behind reaching such heights. The cicada and the *yujiao* bird laughed at it, saying: "I burst forth and fly, striking the elm and sandalwood; sometimes I do not reach them and simply fall to the ground—why go ninety thousand *li* to the south?"

The *tiao* (蜩) is a black, cicada-like creature that sings in the fifth month; the *chan* (蝉) is mottled and sings after the seventh month; the *diaoliao* (蛁蟟) is green—there are many varieties. Commonly, they are all called cicadas, or *qianglang*, or water insects, or the transformations of *qicao* from dung. The *yujiao* (鸴鸠) is a bird originally named for its resemblance to the mountain magpie, hence called *hongjiu*. The *Monthly Ordinances* states: "The *mingjiu* bird smooths its feathers." The commentary by Guo Pu cites: "It resembles a mountain magpie but is smaller, with a short tail, dark blue-black, and very vocal. In the Jiangdong region, it is called *guzhuo* (鹘鸼)."

The *Monthly Ordinances* states that in the second month of spring, the hawk transforms into the *jiu*; the *Royal Regulations* states that in the second month of autumn, the *jiu* transforms into the hawk. The *Zuozhuan* mentions the *Shuangjiu* clan, with Du’s commentary identifying it as the hawk. Since these two creatures transform into one another, the *jiu* may also be called the hawk. These two insects, small and capable of transformation, are thus contrasted with the *kun* and *peng*. "To rise with decision" means to take flight with all one’s strength, akin to the resolve in decisive battle. *Qiang* means to collide or strike. *Yu* and *fang* are the names of two trees. "Sometimes I do not reach them and simply fall to the ground"—at times, even the *yu* and *fang* are beyond my reach, and I must descend to the earth. This describes how the two insects cannot fly high, serving as the *tiao* and *jiu*’s mockery of the *peng*. How is one who ridicules the great Dao from a narrow perspective any different? One who travels to the *mangcang* returns after a day’s journey with a full belly; one who journeys a hundred *li* must prepare provisions the night before; one who travels a thousand *li* must gather supplies for three months. What do these two insects know? *Mangcang* refers to the distant haze, where the land appears green in a single glance. *Guo* describes a full belly, or courage—when the stomach is full, one is bold; when hungry, one is timid. The two insects are the *tiao* and *jiu*. The farther one travels, the more provisions one must accumulate; the broader the *peng*’s wings, the thicker the wind it must gather. What can these two insects possibly understand? Small knowledge does not reach great knowledge; short years do not reach long years. Both instances of *zhi* (知) are read in the level tone, linking the preceding and following phrases—a common stylistic device in Zhuangzi’s writing. The reason these two insects mock the *peng* is simply that their small understanding does not reach the *peng*’s great understanding. Thus, short and long years are used to illustrate small and great knowledge.

How do we know this is so? The morning mushroom knows not the waxing and waning of the moon; the *huigū* knows not spring and autumn—these are short-lived creatures. South of Chu lies the *Mingling*, which takes five hundred years as its spring and five hundred years as its autumn. In high antiquity, there was the great *chun* tree, which takes eight thousand years as its spring and eight thousand years as its autumn. Yet Pengzu alone is renowned for his longevity—how pitiful it is that common people compare themselves to him! The *jun* is a ground fungus; the large ones are called *zhongdao*, while the small ones are called *jun*. The *jun*’s lifespan is measured in mornings, hence the term "morning mushroom." The *huigū* is also a type of cicada. Zheng’s *Comprehensive Records* states: "The *hanman*, properly called *huigū*, sings plaintively in the ninth and tenth months." Xiaoshan says: "The *huigū* cries *zijiuzijiu*, and at year’s end, it has nothing to rely on." The *huigū* Zhuangzi refers to as knowing not spring and autumn is the small, purple-green cicada of the fourth and fifth months. *Ming* refers to the Southern Darkness; *ling* refers to the spirit turtle. The unicorn, phoenix, turtle, and dragon are called the Four Spirits. The *Mingling* is the spirit turtle of the Dark Sea. The morning mushroom and the great *chun* tree, the *huigū* and the *Mingling*, are presented as one plant and one animal in parallel—thus, it is clear that the *Mingling* is not a plant. "Five hundred years as spring, five hundred years as autumn"—the *Records of the Grand Historian* states: "A thousand-year-old turtle measures one foot and two inches." Two sets of five hundred years make up the thousand-year count. "Eight thousand years as spring, eight thousand years as autumn"—the character *chun* (椿) is split into two *eight* and *hundred*, multiplied by ten to yield two sets of eight thousand. This is likely a playful fabrication by the jester, akin to the character *hai* (亥) with its two heads and six bodies. Yet it has deceived the world for a thousand years, and none have seen through it.

Generally, when such things appear, people routinely dismiss them as allegory, unaware that even allegory must have some basis in resemblance. Those who read the *Zhuangzi* closely and see through such fabrications will find the art of the absurd exhausted.

Peng Zu and ordinary people represent the short and long spans of human life. To compare ordinary people to Peng Zu is to make their lot pitiable indeed. This speaks to the vast disparity between short and long lifespans, just as there is between lesser and greater knowledge.

This is what Tang’s inquiry of Ji amounted to:
The passage where Tang questions Ji serves as a parallel to the cicada and the fledgling mocking the roc. In the far northern reaches of the barren lands lies the Dark Sea, a celestial lake. There is a fish there, its breadth spanning several thousand *li*, its length unknown—its name is Kun. There is also a bird there, named Peng, its back like a great mountain, its wings like clouds hanging from the sky. It beats the whirling winds, rising ninety thousand *li*, piercing the clouds, bearing the blue heavens on its back, then setting its course southward, bound for the Southern Darkness.

The quail scoffs at it, saying: "Where is it off to? I leap and flutter upward, rising no more than a few yards before descending, wheeling among the mugwort and wormwood—this is the height of flight! Where is it off to?" Such is the distinction between the small and the great.

The term "ram's horns" describes the spiraling form of the whirling winds. *Dream Pool Essays* records: "In Wucheng County of Enzhou, a cyclone arose from the southwest, towering into the sky like ram's horns, and officials along with all the residents were swept into the clouds." *Miscellaneous Records* further states: "A family in Meizhou kept several hundred fish in a deep pond for over thirty years. One day, under a clear sky without thunder, the pond roared like a storm, and all the fish leapt upward in a spiral like ram's horns, vanishing without a trace." Both accounts capture the essence of Zhuangzi’s original meaning.

"Piercing the clouds at ninety thousand *li*"—at such heights, the clouds lie far below. "Barren land" refers to the saline-alkali flats, as in the *Book of Documents*: "the vast saline shores by the sea." The *yan* (鴳) is a quail, akin to the sparrow and cicada. Fan’s commentary cites the *Rites of the Duke of Zhou*, identifying the *yan* as a quail. Li Xun notes: "The *ru* (鴽) is the *yan*, which is the quail." The *Monthly Ordinances* of late spring state: "Field mice transform into quails." Thus, the *yan* is but a minor transformation, hence its comparison to the cicada and turtledove.

Moreover, Zhuangzi turns to the seashore to find a creature of slight transformation, illustrating how the cicada and turtledove mock the Peng. The turtledove, in its transformation, has lost its predatory instincts, while the cicada, barely freed from the mire, flits low between the elm and sandalwood, devoid of far-reaching vision. Their mockery is thus unsurprising—like rustic villagers who know nothing of great lords and sages.

Though the barren-land quail is small in nature, confined to mugwort and wormwood, it dwells by the sea, where the vastness of the cosmos and the boundlessness of wind and moon are ever before its eyes. Yet even it presumptuously ridicules the great Peng. In human terms, this mirrors those who, though they walk in the footsteps of sages, remain incorrigibly foolish, abandoning all hope of self-improvement.

The passage from *Tang’s Inquiry of Ji* that follows gathers the threads of the preceding text. The earlier account from *Qi Xie’s Records of the Strange* now gives way to *Tang’s Inquiry of Ji*, which presents the matter as factual. Previously, the size of the Kun was said to span "several thousand *li*," here its breadth is confirmed as "several thousand *li*." Earlier, the Peng’s back was described as "several thousand *li*," here it is likened to "a great mountain." Where before it was said to "beat the whirling winds and rise," here the phrase "ram’s horns" is added to depict its form.

This section expounds on the minor transformations of the cicada, turtledove, and barren-land quail, which mock the Peng’s ascent of ninety thousand *li*. The phrase "ninety thousand *li*" appears four times, with the primary aim of elucidating this line. It underscores that the Celestial Lake truly lies ninety thousand *li* from the heavens. The vastness of the void is boundless, and the spirit roams unobstructed, shattering the shallow, cramped views of the worldly and broadening the heart’s capacity for freedom.

Thus, the first passage sets forth a parable, while this section turns to a discussion of human nature.

Those who take pride in fulfilling a single office, matching their conduct to a single village, aligning their virtue with a single lord, or winning the trust of a single state—such men regard themselves no differently than the cicada, turtledove, or barren-land quail. What sets them apart from those small-minded creatures? Yet even Song Rongzi would laugh at them.

Moreover, when the whole world praises him, he feels no added encouragement; when the whole world condemns him, he feels no added discouragement. He has settled the distinction between inner and outer, discerned the boundary between honor and disgrace—this alone suffices. In his dealings with the world, he is not restless or eager. Still, he has yet to establish himself fully.

The phrase "yet even" describes his mocking demeanor, akin to the sense of "still." The earlier group mocked the great with the small; Song Rongzi, in turn, mocks them by mocking the small with the great. Not only can he laugh at the former group, but he also embodies what follows. "Not restless or eager" means he is not anxious, while "establish" means to stand firm. Song Rongzi is not swayed by others’ praise or blame; the division of inner and outer, the boundary of honor and disgrace, are clear in his heart. He believes his self-possession is sufficient. Though he is not anxious about worldly affairs, he has yet to stand firm in solitude.

Liezi could ride the wind and travel, soaring lightly and skillfully, returning only after fifteen days. Yet even he, in seeking good fortune, was not restless or eager. Though he avoided walking, he still depended on something. But one who rides the constancy of Heaven and Earth, harnesses the transformations of the six qi, and roams boundlessly—what does he depend on?

"Lightly" conveys the clarity of the wind; "skillfully" means he excels at it. "Fifteen days" refers to half a month, a span far briefer than the Peng’s half-year breath. "Seeking good fortune" means he relies on the wind to travel, and the wind’s rising is his fortune. "Not restless or eager" means he is not anxious to obtain the wind as his fortune.

One who rides is the master, while one who harnesses is the assistant. The constancy of Heaven and Earth is the Supreme Ultimate moving to generate yang and settling to generate yin. What humans receive to live is called by Daoists the "primordial qi." The six qi—yin and yang, wind and rain, darkness and light—include the Jueyin wind-wood, Shaoyin sovereign fire, Shaoyang ministerial fire, Taiyin damp-earth, Yangming dry-metal, and Taiyang cold-water. Though their names differ, their essence is the same, dispersed throughout Heaven and Earth and present within the human body.

To take constancy as the master and the six qi as the harness is akin to Laozi’s image of thirty spokes sharing one hub. Liezi surpasses Song Rongzi, yet he still depends on something. This group has yet to achieve complete transformation. But one who rides the constancy of Heaven and Earth, harnesses the transformations of the six qi, and spirit-roams without limit—all is drawn from within, so what need is there for external dependence? At this stage, all is transformed.

The following passage refers to those who attain this:

Thus, the perfected person has no self, the spirit-person has no merit, the sage has no fame.

Previous interpretations mistakenly treat these three lines as a conclusion to the above, but they actually introduce what follows. Having described two groups whose transformations are limited, this passage now turns to three groups whose transformations are vast. To be great and transformed is called sageliness; to be sagely and unfathomable is called spirit; the ultimate is called perfection. These three ranks also have degrees of depth. Without merit, there is no achievement, and thus no fame. Without self, even the self vanishes—how then could there be achievement?

The following passages provide evidence for each:

Xu You is a sage; the ethereal immortals of Mount Gushe are spirit-persons; the four masters are perfected persons.

When Yao offered the world to Xu You, he said: "The sun and moon have risen, yet the torch still burns—is it not difficult for it to shine? The timely rain has fallen, yet you still irrigate—is it not laborious to bring moisture? You stand and the world is ordered, yet I still occupy your place. I see my own inadequacy—please, let me yield the world to you."

Xu You replied: "You govern the world, and the world is already well-governed. If I were to replace you, would I do so for fame? Fame is but the guest of reality—would I become a guest?"

The wren nests in the deep forest, yet needs no more than a single branch; the mole drinks from the river, yet takes no more than a full belly. Return and rest, my lord—what use have you for the world? Though the cook neglects his kitchen, the invocator does not leap over the wine vessels and sacrificial stands to take his place.

Xu You withdrew to Mount Ji. The Grand Historian says: I ascended Mount Ji, and atop it lies the tomb of Xu You.

*Stand* means to rise. *Corpse* means master. *Inadequate* means insufficient. Yao said that if Xu You were to rise, the world would be well-governed, yet he himself still occupied the position—seeing his own inadequacy, he could not compare to Xu You.

*Name* is the guest of *reality*. Reality is the host, while name is the guest. "Would I become a guest?"—he refused to pursue fame.

The *jiao'ao* resembles a small yellow sparrow, also called *jiaoqiu*, or *tao que*; it is the "tiao insect" of the *Book of Songs*, commonly said to give birth to eagles. The *yan shu* is the mole, a large rat.

"Return and rest, my lord"—to rest is to cease. When Yao visited Xu You, Xu You told him to return and set aside the matter of yielding the world. The repetition of "my lord" at the end conveys his deep respect.

This explains that the sage has no name, hence his words: "Would I become a name? Name is the guest of reality—would I become a guest?"

Jian Wu asked Lian Shu: "I heard words from Jie Yu that were vast but without measure, going forth but never returning. I was alarmed and terrified by his speech—it was like the Milky Way, boundless and without end, wildly divergent and far from human sentiment." Lian Shu said: "What did his words say?"

He said: "On the distant Mount Miaogu—*Miaogu* as in Song Guangping’s *Rhapsody on the Plum Blossom*, where it is glossed as *ye*—there dwells a spirit-person. Their skin is like ice and snow, their bearing supple as a maiden’s. They eat no grain, but drink the wind and sip the dew. They ride the clouds and qi, mount flying dragons, and roam beyond the four seas. Their spirit is so concentrated that they keep things free from blight and pestilence, and the yearly harvests ripen.

I deem this mad and unbelievable."

Lian Shu said: "Just as the blind cannot partake in the sight of patterns, nor the deaf in the sound of bells and drums, is it only the body that can be blind and deaf? The mind, too, may suffer such afflictions. These words of his are like *this maiden here*—these eight characters form a single phrase.

That person, that power of theirs, sweeps across the ten thousand things to unify them. The world seeks order, yet who would exhaust themselves over the affairs of the world? That person—nothing harms them. Though the great floods rise to the heavens, they do not drown; though great droughts melt metal and stone and scorch the mountains, they do not burn. Their dust and husks could still mold and cast a Yao or Shun—who would trouble themselves with worldly affairs?

The names of persons in Zhuangzi’s words may be real or fictitious; we cannot know whether Jian Wu and Lian Shu were historical, but Jie Yu was the Madman of Chu. Hence the following line: *I deem this mad and unbelievable.* ‘Going forth but never returning’ means speaking without ever looking back. *Jing* is the path before the gate; *ting* is the ground outside the hall. *Greatly divergent* means that while the path and courtyard are not far apart, Jie Yu’s words differ so vastly from ordinary speech that they seem as distant as the path is from the courtyard.

Mount Miaogu appears in the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*. *Naoyue* means pure and refined. *Chuzi* means maiden. *They keep things free from blight and pestilence, and the yearly harvests ripen* describes the transformative influence of their presence. *These words of his are like this maiden here*—these eight characters should be read as a single phrase, referring to Jie Yu. *You* means ‘like’; *shi* means ‘this’; *nü* means maiden, continuing the earlier description of the spirit-person’s bearing as *supple as a maiden’s*. As in the following line, where the word *yan* (words) is followed by *ye* (is), this is a rhetorical softening—reading it as two separate phrases would be incorrect.

*Bangbo* is the sound of rolling stones, describing their power to set the ten thousand things in motion. *Seeking order* means desiring governance. *Bibi* means weary and burdened. *Dust and husks could still mold and cast a Yao or Shun*—what Yao and Shun attained was what the spirit-person discards. This speaks of the spirit-person’s lack of merit. It is said, *there dwells a spirit-person*, and again, *they keep things free from blight and pestilence, and the yearly harvests ripen*; *sweeps across the ten thousand things to unify them*; *the world seeks order*—all speak of merit.

The people of Song took ceremonial caps to Yue, but the Yue people cut their hair and tattooed their bodies, having no use for them. When Yao governed the people of the world and brought peace to the seas, he went to see the Four Masters on Mount Miaogu by the north bank of the Fen River, and there he was so overwhelmed that he forgot his rule over the world. The Four Masters need not be identified by name. Fenyang was Yao’s capital. After meeting the Four Masters on Mount Miaogu, Yao returned to the north bank of the Fen River, where he was as if bereft of his rule over the world—seeing the Four Masters had made him lose himself.

This first presents an analogy: the Yue people cut their hair and tattoo their bodies, so what use do they have for the ceremonial caps of Song? The Four Masters lived in reclusion in the mountains—what did they care for Yao’s governance? This speaks of the perfected person’s selflessness. The Four Masters knew no self, and when Yao saw them, he too lost what was his own.

Hui Shi said to Zhuangzi: "The King of Wei gave me seeds of a great gourd. I planted them, and they grew to hold five *dan*. When filled with water or broth, its strength was not enough to lift itself. I split it open to make ladles, but they were too shallow and wobbled, holding little. Was it not vast and hollow? Yet I smashed it for its uselessness."

Zhuangzi said: "You are truly inept at putting great things to use. There was a man of Song skilled in making an unchapping salve, and his family made their living for generations by washing and bleaching silk floss. A traveler heard of it and offered a hundred gold pieces for the recipe. The family gathered and deliberated: ‘We have washed and bleached silk floss for generations, earning only a few pieces of gold. Now we can sell this skill for a hundred gold in a single morning—let us give it to him.’ The traveler obtained it and presented it to the King of Wu. When Yue raised a rebellion, the King of Wu made him a general. In winter, they fought on the water, and he routed the Yue forces. The king then enfeoffed him with land. The same ability to prevent chapping hands led one to a fief and another to a life of washing silk floss—the difference lay in how it was used.

Now you have a gourd that holds five *dan*—why not consider making it into a great float and drifting upon the rivers and lakes? Instead, you fret that it is too shallow and wobbles, holding little. Then your mind is still like the tangled *peng* plant—bound to the thickets of the hills and marshes, unable to float upon the rivers and lakes."

This speaks of the use of a single vessel not yet transformed—if one were to float upon the rivers and lakes, it would be transformed.

Hui Shi said to Zhuangzi: "I have a great tree—people call it the *chu*. Its trunk is gnarled and swollen, not fitting the inked line; its branches are twisted and curled, not fitting the compass or square. It stands by the road, but carpenters do not glance at it. Now your words are vast but useless—everyone rejects them alike."

Zhuangzi said: "Have you never seen the wildcat or the weasel? The *li sheng* is the weasel, shaped like a flying squirrel, reddish-yellow, with a large tail. It can devour mice—commonly called the *mouse-catcher*."

Guo Pu states: "In the region east of the Yangtze, it is called *sheng*." Crouching low, it lies in wait for the wandering creatures—*ao* (a term for creatures that roam freely, such as fowl and rodents). Leaping east and west, it bounds without regard for high or low, only to be caught in the snare’s trap—*pi* (entering the mechanism, as if subjected to punishment). It dies in the hunter’s net.

Now consider the *li* ox—its size like clouds hanging from the heavens. This creature may be great, yet it cannot catch mice. The *li sheng*, though small, can capture prey but instead meets harm. The ox, vast yet unable to seize mice, preserves its life. Now you possess a great tree—why not plant it in the land of Nothing Whatever, the boundless wilds? Wander idly by its side, recline carefree beneath its shade. Unfelled by axes, untouched by harm, though of no use—where then lies its suffering?

This speaks of the use of a single tree not yet transformed—if one were to plant it in the land of Nothing Whatever, the boundless wilds, it would be transformed.

This chapter, titled "Free and Easy Wandering," is threaded throughout by a single character: *transformation*. The first section describes the transformations of the *kun*-peng, the cicada, the fledgling dove, and the quail—each differing in scale, and thus their flight in height and depth. The second section speaks of human transformation, which likewise varies in magnitude, and thus the quality of their free and easy wandering. The third section shows that one may transform the useless into the useful and thereby achieve free and easy wandering.

For what Heaven bestows is fixed in measure—how could the cicada, fledgling dove, or quail be forced to match the *kun*-peng? Yet among humans, whether wise or foolish, worthy or unworthy, all may ascend the great Dao. Some, however, see themselves as the cicada, fledgling dove, or quail. Thus, at the chapter’s end, it is made clear: though one may resemble the hollow gourd, too frail to lift, or the *chu* tree—gnarled, swollen, and twisted—if one adapts to one’s nature and transforms with circumstance, how could one fail to achieve free and easy wandering?

*The Classic of Southern Florescence, Following the Original Text, Scroll the First* (concluded)
*The Classic of Southern Florescence, Following the Original Text, Scroll the Second*

**Lu Lingzhu Peak’s Luo Miandao, Recorded by Disciple Peng Xiang (Collated Text)**

**Inner Chapters: On the Equality of Things (Part One)**

During the Warring States period, scholarship was in disarray, and disputes over right and wrong swarmed like bees. The ways of the world and the hearts of men had sunk into deep delusion. Confucians sought to silence heterodox doctrines and bar perverse conduct to safeguard the Way of the former sages. The teachings of Laozi and Zhuangzi, however, simply preserved their innate nature, never contending with others. Affirming what was right because it was right, rejecting what was wrong because it was wrong—thus, by allowing their inequalities to find their own equilibrium, they achieved harmony.

This chapter largely engages with Master Gongsun Long, addressing questions such as "Is this right because that is wrong?" or "Is this wrong because that is right?"—debates over "white horses are not horses," the hardness and whiteness of stone, and the sameness or difference of things, all of which stem from Gongsun Long’s arguments.

Nanguo Ziqi leaned against his armrest, sat in stillness, lifted his gaze to the heavens, and exhaled deeply. His breath issued forth with a sound like *ta*, as if he had lost his other half. Nanguo Ziqi’s posture—leaning low over the armrest—was one of deep repose. *Xu* describes the act of opening the mouth to release breath; *ta* signifies the closing of the lips. One moment he bowed forward over the armrest, the next he tilted his head back to exhale, then suddenly closed his mouth. In an instant, three transformations unfolded, vivid as a painting. "Losing his other half" refers to the spirit’s pairing with form; here, he had cast off ears, eyes, and bodily frame as if dead.

Yan Cheng Ziyou stood in attendance before him and asked, "What is this state you have entered?" Could one’s form truly be made like withered wood, and one’s heart like dead ashes? The one who now leans upon the armrest is not the same as the one who did so before.

Yan Cheng Ziyou was a disciple of Ziqin, surnamed Yan, given name Yan, styled Ziyou, posthumously honored as Cheng. The word "could" expresses his astonishment at the possibility. He marveled that a person’s form and heart could be brought to such a state, finding it both strange and admirable. The "before" refers to the moment when Ziqi leaned upon the armrest and gazed heavenward—then, he had seemed alive; now, he appeared as if dead.

Ziqi replied, "Yan, is it not fitting that you ask this? Is it not fitting that I, in this state, should be questioned? Now, I have lost myself—do you understand?" The *shen* (spirit) is "I," the *xing* (form) is "me." Though *wu* (I), *wo* (me), and *ji* (self) may seem interchangeable, *wo* and *ji* still carry a sense of personal attachment. Thus, Confucius spoke of "overcoming the self" when instructing Yan Yuan. Daoist cultivation refines the primordial spirit, regarding the body as something to be cast aside. In the moment when Ziqi’s breath issued forth with *ta*, his spirit withdrew into stillness, and both body and heart dissolved. Hence, he said, "Now, I have lost myself." The question "Do you understand?" was Ziqi’s way of turning the inquiry back to Ziyou, then proceeding to explain.

"You have heard the *lai* of man but not the *lai* of earth; you have heard the *lai* of earth but not the *lai* of heaven. The *lai* of man leads to the *lai* of earth, and the *lai* of earth leads to the *lai* of heaven. If you were to hear the discourse on the *lai* of earth and heaven, you would understand why I have lost myself."

Ziyou said, "May I ask the method?"

Ziqi replied, "The great clod—when it exhales its breath, called wind, nothing stirs. But when it stirs, the myriad hollows roar in fury. Have you alone never heard the long, sighing gusts? The great clod is heaven and earth. *Canliao* is the sound of the ceaseless wind, which, when prolonged, reaches far and wide. The mountain forests bend and sway, the great trees of a hundred spans hollowed with cavities—some like noses, some like mouths, some like ears, some like beams, some like rings, some like mortars. Noses, mouths, and ears resemble human forms; beams are stacked with gaps; rings are round and hollow within; mortars are sunken in the center—these resemble the shapes of vessels. Some resemble *jia* (pools), some resemble *wu* (puddles). *Jia* are where water gathers in ox tracks; *wu* are where water flows into depressions—these resemble the forms of earth. Some sounds are rushing, some whistling, some hissing, some inhaling, some wailing, some howling, some deep, some shrill. The first group describes shapes; this group describes sounds. *Ji* is like the rush of water; *xiao* is like the whir of an arrow in flight; *chi* is like a shout; *xi* is like a gasp; *jiao* is like a cry; *hao* is like a lament; *yao* is like a deep murmur; *yao* is like the caw of a crow. The first sounds lead, and the following sounds respond. *Yu* and *gan* are interchangeable—Han Fei said, "The *gan* is the chief of the five notes." *Yu* describes the chorus of hollows, like fish mouths gaping in unison. A gentle breeze brings a faint harmony; a whirlwind brings a mighty harmony. When the fierce wind subsides, all the hollows fall silent.

The words "leading" and "responding" illustrate the nature of *lai*. Have you alone never seen the *tiaotiao* and *diaodiao*? *Tiaotiao* and *diaodiao* describe the swaying of treetops in the wind, a gentle, trembling motion. Today, people colloquially call a light breeze "the *tiaotiao* wind." Tea-pickers refer to tender shoots as *diao*, plucking them with a *diaodiao* motion. This passage describes the *lai* of earth: when the wind rises, there are many sounds; when it ceases, all is suddenly still. Human life is just like this—this leads into the following discussion.

Ziyou said, "The *lai* of earth is the chorus of the myriad hollows; the *lai* of man is the harmony of pipes and flutes. May I ask about the *lai* of heaven?"

Ziqi replied, "The wind blows in ten thousand different ways, yet each finds its own tone and measure. All things take their forms as they will—who is it that stirs them to anger?" The word "self" here corresponds to the earlier "me." Heaven’s generation of things is like the blowing of the wind. To depict the *lai* of heaven, the text employs the word "blow." Though the wind blows in ten thousand different ways, it allows each to arise as if of its own accord, with the Creator having no part in it. All these manifold transformations are self-generated, their forceful emergence—who, then, is responsible?

The term "anger" (*nu*) must not be narrowly understood as human emotion; it signifies the violent emergence of manifold transformations, like the wind’s fierce onslaught that rouses the myriad hollows to a sudden clamor.

Great wisdom is expansive (*xianxian*), small wisdom is divisive (*jianjian*).
Grand speech blazes (*yanyan*), petty chatter drones on (*zhanzhan*).
*Xianxian* means layered defense upon defense.
*Jianjian* means gap upon gap.
*Yanyan* describes a scorching, overpowering presence.
*Zhanzhan* conveys incessant, nagging insistence.
This speaks of the differences in people’s understanding and speech.

In sleep, the *hun*-souls intermingle; in waking, the body opens to the sounds of instruction, engaging with things in ceaseless construction, daily waging battle in the heart.
Shao Kangjie said: The spirit (*shen*) is the master of man. When one is about to sleep, it resides in the spleen; in deep sleep, it rests in the kidneys; when about to wake, it stirs in the liver; and upon full waking, it settles in the heart. What Zhuangzi calls the *hun* is what Kangjie terms the *shen*. In sleep, the spirit converges in the kidneys, and the body is still; in waking, the spirit abides in the heart, and the body moves. Upon waking and encountering things, countless schemes arise—this is elaborated below.

*Man* (slow, tangled) is pronounced like *mo* (莫) and *ban* (半) combined.
*Man* refers to undecorated silk, likening it to schemes so intricate they remain unseen. The *Shengyun Song* in the *Book of Rites* says, "The rites are *manman* (错综) indeed," also invoking the sense of intricate interweaving.
*Jiong* (cramped) and *mi* (dense).
*Jiong* means digging into the earth to conceal things.
*Mi* means fine and close-knit.
These three phrases describe the machinations of men, their syntax paralleling the earlier "impetuous" (*ji*) and "resonant" (*xiao*).

Small fears leave one trembling (*zhuizhui*), great fears leave one entangled (*manman*).
In minor fear, one trembles and cannot hold oneself together; in great fear, one’s schemes become like woven threads.
The syntax here parallels the earlier "small harmony."

Their eruption is like the release of a crossbow’s trigger—this describes their judgment of right and wrong.
Their outward discharge is like an arrow loosed from a bow, the trigger (*ji gua*) inevitably finding its mark. Such is their fixation on right and wrong.

Their withering is like autumn and winter, speaking of their daily decline.
Their drowning in what they do makes it impossible for them to return.
Their sorrowful decay is like autumn’s desolation, speaking of how their hearts, scorched by toil and thought, daily waste away. This is further emphasized: such is the result of their submersion in worldly desires, rendering them incapable of self-reflection.

Their stifling and sinking are like a sealed letter, speaking of their aging in the mire.
The heart near death cannot be revived.
Their suffocation is like a bound and fastened letter, speaking of how they end their days in the ditches of craving. This is further emphasized: such is the heart nearing death, beyond restoration.

From "their eruption like the release of a crossbow’s trigger" to "beyond revival," the meaning parallels "when the fierce wind subsides, the myriad hollows fall silent"—have you not seen the swaying and trembling of reeds?

Here, the text plainly declares the collapse of moral instruction. Yet below, joy, anger, sorrow, and delight suddenly arise again, revealing the surplus of its literary power.

Joy, anger, sorrow, and delight; anxiety, sighing, transformation, and vexation; attachment, composure, abandon, and poise.
The character *zhi* (慹) combines "grasp" (*zhi* 执) and "heart" (*xin* 心), signifying a heart bound and constrained.
*Yao* means governance; *yi* means dissipation; *qi* means the opening of clarity; *tai* means demeanor. Xunzi likewise said: "None fail to be beautiful and alluring, adorned in strange garments and womanly finery, with blood, *qi*, demeanor, and bearing."
Confucian texts speak only of the seven emotions, but Zhuangzi expands them to twelve.

Music emerges from emptiness; mushrooms form from steam.
These twelve are like music arising from the void or morning mushrooms sprouting from earth’s vapor. The pitch-pipes begin with the generation of one from nothing, then proceed from one to three, to nine, passing through the twelve earthly branches to reach 177,147—the true number of the Yellow Bell. From this, the twelve pitches are derived through reduction and augmentation. This is what is meant by "music emerging from emptiness" and "mushrooms forming from steam"—both born of the void, serving to depict the *lai* of heaven.

Day and night succeed one another before us, yet none know where they sprout.
Enough, enough!

At dawn and dusk we attain this—from whence does it arise?
A sigh, and words: enough, enough!
Mortals rest content with this, attaining it but at dawn and dusk, yet mistaking it for the very source of their being.

This passage speaks of heaven’s *lai*.
Heaven’s generation of humanity produces manifold emotions—
just as when the wind stirs, manifold sounds arise.
From the opening of the chapter to this point, Zhuangzi recounts the dialogue between Ziqi and his disciple, and what follows expands upon this theme.

Without that, there is no self; without the self, nothing is received.
This comes close, yet one knows not what compels it.
As if a true master existed, though no trace of it appears above.
Its efficacy is trustworthy, yet its form remains unseen—it has power but no shape.

Taking Ziqi’s "the wind blows in ten thousand different ways, yet each finds its own accord" and extracting its essence:
the argument that without that there is no self corresponds to "the wind blows in ten thousand different ways, yet each finds its own accord";
without the self, nothing is received corresponds to "each finds its own accord."
Without that creator, there would be no burden of self; without this self, how could one grasp so many transformations?

Ziqi’s explanation comes close, yet still falls short—
it fails to reach the point where these transformations are commanded.
That which commands and is obeyed is as if a true master existed.
This true master is the wondrous truth of the Limitless, the union of the two qi and the five phases that constitute human life.

Within the human body resides this true master, and thus blood and qi are commanded,
the manifold transformations gathered in stillness, though no sign or omen is discernible.
There is no place to apply effort, yet its efficacy is verifiable—
the person who attains the Dao may deeply believe in it, yet ultimately cannot grasp it.
Though there is the union of Kan and Li, the embrace of mother and child,
and lingering affection, its essence remains formless.
This is the great Daoist principle from which the alchemical classics arise.

The hundred bones, nine orifices, six viscera—
the kidneys alone are two—all abide within, yet with whom shall I be intimate?
Do you delight in all equally?
Or is there partiality?

Are all these but servants and handmaids?
Are servants and handmaids insufficient to govern one another?
Do they take turns as lord and subject?
Yet there is a true lord abiding within.

Turning inward to my own body,
the hundred bones, nine orifices, and six viscera—none are not fully present.
With whom shall I be intimate?
Again posing the question to myself:
Do you delight in all equally?
Or is there partiality?
There is no principle of partial love.

If this be so, then are all but servants and handmaids?
If all are servants and handmaids, they are insufficient to govern one another,
and must alternate as lord and subject.
Yet in winding through these doubts and difficulties, the conclusion remains:
there is a true lord abiding within.
It is the true lord that commands the hundred bones, nine orifices, and six viscera.
The true lord is the true master—here only the terms "lord" and "subject" vary.

Whether one attains this reality or not,
it neither benefits nor harms the true.
Once receiving this completed form,
it does not perish until its end.
This "reality" is the reality of having form yet being formless.
Whether one attains this reality or not,
it remains within the true,
neither diminishing nor enhancing it—ever thus.

From the moment one receives form, this true essence exists,
born together with life, never lost,
awaiting only the exhaustion of form.
Yet in contending and yielding with things,
one’s actions race to their end like a galloping horse,
and none can halt them—is this not lamentable?
A lifetime of toil without seeing success,
wearily laboring without knowing one’s destination—how can this not be pitiful?
People call this "not dying," but what benefit is there?
When form transforms, the heart transforms with it—
can this not be called great sorrow?

"Contending" means mutual harm;
"yielding" means mutual submission.
Form bears the true, and when form transforms, the heart transforms with it.
What was said to "not perish" is also exhausted.
Thus it is said: "Is this not lamentable?"
Again: "How can this not be pitiful?"
And again: "Can this not be called great sorrow?"
The words grow ever more poignant, its warning to the world ever deeper.

Is human life inherently so deluded?
Am I alone deluded, while others are not?
"Deluded" means ignorant and unknowing.

This passage follows Zhuangzi’s discourse on the heavenly pipes,
pointing to the original endowment of the true master at life’s inception.
The subsequent sections vividly expound on right and wrong,
showing that such disputes are futile—
one need only clarify this true master.

If one follows the completed heart and takes it as teacher,
who alone would lack a teacher?
Why must one know the alternation of days and nights to realize the heart’s self-appropriation?
Even the foolish possess this.
Before the heart is formed and yet there is right and wrong,
it is like arriving in Yue today yet claiming to have arrived yesterday—
turning nothing into something.
Even if the divine Yu existed, he could not know such things—
how much less could I?

"Following the completed heart and taking it as teacher"—
this continues from the exchange between Ziqi and his disciples.
"Why must one know the alternation of days and nights to realize the heart’s self-appropriation"—
this follows from the idea that day and night alternate, each appropriating its time.
The "completed heart" is the heart governed by the true master,
the lord of all affairs.
Without this lord, the heart wanders east and west, never settled.
Those who study the Way need not seek teachers externally—
turning inward, the heart itself is sufficient.
This true master resides in all people,
present even in the ignorant and unknowing,
not only in those who understand the principle of day and night alternating, each self-appropriating.
Even the foolish possess it.

If one does not know where the true master resides,
and the heart is not yet formed yet already engages in disputes over right and wrong,
it is like setting out for Yue today yet claiming to have arrived yesterday—
is there any such principle?
Even the divine Yu could not turn nothing into something to know the affairs of the world—
how much less could I?

Speech is not like the wind’s blowing.
Speakers have words, yet what they say remains unsettled.
Is there truly speech?
Or has there never been speech?
Does it differ from the chirping of fledglings?
Is there distinction, or is there none?

This continues from the idea that the myriad things are not blown by the same wind.
Human speech is not like the heavenly pipes’ blowing of the ten thousand things, which is without intent.
Rather, it is the words of speakers.
Once issuing from people, how can it be free of partiality?
Thus its words remain unsettled, unreliable as a standard.
Being unsettled, people regard it as if it were nothing.
Hence it is said: Is there truly speech?
Or has there never been speech?

Fledglings just hatched from the egg—
people hear the sounds of birds:
magpies herald good fortune, crows ill fortune,
storks’ cries foretell rain, cuckoos’ urge plowing—
these can be taken as reliable.
But the chirping of fledglings is unsettled and cannot be taken as reliable.
Human speech, being unsettled, is likewise.

Where is the Dao hidden that it has truth and falsehood?
Where is speech hidden that it has right and wrong?
Where does the Dao go that it does not abide?
Where does speech abide that it cannot be relied upon?
The Dao is hidden in minor accomplishments,
speech is hidden in florid embellishment.
Thus arise the disputes of Confucians and Mohists,
each affirming what the other denies and denying what the other affirms.
To affirm what the other denies and deny what the other affirms,
nothing is better than illumination.

Summarizing the above: the Dao and speech are made the subject of discussion—
what is called the "completed heart" is the Dao.
The great Dao is originally without truth or falsehood—
why is it hidden and thus has truth and falsehood?
Perfect speech is originally without right or wrong—
why is it obscured and thus has right and wrong?
Where has the Dao gone that it does not abide?
Why does speech abide yet cannot be relied upon?
These are rhetorical questions, answered thus:
the Dao is hidden in the arts of minor accomplishments,
speech is hidden in superficial and insubstantial doctrines,
hence the mutual disputes of the Confucian and Mohist schools.

As the Mohists deem the Confucians wrong, they naturally regard themselves as right. The Confucians, in turn, regard themselves as right and thus deem the Mohists wrong. Each affirms what the other denies and denies what the other affirms—this is the root of the endless disputes between the Confucians and Mohists. To further clarify: rather than striving to affirm what the other denies and deny what the other affirms, it is better to illumine one’s true master—meaning, to attend to one’s own cultivation and cease idle disputation over right and wrong.

The preceding passage concludes: "Human life is inherently thus obscured." If I alone am obscured, do others not also suffer the same obscurity? Thus, this passage concludes: "nothing is better than illumination." Illumination means knowing where the true master resides; the obscured do not know this.

The following two sections reiterate this point, deepening its emphasis. Things are not without their "that"; things are not without their "this." From the perspective of "that," one does not see; from the perspective of knowing, one knows. Thus it is said: "That emerges from this, and this also arises from that." This is the doctrine of the mutual arising of "that" and "this."

The previous section spoke only of right and wrong, yet right and wrong truly arise from the opposition of self and other. Thus, this section takes "that" and "this" as its subject. "Things are not without their 'that'" means all things possess self and other—what is "that" to me is "that" to another, and what is "I" to me is "that" to another. "Things are not without their 'this'" means all things possess right and wrong—what is right to another is "this" to me, and what is right to me is "this" to another.

"From the perspective of 'that,' one does not see" means that from another’s viewpoint, one does not perceive their "that." Why is this so? Because the other regards their "that" as "I," and thus does not see it as "that." If the other takes their wrong as right, they do not see what is truly right. "From the perspective of the self, one knows" means that from one’s own viewpoint, one perceives the other’s "that." Seeing where the other is not right, one knows what is truly right. This shows that one must not be self-blinded, as others may not know themselves—yet from the self’s perspective, one knows.

"That emerges from this" means that my designation of the other as "that" and the other’s designation of me as "that" both arise from self-affirmation. Thus, when I affirm myself, I ascribe wrong to the other and call them "that." When the other affirms themselves, they ascribe wrong to me and call me "that." "This also arises from that" means that my self-affirmation and the other’s self-affirmation both depend on the division between self and other. When I take the other as "that," I necessarily ascribe wrong to them and regard myself as right. When the other takes me as "that," they necessarily ascribe wrong to me and regard themselves as right. Thus, "that" and "this" arise endlessly.

Yet even as they arise, they perish; even as they perish, they arise. Even as they are affirmed, they are denied; even as they are denied, they are affirmed. Though the doctrine of "that" and "this" arises, it is appended with "even as they arise," followed by a turn: "though..." For where there is arising, there is perishing; where there is perishing, there is arising. Where there is affirmation, there is denial; where there is denial, there is affirmation. Viewed through the principle of cyclical recurrence, the doctrine of "that" and "this" must have a time of cessation, and what now cannot be equalized must have a time when it can.

Thus, one follows right because of wrong, and follows wrong because of right. Therefore, the sage does not proceed by this path but illumines it by the heavens—this, too, is following right. For now, one follows their right and wrong, awaiting their natural resolution. It must be reiterated: one follows right because of wrong, and follows wrong because of right. Having followed right, one also follows wrong; having followed wrong, one also follows right. If one follows only one without the other, one remains mired in contention. Thus, the sage does not tread the path of right and wrong but transcends beyond things. Or, if there is right and wrong, one merely illumines it by the heavens—meaning, whether right or wrong, the heavens themselves oversee it. This is also a way of following right. Though it speaks of following right and wrong, it then speaks only of following right—this is an ellipsis. The following text further clarifies the doctrine of following right.

"This" is also "that," and "that" is also "this." "That" has its own right and wrong, and "this" has its own right and wrong. Is there truly a "that" and "this"? Or is there truly no "that" and "this"? This is the sage’s way of following right. Whatever is "this" is also ascribed to "that," and whatever is "that" is also taken as "this." Thus, this entirely regards "that" as right and acknowledges its own wrong, while that entirely regards this as right and acknowledges its own wrong. Each has its own right and wrong—how, then, can they be divided into "that" and "this"?

When "that" and "this" find no counterpart, this is called the pivot of the Dao. The pivot attains the center of the ring, responding to the infinite. "This" is one infinity, and "not-this" is another infinity. When "that" and "this" do not oppose each other, there is no right and wrong—this is called the pivot of the Dao. Another explanation of the pivot: the pivot is the door’s bolt. Placed at the center of the ring, it cannot be fixed yet opens and closes endlessly. The sage holds the pivot of the Dao and simply listens to the endless right and wrong—this, too, is following right. Thus it is said: "nothing is better than illumination," echoing the earlier conclusion.

To use a finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger is not as good as using a non-finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger. To use a horse to illustrate that a horse is not a horse is not as good as using a non-horse to illustrate that a horse is not a horse. Heaven and earth are one finger; the myriad things are one horse.

What can be affirmed is affirmed; what cannot be affirmed is not affirmed. The Dao is enacted and becomes; things are named and thus are. How is it so? It is so because it is so. How is it not so? It is not so because it is not so. Things inherently have what they affirm; things inherently have what they accept. There is nothing that is not so; there is nothing that cannot be accepted.

This follows from the earlier passage on "what can be affirmed and what cannot be affirmed." During the Warring States period, there was a debate over whether a white horse is not a horse—hence the reference. It then turns to the finger on one’s own body to discuss both together.

This is the finger, yet debaters must insist: using what is not a finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger is superior to using the class of fingers to illustrate that it is not a finger. This is the horse, yet debaters must insist: using what is not a horse to illustrate that a horse is not a horse is superior to using the class of horses to illustrate that it is not a horse.

If heaven and earth are combined into one finger, then what people call a "finger" is no longer a finger. If the myriad things are summed into one horse, then what people call a "horse" is no longer a horse. From a perspective of attachment, one must regard a finger as a finger and a horse as a horse. From a perspective free of attachment, heaven and earth may be called a finger, and the myriad things may be called a horse. Thus, one must not cling to assertions of right and wrong. One should simply affirm what can be affirmed and reject what cannot be affirmed.

The Dao originally has no name; people enact it and thus establish the name of the Dao. Things originally have no name; people designate them and thus establish the names of things. Therefore, what is "so" and what is "not so" are merely human impositions. To affirm what is affirmed and deny what is denied is acceptable. Though things inherently possess their own principles—what is "so" and what is "acceptable" are also viewed as such—then nothing is not so, and nothing is unacceptable.

Here, *duan di* (端的) means "principle" or "definite basis." Thus, consider the examples of the rafter and the pillar, the ugly *Li* and the beautiful *Xishi*, the vast and the strange—all are united through the Dao. The rafter (*ting*) is the beam of a house; the pillar (*ying*) stands on the east and west sides of the hall. *Li* is an ugly person; *Xishi* is a beautiful one. The rafter lies horizontally while the pillar stands vertically; *Li* is hideous while *Xishi* is lovely. Though their forms are strange and varied, all are united through the Dao.

Their division is their completion; their completion is their destruction. All things are neither completed nor destroyed, for they return to unity. Their division is their fragmentation. What some regard as completion may be destruction for others. Thus, all things are neither completed nor destroyed; when reversed and united, they are but one. If one observes only from the perspective of completion, one does not see destruction. If one observes only from the perspective of destruction, one does not see completion. Observing the cycle of completion and destruction, one sees that completion may become destruction, and destruction may become completion—yet in reality, they are one.

Only the enlightened know that all is united, and thus they do not force their views but lodge them in the ordinary. The ordinary (*yong*) is use; use is unity; unity is attainment. Attaining this is near the subtlety of the Dao. Proceeding thus, acting without knowing why it is so—this is called the Dao.

The enlightened know that all is united, so they do not impose their views but lodge them in practical affairs. Lodging in practical affairs is use; use is unity. Unity is attainment in the heart. Having attained this, one approaches the Dao. This is what is meant by "proceeding thus." Acting habitually without knowing why—this is called the Dao.

To exhaust one’s spirit in forcing unity without recognizing their sameness is called "morning three." What is "morning three"? It is said: The monkey keeper distributed chestnuts, saying, "Three in the morning and four in the evening." All the monkeys were angry. He then said, "Very well, four in the morning and three in the evening." All the monkeys were pleased. The substance remained unchanged, yet joy and anger were employed—this, too, proceeds from the same principle.

Thus, the sage harmonizes with right and wrong and rests in the evenness of heaven—this is called "walking two paths." Those who labor their minds with one-sided views do not understand unity. Like the monkeys, who were unaware of their own reactions, the keeper accommodated their desires without altering the substance—this, too, follows the principle of "proceeding thus." Therefore, the sage affirms what is affirmed and denies what is denied, harmonizing them so there is no contention and all rests in the balance of heaven’s principle. This is called "walking two paths without contradiction."

The knowledge of the ancients reached its utmost. To what extent? To the point of believing there had never been any things—this is the utmost, the ultimate, beyond which nothing can be added. Next were those who believed things existed but had never been bounded. Next were those who believed boundaries existed but had never been right and wrong. The manifestation of right and wrong is the diminishment of the Dao. The diminishment of the Dao is the formation of attachment.

Following the preceding discussion of the sage, we now speak of the ancients. Those who believed there had never been any things were like the *wuji* (the Ultimate of Non-being). To know this is to reach the utmost, the ultimate, beyond which nothing can be added. The *Doctrine of the Mean* also says: "The workings of Heaven are without sound or scent—this is the utmost." If there had never been any things, how could there be right and wrong? Harmonizing right and wrong is the work of later sages; the ancients had no such distinctions. Is there truly completion and diminishment? Is there truly neither completion nor diminishment?

Completion and diminishment exist, and thus Master Zhao played the zither; completion and diminishment do not exist, and thus Master Zhao did not play the zither. When Master Zhao played the zither, when Music Master Kuang wielded his baton, and when Master Hui leaned against the parasol tree—these three men’s wisdom approached the subtlest point, all reaching their zenith, and thus their deeds were recorded in their later years. Only because they cherished their arts, distinguishing themselves from others, did they cherish them, desiring to make them manifest. Yet what they sought to illuminate was not meant to be illuminated, and so they ended in the obscurity of the "hard and white" debate. Moreover, Master Zhao’s son spent his life continuing his father’s legacy, yet in the end achieved nothing. Could this truly be called completion? If so, then I too have completed something; if not, then neither things nor I have ever completed anything. Thus, the radiance within confusion and doubt is what the sage contemplates. To refrain from employing such distinctions while lodging them in the ordinary—this is called "illumination by means of the Dao."

*Wen* was Master Zhao’s given name. *Zhi ce* refers to the baton used to play the zither. *Wu* denotes the parasol tree, from which zithers and lutes are made. *Ji* means "approaching." *Zai* means "to record." *Mo nian* refers to old age. *Lun* signifies "continuation."

To speak of completion and diminishment: if one assumes their existence, it is like Master Zhao playing the zither; if one assumes their absence, it is like Master Zhao not playing the zither. Let us take Master Zhao’s zither-playing as an example. Master Zhao’s skill, along with that of Music Master Kuang and Master Hui, approached the subtlest point of wisdom, all being consummate masters of their arts. That he could rival Music Master Kuang and Master Hui demonstrates his mastery of the zither. His lifelong dedication to this art stemmed from his deep love for it, desiring to distinguish himself from others. Yet his devotion aimed only to make his art manifest to the world. What he sought to illuminate was not meant to be illuminated, and so he ended in obscurity, like the "hard and white" debate, which begins with clarity but ends in confusion. Master Zhao achieved no true completion, and his son, who spent his life continuing his father’s work, likewise found no one to appreciate his art. From this perspective, had Master Zhao never learned to play the zither, how could there have been completion or diminishment? Only because he loved playing the zither did completion and diminishment arise. If we judge by present appearances: if such dedication can be called completion, then I too have completed something; if not, then neither things nor I have ever completed anything. Thus, within the confusion and doubt that obscures all distinctions, there lies a radiance—the very thing the sage contemplates. To refrain from employing such distinctions while lodging them in the ordinary is what is meant by "illumination by means of the Dao."

Here ends the second scroll of the *Xunben* edition of the *True Classic of Southern Florescence*.
Here begins the third scroll of the *Xunben* edition of the *True Classic of Southern Florescence*.

Here begins the *Inner Chapters: On the Equality of Things (Part Two)*, collated by Lu Lingzhu’s disciple Peng Xiang of Bamboo Peak in Luling.

Suppose I now speak of something—how could one know whether it belongs to the same category as this or not? Whether it belongs or does not belong, if both are taken as belonging, then it differs in no way from that. This passage follows from the earlier discussion of "those who have speech."

Now, if we take human speech—whether it resembles this or does not resemble this—all are alike in being speech. If this is deemed right, then that too is deemed right; if this is deemed wrong, then that too is deemed wrong. They are fundamentally alike. Or if this is deemed right while that is deemed wrong, they are still alike in their shared wrongness; if this is deemed wrong while that is deemed right, they are alike in their shared rightness. All are of one category, differing not from the other’s claims and thus beyond contention.

Yet let me attempt to speak of it: There is a beginning; there is that which precedes the beginning; there is that which precedes even that which precedes the beginning. There is being; there is non-being; there is that which precedes non-being; there is that which precedes even that which precedes non-being. Suddenly there is being and non-being, yet one cannot know which of the two truly is or is not. Now I have something to say, yet I do not know whether what I call it truly has meaning or truly has no meaning.

Let me attempt to speak of it, let me try to speak of it. What was spoken before was human speech; what I now say is my own speech. Yet, taking another turn, if we examine the origins of Heaven and Earth and the myriad things, there was neither being nor non-being, nor even the absence of non-being. The discourse of being and non-being arose only in later ages. Thus, human speech is not to be relied upon, nor is my own speech truly meaningful.

In all the world, nothing is greater than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small; nothing lives longer than a child who dies young, yet Peng Zu is short-lived. Heaven and Earth are born together with me, and the myriad things and I are one. Since we are already one, how could there be speech? Since we have already called it one, how could there be no speech? One and speech make two; two and one make three. Proceeding thus, even the most skilled mathematician cannot arrive at a sum—how much less the ordinary person? Therefore, from non-being to being, reaching three, how much more so from being to being? There is no need to proceed further; let it rest here.
