The tip of an autumn hair is inherently small, yet if we say the tip of an autumn hair cannot be further divided, it may be called great. Beyond Mount Tai, there are still greater things, so it may be called small. A child who dies young is inherently short-lived, yet if we name it as such, it has lived its years, so it may be called long-lived. Beyond Peng Zu, there are still longer-lived beings, so he may be called short-lived. But when viewed through Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things, there are no distinctions. Human life begins with a body that already contains a Heaven and Earth. Thus, Heaven and Earth are born together with me. There is nothing among the myriad things that is not so—thus, the myriad things and I are one body. What distinction is there between small and great, long-lived and short-lived?

Since we are one, there is no room for speech. If one insists on speaking of "one," then speech proliferates, and right and wrong become endless. Therefore, one should return to the origin of emptiness and non-being, resting in what is so—this responds to the earlier "let it rest here."

The Dao has never had boundaries; speech has never had constancy. Yet for the sake of this, distinctions arise. Here, the Dao and speech are set in contrast to boundaries, further refining the notion of limits.

Let me speak of these boundaries: there is left and right, there are ranks and principles, there are divisions and debates, there are competitions and contentions. These are called the eight virtues. *Ranks* refer to order; *principles* refer to appropriateness. Having established order, one must also ensure that each matter is fitting. *Divisions* refer to distinctions; *debates* refine them further. *Competition* pertains to the mind, while *contention* pertains to force. As the *Zuo Tradition* says: "Not competing in mind but contending in force."

Beyond the six directions, the sage preserves but does not discuss; within the six directions, the sage discusses but does not deliberate; regarding the *Spring and Autumn Annals* and the intentions of the former kings, the sage deliberates but does not debate. The *Spring and Autumn Annals* records the intentions of the former kings—it is a historical text. In the *Rites of Zhou*, the lesser scribe is charged with the records of the state. Thus, Heaven and Earth, past and present, are all that the sage does not speak of. *Deliberation* is more detailed than *discussion*; *debate* is more detailed than *deliberation*.

Therefore, where there are divisions, there are also non-divisions; where there are debates, there are also non-debates. One asks: Why? The sage embraces them.

The scroll is to be stored away. The multitude debate it to display to one another. Thus it is said: in debate, there are things unseen. One seeks to see them yet paradoxically does not.

The great Dao is not named; great disputation does not speak; great benevolence is not benevolent; great integrity is not modest—*qiàn* (慊) here means "to be sated with things," implying the heart’s attachment to them. Some are sated with satisfaction and sufficiency, others with resentment and lack. This speaks of how great integrity does not take integrity as self-sufficiency. Great courage does not harm.

When the Dao is made manifest, it ceases to be the Dao; when words are debated, they fall short; when benevolence is constant, it fails to achieve completion; when one relies on courage to harm others, it is no longer the true Dao. To display it is to render it insufficient as the Dao. To debate it is to leave gaps unaddressed. Confined by habitual attachments, benevolence becomes limited and fails to fulfill its nature. Integrity, though pure, is not trusted—self-proclaimed purity cannot win others’ faith. Courage, when used to harm, does not achieve greatness.

These five, when rounded, nearly approach the square. *Rounded* (圆) means circular, adaptable. The great Dao is not named, and great courage does not seek gratification. These five principles are fundamentally fluid and natural; when the Dao is made manifest, it ceases to be the Dao, and when courage harms, it fails to achieve greatness. Yet if these five are rigidly adhered to, they nearly become square.

Thus, to know where to stop in what one does not know is perfection. Who knows the disputation of the unspeaking, the Dao of the unspoken? If one can know this, it is called the Heavenly Treasury. It may be drawn from without ever being exhausted, poured from without ever being depleted, yet one knows not whence it comes—this is called *preserving illumination*. *Preserving* (葆) means to nurture. Before understanding this principle, one says: "Nothing compares to clarity." Once this principle is grasped, one nurtures its radiance.

This passage is divided into five sections: the first concludes with *obscurity* (芒), the second, third, and fourth with *clarity* (以明), and the fifth with *preserving illumination* (葆光). *Obscurity* is the reverse of clarity, yet both are forms of brilliance. The general meaning is that externally, one follows what is so, while internally, one naturally illuminates the truth. Thus, the phrase "follow what is so" appears five times, while "clarity" appears three.

Therefore, in ancient times, Yao asked Shun: "I wish to attack Zong, Kuai, and Xu’ao, yet when I face south, I feel unsettled. What is the reason?" Shun replied: "Those three rulers still dwell among the brambles and reeds. Why do you feel unsettled? In the past, when ten suns appeared together, all things were illuminated—how much more so for virtue that surpasses the sun?" Zong, Kuai, and Xu’ao were three states. Zong was one, Kuai two, and Xu’ao three. The "three rulers" were the lords of these states. *Still* (犹) means "yet"; *dwell* (存) means "to be present"; *you* (若) means "you." These three rulers, secluded in remote places, had not yet witnessed the transformative power of virtue, like being concealed among overgrown weeds. What cause is there for resentment? One should instead return to one’s own virtue. Even the sun has places it cannot illuminate; when ten suns shine together, nothing is left unlit. How much more so for virtue’s illumination, which far exceeds the sun’s? Though these three states are remote, they will eventually cut through the brambles and thorns to come and submit—why attack them?

Nie Que asked Wang Ni: According to the *Biographies of Lofty Scholars*, Wang Ni was a worthy man of Yao’s time. The *Heaven and Earth* chapter states he was Nie Que’s teacher. "Do you know what all things agree upon as right?" He replied:

"How could I know this? Then are things without knowledge?" He replied: "How could I know this? Nevertheless, let me try to speak of it: How can one be certain that what I call knowing is not not-knowing? How can one be certain that what I call not-knowing is not knowing? Now let me ask you this: When people sleep in damp places, they suffer from lower back pain or partial paralysis—is this not so? Yet loaches do not. When dwelling in trees, one trembles with fear—is this not so? Yet monkeys do not. Which of these three knows the proper place to dwell? People eat domesticated animals; deer eat grass. Centipedes relish snakes; owls delight in mice. Which of these four knows the proper taste? Apes mate with gibbons; deer couple with elk; loaches swim with fish. Mao Qiang and Lady Li were considered beauties by men, yet fish dive deep at the sight of them, birds soar high, and deer flee in haste. Which of these four knows the proper beauty of the world? From my perspective, the distinctions of benevolence and righteousness, the paths of right and wrong, are tangled and confused. How could I discern their distinctions?"

Nie Que said: "If you do not know benefit and harm, does the perfected person truly not know benefit and harm?" Nie Que took Wang Ni to be a perfected person, so he found it strange that he did not know.

Wang Ni replied: "The perfected person is divine. Great marshes may burn, yet they cannot be made hot; rivers and lakes may freeze, yet they cannot be made cold. Lightning may split mountains, winds may shake the seas, yet they cannot be startled. One such as this rides the clouds and qi, mounts the sun and moon, and roams beyond the four seas. Life and death do not alter them—how much less the distinctions of benefit and harm!"

Ju Quezi asked Chang Wuzi: "I have heard from the Master that the sage does not engage in worldly affairs, does not pursue profit, does not avoid harm, does not delight in seeking, does not follow the Way, is not constrained by adherence to the Way, speaks without speaking, and speaks of what is without speech. The Master considered these careless words, but I regard them as the practice of the wondrous Way. What do you think?"

Chang Wuzi replied: "Even the Yellow Emperor would have found this enlightening to hear, yet how could one like Qiu (Chang Wuzi’s name) comprehend it? Moreover, you are too hasty in your calculations—seeing an egg and demanding a rooster to crow at dawn, seeing a slingshot and craving roasted quail. Let me speak to you recklessly, and you may listen recklessly—how about that? Abiding by the sun and moon, embracing the cosmos, aligning the harmonious, setting aside the slippery and confused, and serving with reverence. The masses toil endlessly, while the sage appears foolish and dull, blending the myriad ages into a single purity. All things are thus, and this is how they are enfolded within one another. How do I know that delighting in life is not delusion? How do I know that hating death is not like a youth lost and unable to find his way home?"

The Lady of Li was the daughter of the border warden of Ai. Though not from the Li Rong tribe, she was still subject to the custom where men were esteemed and women held cheap. When the state of Jin first acquired her, she wept until her tears soaked her collar. Yet once she arrived at the royal court, sharing the king’s bed and feasting on fine meats, she came to regret her tears.

How do I know that the dead do not regret their former toil for life? One who dreams of drinking wine may wake weeping; one who dreams of weeping may wake to go hunting. In the midst of a dream, one does not know it is a dream. Within the dream, one may even interpret the dream—only upon waking does one realize it was a dream. And only with great awakening does one know that all this was a great dream. Yet fools believe themselves awake, priding themselves on their knowledge: "Are you a lord? A herdsman?"—thus dividing noble and base. How rigid! How shallow!

I and you are both dreaming—what I call your dream is also a dream. Even this discourse is a dream. Such words are called *diaogui*—utterly strange. Should a great sage arise in ten thousand generations to unravel this, it would be as if encountering him at dawn or dusk. To speak of ten thousand generations as dawn and dusk is to say such a one is rare—perhaps never to appear. Yet if he were to arise after ten thousand generations, it would still be as near as dawn or dusk.

If you and I were to debate, and you prevailed over me, or I over you—would you truly be right and I wrong? Or I right and you wrong? Or might we both be right, or both wrong? You and I cannot know. Others are likewise shrouded in darkness. How could we entrust judgment to another? If we sought one who agrees with you to decide, how could they judge fairly, being of your mind? If we sought one who agrees with me, how could they judge fairly, being of my mind? If we sought one who differs from both of us, how could they judge fairly, being unlike us? If we sought one who agrees with both of us, how could they judge fairly, being like us? Thus, neither you, nor I, nor others can know—must we await that sage of ten thousand generations?

What does it mean to harmonize with the *tianni*?

Ququezi posed the question, for this too is a common expression among Daoists.

Longwuzi replied: "Is" is not "is," "not" is not "not." If "is" were truly "is," then its difference from "not-is" would require no debate. If "so" were truly "so," then its difference from "not-so" would require no debate. In all matters, one should affirm what others deny; in all words, one should assent to what others reject. If what I affirm is truly affirmed, then this matter is settled—other matters, though denied, need not be contested for their difference. If what I assent to is truly assented to, then this word is settled—other words, though rejected, need not be contested for their difference.

Transforming voices depend on one another, yet seem not to depend. *Huasheng*—like the "influence of culture" in books, or the "voice of benevolence" Mencius spoke of—refers to those who await instruction to be transformed. How much finer to be transformed without awaiting instruction! To be free of debate is already excellent; to be free even of voice is yet a higher level.

To harmonize with the *tianni*: *Tian* means "natural," *ni* means "incipience." The moment a beginning emerges, it arises from the naturalness of *tian*, without awaiting its full manifestation—this is called harmonizing with the *tianni*. To follow this with *manyan*—with wandering and unfolding.

To exhaust one’s years thus—
Forgetting age, forgetting rightness,
one stirs into the boundless,
and so abides in the boundless.
*Stirs*: to arise.
*Abides*: to rest.
*Boundless*: the limitless.
Arising in the limitless, one rests in the limitless.

*Wangliang* asked the shadow, saying—
*Wangliang*: the faint darkness beyond the shadow, what was earlier called the shadow’s shadow.
It hovers indistinctly, as if there were a middle between it and the shadow, hence the name *wangliang*.
“Just now you were walking, now you are still;
just now you were sitting, now you rise.
Why are you so lacking in constancy?”

The shadow replied:
“Do I depend on something to be thus?
What I depend on is form.
Does what I depend on in turn depend on something else to be thus?
The movement of form—something else causes it to be so.
Do I depend on the snake’s belly-scales or the cicada’s wings?
The snake moves by its belly-scales, the cicada flies by its wings—yet once these creatures shed their forms, they become shapes that can neither walk nor fly.
The form I depend on is like the snake’s scales or the cicada’s wings: once the spirit departs, it can no longer move of itself.
How could I know why it is so, or why it is not so?

When people speak of what is so and what is not so, it is like form.
My being so or not so is like the shadow.
And what makes it so or not so is like form, which cannot know its own movement or stillness—how then could I know it?

Once, Zhuang Zhou dreamed he was a butterfly, fluttering and flitting about, a butterfly indeed.
He felt himself following its joy—was it not so?
Yet he did not know it was Zhou.”

Suddenly he awoke—startled, bewildered, as if roused from a trance. It was Zhou. Yet how could he know whether Zhou had dreamed he was a butterfly, or the butterfly now dreamed it was Zhou? Waking is but another dream, and thus it seemed the butterfly’s dream had become Zhou. Between Zhou and the butterfly, there must be a distinction. Yet if Zhou and the butterfly cannot know their own division, how could one discern the boundary between this and that, self and other? This is called the transformation of things.

This is the principle of the myriad things’ transformation: when one contemplates the metamorphosis of things, one sees that they need not be equal. This chapter is titled "Equalizing Things," and thus concludes by returning to the word *thing*.

The second chapter of the *Nanhua* is widely deemed difficult to read. Here it is divided into three major sections: from Nan Guo Ziqi sitting with his back to the desk to *"this is how they come into being"* forms the first section, which itself contains three subsections—first, the dialogue between the master and disciple in their confusion; next, the earthly pipes; and finally, the heavenly pipes. From *"without that, there is no self"* to *"this is called preserving one’s inner light"* forms the second section, which contains five subsections—first, following Ziqi’s discourse on the heavenly pipes, it speaks of man’s inherent true master yet his ignorance of it; the subsequent subsections largely excerpt Gongsun Long’s paradoxes: the second on affirmation and negation, the third on right and wrong, the fourth on self and other, and the fifth on *"not the finger"* and *"not the horse."* The conclusion is that nothing surpasses silence. From *"Yao questioned Shun"* to the end forms the third section, serving only to cite evidence in support of the preceding section’s meaning, arranged as in the previous chapter. It contains four subsections: the first speaks of virtue advancing beyond the sun to clarify the earlier exposition; the second speaks of the perfected person transcending life and death, and thus beyond right and wrong; the third speaks of the Dao as needing no cultivation, and thus beyond right and wrong; the fourth and fifth offer two analogies, showing that even within the human body there are things one cannot know—how then could one know right and wrong? Thus, one can only listen to the inequality of things and equalize them.

Here ends the third scroll of the *Xunben* edition of the *True Classic of Nanhua*.
Here begins the fourth scroll of the *Xunben* edition of the *True Classic of Nanhua*.

Lululing Bamboo Peak’s Luo Miandao, as recorded by his disciple Peng Xiang, collated and punctuated

**Inner Chapters: The Pivot of Nurturing Life**
This chapter speaks of the pivot of nurturing life. It first states that nurturing life must accord with its natural course; later, it states that though one speaks of nurturing life, one should in truth regard death and life as one.

Our life is bounded, yet knowledge is boundless. To pursue the boundless with the bounded is perilous; and to do so in the name of knowledge is perilous indeed.

In doing good, do not seek fame; in doing evil, do not court punishment. Follow the central path as your constant guide—thus can you preserve the body, fulfill life, nurture kin, and live out your years.

*Near* means to draw close. *Follow* means to accord. *Supervisor* refers to the center, as the seam down the back of a garment is called the *du*; this is the sense intended here. Those who do good inevitably gain renown; those who do evil inevitably incur punishment. To nurture life well is to relinquish knowledge and forget both good and evil. Thus, in doing good, one does not draw near to fame’s allure; in doing evil, one does not draw near to punishment’s harm. For without the pursuit of good or evil, one naturally transcends the confines of punishment and reputation.

Yet do not cling to the center as a rigid rule. The *center* of which Laozi speaks—embracing the One and guarding the center—does not mean straddling the middle between good and evil. Zhu Xi, in his postscript to the *Supreme Polarity Discourse*, criticizes this interpretation, though it may not reflect the original intent.

Cook Ding, carving an ox for Lord Wenhui of Liang (King Hui of Liang), offers an analogy for nurturing life. Where his hand touches, where his shoulder leans—his strength applied, his shoulder tilts. Where his foot treads, where his knee presses (the sound *ji*, as the knee braces)—*huo!* (a crisp sound), the blade enters with a *xiang* (resonant rush), and the knife slices with a *huo* (sharp crack).

The *xu* sound—still a faint resonance, yet audible. The *huo* sound, however, is loud and clear. As the blade moves in rhythm, it is as though playing music, every stroke attuned to harmony. It matches the dance of *Sanglin*, and aligns with the cadence of *Lishou*. The *Zuozhuan* annotates: *Sanglin* was the music of the Shang sovereigns. The *Record of Rites* states: feudal lords used *Lishou* as their rhythmic measure. It further notes: *Lishou xu*—the moment when music converges. And again: feudal lords timed their audiences with the Son of Heaven to this measure. These two musical pieces are invoked because Tang prayed at *Sanglin*, offering himself as sacrifice, while the feudal lords sang *Lishou* in archery rites to rebuke those who failed to attend court. Both hold meaning for the art of ox-carving.

Lord Wenhui exclaimed, *"Xi!"*—a sigh of admiration—"How sublime! Has skill ever reached such heights?" Cook Ding laid down his blade and replied: "Your servant pursues the *Dao*, advancing beyond mere technique. When I first began carving oxen, all I saw was the whole beast. After three years, I no longer saw the ox entire. Now, I encounter it with spirit rather than sight; my senses halt while my spirit moves. The eye’s office governs vision. I follow the natural pattern—*tian* as spontaneity, *li* as the inherent grain. The ox’s body possesses a natural structure; I cleave along its lines. I strike the great gaps (*pi* the wide fissures), guide the blade through the spacious hollows (*kuan*), and proceed as its fixed nature dictates. I have never touched the sinew-bound joints (*jing qing*), let alone the great bones (*gu*). A skilled cook replaces his knife yearly—he only cuts flesh. An ordinary cook changes his blade monthly—he hacks bone and dulls the edge. My knife has endured nineteen years—nineteen, a number accumulating to unity—and has carved thousands of oxen, yet its edge remains as if newly whetted from the grindstone. The blade’s edge is razor-thin, while the joints have space. To enter the space with what has no thickness—how vast the room for the blade’s play! Thus, after nineteen years, my knife is still as if newly sharpened. Yet whenever I encounter a dense cluster of bones, I perceive the difficulty, grow wary, fix my gaze, slow my movements, and move the blade with utmost care. Then—*huo!*—the ox falls apart like clods of earth. I stand holding the knife, look around in satisfaction, linger with self-assurance, then wipe the blade clean and put it away."

Lord Wenhui said, "Excellent! From Cook Ding’s words, I have learned the art of nurturing life." Here, the discourse takes another turn: though my knife-work is indeed refined, whenever I reach a dense cluster, I recognize the challenge and dare not grow complacent. My former reliance on spirit rather than sight now sharpens into focused attention; my once-fluid motion slows to deliberate care; my blade’s effortless play becomes a movement of utmost precision. Only when the ox dissolves—*huo!*—like earth crumbling to the ground do I stand holding the knife, survey the scene with an unoccupied gaze, pause in quiet triumph, then tend to the blade and store it away. How different is the Way of nurturing life from this? Gongwen Xuan...

Gongwen Xuan was a native of Song. Upon seeing the Right Minister—a position held in the Song court alongside the Left Minister—he exclaimed in astonishment, "What manner of man is this? How came he to be one-legged? Is this a natural deformity, or the result of punishment?" For to be one-legged was to be maimed, as in the case of Sun Bin. "Was this fate’s decree, or man’s doing?" he wondered. Then he answered himself: "It was Heaven, not man. Heaven’s creation made him thus solitary; man’s form is meant to be paired. From this we know it was Heaven’s work, not man’s."

He continued: "Man is born with two feet; that he now has but one is Heaven’s doing. To suffer such a fate is simply the course of destiny—nothing more than Heaven’s will."

A marsh pheasant takes ten steps to peck, a hundred steps to drink, yet it does not seek to be kept in a cage. Though its spirit may thrive, it finds no goodness in confinement. The pheasant in the marsh drinks and pecks at ease, never desiring the nourishment of a cage. Yet if caged, though its vitality may be preserved, it remains constrained—this is not true well-being. What matter if one is one-legged? But to serve as Right Minister, bound by office, is to court disaster. This is to lose the Way of nurturing life.

When Lao Dan died, Qin Shi went to offer condolences. He wailed three times and departed. A disciple asked, "Was he not your master’s friend?" "He was," Qin Shi replied. The disciple pressed, "Then is such brief mourning proper?" Qin Shi answered, "At first, I thought him a true man, but now I see he was not." He referred to Lao Dan. "At first, I took him for one of Lao Dan’s disciples, but now I see he was not—he was still entangled in worldly sentiment."

"When I entered to offer condolences, I saw elders weeping as if for a son, and youths wailing as if for a mother. In their gathering, there must have been those who spoke without seeking to speak, who wept without seeking to weep. This is to flee from Heaven and betray one’s true nature, to forget what one has received—what the ancients called ‘the punishment of fleeing from Heaven.’ To gather and be moved thus is to flee from the principles of Heaven, to violate one’s true feelings, and to forget the destiny one is born with. In the ancient age of emptiness and tranquility, such behavior would be deemed a transgression against Heaven, a fault in the rites of mourning."

"When he came, the Master followed the time; when he departed, the Master followed the flow. To dwell in accord with the time and abide in the natural course is to remain untouched by joy or sorrow—what the ancients called ‘the celestial release.’ Life arrives as the time comes; death departs as the time flows. To accept the time is to find no excess in life’s joy; to follow the flow is to find no lack in death’s sorrow. The ‘celestial release’ refers to Heaven’s workings through yin and yang, the Five Phases, giving rise to all things. Man receives these at birth, bound by joy and anger, sorrow and happiness, fortune and misfortune, regret and blame—all that entangles him. Yet if one dwells in accord with the time and abides in the natural course, sorrow and joy cannot enter, and the bonds of Heaven are loosed."

"The men of old were thus, which is why Qin Shi wailed but three times before departing. A finger may trace the length of firewood, but the firewood is boundless while the finger is finite. Yet when the fire is passed from one to the next, its end is unknown. This is another parable to illustrate the principle of life and death. No man is immortal—why then grieve excessively?"

The preceding sections spoke of nurturing life; this one speaks of viewing life and death as one. To see life and death as one is the very essence of nurturing life. Those who study the Way should take this as the foundation of their practice.

*Thus concludes the fourth scroll of the Xunben edition of the Nan Hua True Classic. The fifth scroll follows.*

Bamboo Peak of Lulin, recorded by Luo Miandao, collated by disciple Peng Xiang

**Inner Chapters: The Human World**

How could one soar high and flee far to escape the human world? Thus this chapter expounds the Way of dwelling in the human world.

Yan Hui sought an audience with Confucius and requested leave to depart.
"Where are you going?" asked Confucius.
"To the state of Wei."
"What will you do there?"
Hui replied: "I have heard that the lord of Wei, Duke Chu, is in his prime, yet his conduct is willful. He treats his state lightly and fails to perceive his faults. He squanders the lives of his people, and the dead are measured by the land as if they were reeds in the marsh—so numerous that the people have nowhere to turn."

*Quantified as if by measure. Marshes like Yunmeng. Reeds, a kind of grass. The dead are so vast that, measured against the land, the people perish like grass in the marsh.*

Chen Feng Hua said: "I am daily worn down by war, bones lie exposed like wild grass, and the people have nowhere to turn. They move and are ensnared by punishments, with no escape."

Hui continued: "I once heard you say, Master, that one should leave well-governed states and go to those in chaos. The gate of a physician sees many illnesses—just as a physician desires many illnesses to practice his craft, so too may one practice the Way in a disordered state. I wish to ponder the principles I have heard from you and consider their application, that perhaps Wei may yet be cured."

Confucius said: "Alas! You are likely to go and be executed. The Way does not tolerate杂乱.杂乱 leads to excess, excess to disturbance, disturbance to anxiety, and anxiety cannot be remedied.

The perfected ones of old first established it within themselves before extending it to others. If what is established within yourself is not yet settled, how can you attend to the conduct of a violent man?

Moreover, do you know where virtue is eroded and whence knowledge arises? Virtue is eroded by fame, and knowledge arises from contention. Fame is a means of mutual conflict; knowledge is an instrument of strife. Both are inauspicious tools, not to be employed in all circumstances.

Though your virtue be profound and your trust unshakable, if you have not yet aligned with the people’s qi, and though your fame be uncontentious, if you have not yet reached their hearts—

*Profound trust resides in one’s qi, hence it is said not yet aligned with the people’s qi. Non-contention resides in the heart’s intent, hence it is said not yet reached their hearts.*

—if you forcibly present the language of仁义 and the墨绳 of standards before a violent man, you will make others resent what they perceive as your superiority. This is called *disaster-bringing*. One who brings disaster to others will surely have disaster reflected back upon them."

Should you bring disaster upon others, you court peril for yourself.

Moreover, if you merely delight in the worthy and despise the unworthy, why seek to distinguish yourself thus? If you, without instruction, remain silent, lords and princes will surely exploit others to vie for advantage, and their eyes will dazzle you—your vision will be confounded by their glare. Their countenance will level you—your expression will harmonize with theirs. Their mouths will entangle you—they will justify themselves in self-defense. Your demeanor will mirror theirs—adopting the posture of one struck and kneeling. Your heart will conform to theirs—thus solidifying their wrongdoing. This is like quenching fire with fire, or flood with flood—it is called *adding to the excess*. Unable to correct, you only compound the error. To follow their beginning without end is perilous; if you speak earnestly yet without credibility, you will surely perish before the violent.

They follow their own beginnings—how could their malice ever reach an end? If you admonish them, they will surely deem your words untrustworthy. Enraged by your sincerity, you will not escape punishment or execution.

Of old, Jie slew Guan Longfeng, and Zhou slew Prince Bigan—both had cultivated their persons, humbling themselves to soothe the people, yet in doing so defied their superiors. Thus, their rulers punished them for their cultivation. These were men who prized fame.

In ancient times, Yao attacked Congzhi and Xu’ao, and Yu attacked Youhu. Their states became desolate ruins—*xu* means dwellings left empty; *li* means those who die without heirs. Their bodies were executed, their wars unceasing, their pursuit of substance insatiable. These were men who sought fame and substance.

Have you alone not heard of this? Fame and substance are what even the sages cannot fully master—how much less can one like you! Cong, Zhi, Xu’ao sought to contend with Yao for fame and substance; Youhu sought to contend with Yu for fame and substance. Even Yao and Yu could not endure them and so attacked—how much less can you!

Yet if you must have some means, come and speak to me.

Yan Hui said: "If I am upright yet empty, diligent yet single-minded—would that suffice?"
The Master said: "How could it? How could that suffice?
When one’s yang-qi swells to overflowing brilliance, when one’s countenance shifts without constancy—this is what ordinary men dare not oppose. Following and pressing upon what moves others, one seeks to indulge the heart’s desires. Such is called the virtue of gradual accumulation, yet even this cannot be achieved—how much less the great virtue!
One will remain obstinate and unyielding, outwardly compliant yet inwardly uncritical. How could this ever suffice?
Uprightness and humility serve to exhaust one’s own capacities; diligence and single-mindedness serve to enter into others.
When one’s yang-qi swells to overflowing brilliance, it means the Lord of Wei, taking his yang-nature as self-sufficient, acts with unbounded self-satisfaction. When his countenance shifts without constancy, it means his joy and anger have no fixed pattern, so no one dares oppose him. *An* is the same as *pressing*; *gan* means *to touch*. *Rong-yu* means *unrestrained indulgence*. Thus, following and pressing upon what touches him, he seeks to indulge his heart’s desires.
This is called the virtue of gradual accumulation, yet even this cannot be achieved—how much less the great virtue!
*Obstinate* means *unyielding*. *Zi* means *to slander*. He will remain obstinate and unyielding, while you must outwardly comply yet inwardly cease to criticize him. In that case, what you call upright yet empty, diligent yet single-minded—how could this suffice? Outward compliance without inward criticism is precisely what was earlier called *indulging and shaping the heart’s desires*.
Thus, I am inwardly straight yet outwardly yielding, achieving and aligning above.
*Inwardly straight* means *to follow Heaven as one’s companion*. One who follows Heaven as his companion knows that the Son of Heaven and himself are both children of Heaven. Does he alone seek that others praise his words, or that others condemn them? One who acts thus is called a childlike man. This is what it means to follow Heaven as one’s companion.
Yan Hui further spoke of these three principles: *Inwardly straight yet outwardly yielding* means to be direct within yet accommodating without; *achieving and aligning above* means to present one’s achievements and align them with the ancients. Being inwardly direct is to follow one’s Heavenly nature. Thus it is said..."

To follow Heaven as one’s companion is to know that the Son of Heaven and oneself are both children of Heaven. Both are but children—how could one surpass the other and seek alone that others follow one’s own words? Would this not mean that one has surpassed even the Son of Heaven? If one can grasp this principle and not seek to prevail through one’s own judgments, then one remains whole. This is the intent of Heaven in treating all as its children. Thus it is said: one who acts thus is called a childlike man. To be like a child is to remain in a time before right and wrong are divided. This is what it means to follow Heaven as one’s companion.

To be outwardly yielding is to follow others as one’s companions. Bowing, kneeling, and curving one’s hands—these are the rites of a minister. If all others do so, how dare I refuse? To do what others do is to leave no room for blame. This is what it means to follow others as one’s companions. To be outwardly accommodating is to navigate human affairs with flexibility. Thus it is said: to follow others as one’s companions.

To achieve and align above is to follow the ancients as one’s companions. Though one’s words may instruct, their truth belongs to the ancients, not to oneself. If one acts thus, though direct, one does no harm. This is what it means to follow the ancients as one’s companions. To present the ancients’ established sayings is to speak not merely as recitation or instruction, but as admonition. For these are the offices of the ancients, not one’s own. Thus, though direct, one avoids causing harm. This is what it means to follow the ancients as one’s companions.

Is this then acceptable?
Confucius said: Alas, how could it be! There are too many policies and laws, yet no deliberation. One cannot interpose deliberation into them. Though steadfast, one remains blameless. Yet this is all there is to it—how could it reach the transformation of one who still follows his own mind? This is still to take one’s own mind as one’s teacher.

Yan Hui said: I have no further way forward. May I ask the method?
Confucius said: Fast, and I will tell you. To act while possessing—is this easy? Those who find it easy are not favored by the August Heaven.

Yan Hui said: "Hui’s family is poor. For months now, I have neither drunk wine nor eaten meat. If this is so, may I consider it fasting?"

Confucius said: "This is the fast of sacrificial rites, not the fast of the mind."

Yan Hui said: "May I ask about the fast of the mind?"

Confucius said: "Unify your will. Do not listen with the ears but listen with the mind; do not listen with the mind but listen with qi. Listening stops at the ears, the mind stops at the tally. Qi is that which is empty and awaits things. The tally is what is called one’s nature. As Yang Xiong said in *Da Bin Xi*: 'Be cautious in cultivating your will, guard and observe the celestial tally.' This speaks of nature and the celestial tally, hence it is called the tally. Like a tally or contract, hearing sound stops at the ears, thought stops at the tally. Only qi has no thought—it is merely empty and awaits things. Only the Dao gathers in emptiness. Emptiness is the fast of the mind."

Yan Hui said: "Before I had begun to be employed, I was truly myself. Once employed, I would no longer be myself. If I do not know the Dao, then my hundred limbs would have no one to obey. Truly, this is one—once Yan Hui attains the Dao, there is only emptiness. How could this body remain? Can this be called emptiness?"

Confucius said: "You have exhausted it. I will tell you! If you can enter and wander within its enclosure without being moved by its name—entering, then speak; not entering, then stop. Without gate, without harm, dwelling in one abode and lodging in inevitability—then you are near. Take birds as an analogy: the state of Wei is like a cage for its ruler, the dragon. Yan Hui wishes to go to Wei, as if entering a cage. But if you enter and wander within the state without being moved by its illustrious name, this is called 'entering and wandering.' Though you enter, you retain the meaning of roaming freely, not constrained by circumstance. If you gain entry, then admonish; if you do not, then stop—there is no need to insist on entry. Yet if you do enter, consider the way of dwelling: Wei is not a place where one should reside. Without a single gate that is not harmful, you may only secure one dwelling and lodge this body in inevitability. To lodge is to reside without attachment. If you do this, you will be near to the Lord of Wei. 'Near' means close. This is to instruct Yan Hui on how to enter, dwell, and wait—remaining long enough to transform naturally. What follows is the way to effect that transformation.

To erase traces is easy; to walk without treading is difficult. It is easy for one to erase their tracks, but how can one not tread at all? To be employed by man is easy to feign; to be employed by Heaven is difficult to feign. Though human affairs contain the principles of Heaven, man may be deceived, but Heaven cannot. These four lines speak of Yan Hui’s need to efface his form and traces, and discard superficial falsity.

I have heard of flying with wings, but never of flying without wings. This analogy applies to the following line: I have heard of knowing with knowledge, but never of knowing with non-knowledge. Knowing without knowledge is great knowledge—yet I have never heard of such a person. These four lines speak of Yan Hui’s need to cast aside cunning intelligence.

Gaze upon that empty chamber: in an empty room, brightness is born, and auspiciousness comes to rest. This teaches Yan Hui the principle of an empty mind. Gazing upon the space within a gate and chamber is like the heart within a person. When an empty room is unobstructed, brightness naturally arises, and a hundred auspicious signs gather. When the mind is empty and unobstructed, it naturally becomes luminous, and ten thousand virtues congeal and rest. 'Comes to rest' means to rest and rest again. In the *Book of Changes*, yin and yang flow through the nine stars, and the central palace’s unobstructed brightness is considered auspicious—this also takes emptiness and lack of obstruction as its meaning. If one does not come to rest, this is called 'sitting in motion.' When the mind is unrestrained and knows no limit, the body may be still, but the mind is not—hence it is called 'sitting in motion.'

If you follow the ears and eyes, communicating inwardly but outwardly abandoning the mind’s knowledge, then ghosts and spirits will come to dwell—how much more so men! The ears and eyes are external, yet if you follow them inwardly, and the mind’s knowledge is internal, yet if you cast it outwardly—there is only emptiness. If this is so, then ghosts and spirits will come to dwell with you—how much more so men! The word 'dwell' responds to the earlier 'abode.'

This is the transformation of the ten thousand things, the pivot of Yu and Shun, the lifelong practice of the ancient emperors Fu Xi and Qi. How much more so for one who is merely scattered and common! The principle of an empty mind is the means by which the ten thousand things are moved to transformation. It is the pivot by which Yu and Shun governed. It is what Fu Xi and Qi practiced throughout their lives. How much more so for the Lord of Wei, who is but a scattered and common man—how could he be difficult to transform?"

The concluding passage about scattering horses is not part of the main text.

When Gongzi Gao was about to be sent on a mission to Qi, he asked Confucius: "The king has entrusted a weighty task to Zhuliang. Qi’s treatment of envoys is likely to be respectful but not urgent. Even a single state is not yet ready to be moved—how much less the feudal lords! I am deeply apprehensive."

You once told Zhuliang: "In all matters, whether great or small, few are not brought to completion through joyful harmony. If a matter is not completed, there will surely be human calamities—punishment and blame. If it is completed, there will surely be the calamities of yin and yang—worry and illness from imbalance. Only one who possesses virtue can achieve success or failure without calamity."

"I eat coarse food and do not seek refinement. My kitchen stove is without desire for coolness. Now, upon receiving my mission in the morning, I drink ice in the evening—is this not internal heat? Coarse food is rough fare. 'Not seeking refinement' means not insisting on fine quality. 'Desire for coolness' refers to one who suffers from heat and seeks coolness. I eat only coarse, rough food and do not insist on refinement. My kitchen stove is simple, and the cook need not suffer from heat and long for coolness. Yet now, upon receiving my mission to Qi in the morning, I drink ice in the evening—this is the excess of worry and thought leading to internal heat, not the fault of food and drink."

"Before I have even reached the true nature of affairs—indeed, before I have grasped their essence—I have already incurred the harm of yin-yang imbalance. If the matter fails, there will inevitably be human-caused calamity; these are two dangers. As a minister, I am not equal to this task. Pray, tell me what to do."

Confucius said: "There are two great precepts in the world. The first is fate; the second is duty. Your love for your parents is fate—it cannot be untied from the heart. This is the principle of Heaven’s mandate, the natural arising of benevolence and love, which never parts from this heart. A minister’s service to his lord is duty—there is nowhere that is not the lord’s domain. 'Under heaven, none is not the king’s land; within the seas, none is not the king’s subject.' There is no escape within heaven and earth. These are called the great precepts.

Thus, in serving one’s parents, one finds contentment without choosing the place—this is the utmost of filial piety. In serving one’s lord, one finds contentment without choosing the task—this is the height of loyalty. In attending to one’s own heart, one remains unwavering in joy, unshaken by changing circumstances, accepting what cannot be helped as if it were fate—this is the perfection of virtue. As a minister and son, one must have something that cannot be obstructed. Focus on the true nature of affairs and forget the self—how then could one have leisure to delight in life and fear death? You may proceed in this way.

Let me further share what I have heard: In close dealings, trust is naturally established without need for words. In distant dealings, loyalty must be expressed through words, and words must sometimes be conveyed by others. Conveying words that bring joy or anger to both parties is the most difficult task in the world. When both sides are pleased, their words will overflow with excessive praise; when both are angry, their words will overflow with excessive blame. If two states are both pleased, their words will inevitably contain exaggerated commendation; if both are angry, their words will inevitably contain exaggerated slander. This is why conveying such words is so difficult. All exaggeration is falsehood; falsehood cannot be trusted, and when trust is lacking, the messenger incurs misfortune.

Thus, the *Model Sayings* states: 'Transmit the constant truth of affairs; do not transmit exaggerated words, and you will be nearly blameless.' Moreover, those who contend with skill and strength begin with openness but often end in secrecy; when excess prevails, cunning abounds. Those who drink wine with propriety begin in order but often end in chaos; when excess prevails, strange amusements multiply. All things are like this. They begin with sincerity and trust but often end in vulgarity and coarseness. Their inception is simple, but their conclusion is inevitably grand.

Words are like wind and waves; actions are like gain and loss. Wind and waves are easily stirred; gain and loss are easily endangered. Words, being insubstantial like waves following the wind, are easily set in motion. Actions, being real, constantly risk loss—hence, they are easily imperiled. Thus, anger arises without cause, and clever words become biased. When a beast is dying, it does not choose its sounds; its breath is ragged, and in this state, a vicious heart arises. If one encounters someone who vents their anger without regard for reason, one can only respond with clever, biased words—like a dying beast that cannot choose the quality of its sounds, only aware of its ragged breath. In this way, the harm of yin-yang imbalance arises, and a mental affliction takes root.

When severity and exactitude reach their extreme, there will inevitably be an unworthy response, though one may not realize it. If one does not know how this comes to be, who can foresee the outcome? Thus, the *Model Sayings* states: 'Do not alter commands; do not urge completion—excess leads to harm.' Altering commands and urging completion endanger affairs. These two lines summarize the two *Model Sayings*: transmitting exaggerated words is excessive, and altering commands or urging completion is adding to or changing them, which leads to peril.

What is beautifully accomplished takes time; what is badly accomplished cannot be undone. The perfection of beauty is difficult and requires long effort to achieve. The completion of evil is swift and leaves no time for correction. Can one not be cautious?

Moreover, riding things to roam the mind and entrusting oneself to necessity to nurture the center—this is perfection. Why contrive a response? It is best to deliver the message—this is the difficult part. Why fabricate clever deceit to report to the Lord of Qi? Simply convey the Lord of Chu’s command. Yet I fear Lord Ye will deem this impractical, so I conclude by saying: this is the difficult part—referring to the earlier statement that this is the most difficult task in the world."

Yan He, a recluse of Lu, was about to serve as tutor to the crown prince of Duke Ling of Wei. He asked Qu Boyu: "There is a man here whose nature is as if Heaven itself were destructive. If I deal with him without restraint, I endanger the state; if I deal with him with restraint, I endanger myself. His knowledge is just enough to recognize others’ faults but not to understand why they err. In such a case, what should I do?"

Qu Boyu said: "Excellent question! Be on guard, be careful, and rectify yourself! In form, do not be distant; in heart, do not be discordant. However, these two approaches have their pitfalls. In form, do not be too intimate; in heart, do not be too expressive. If your form is close but not intrusive, you will not be estranged, yet you must not indulge his preferences and follow him deceptively. If your heart is harmonious but not outwardly manifest, you will not be contrary, yet you must not display it externally and boast.

If your form is too intimate, you risk collapse, ruin, downfall, and stumbling. If your heart is too expressive, you risk becoming a reputation, a name, an omen, or a calamity. If he behaves like a child, then act like a child with him. If he is without boundaries, then be without boundaries with him. If he is without limits, then be without limits with him."

To attain this is to enter the realm of flawlessness. How could this be in vain? You must reach the state where flaws and errors do not arise.

Do you not know the mantis? In its fury, it raises its arms to block the chariot’s wheel, unaware of its own incapacity—yet this is the very beauty of its ability. The mantis takes its power to obstruct as its finest trait.

Beware, take heed: when one accumulates boasts of their own beauty and then provokes, they are on the brink. To boast is to vaunt. And you—you are nearly imperiled. If you amass praise for your own talents and beauty, then challenge with them, you court danger.

Do you not know the keeper of tigers? He dares not feed it living prey, for fear of the rage that comes with killing; he dares not give it whole prey, for fear of the fury that comes with tearing it apart. Living prey must be slain, whole prey must be rent—thus stirring its wrath. He times its hunger and satiety, understanding the anger in its heart. Though tiger and man are of different kinds, the tiger flatters and submits to its keeper—this is compliance. Thus, what it kills is defiance. Such is the nature of compliance and defiance.

The lover of horses uses a basket to collect their dung and a conch-shell vessel to hold their urine. (The *shen* is a clam, used here to adorn the vessel.) Yet if a mosquito or horsefly alights upon the horse and the groom fails to brush it away at once, the horse will snap its bit, shatter its bridle, and trample its chest. Love may reach a point where it brings loss—can one not be cautious?

The tiger and horse are natures meant to illustrate the case of the Crown Prince Wei.

Carpenter Shi traveled to Qi and, arriving at Quyuan, saw an oak that served as the village shrine. Its girth could shade an ox, its trunk measured a hundred spans around (a span here being the width of a hand), its height rivaled the mountains, and only after rising seventy feet did it put forth branches. A dozen or more of its side limbs could have been made into boats. Spectators thronged like a marketplace, yet the master carpenter did not so much as glance at it but walked on without pause. His disciple, having feasted his eyes, caught up with Carpenter Shi and said, “Since I first took up the axe to follow you, Master, I have never seen timber so magnificent. Why did you not even look at it but walk on without stopping?”

“Enough,” said the carpenter. “Say no more. It is a useless tree. Make a boat from it, and it will sink; make a coffin, and it will rot at once; make a vessel, and it will break at once; make a door, and it will ooze sap like a *men* tree; make a pillar, and it will be eaten by worms. It is a tree of no use—hence its longevity.”

When Carpenter Shi returned home, the shrine oak appeared to him in a dream and said, “With what would you compare me? Would you compare me to the so-called ‘fine timber’? Think of the hawthorn, pear, orange, and pomelo—all the fruit-bearing trees. When their fruit ripens, they are stripped; when it is plucked, they are abused. Their great branches are broken, their small branches are torn. This is how their abilities bring suffering to their lives. They do not live out their natural span but die halfway, struck down by the world’s blows. All things are like this. As for me, I have sought to be of no use for a long time. Nearly did I die—now at last I have found it: this is my great use. Had I been of use, would I have grown so vast? Moreover, you and I are both things—how can one thing judge another? And you, a nearly dead man of no consequence—what do you know of a useless tree?”

Carpenter Shi awoke and interpreted his dream. His disciple said, “If it sought to be of no use, why does it serve as a shrine?”

“Hush!” said the carpenter. “Say nothing. It merely lodges there, enduring the scorn of those who do not understand it. Were it not a shrine, would it not have been felled long ago? Moreover, what it preserves differs from what the common run preserves—how far removed is it to praise it with conventional standards!”

Master Nanbo Ziqi was wandering in Shangqiu when he saw a remarkable tree. A thousand four-horse chariots could shelter beneath it, its foliage shading all they relied upon. Ziqi said, “What tree is this?”

This must possess extraordinary timber, must it not? Yet gazing upward at its slender branches, they are gnarled and crooked—unfit for ridgepoles or beams. Looking down at its massive roots, they split like a broken axle, fissured and useless for coffins or outer caskets. Tasting its leaves, the mouth festers and is wounded; inhaling its scent, it drives one to raving madness for two days without cease.

Ziqi said, “This is indeed a useless tree—yet it has grown to such immense size! Alas!” Ziqi sighed to himself, “The spirit-like person employs this very uselessness.”

In the lands of Jing in Song, where catalpa, cypress, and mulberry thrive, those saplings of a hand’s span or two are felled to fashion perches for macaques; those of three or four arm-spans are hewn to craft the grand beams of renowned halls; those of seven or eight arm-spans are sawn for the flawless side-planks sought by noble households and wealthy merchants. Thus, before their natural span is complete, they meet an untimely end beneath the axe—such is the calamity of being useful. Here, then, is another instance of usefulness as peril, and thus the paradox is resolved.

The *Han "Treatise on Suburban Sacrifices"* records that the ancient Son of Heaven customarily performed the "Spring Liberation Rites," speaking of absolving sins to seek blessings. They would offer a white-browed ox, a snub-nosed pig (for its nose was upturned and high, with a forehead crease), or a man afflicted with hemorrhoids—none of these could be used to ford the river, nor could they be sacrificed to the River God. The shamans and invocators knew this well, for such things, being of common use to men, were deemed inauspicious. Yet this is precisely what the spirit-like person regards as the greatest auspice.

There was one named Zhili Shu—
*Zhili* describes his incomplete form; *Shu* was his name.
His chin was hidden in his navel,
—*Qi* (navel) here is interchangeable with *ji* (to rise)—
so that the hunchbacked could not see his chin, concealed as it was between his belly. His shoulders towered above his crown,
—his head was lowered, his shoulders rising higher than his head—
and his topknot pointed to the heavens.
*—Cuo* (to gather) is read as *cu* (to bind); he gathered his hair into a topknot. In ancient times, the topknot was worn near the nape, so when the spine was bent and the head lowered, the topknot would point upward.*
His five *guan* were above,
*—Guan* refers to the *shu* (transport points); the *shu* of the five viscera were all situated above.*
his two thighs formed his ribs,
—his spine was within his thighs, so his two thighs served as his ribs—
and he mended needles to sew clothes,
—he could twist needles for sewing garments—
enough to feed his mouth. He would shake the divining stalks and scatter the sacred grain,
*—Gu* means to move the stalks for divination, as when people today draw lots. *Jing* refers to the refined rice used to offer to the spirits. The *Chu Ci* speaks of "holding peppered grain to summon them," where the note explains *jing* as refined rice. *Bo* means to arrange it as an offering to the spirits.*
thus performing divination and sacrificing to the spirits,
enough to feed ten men.
—His earnings were not merely enough to fill his own mouth.*
When the authorities conscripted warriors, Zhili would stretch his arms among them,
—relying on his uselessness, he did not hide himself—
and when great labor was demanded, Zhili, citing his chronic illness, was exempted from service.
—Being unfit for labor, when the authorities distributed grain to the sick, he received three *zhong*, six *hu*, and four *dou*, along with ten bundles of firewood.*
Thus, one whose form was as fragmented as Zhili’s could still nourish his body and live out his natural years—how much more, then, one whose virtue is fragmented!

When Confucius traveled to Chu, Jie Yu, the Madman of Chu, wandered past his gate and called out:
"Phoenix! Phoenix!
How your virtue has declined!
The age to come cannot be awaited,
the age past cannot be pursued.
When the world follows the Dao, the sage achieves his work;
when the world loses the Dao, the sage preserves his life.
In this present time, one can but barely escape punishment.
Fortune is lighter than a feather, yet none know how to bear it;
disaster is heavier than the earth, yet none know how to avoid it.
Enough! Enough!
Approach others with virtue.
Perilous! Perilous!
Mark the ground and hasten forth.
—One should let their steps go where they may; if one marks the ground as their destination and rushes toward it, they will meet exhaustion and danger.*
*Miyang! Miyang!*
Do not harm my shoulders as I walk!
*—Miyang* is the *mijue* (bracken fern). It grows thickly and can obscure the path of the sun, hence *miyang* (obscuring the yang). This is spoken metaphorically.*
I walk in crooked paths,
*—Que* is read as *xi* (a gap)—*
do not harm my feet!"

The above is appended by way of commentary on the *Analects*; what follows are the words of *Zhuangzi*.

Mountain trees invite their own felling;
fat and fire hasten their own burning.
Cinnamon is edible, so it is cut down;
lacquer is useful, so it is slashed.
All men know the usefulness of the useful,
yet none know the usefulness of the useless.

The foregoing concerns the oak of the sacred altar and the mulberry of Shangqiu—thus is this discourse tied to the theme of trees.

*The True Classic of Southern Florescence, Following the Original Text, Scroll Five* (ends here)
*The True Classic of Southern Florescence, Following the Original Text, Scroll Six*
Recorded by Lu Lingzhufeng and Luo Miandao; collated by disciple Peng Xiang

**Inner Chapters: "Virtue’s Full Manifestation"**
*Fu* means "verification."
When virtue is full within, it is verified without.
Though the form be incomplete, it is no hindrance—*Liezi* has a chapter titled "Verification."

In Lu there was an amputee named Wang Tai. (Amputees are called *wu*; *tai* is his given name.) Those who followed him were as numerous as those who followed Confucius.

Zhou Changji asked Confucius, "Wang Dai is an amputee, yet those who follow him divide Lu equally with you. When he stands, he does not teach; when he sits, he does not discourse. Yet they come to him empty and depart full. Is there truly a teaching without words, a shaping of the mind without form? What manner of man is he?"

Confucius said, "This master is a sage. I have merely lagged behind and not yet gone to him. I too shall follow him, let alone those who are not my equal! Why should I rely on the multitudes of Lu? I shall lead all under Heaven to follow him."

Changji said, "Though he is an amputee, he surpasses you, Master—how much more so the common run of men! If this is so, how does he employ his mind?"

Confucius said, "Life and death are great matters, yet they do not alter him. Though heaven and earth collapse and fall, he would not be lost with them. He examines what is without reliance and does not shift with things; he commands the transformations of things while guarding their root."

Changji said, "What does this mean?"

Confucius said:

Seeing from what sets them apart, the liver and gallbladder are as distant as Chu and Yue; seeing from what unites them, the myriad things are all one. Such being the case, he wanders his mind in the harmony of virtue without knowing what the ears and eyes deem fitting. He regards what things share as one and does not see what they lose—seeing the loss of a foot as one would leave behind a clod of earth.

Changji said, "He cultivates himself. Through his knowledge, he attains his mind; through his mind, he attains the mind that is constant. Why then do people hold him in such esteem?"

Changji, though he had heard Confucius’ words, still did not grasp why Wang Tai was as he was. That man, in cultivating himself—judging by his knowledge—possessed no divine wisdom but merely the understanding his thoughts could reach; judging by his mind, he had no sagely heart but only the mind common to all. Why then should people revere him?

Confucius said, "People do not mirror themselves in flowing water but in still water. Only stillness can still the many. Water is one, yet it has places where it flows and places where it rests. People do not go to its flowing parts to see their reflections but to its still parts. This illustrates that Changji need not view Wang Tai as extraordinary but should simply see him as one who has attained the constant mind. His excellence lies precisely in stillness. The many come to rest where the still water is—all streams return to it and cease their flow. Wang Tai, with his constant mind, transforms others in the same way.

Endowed by earth, only the pine and cypress remain verdant through winter and summer. Endowed by heaven, only Shun alone was upright. Fortunate indeed that he could rectify his own life and thereby rectify the lives of all. This further clarifies the principle that only stillness can still the many. All grasses and trees receive their mandate from earth, yet only the pine and cypress remain green through winter and summer. All people receive their mandate from heaven, yet only Shun attained uprightness. Amid the chaos of countless deviations, were it not for Shun’s uprightness, nearly the whole world would have fallen into depravity. Fortunate that Shun could rectify his own life and thereby rectify the lives of all under heaven—thus we see that only stillness can still the many.

The proof of preserving one’s beginning lies in the substance of fearlessness. A single brave warrior can charge into nine armies. Those who seek fame and bind themselves to their resolve are already like this—how much more so one who governs heaven and earth, stores the myriad things, merely lodges in the six limbs, mirrors the ears and eyes, unifies the knowledge of knowing, and whose mind has never died!

The proof of preserving one’s beginning is that it must yield verifiable signs, making it trustworthy. The substance of fearlessness is that true courage does not depend on others’ recognition. The nine armies refer to the ancient military formation based on the well-field system, where each army was divided into nine units that took turns in battle. Those who seek fame are those who preserve their beginning, while those who bind themselves to their resolve embody the substance of fearlessness. To govern heaven and earth means to take heaven and earth as one’s office and steward them. To store the myriad things means to treat all things as one’s treasury. *Zhi* means "merely." *Yu* means to lodge without attachment. *Xiang* means to mirror without being real. "Unifying the knowledge of knowing while the mind has never died" means unifying the knowledge that is known without the mind ever being trapped by things and perishing. This responds to the earlier lines: "Through his knowledge, he attains his mind; through his mind, he attains the mind that is constant."

The preceding two lines show that even those who seek fame and bind themselves to their resolve can become braver than ten thousand men—how much more so Wang Tai, who unifies his physical form and mind, and is not to be compared with those who seek fame and bind themselves to their resolve! Could he not move the multitudes of Lu?

He will choose a day to ascend to the heights. People will then follow him. How would he deign to concern himself with things?

*Jia* means "to reach." *Dengjia* means "ascending to immortality," rising to heaven. In the *Great Master*, it is written: "Ascending to the heights of the Dao." It is also pronounced *ge*. Cui’s version compares him to the stars below, saying that though he has life, he has no parents or death, and after ascending for three years, his form vanishes—also meaning he rises to heaven. This means that one day he will ascend to immortality and leave the human world behind. This is why people follow him. From this perspective, how would he deign to concern himself with things? The word "things" here responds to the earlier "Why then do people hold him in such esteem?"

Daoist traditions often speak of choosing a day to ascend to immortality. Shentu Jia—of the Shentu clan—was an amputee who studied under Bohun Wuren (in the *Miscellaneous Chapters*, written as *Maoren*) alongside Zichan of Zheng. Zichan said to Shentu Jia, "If I leave first, you stay behind; if you leave first, I will stay behind." He was ashamed to walk alongside an amputee. The next day, they again sat together in the same hall. Zichan said to Shentu Jia, "If I leave first, you stay behind; if you leave first, I will stay behind. Now I am about to leave—can you stay behind? Or not?" He asked this to ensure they would not leave together. "Moreover, when you see the one in power, you do not avoid him—do you consider yourself equal to the one in power?" (Here, Zichan refers to himself as the one in power.)

Shentu Jia replied, "In Master Bohun Wuren’s school, is there really such a thing as one in power? Those who dwell in the Master’s school should forget distinctions of noble and base. Yet you delight in your position of power and place others behind you. I have heard that when a mirror is bright, dust does not settle on it; if it settles, the mirror is not bright. When one dwells long with the worthy, one commits no errors. Dwelling with the worthy to avoid errors is like using a mirror to grind away dust. Now, what you take as most important is the Master—you take from others to enlarge your virtue."

And yet you still speak in such a way—is this not a fault? You have not yet ceased to err."

Zichan said, "Since you are already as you are, do you still contend with Yao in goodness? Do you not reckon your virtue insufficient to turn back upon yourself? None who do good surpass Yao, yet you contend with Yao in goodness—this is to desire to surpass Yao and become the foremost man. You, having been careless and thus suffered amputation, still wish to contend with Yao in goodness. I fear your virtue is insufficient to turn back upon yourself and redress the fault of your amputation—how could you surpass Yao?"

Shentu Jia replied, "Those who explain their faults and yet are not deemed worthy of death are many; those who do not explain their faults and yet are not deemed worthy of life are few. *Self-explanation* means to state one’s own case. Following the previous mention of *fault*: if one states one’s faults, then one’s transgression is not great, and thus one suffers only amputation—those whose bodies are not deemed worthy of death are many. If one does not state one’s faults, others deem one’s crime extreme, deserving not merely amputation, and those whose bodies are not deemed worthy of life are few. To know there is no remedy and yet accept it as fate—only one of virtue can do this.

"Wandering within Yi’s drawn bow: the center is the very place where the arrow strikes. Yet to miss it is fate. Yi was skilled in shooting, and to wander within the range of his bow was to be in the place where the arrow must strike. Yet some escaped death by missing—this was fate. That I did not escape punishment was likewise fate.

"Many have mocked me for my incomplete foot, and I would flare up in anger. But when I came to the Master’s place, my anger would vanish and return to its source. Did the Master not wash me with goodness? I have wandered with you for nineteen years, yet you have never once taken me for a cripple. Now you wander with me within the bounds of form and yet seek me beyond form—is this not a fault? *Within the bounds of form*—virtue. *Beyond form*—amputation. To repeat: is this not a fault?"

Zichan, startled, changed his expression and said, "You need not speak thus."

A cripple from Lu, Shushan Wuzhi, came to see Confucius. Confucius said, "You were not careful, and already suffered such misfortune. Though you come now, what can be done?"

Wuzhi replied, "I, not knowing my duty, failed to preserve my wholeness and treated my body lightly—thus I lost my foot. Now that I come, what remains of my honored foot still endures. *The right is honored*; though my left foot was amputated, my right foot remains. Thus I strive to preserve my wholeness. Heaven covers all without exception; Earth bears all without exception. I took you, Master, to be like Heaven and Earth—how could I have known you would be thus, unable to bear my amputation?"

Confucius said, "I am indeed coarse. Why not come in? Let me share with you what I have heard." Wuzhi departed.

Confucius said, "Disciples, take heed. Wuzhi, though a cripple, still strives to study and redress his past misdeeds—how much more should one of complete virtue do so!"

Wuzhi said to Lao Dan, "Is Confucius not yet a perfected person? Why does he show such deferential respect, treating his disciples as if they were his teachers? He seeks to make his name known through strange and marvelous deeds, unaware that the perfected person takes these as his own fetters."

Lao Dan said, "Why not simply lead him to see death and life as a single thread, the permissible and the impermissible as a single strand, and thus free him from his fetters? Would this not suffice?"

Wuzhi said, "Heaven has punished him—how can it be undone?"

Duke Ai of Lu asked Confucius, "In Wei there is an ugly man named Aitai Tuo. Men who dwell with him grow attached and cannot leave. Women who see him say to their parents, 'Rather than be another man’s wife, I would be this man’s concubine'—and there are more than ten such cases. He has never been heard to initiate speech; he merely harmonizes with others. He holds no position of rulership to save others from death, nor does he amass wealth to distribute it below..."

The full moon’s fullness is likened to a man’s belly. Yet he startles the world with his ugliness, harmonizing without leading, his wisdom confined to no realm—yet male and female gather before him. These three phrases mark a turn in the preceding passage. "Male and female" refer to the men and women [mentioned earlier]. There must be something extraordinary about him.

I summoned him to observe, and indeed, his ugliness startled the world. Yet when he dwelt with me, before a month had passed, I took note of his character; before a full year had elapsed, I trusted him. With no minister in the state, I offered him the governance. He responded with hesitation, as if declining. I felt shame—was I the shameful one?—and so turned inward. In the end, I entrusted him with the state. Soon after, he left me and departed. I grieved as if bereft, as though the kingdom held no joy for me. What manner of man is he?

Confucius said, "I once journeyed to Chu and chanced upon piglets nursing from their dead mother. After a moment, they shivered, glanced about, then abandoned her and fled. They did not see themselves in her, nor recognize her as their kind. What they loved in their mother was not her form, but that which animated her form. When they no longer saw themselves in her, the piglets knew only that their sustenance was gone; when they no longer recognized their kind, it was because the milk no longer resembled what they had known. That which animates form is spirit. The piglets, unaware of their mother’s death, fled when the spirit no longer connected.

This illustrates that in former days, your dealings with Aitai Tuo were not an exchange of forms but of spirits. To lose him is like the piglets losing their mother’s spirit.

Those who die in battle are not buried with funeral fans; those who have lost their feet do not cherish their shoes. In both cases, the root is gone. Form is the root, while the fan and shoes are mere adornments. The consorts of the Son of Heaven do not cut their nails or pierce their ears, for one who draws near the ruler must not mar their form. A man who takes a wife remains apart from court for a time—*The Rites* prescribe that one in three years’ mourning or newly wed does not serve. If one whose form is whole may yet be thus, how much more one whose virtue is whole?

Now Aitai Tuo wins trust without speaking, draws near without merit, and makes men offer him their states, fearing only that he will not accept. He must be one whose talents are whole and whose virtue does not manifest in form."

Duke Ai said, "What does it mean for talents to be whole?"

Confucius replied, "Life and death, existence and annihilation, success and failure, wealth and poverty, worthiness and unworthiness, slander and praise, hunger and thirst, cold and heat—these are the changes of affairs, the workings of fate. They alternate day and night before us, yet knowledge cannot fathom their beginning. Thus they cannot disturb harmony or enter the mind’s sanctuary. To make one’s joy unbroken, to let day and night flow without gap, and to meet all things with the springtime of the heart—this is to bridge the intervals and bring forth the season within. The season does not arise from the qi of yin and yang, but from one’s own heart. This is what it means for talents to be whole.

What does it mean for virtue not to manifest in form? Stillness is the fullness of water at rest. It may serve as a model, for it preserves its inner stillness without outward disturbance. Virtue is the cultivation of inner harmony. When virtue does not manifest in form, things cannot depart from it. Duke Ai’s attachment to Aitai Tuo is such a case."

Another day, Duke Ai told Confucius’ disciple Minzi, "At first, I thought that by facing south as ruler of the world, upholding the people’s order, and grieving over their deaths, I had attained the utmost understanding. Now, hearing the words of a perfected person, I fear I lack substance, have treated my body lightly, and lost my state. I and Kong Qiu are not lord and subject, but friends in virtue."

Yin Qi, Zhi Li, Wu Shen—one guarded the city gate by standing on tiptoe, another was a man of broken form, and the last had no heels. Yet when they advised Duke Ling of Wei, he delighted in them, and when he looked upon whole men, their necks seemed too slender.

The neck is called *dou*. *Jianjian* describes its slender, elongated appearance. Delighting in the heel-less man while gazing upon whole men, the duke found their necks thin and long—ugly and unworthy of regard. The *Rites of Zhou*’s "Artificer" states: "He counts the eyes and measures the neck." The commentary notes: "A long neck," suggesting *jian* and *gu* share the same meaning.

Another man with a great goiter like a jar or urn advised Duke Huan of Qi. The duke delighted in him, yet when he looked upon whole men, their necks seemed *jianjian*. Thus, virtue may excel while form is forgotten. If one does not forget what ought to be forgotten but forgets what ought not to be forgotten, this is called true forgetting. What ought to be forgotten is form; what ought not to be forgotten is virtue. Yet the world forgets virtue while clinging to form—this is true forgetfulness.

Therefore, the sage wanders freely, yet deems knowledge a blight, restraint glue, virtue a bond, and skill trade. The sage does not scheme—why employ knowledge? He does not carve—why employ glue? He suffers no loss—why employ virtue? He hoards no goods—why engage in trade? These four are the fasting of Heaven. The fasting of Heaven is the sustenance of Heaven. Having received sustenance from Heaven, why seek it from man?

*Nie* means ominous blight; *yue*, strict restraint; *jiao*, fixation; *jie*, obligation; *gong*, craft; *shang*, merchant. The sage’s heart wanders in Heaven, yet he regards the wise as ominous, the restrained as rigid, the virtuous as bound to obligation, and the skilled as mere merchants. The sage does not plot—why use cunning? He does not carve—why use glue? Having nothing to lose, why practice the Way? Having no desire for gain, why engage in trade? Of these four lines, two are direct statements, two are metaphors. The term *shang* prompts the fourfold repetition.

It is the nurturance of Heaven—what Heaven bestows is constant. Receiving Heaven’s nurturance is to be fed by Heaven. Having received sustenance from Heaven, why seek it from man? This is like attaining Heaven’s nobility and not pursuing man’s rank.

Possessing the form of man but not the passions of man: with the form of man, one mingles among men; without the passions of man, right and wrong do not adhere to one’s person. How tiny and insignificant—this is what binds one to humanity! How vast and grand—to stand alone in Heaven’s completion. *Ao* means release, the idea of attaining through letting go. This speaks of having no passions. Below, the text cites the debate with Huizi to expound the meaning of no passions.

Huizi said to Zhuangzi: "Do men truly have no passions?"
Zhuangzi replied: "Yes."
Huizi said: "If a man has no passions, how can he be called a man?"
Zhuangzi said: "The Dao gives him his appearance, Heaven gives him his form—how can he not be called a man?"
Huizi said: "If he is called a man, how can he have no passions?"
Zhuangzi said: "This is not what I mean by passions. What I call having no passions is that a man does not inwardly harm his body with likes and dislikes, but constantly follows nature without embellishing life."
Huizi said: "Without embellishing life, how can one preserve one’s body?"
Zhuangzi said: "The Dao gives him his appearance, Heaven gives him his form—he does not inwardly harm his body with likes and dislikes. Now you strain your spirit externally, exhaust your essence, lean against a tree chanting, or rest on a withered paulownia in slumber. Heaven chose your form, yet you clamor about ‘hard and white.’"

The Dao gives him his appearance, Heaven gives him his form—this is nature. "Not embellishing life" means that human life has the natural endowment of Heaven, to which not a hair’s breadth of human effort may be added. *Gaowu* refers to what is now called withered paulownia. *Wutong* and *gaotong* are synonymous, though the term may apply to various types of paulownia.
