Only the withered paulownia is used for lutes and zithers, hence the name. "Resting on it in slumber" means the instrument is always at hand, so even in weariness or drowsiness, one still clings to it. Standing, one leans against a tree chanting; sitting, one rests on a withered paulownia in slumber—these are the deeds of one who strains the spirit and exhausts the essence, who spurns Heaven’s gift to engage in debates over "hard and white."

*The True Classic of Southern Florescence, Following the Original Text, Scroll Six* ends.
*The True Classic of Southern Florescence, Following the Original Text, Scroll Seven* begins.

*The Record of Lu Lingzhu Peak by Luo Miandao, collated by his disciple Peng Xiang*

**Inner Chapters: The Great Master Teacher**
This chapter expounds the esoteric essentials of the Dao, hence its title *The Great Master Teacher*.

To know what Heaven does and what man does—this is perfection.
To know what Heaven does is to be born of Heaven;
To know what man does is to nurture with what his knowledge knows what his knowledge does not know, so that he completes his Heaven-appointed years without dying prematurely—this is the fullness of knowledge.

To know what Heaven does is to embody the spontaneity of Heaven’s Dao.
To know what man does is to fulfill what human affairs ought to be.
To be born of Heaven is to follow Heaven in being born.
What knowledge knows includes such things as cultivating goodness, shunning evil, pursuing fortune, and avoiding misfortune.
What knowledge does not know is the utmost reality within oneself, which all possess yet do not recognize. If one can nurture it without harm, one may also complete one’s Heaven-appointed years without dying prematurely—this, too, is the fullness of knowledge.

Yet there is peril.
For knowledge must await something before it is *settled*, and what it awaits is precisely what is not settled.
How do we know that what I call Heaven is not man?
Or that what I call man is not Heaven?

Even so, in what is called knowing what man does, there remains cause for concern: though man may know it, he must still await Heaven before he attains certainty.
*Settled* means *fixed*.
Yet what he awaits from Heaven is itself without fixity.
As with Yan and Robber Zhi, who lived long—goodness does not necessarily bring blessing, nor evil calamity—how then can we be sure that what I call Heaven does not belong to man, or what I call man does not belong to Heaven?

Only with the true person does true knowledge arise.
The true person alone truly knows the distinction between Heaven and man.

What is the true person?
The true persons of old did not resist the few—though they gained little, they did not resent it.
They did not vaunt their achievements—though they succeeded, they did not inflate their qi.
They did not scheme for affairs—*affairs* encompasses all undertakings. They entrusted everything to spontaneity and did not plan ahead.

Such were they: when they erred, they did not regret; when they acted rightly, they did not congratulate themselves.
Such were they: they ascended heights without trembling, entered water without getting wet, entered fire without feeling heat—this is knowledge that has ascended to the utmost in the Dao.

Thus the true persons of old:
In sleep, they did not dream; in waking, they had no cares.
Their food was not savored; their breath was deep.
The true person’s breath reaches the heels; the common person’s breath reaches only the throat.
Deep breath is as if stored in the feet; shallow breath emerges from the throat—though in truth, the *qi*-sea is the root of breath.
Those who *qufu* (flatter and fawn) have constricted throats, their speech like lewd music.
*Qufu* refers to those who ingratiate themselves with others. Their words, uttered between the throat, resemble wanton melodies.
The deeper one’s desires, the shallower one’s heavenly mechanism.
The *heavenly mechanism* is the natural movement of qi, that is, breath. Deep desires make breath shallow.
Thus those who *qufu* have deep desires, and their speech, like croaking, reveals a shallow heavenly mechanism.

The true persons of old did not delight in life, nor did they abhor death.
They did not rejoice in going forth, nor did they resist returning.
They came and went with effortless ease, nothing more.
They did not forget their beginning, nor did they seek their end.
They received and rejoiced in it; they forgot and returned to it.
This is called not using the mind to cast aside the Dao, not using man to assist Heaven—this is the true person.

Such were they:
Their minds were steadfast—*mind* here means *intent*, which did not wander.
Their demeanor was tranquil; their foreheads broad and smooth.
Their bearing was chill as autumn, warm as spring.
Their joy and anger passed through the four seasons, harmonizing with things without anyone knowing their limit.

Thus when the sage employs troops, he may destroy a state yet not lose the people’s hearts.
His beneficence extends to ten thousand generations, yet he does not act out of love for others.
Therefore, one who delights in connecting with things is no sage;
One who has partiality is not humane;
One who relies on the seasons is not worthy—when primordial qi is undifferentiated, how can the four seasons be distinguished?
One who does not connect benefit and harm is no gentleman—when they are connected and made one, how can benefit and harm be distinguished?
One who pursues fame at the cost of self is no scholar;
One who loses self is not true—such cannot employ others, nor be employed by others.

Like the ancient worthies—*Hujie*, *Wuguang* (a man of the Yellow Emperor’s time, whose ears were seven inches long), *Boyi*, *Shuqi*, *Jizi* (also called *Xuyu*; *Shizi* says Jizi’s name was *Xuyu*, though some say it was *Bigan*), *Jita*, *Shentu Di* (men of the Yin dynasty; *Xunzi* records that the latter carried a stone and drowned himself in the river)—they allowed themselves to be employed by others, followed others’ paths, yet did not follow their own.

The true persons of old:
Their appearance was fitting yet not cliquish—they harmonized with things without forming factions.
They seemed insufficient yet did not defer—humble and retiring, they did not lightly submit.
They were companionable yet not rigid—their bearing was relaxed and self-possessed, like a vessel with edges yet not hard or stubborn.
They were expansive yet not florid—their qi was vast and open, though empty, it was not merely ornamental.
They were buoyant, as if pleased!
Yet restrained, as if compelled!
*Buoyant* means exuberant; *restrained* implies self-suppression. Though they seemed pleased, there was something they could not help.
They were full in their expression toward me, yet steadfast in their virtue toward me—though their appearance was full, their virtue was steadfast.
They were coarse, as if worldly—*coarse* means unrefined. Though they did not cultivate refinement, blending with the vulgar, they were vigilant in their loftiness and unruliness, beyond control.

Their affinity was boundless in their love of leisure, their oblivion so profound they forgot their own words. *Boundless* means continuous and unbroken; *oblivion* means abandonment and forgetting. Though their manner was sealed and impenetrable, with nothing to glimpse or trace, they remained forgetful, never boasting of themselves.

They took punishment as their foundation, ritual as their wings, wisdom as their timing, and virtue as their path. Taking punishment as their foundation meant they were lenient in their severity; taking ritual as their wings meant they could move through the world; taking wisdom as their timing meant they acted only when compelled by circumstance; taking virtue as their path meant that, like one who walks to the hills, they were seen as diligent in practice—yet their progress was natural.

Ritual is the root of personal conduct. The root serves as the foundation, while punishment aids governance. The law serves as wings; yet though punishment is established, it is tempered by leniency and does not kill, thus it can be the foundation. Though ritual is strict, it can be practiced throughout the world, thus it can be the wings. Virtue governs the natural—like the movement of the four seasons, it operates without deliberate intent to encompass all things. Like a traveler following a path, one responds to affairs not by force but by necessity, acting naturally. Thus, virtue’s cultivation is not forced, like accidental footprints on a mountain—those who do not understand may truly believe it is diligent effort. Therefore, their love of it was singular, and their lack of love was also singular.

Their singularity was singular, and their lack of singularity was also singular. The singular joined with Heaven as its companion; the non-singular joined with man as its companion. Heaven and man do not overcome one another—this is called the *True Person*. Those who loved it were fundamentally one with me; those who did not love it also regarded it as one. Thus, their oneness with me was singular, and their lack of oneness was also singular. The singular naturally joined with Heaven as its companion; the non-singular naturally joined with man as its companion. Heaven and man do not overcome one another—why then introduce likes and dislikes between them?

Life and death are a matter of fate, as constant as night and day—this is Heaven. What man cannot interfere with is the nature of things. They took Heaven as their father and cherished it, yet how much more should they cherish what transcends it! Men take having a lord as better than themselves and die for it, yet how much more should they value what is true!

When a spring dries up, fish find themselves stranded on land, breathing moisture onto one another, wetting each other with foam—better to forget one another in rivers and lakes. Rather than praising Yao and condemning Jie, it is better to forget both and be transformed by the Dao.

The nature of things is their true principle. Taking Heaven as one’s father means the father is the Heaven of the child. What transcends this is the Dao, loftier than the father. What is true in this Dao is the true lord. To be transformed by the Dao is to be dissolved in it, so that praise and blame vanish.

Life and death are fate, like Heaven’s constancy of night and day—beyond human power to alter, an unchanging principle of things. One should return to this Dao alone. This Dao is more revered in man than lord or father, yet man does not realize it. Rather than entangling oneself in the fray of praise and blame, how much better to rise above it in this Dao.

The Great Clod burdens me with form, wearies me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death—thus, to treat life well is to treat death well. Hiding a boat in a ravine or a mountain in a marsh seems secure, yet in the dead of night, a strong man may carry them away, and the unwitting will not know. Even when small and large are hidden appropriately, there is still escape. But to hide the world within the world is to leave no room for escape—this is the great truth of things.

Men delight in possessing human form, yet if this form undergoes ten thousand transformations without end, how can its joy be measured! Thus, the sage wanders where things cannot escape and all are preserved. Embracing early death and old age, beginning and end—people emulate this, let alone the myriad things bound to it and awaiting the one transformation!

To treat life well and death well—this "well" belongs to the Creator. To embrace early death and old age, beginning and end—this "well" belongs to man. Embracing early death means that though one dies young, it is not a bad death. The one transformation differs from the ten thousand transformations: the ten thousand transformations are the myriad changes wrought by man, while the one transformation is the great leap, unbound by the Dao.

Returning to life and death: life inevitably leads to death, yet those who cling to life and shun death still cannot escape. It is like hiding a boat in a ravine or a mountain in a marsh, believing it secure—unaware that in the dead of night, the boat may be moved by a strong man, the mountain felled by force, all beyond expectation and unnoticed. Though the ravine is vast and the boat small, the marsh vast and the mountain small, each hidden appropriately, there is still escape. But to hide the world within the world is to leave no room for concealment—no place to flee. Thus, rather than hiding with intent, it is better to hide without intent.

What is meant by "hiding the world within the world" is like tilling fields and digging wells, unaware of the Emperor’s power. The world, too, is a thing—thus, this is the great and constant truth of things. Earlier, only the nature of things was mentioned; here, "constant" and "great" are added, extending the analogy of hiding boats, mountains, and the world. It seems strange, so the word "constant" broadens it to the world, while "great" returns to the human body. Thus, it is said that even briefly possessing human form is cause for joy. What is precious about human form is that it contains ten thousand transformations—how can its joy be measured?

Thus, the sage wanders where things cannot escape and all abide—this is what is meant by "hiding the world within the world." The sage grasps the essence of hiding the world, enabling the people to nurture life, bury the dead, and suffer no plague of epidemics or calamities. Yet people still admire and emulate such governance—how much more so when the Dao is hidden within the body, for it is the root of the ten thousand things and the source of all transformation, nearer and more intimate than hiding the world. Is there anything easier?

The Dao has sentiment and sincerity, is without action and without form; it may be transmitted but not received, attained but not seen. It is self-originating, self-rooted, existing before heaven and earth, enduring since ancient times. It spiritually animates ghosts and gods, gives birth to heaven and earth. Before the Supreme Ultimate, it is not lofty; below the six directions, it is not profound. Born before heaven and earth, it is not ancient; older than antiquity, it is not aged.

The term "Dao" reveals the Great Ancestral Master as possessing sentiment and sincerity, being without action and without form—just as the *Qiwu* chapter states: it may be practiced, is trustworthy yet without visible form, has sentiment yet no shape. It has the sentiment of union, the sincerity of verification, yet no deliberate action and no tangible image. It may be transmitted orally but not handed over, attained in the heart but not seen with the eyes. For it is formless, the root of yin-yang and the five phases, existing before heaven and earth. Ghosts and gods rely on it for their numinosity, heaven and earth rely on it for their generation—this is what Laozi calls the "root of heaven and earth." Humans share the same pivot with creation, so within the body there is also a Supreme Ultimate. Laozi and Zhuangzi’s discourse on the Dao extends only this far. Later generations borrowed metaphors like lead, mercury, dragon, and tiger to make it clearer and more accessible.

It is not lofty, not profound, not ancient, not aged—all are words of praise. Fu Xi grasped it to harness heaven and earth; Fuxi attained it to inherit the mother of qi. The Northern Dipper holds it, remaining unconfused through the ages; the sun and moon hold it, ceaselessly revolving. Kanpi grasped it to inherit Kunlun, seizing the mother of primordial qi—the Northern Dipper is the celestial axis. Sima Biao says: Kanpi is a spirit with a human face and beast’s body. Feng Yi attained it to roam the great rivers. Sima Biao says: According to the *Qingling Zhuan*, Feng Yi was a man from Dishou in Tongxiang, Huayin, who ingested eight minerals, became a water immortal, and is known as the River Earl. Another account says he drowned in the river on the gengzi day of the eighth month. Jianwu grasped it to dwell on Mount Tai. Sima Biao says: A mountain spirit who did not die until the time of Confucius. The Yellow Emperor attained it to ascend to the cloudy heavens; Zhuanxu grasped it to reside in the Dark Palace. The Yellow Emperor ascended to heaven through the Dao. The Dark Palace is the palace of the north. The *Monthly Ordinances* says: Its emperor is Zhuanxu, its spirit is Xuanming. Yuqiang holds it, standing at the North Pole. Sima Biao says: The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* states: On an islet in the Northern Sea there is a spirit with a human face and bird’s body, wearing two blue snakes as earrings and treading two red snakes, named Yuqiang. The Queen Mother of the West attained it, seated in Shaoguang. The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* says: The Queen Mother of the West resembles a human, with a dog’s tail and disheveled hair, wearing a sheng-ornament and skilled in whistling, dwelling by the banks of the Xun River. The *Inner Biography of Emperor Wu of Han* says: The Queen Mother of the West and the Lady of the Upper Prime descended to the emperor, beautiful in appearance, divine immortals. Shaoguang, according to Sima Biao, is a cave name. Cui says: a mountain name. Another says: the name of the empty realm in the west. None know her beginning, none know her end. These two lines refer to the Queen Mother of the West. Peng Zu attained it, living from the time of Youyu through the Five Lords; Li says: The Five Lords are Kunwu of Xia, Da Peng and Shiwei of Yin, and Duke Huan of Qi and Duke Wen of Jin in Zhou. Fu Yue grasped it to assist Wu Ding, ruling the world, riding the eastern cord and the tail of the Winnowing Basket to join the ranks of the stars. Sima Biao says: Fu Yue is a star above the tail, meaning he rode between the eastern cord and the tail of the Winnowing Basket. The Winnowing Basket and Dipper are the eastern cord of the Milky Way’s ford. Cui’s version adds twenty-two more characters here, but they are inappropriate to the meaning. From the beginning of this chapter to this point, the essentials of the Great Dao are fully expounded. Yet fearing people might cling rigidly, the following text further elaborates that life and death are both void, broadening their vision—akin to the teachings of the School of Principle and the School of Nature.

Nanbo Zi Kui asked Nü Ou: "You are advanced in years, yet your complexion is like that of a child. How is this?" He replied: "I have heard the Dao." Nanbo Zi Kui said: "May I learn it?" He said: "Alas! How could you?"

You are not the right person for this.

Now, there was one named Bu Liangyi who possessed the talent of a sage but not the Dao of a sage, while I possess the Dao of a sage but not the talent of a sage. I wished to teach him, hoping he might indeed become a sage. If not, to impart the Dao of a sage to one with the talent of a sage would be easy indeed. Yet I still persisted in teaching him: after three days, he was able to put the world outside himself. Once he had put the world outside himself, I persisted further, and after seven days, he was able to put things outside himself. Once he had put things outside himself, I persisted further, and after nine days, he was able to put life outside himself. Only after putting life outside himself could he attain clarity at dawn. With a single dawn, he penetrated all—no need to wait for seven or nine days. After attaining clarity at dawn, he could perceive the solitary. After perceiving the solitary, he could transcend past and present. After transcending past and present, he could enter the realm of neither death nor life. That which kills life does not die; that which gives life to life does not live. Withering the form and deadening the heart is how one kills life yet does not die; indulging the passions and following desires is how one gives life to life yet does not live.

As a thing, it sends off all without exception, welcomes all without exception, destroys all without exception, and completes all without exception. Its name is *yingning*—tranquility through disturbance. *Jiang* means to send off. Though things may strike against it, they naturally settle into tranquility. *Yingning* is that which is completed only after disturbance.

Nanbo Zikui said: "From whom did you hear this?"
He replied: "I heard it from the son of *Fumo*. The son of *Fumo* heard it from the grandson of *Luosong*. *Cheng* means to complete and settle. *Fumo* and *Luosong* are borrowed names for writing and recitation. The grandson of *Luosong* heard it from *Zhanming*; *Zhanming* heard it from *Niexu*. *Zhanming* and *Niexu* are borrowed names for seeing and hearing. *Niexu* heard it from *Xuyi*; *Xuyi* heard it from *Yuming* and *Yu’ou*. *Xuyi* and *Yushu* are borrowed names for laborers’ songs and chants. *Yu’ou* heard it from *Xuanming*; *Xuanming* heard it from *Canliao*; *Canliao* heard it from *Yishi*. *Canliao* refers to the vast and boundless beginning, the profound beyond profundity.

Zisi, Ziyu, Zili, and Zilai said to one another: "Who can take non-being as the head, life as the spine, and death as the tailbone? Who knows that death and life, existence and nonexistence, are one body? That one shall be my friend." The four looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in perfect accord, and so they became friends.

Soon after, Ziyu fell ill, and Zisi went to visit him.
Ziyu said of himself: "How magnificent! The Creator is making me like this—so contorted!" *Juju* means extreme curvature. *Jiang* suggests it may grow even worse. His back was hunched, his spine exposed.

The five orifices rise above, his chin sinks to his navel, his shoulders tower above his crown—these three phrases are explained in *"Among the People."* His neck vertebrae jut toward heaven. *Juzhui* refers to the cervical vertebrae. *Ju* means "joint." The twenty-four spinal joints protrude like tumors. These five lines all describe Ziyu’s form. The qi of yin and yang has fallen into disorder. This single line speaks of his illness. Yet his heart remains untroubled, and leaning on his staff, he gazes into the well, saying: "Alas! The Creator is once more making me like this—so contorted!"

He does not suffer from his affliction. Leaning on his staff and dragging himself forward, he naturally gazes into the well, his form already bent. Now, stricken again, his contortion will only grow worse. Zisi said: "Do you resent it?" Zisi spoke in jest: "Do you resent this?"

Ziyu replied: "Why would I resent it? If gradually I were transformed, and my left arm became a rooster, I would use it to crow at dawn. If gradually my right arm became a crossbow, I would use it to hunt owls for roasting. If gradually my corpse became a wheel, and my spirit a horse, I would ride it—why would I need another chariot? Moreover, what comes is timing; what goes is compliance. Abiding in timing and complying with change, sorrow and joy cannot enter. This is what the ancients called *xuanjie*."

*Jin* means "gradual." *Jia* means "suppose." *Shi* means "to serve." *Xuan* is written as *xuan* (suspended), meaning *jie* (release). Like the release of something suspended, it is akin to the untying of an inverted hanging. Those who cannot free themselves are bound by things. When something is suspended above, if it suddenly unbinds itself, it is called *xuanjie*. Those who cannot free themselves have been forcibly tied by others, leaving no way to untangle themselves. This speaks of how deeply the heart is entangled by worldly concerns. Moreover, things have long been unable to overcome Heaven. Why should I resent it?"

Soon after, Zilai fell ill, gasping as if near death. His wife and children gathered around, weeping. Li went to visit him and shouted: "Away! Begone! Do not disturb his transformation!"

The Daoists regard death as transformation, so Li shouted at them to leave and not disturb this transformation. Leaning against the doorway, he spoke to Zilai: "How wondrous is the work of creation! What will it make of you next? Where will it send you? Will it turn you into a mouse’s liver? Or an insect’s limb?"—for none can know into what form one might transform, or whether it may become something exceedingly small.

Zilai said: "To parents, a child must obey their commands, no matter if they send them east, west, south, or north. The forces of yin and yang govern man no less than parents do. If they now draw near to my death and I refuse to heed them, I would be obstinate—what fault would lie with them? The great earth bears me with form, wearies me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me in death. Thus, to treat my life well is to treat my death well.

"Now, when a great smith casts metal, if the metal leaps up and cries, *'I must become a* *Mo Ye* *sword!'* the smith would surely deem it ill-omened. Likewise, if one who has taken human form were to say, *'I am human! I am human!'* the Creator would surely deem them an ill-omened person. It is not necessary to insist on transforming into a human—what harm is there in becoming another thing? Now, if we take heaven and earth as a great furnace and creation as a great smith, where could one go and not be acceptable?"

Then Zilai fell into a peaceful slumber and awoke with a start—*peaceful* as in tranquil, and *awoke* as in the suddenness of waking. For life is but a dreamlike death, and death but an awakening.

Zisang Hu, Mengzi Fan, and Ziqin Zhang became friends and said to one another: "Who among us can join together in non-joining, act for one another in non-acting? Who can ascend to heaven, roam the mists, soar boundlessly into the infinite, and forget life without end or limit?" The three looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in perfect accord—*soar* as in playful ascent. Since all three could do as they described, they no longer needed to respond, but simply exchanged smiles, their hearts free of discord. Thus they became friends, harmonious and without gap.

When Zisang Hu died, his body had yet to be buried. Upon hearing this, Confucius sent Zigong to attend to the funeral rites. Zigong went to wait upon the proceedings—his task being to prepare the mourning rituals. As all three were men of Lu, Zigong was dispatched.

There, one wove reed mats, another plucked the zither, and together they sang in harmony:
*"Ah, come, Zisang Hu!*
*Ah, come, Zisang Hu!*
*You have returned to your true nature,*
*While we still remain as men—oh!"*

Zigong hastened forward and said, "May I ask: is it proper to sing before the corpse?"
The two looked at each other and smiled, saying, "What does he know of the meaning of ritual?"
"The essence of ritual arises from human feeling—if feeling does not stir, what use is ritual?"

Zigong returned and reported to Confucius: "What manner of men are they? They have cultivated their conduct to the point of disregarding their own forms, singing before the corpse without change of countenance. I have no words to describe them. What manner of men are they?"
Confucius said, "They wander beyond the bounds, while I wander within them. Outer and inner do not reach one another—had I sent you to offer condolences, I would have been crude indeed. They are now companions of the Creator, roaming in the single qi of Heaven and Earth. *Bounds* here means the established norms. *Companions of the Creator* means they follow what gives them life and become one with it. Heaven and Earth generate all things through nothing more than a single qi; human life in the world is but a roaming within this single qi of Heaven and Earth. To them, life is an excrescent growth, death the bursting of a boil. If this is so, how could they know where life and death, before and after, truly lie? Borrowing from strange things, entrusting themselves to shared form; forgetting liver and gall, neglecting ears and eyes; circling through beginning and end, unaware of origin or end—vagrant beyond the dust, carefree in the work of non-action. How could they be muddled by worldly ritual, seeking to impress the eyes and ears of the crowd?"

Zigong said, "Then upon what bounds does the Master rely?"
*"Muddled"* means confused. *"The eyes and ears of the crowd"* refers back to the earlier question about ritual. Earlier, you spoke of wandering within bounds—now, seeing this, I wonder upon what bounds you rely.
Confucius said, "I am one of Heaven’s punished people. Yet even so, I share this with you."
Confucius referred to himself: "I cannot be like them—I am one of those punished by Heaven. Though this is so, I will share this Way with you."
Zigong said, "May I ask what this bound is?"
Confucius said, "Fish are made for water, men are made for the Dao. Those made for water dig ponds to be nourished; those made for the Dao find stillness without effort. *Bound* here means the method by which one attains mastery. Fish made for water dig ponds and are thus nourished; men made for the Dao find stillness without effort. Thus it is said: Fish forget one another in rivers and lakes, men forget one another in the arts of the Dao. Forgetting in rivers and lakes means no longer needing to dig ponds; forgetting in the arts of the Dao means no longer needing effortless stillness—being made for something is not as good as forgetting it."

Zigong said, "May I ask about the *unconventional person*?"
Confucius was often out of step with the world, so Zigong asked about the *unconventional person*.
Confucius said, "The *unconventional person* is unconventional among men but equal to Heaven. Thus it is said: Heaven’s petty man is man’s noble man; man’s noble man is Heaven’s petty man."

Yan Hui asked Confucius, "Mengsun Cai—when his mother died, he wept without tears, his heart felt no grief, and in his mourning station he showed no sorrow. Lacking these three, he yet overshadowed Lu with his mastery of mourning. Is it truly possible to gain a reputation without substance?"
Yan Hui found this particularly strange.
Confucius said, "Mengsun Cai has exhausted it—he has advanced beyond knowledge. He surpasses those who know mourning rites. He sought simplicity but could not attain it—yet he has already simplified. Those who study the Dao desire to diminish their emotions but cannot; Mengsun Cai has already diminished his. Mengsun Cai does not know why he lives, nor why he dies. He does not know what to pursue first, nor what to follow after—whether death comes first or later. If he has transformed into a thing, does he wait for the transformations he does not know to reach him? And if he is about to transform, how could he know that he will not? If he is about not to transform, how could he know that he already has? *Transformed into a thing* refers to those already transformed; *transformations reaching him* refers to those not yet transformed. *Transformations he does not know* means those he does not realize have reached him. It is as if those who died earlier transformed into things, waiting for the transformations they do not know to reach them—yet those who are about to die, how could they know they will not transform? And those about not to transform, how could they know they already have? I and you are but dreamers who have not yet awakened—how could we know? Moreover, he has a startling form but an undamaged heart. *He* refers to those who study the Dao: though their physical changes may seem alarming, they do not harm the heart. He has a temporary dwelling but no emotional death. Human life in the world is fleeting as a single morning, yet this does not kill his emotions. Mengsun Cai alone awakens—when others weep, he weeps too, following their *so-ness*. *So* is a familiar term, like *you* or *thou*, the opposite of *I*. Parents are one’s close kin, while others may be called *you* or *thou*. Now Mengsun Cai merely awakens to others weeping and weeps with them—his weeping does not come from the heart but from their *so-ness*, treating his kin as if they were others’ kin. And yet, we call this *our* ears—how could we know what we truly mean by *our*?"

The base text originally had only the single character *qie* (且), but adding *ye* (也) softens the phrasing for readability. *Qiefu* (且夫) and *qie* (且) share the same meaning. Following the earlier *nai* (乃), the *wu* (吾) below naturally arises. Suppose Confucius were to say: "Yet *we*—I and Yan Hui—dare not view our kin as Mengsun Cai does, treating them as if they were others’ kin, calling them *our* own. But how can we truly know what we mean by *our*?"

The text below elaborates this meaning:
Yet have you ever dreamed of being a bird soaring to the heavens, or a fish diving deep into the abyss? Do you know whether the speaker now is awake—or dreaming? Ordinary dreams of fish or birds are not so strange, but to dream of being a crow flying straight to the sky, or a fish plunging directly into the depths—clearly, in that moment, one’s self *is* the bird or fish. Yet here we are, speaking now—how can we be sure this is waking? Might it not be the dream instead? Seen thus, what we call *our* own remains unknowable. Earlier, it was said, "We are merely dreaming and have not yet awakened"; now it is said, "Even waking may be a dream"—this goes a step deeper.

One arrives at joy before laughter, offers laughter before arranging it.
*Zao* (造) means "to reach"; *shi* (适) means "contentment." Joy naturally gives rise to laughter, but when one suddenly attains a state of perfect contentment, true delight arises before laughter can follow. Then, when laughter is spontaneously offered, it comes without preparation. *Pai* (排) means "to arrange." Spontaneous laughter arises unconsciously, without need for deliberate arrangement—this illustrates the natural, uncontrived truth that does not depend on rigid adherence to ritual and propriety.

To submit to fate and embrace transformation is to enter the vast and solitary One.
Expanding on *pai* (排): human life’s wealth or poverty, high or low status, are all predetermined, like the arrangements of creation—one should simply accept them. Death dissolves all, returning to nothingness, which carries the sense of vast emptiness. *Yi* (一) refers to the primordial state before the Great Ultimate divided. To "enter the vast and solitary One" is to return to that origin.

Yierzi visited Xu You and asked: "How did Yao assist you?"
Yierzi replied: "Yao told me: 'You must personally practice benevolence and righteousness, and clearly distinguish right from wrong.'"
Xu You said: "Then why have you come to me now? Yao has already branded you with benevolence and righteousness, and mutilated you with right and wrong. How will you wander the boundless, carefree, and ever-shifting paths?"
*Zisui* (恣睢) describes unbridled freedom; *zhuanxi* (转徙) means unrestrained movement.
Yierzi said: "Even so, I wish to wander within their bounds."
Xu You said: "You cannot. The blind have no part in the beauty of brows and eyes, nor the color of faces; the sightless have no share in the splendor of blue and yellow, embroidered patterns and brocade."
*Wutongzi* (无瞳子) refers to the blind; *gu* (瞽) refers to those with pupils but no sight.
Yierzi said: "Yet Wu Zhuang lost her beauty, Ju Liang lost his strength—"
Both are hypothetical names: *Wu Zhuang* (无庄) means "without adornment," *Ju Liang* (据梁) means "relying on brute force."
"—and the Yellow Emperor lost his wisdom, all through the refining fires of the forge."
*Chui* (锤) is interchangeable with *zhui* (锤).
"How can we know that the Creator will not heal my branding and mend my mutilation, allowing me to ride the perfected Way and follow you?"
Xu You said: "Alas!"

Unknowable it remains. Let me speak to you of its great outline!

O my master! O my master—this is what is meant by the Great Ancestral Master! One who nurtures the ten thousand things yet claims no righteousness, whose blessings span ten thousand generations yet claims no benevolence, who is older than high antiquity yet claims no eld, who encompasses heaven and earth, carves and shapes all forms yet claims no craft—this is the realm in which one roams.

Yan Hui said: "I have advanced."
Zhongni said: "What do you mean?"
He replied: "I have forgotten benevolence and righteousness."
Zhongni said: "Good, yet still not enough."

Another day, he appeared again and said: "I have advanced."
Zhongni said: "What do you mean?"
He replied: "I have forgotten ritual and music."
Zhongni said: "Good, yet still not enough."
*(For Laozi and Zhuangzi, benevolence and righteousness arise from human artifice, thus inferior to ritual and music.)*

Yet another day, he appeared again and said: "I have advanced."
Zhongni asked: "What do you mean?"
He replied: "I have attained *zuowang* (sitting-forgetting)."
Zhongni started and said: "What is *zuowang*?"
Yan Hui replied: "I have cast off my limbs and body, dimmed my hearing and sight, severed myself from form and abandoned knowledge, merging with the Great Thoroughfare—this is *zuowang*."

Zhongni said: "To merge is to have no preferences; when nothing is not merged with, there are no private attachments. To transform is to have no constancy; only by following transformation can there be no fixed abode. And you have indeed proven yourself worthy! I, Qiu, would follow after you."

Ziyu and Zisang were friends, and for ten days it rained without cease. Ziyu said: "Zisang must be in dire straits." He wrapped up rice and went to feed him.

Arriving at Zisang’s gate, he heard sounds like singing, like weeping—plucking his zither, Zisang cried: "Father! Mother! Heaven! Man!" His voice could no longer bear the strain, yet he hastened to recite his verse. (The word *qu* means "to hasten," for his voice was spent with exhaustion; *quju* means to recite without melody or tune.)

Ziyu entered and asked: "Why do you sing your verse in this way?"

Zisang replied: "I pondered who brought me to this extremity, but found no answer. Would my parents wish me to be poor? Heaven covers all without partiality; Earth bears all without favor. Would Heaven and Earth single me out for poverty? I sought the cause, yet could not find it. And so I have come to this extremity—it is fate, nothing more!"

*The Seventh Scroll of the Nan Hua Zhen Jing, Following the Original Text, Concludes Here*
*The Eighth Scroll of the Nan Hua Zhen Jing, Following the Original Text*
*Recorded by Lu Lingzhufeng’s Luo Miandao, Collated by His Disciple Peng Xiang*
*Inner Chapters: "The Adjustment of Controversies"*

This chapter serves to guide emperors and kings in their governance; should they comprehend its meaning, they would return to the era of High Antiquity. Thus concludes the Inner Chapters.

Nie Que questioned Wang Ni four times, and four times received no answer. Thereupon Nie Que leapt with great joy and went to inform Master Pu Yi. Cui Yun notes: *He threw on his robe.* Master Pu Yi said: "Do you now understand?"

The Lord of Yu did not equal the Lord of Tai. Li notes: *The Lord of Tai refers to the Great Court Clan.* The Lord of Yu concealed benevolence to entice others—though he won the people, he had not yet transcended the realm of the artificial. The Lord of Tai lay at ease, rose unhurriedly, now taking himself for a horse, now for an ox. His knowledge was true to reality, his virtue utterly genuine, and he had never entered the realm of the artificial.

The Lord of Yu harbored and concealed the Dao of benevolence to bind the hearts of men—thus he too won the people. Yet he still emerged from human contrivance, unlike one who abides in non-contrivance, where all things commune without communion. Hence it is said: *He had not yet transcended the realm of the artificial.* As for the Lord of Tai of High Antiquity, he dwelt in natural spontaneity, without deliberate action. Sometimes he took himself for a horse, sometimes for an ox, with no fixed designation. This was because the Lord of Tai’s knowledge was truly authentic, his virtue profoundly real. Unlike the Lord of Yu, who embraced benevolence to entice others, his state was not intentional. Thus it is said: *He had never entered the realm of the artificial.* The phrase *had not yet transcended* gives rise to the phrase *had never entered.*

Jian Wu met Jie Yu the Madman. Jie Yu asked: "What did Ri Zhongshi tell you?" Jian Wu replied: "He instructed me that a ruler must issue ordinances based on his own person, establishing enduring standards. Using his own conduct as a model, people would dare not disobey and would be transformed thereby."

Jie Yu said: "This is deceptive virtue. In governing the world, it would be like crossing the sea by digging a river or making a mosquito bear a mountain. If you use standards to regulate conduct, people would drown in the sea; digging a river would be impossible; a mosquito could not bear a mountain. Does the sage’s governance concern externals? He rectifies himself and then acts, firmly accomplishing only what he is capable of. Does the sage’s governance extend to externals? He merely fulfills what lies within his nature. He rectifies himself and then acts, firmly and truthfully doing only what he can. Moreover, birds fly high to avoid the harm of arrows and nets, and field mice burrow deep beneath sacred mounds to escape the peril of fumigation and excavation—yet you would call these two creatures witless! If a ruler sought to align people with laws and measures, they would all flee to avoid them. Birds and mice know to avoid harm—how much more so humans! Would you call these two creatures witless?"

Tian Gen wandered to Yin Yang and arrived at the banks of the Liao River, where he encountered an unnamed man and asked: "I beg to inquire about governing the world." The unnamed man replied: "Begone."

You are a vulgar man—why do you ask so impatiently? Your question brings no joy. I am now about to become human with the Creator of Things. When weary, I shall ride the boundless bird, soaring beyond the Six Directions to wander in the realm of Nothingness, dwelling in the vast and open wilds. Why do you trouble my heart with governance of the world?

The unnamed man replied: "Let your mind wander in stillness, harmonize your qi with the void, follow things as they are without imposing your private will—and the world will be well-ordered."

Yang Ziju visited Lao Dan and said: "Suppose there were a man swift and strong, keen and unyielding, penetrating and clear in all things, tireless in learning the Dao. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?"

Lao Dan replied: "In the eyes of the sage, he is but a convict laborer, bound by skill and toil, wearing out his body and startling his mind. Moreover, the patterns of tigers and leopards attract hunters, just as the agility of gibbons and macaques brings them to the leash of the trapper’s hound. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?"

Yang Ziju, startled, asked: "Then may I inquire about the governance of an enlightened king?"

Lao Dan said: "The governance of an enlightened king covers all under heaven yet seems not to originate from himself. His transformation lends to the ten thousand things, yet the people do not rely on him. No name can be given to his achievement, and all things delight in themselves. He stands in the unfathomable and wanders in Nothingness."

In Zheng there was a divine diviner named Ji Cheng, who knew the life and death, survival and perishing, fortune and misfortune, long life and early death of men, predicting them by year, month, or day with godlike precision. When the people of Zheng saw him, they all fled. When Liezi saw him, he was enchanted by his art and returned to tell Huzhi: "At first I thought your Dao was supreme, but now I see there is something even greater."

Huzhi said: "I have shown you only its outward form, not its inner reality. Do you truly believe you have attained the Dao? I have shared with you only its surface, not its essence. How can you claim to have obtained the Dao?"

"A flock of hens without a rooster—how then could they lay eggs? *The Kinship of the Three* states: 'A hen cannot lay eggs alone,' for to pursue a one-sided art is to fail in attaining the Dao. This illustrates where Liezi has yet to grasp its inner reality. Yet if you set the Dao in opposition to the world, can you truly expect others to believe you? If you insist on elevating the Dao above the world, how can you demand that people trust you? Thus, they will turn away from you."

Huzhi said, "Bring him here and let me test him."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Cheng to meet Huzhi. When they emerged, Ji Cheng said to Liezi, "Alas! Your master is dead. He cannot live. He will not last ten days. I saw something strange—his qi resembled damp ashes."

Liezi entered and, weeping until his robe was soaked, told Huzhi what had transpired. Huzhi said, "I showed him the earthly pattern—still and unshaken, neither stirred nor disturbed. He saw only my sealed virtue-mechanism. Bring him again."

"Mountains, rivers, grasses, and trees are the earth’s patterns. When grasses and trees sprout, they rest in tranquil stillness, undisturbed, each following its own nature without need of correction. As the *Scripture of Salvation* says: 'The divine wind is silent; mountains and seas conceal the clouds. The heavens are free of drifting haze; the four qi are bright and clear. In a single land, earth and soil, mountains, rivers, forests, and trees lie level as one, no longer high or low.' This is what I mean by the sealed virtue-mechanism—concealed and unseeable. I showed him this, so he could not fathom me. Tell Liezi to bring him again tomorrow."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Cheng to see Huzhi once more. When they emerged, Ji Cheng said to Liezi, "Fortunate that your master met me! He is restored—fully alive! I saw his sealed adaptability."

Liezi entered and reported this to Huzhi. Huzhi said, "Earlier, I showed him heaven and earth—names and realities could not enter his mind, yet the mechanism stirred from the depths. He glimpsed only my good-mechanism. Bring him again."

"I saw his sealed adaptability—within concealment, there was flexibility, subtly different from yesterday’s sight. Sealed adaptability contrasts with sealed virtue. 'Heaven and earth' refers to the twelve celestial stems, the expanse of heaven. This term pairs with 'earthly pattern.' Earth governs stillness; heaven governs movement. By showing him heaven and earth, I meant that outwardly, names and realities could not penetrate his heart, while inwardly, the mechanism arose from the deepest place. Ji Cheng barely perceived the faint stirrings of my good-mechanism, yet much remained beyond his grasp. Tell him to return tomorrow."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Cheng to see Huzhi again. When they emerged, Ji Cheng said, "Your master is unsettled—I cannot divine him. Let him compose himself, and I will try again."

Liezi entered and told Huzhi. Huzhi said, "I just showed him the supreme harmony where nothing prevails—he saw only my balanced qi-mechanism. The depths of the *ni*-whirlpool, where Liezi writes *pan* (rice-water), should read *shen* (sediment), also meaning 'juice.' A still-water *shen* forms a deep pool; a flowing-water *shen* forms a deep pool. There are nine names for such pools, and here I used three. Bring him again after he has fasted to unify his mind."

"Supreme harmony" refers to the great equilibrium, akin to the "great peace." It lies between the sealed virtue-mechanism and the good-mechanism, with nothing surpassing it. The balanced qi-mechanism stands between earthly pattern and heaven-earth, where movement and stillness are evenly matched—this is equilibrium. The term "balanced" contrasts with "adaptability" above. By showing him the supreme harmony where nothing prevails, I meant the balanced qi-mechanism.

The *ni*-whirlpool is where the *ni* lingers. *Pan* is rice-water, the foam on a deep pool’s surface. A still-water *pan* forms a deep pool when water flows above but pools below. Where the *ni* lingers, where still water pools, where flowing water pools—all are called deep pools. The *Liezi*’s "Yellow Emperor" chapter lists nine names for deep pools: the *ni*-whirlpool’s *pan*, the still-water’s *pan*, the flowing-water’s *pan*, the overflowing-water’s *pan*, the surging-water’s *pan*, the rushing-water’s *pan*, the dammed-water’s *pan*, the Fen River’s *pan*, and the rich-water’s *pan*. *The Master of Huainan* also speaks of the nine-jade deep pools. Here, only three are mentioned, leaving others unused. The still-water deep pool exemplifies the earthly pattern.

The *Du*-virtue mechanism remains utterly unmoving. The *ni*-whirlpool’s deep pool serves as a metaphor for heaven and earth. Though the adept’s mechanism resembles still deep-pool water, the presence of *ni* coiling within reveals a subtle motion amid stillness. The flowing-water deep pool illustrates the supreme harmony of unopposable forces, the balanced qi-mechanism—where water flows above and pools below, each half in perfect equilibrium—thus serving as an analogy for the *taichong* protective qi.

If one cannot grasp these three examples, how could one comprehend the rest? Therefore, permission was granted for another encounter. The next day, he was again brought before Master Huzǐ. Before standing firmly, the visitor lost composure and fled. Master Huzǐ said, "Pursue him." Liezi chased but could not overtake him. Returning, he reported to Master Huzǐ: "He has vanished, he is lost—I could not reach him."

From his perspective, it was *vanished*; from ours, it was *lost*. Master Huzǐ said, "Earlier, I showed him what had never yet departed from my lineage. I met him with emptiness and *weiyi* (yielding compliance), leaving him unsure of who was who, so he took me for a follower, for a drifting current—hence his flight. My lineage is that of the great lineage-master. *Weiyi* means compliance. *Unsure of who was who* means no distinction between self and other. *Follower* describes one who trails like a younger brother after an elder. *Drifting current* likens one to a wave carried by the flow. Though transformations are boundless, they never depart from the great lineage. The great lineage is the root-source of the Dao—nothing but emptiness, without division between you and me. Taking me for a follower or drifting current, he could not fathom my nature and so fled."

Thereafter, Liezi returned home, believing he had never truly begun to learn. For three years, he did not venture out, cooking for his wife, eating like a pig eats human food, remaining indifferent to all affairs. He carved away ornamentation to restore simplicity, standing solitary as an unhewn block. *Carved away ornamentation to restore simplicity* means stripping away embellishment to return to plainness.

In this tumultuous age of divided domains, one should conclude life with this alone. Do not be a master of fame, a repository of schemes, a bearer of tasks, or a lord of knowledge. Embody the boundless and wander without trace. Exhaust what is received from heaven without claiming attainment—nothing but emptiness, echoing the earlier *emptiness*.

The sage’s mind functions like a mirror: neither advancing nor retreating, responding without hoarding, thus mastering things without harm. Like a mirror’s clarity, it neither welcomes arriving things nor sends off departing ones, merely reflecting without injury from phenomena. The following text explains how one becomes harmed.

The Emperor of the Southern Sea was Shu, the Emperor of the Northern Sea was Hu, and the Emperor of the Central Realm was Hundun. Shu and Hu would often meet in Hundun’s land, and Hundun treated them with great kindness. Shu and Hu conspired to repay Hundun’s virtue, saying: “All people have seven apertures—for seeing, hearing, eating, and breathing. Only this one has none. Let us try to bore them for him.” Each day they bored one aperture, and on the seventh day, Hundun died.

This chapter is titled "Fit for Emperors and Kings," and thus all it speaks of concerns the affairs of emperors and kings. At the chapter’s end, the passage where Master Huzi reveals himself to Ji Xian clarifies the Dao of responding to the world in accord with the times, ultimately returning to the theme of the Emperors of the Southern and Northern Seas.

The seven Inner Chapters are the most refined and precise, each first given a title before being composed.

*The True Classic of Southern Florescence*, Following the Original Text, Scroll Eight—Concluded
*The True Classic of Southern Florescence*, Following the Original Text, Scroll Nine
Recorded by Luo Miandao of Zhufeng in Luling; collated by his disciple Peng Xiang

**Outer Chapters: "Webbed Toes"**
The first seven chapters each have specially crafted titles, and the meaning of the chapter concludes with them. From this point onward, titles are taken from the opening characters of each chapter without particular intent, serving merely to elaborate on earlier themes.

Webbed toes and extra fingers emerge from nature yet exceed virtue; wens and tumors emerge from form yet exceed nature. *Webbed toes* refers to the big toe being joined to the second toe. *Extra fingers* refers to additional fingers growing beside the hand. *Wens* are growths of flesh attached to the body, hence called *attached wens*; tumors are masses suspended from the body, hence called *hanging tumors*. *Nature* means life; *virtue* means attainment.

Excess denotes superfluity. Webbed toes and extra fingers arise from heaven’s creation, yet from the perspective of what humans attain, they are superfluous; wens and tumors emerge after physical form, yet from the perspective of heaven’s creation, they are superfluous. Those who employ仁义 (humaneness and rightness) in manifold ways lodge them within the five viscera, yet this is not the true alignment of the Dao’s virtue/power.

*Manifold ways* means multiple approaches. *Lodged within the five viscera* refers to associations such as the liver-spirit with humaneness and the lung-spirit with rightness.

Thus, what is conjoined to the foot is but useless flesh; what branches from the hand is but an extraneous digit. The phrase *manifold ways* extends the analogy of webbed toes and extra fingers to the dispositions of the five viscera—excessive deviation in the practice of humaneness and rightness, and over-elaboration in the application of intelligence.

Thus, what is conjoined to clarity disrupts the five colors, indulges in ornate patterns, and dazzles with azure, yellow, embroidered robes, and ceremonial garments—is this not the case with Li Zhu? Sima [Biao] states: "A man of the Yellow Emperor’s time who could see the tip of an autumn hair at a hundred paces." Another account says he could see the point of a needle at a thousand *li*. Mencius calls him Li Lou.

What is excessive in hearing disrupts the five tones, indulges in the six pitch-pipes, and resounds with metal, stone, silk, bamboo, Huangzhong, and Dalü—is this not the case with Shi Kuang?

What branches from humaneness plucks at virtue and obstructs nature to garner fame, causing the world to clamor in support of unattainable standards—is this not the case with Zeng [Can] and Shi [Qiu]?

*Plucks* means to draw out. *Clamor* is like the stirring of a reed-organ’s pipes. *Zeng* refers to Zeng Can. *Shi* refers to Shi Qiu.

Those who are paired in sophistry pile tiles and knot cords to craft elusive phrases, roaming their minds amid the rigid debates of "hard" and "white," "same" and "different"—is this not the exhaustion of half-step reputations in useless words? And are not Yang [Zhu] and Mo [Di] the very ones?

*Piling tiles* means stacking vessels without toppling them. *Knotting cords* means untying them with skill—small tricks of dexterity, likening the sophist’s art. *Elusive phrases* are evasive words. *Exhaustion* means weariness. *Half-step* refers to half a pace, as if the sophist’s labor were that of a worn-out man hobbling forward.

Thus, all these are the many paired and branching paths, not the ultimate correctness of the world. That which is truly correct—here, the first *correct* may be a scribal error for *ultimate*—does not lose the truth of its nature and destiny. Therefore, what is joined is not called *paired*, and what branches is not called *forked* (a term for feet with extra toes); what is long is not called excessive, and what is short is not called deficient. Hence, though the teal’s legs are short, lengthening them brings sorrow; though the crane’s legs are long, shortening them brings grief. Thus, what is long by nature must not be cut, and what is short by nature must not be extended—there is nothing to remove or add that would not cause distress.

Is it not that humaneness and rightness run counter to human nature? Why else do those humane men bear so much sorrow? Moreover, those whose toes are paired—severing them brings weeping; those whose hands have extra fingers—cutting them brings wailing. Whether in excess of the proper number or deficient, their sorrow is the same. Now, the humane men of this age gaze with bleary eyes at the world’s calamities, while the inhumane tear at the truth of their nature and destiny to glut themselves on wealth and honor. Is it not that humaneness and rightness run counter to human nature?

Since the time after the Three Dynasties, why has the world been so clamorously noisy? *Clamorous* (pronounced *xiāo*) is a sound—since the time after the Three Dynasties, why has the world’s discourse on humaneness and rightness been so noisily clamorous?

Those who rely on hooks, marking lines, compasses, and squares to achieve correctness are carving away their nature; those who depend on inked cords, glue, and lacquer to achieve solidity are encroaching upon their virtue. Bending and folding with rites and music, cooing and murmuring about humaneness and rightness to soothe the world’s heart—this is to lose what is constant and natural. The world has its constancy: the curved do not need hooks, the straight do not need marking lines, the round do not need compasses, the square do not need squares, adhesion does not need glue and lacquer, and binding does not need ropes. Thus, the world grows abundantly of itself, unaware of how it grows; all attain their due, unaware of how they attain it. Therefore, from ancient times to the present, there is no duality—it cannot be diminished.

*A hook* is a tool for drawing curves. *Abundantly* means flourishingly. The nine-character phrase thus treats ancient and modern as one, without sign of decay. Then why should humaneness and rightness cling like glue, lacquer, and ropes, wandering between the Dao and virtue? It only confounds the world. Zhuangzi and Laozi exalted the Dao and virtue while belittling humaneness and rightness, hence these words.

Minor delusion confuses direction; major delusion alters one’s nature. How do we know this is so? Since the time of Yu’s dynasty, when humaneness and rightness were raised to disturb the world, none have not rushed to answer the call of humaneness and rightness. Is this not to exchange one’s nature for humaneness and rightness? Let us test this claim: since the time after the Three Dynasties, the world has not failed to exchange its nature for things.

The petty man sacrifices himself for profit; the scholar sacrifices himself for fame; the grandee sacrifices himself for his house; the sage sacrifices himself for the world. Though these several types differ in their undertakings and their reputations bear different names, their injury to their nature and their self-sacrifice are one.

Zang and Gu—according to the *Fangyan*, in the northern borders of Qi and the northern outskirts of Yan, a male who marries a slave woman is called *Zang*, and a female who marries a slave is called *Gu*; thus, taking *Zang* as a name for the good, they also name the other *Gu*—were two men who herded sheep together and lost them all. When asked what Zang was doing, he was found clutching a bamboo slip, reading a book.

When asked what Gu was doing, he was found playing *bo* and *sai*—*ce* (bamboo slips, two feet and four inches long); *sai* (to be read as *xi*, as in *The Book of Han*: Wuqiu Shouwang was skilled at *ge*, with the Five Palace Attendants’ note: *bo* and *sai*)—to pass the time. Though these two men differed in their pursuits, their loss of sheep was the same.

Boyi died for fame beneath Shouyang, while Robber Zhi died for profit upon Dongling. Though these two men differed in what they died for, their injury to life and harm to nature were the same. Why must Boyi be right and Robber Zhi wrong? The world is wholly given to self-sacrifice. Those who sacrifice themselves for benevolence and righteousness are commonly called gentlemen; those who sacrifice themselves for wealth and property are commonly called petty men. Though their sacrifices are one, some are called gentlemen, some petty men. Yet if we consider their injury to life and harm to nature, then Robber Zhi is no different from Boyi—what room is there to distinguish between gentleman and petty man?

Moreover, those whose nature is bound to benevolence and righteousness, though as accomplished as Zengzi and Shi Yu, are not what I call truly good. Those whose nature is bound to the five flavors, though as discerning as Yu’er—*Shizi* says: "Yu’er seasoned the ruler’s food with ginger"; *Huainanzi* writes it as *Shen’er*—are not what I call truly good. Those whose nature is bound to the five tones, though as skilled as Music Master Kuang, are not what I call truly keen of hearing. Those whose nature is bound to the five colors, though as perceptive as Li Zhu, are not what I call truly clear of sight.

What I call good is not the practice of benevolence and righteousness, but the preservation of one’s inherent power alone. What I call good is not the pursuit of benevolence and righteousness, but the fulfillment of one’s nature and destiny. What I call keen of hearing is not hearing others, but hearing oneself. What I call clear of sight is not seeing others, but seeing oneself.

For one who does not see himself but sees others, who does not attain for himself but attains for others, attains what others attain yet does not attain what he himself should attain—such a one follows others’ fulfillment without fulfilling himself. Whether Robber Zhi or Boyi, to follow others’ fulfillment without fulfilling oneself is equally perverse and excessive.

I am ashamed before the Dao and its virtue; thus, above, I dare not adopt the conduct of benevolence and righteousness, and below, I dare not indulge in perverse and excessive actions.

*The Nanhua True Scripture, Following the Original Text, Scroll Nine* ends here.
*The Nanhua True Scripture, Following the Original Text, Scroll Ten* begins.

Bamboo Peak of Lulin, recorded by Luo Miandao, collated by disciple Peng Xiang

*Outer Chapters: "Horse's Hooves"*

The horse—its hooves can tread frost and snow, its coat can ward off wind and cold. It grazes on grass and drinks from streams, lifts its feet to gallop across the land. Though there be ritual terraces and grand chambers, they are of no use to it.

Zheng Xuan, the Han scholar, notes: "In ancient texts, *yi* (義) was written for *yi* (儀)." The *yitai* refers to the "terrace gate" mentioned in the *Jiao Te Sheng*—a raised earthen platform with a roofed structure, its center serving as the gate, thus establishing the model for palace architecture. Even today, the term *yimen* persists. The heavens, too, have their "Grand Terrace" court. The *luzhen* denotes the main hall. To speak of *yitai* and *luzhen* is to encompass the entirety of palace architecture.

Then came Bo Le—pronounced *Luo*, surnamed Sun, given name Yang—renowned for his mastery of horses. The *Star Canon of the Shi Clan* states: "Bo Le is the name of a celestial star governing the finest steeds of heaven." Sun Yang’s skill in driving horses earned him this name. He declared, "I excel at managing horses." He branded them, pared their hooves, carved into their flesh, and flayed their joints. He bound them with bridles and halters, tethered them to blackened racks—of these horses, twelve or thirteen in ten would perish. He starved and parched them, drove them at full gallop, drilled them into uniformity. Before them lay the torment of bit and rein, behind them the threat of whip and goad—by then, more than half the horses had died.

The *jue* is the mouthpiece; the *shi* refers to the ornamental tassels beneath the horse’s jaw.

The potter says: "I excel at working clay." He kneads the earth.

The round conforms to the compass, the square to the carpenter’s square. The craftsman says: “I excel at working wood.” The curved meets the hook, the straight aligns with the marking-line. Yet do clay and wood by nature desire to conform to compass, square, hook, and line? Still, generation after generation, people praise Bo Le for his skill in managing horses, and potters and carpenters for their mastery of clay and wood. This is also the error of those who govern the world.

I believe those who truly excel at governing the world do not act thus. The people possess an enduring nature: they know to clothe themselves and till the soil to feed themselves—this is called shared virtue. Unified yet impartial, this is called the freedom of Heaven. Thus, in the age of utmost virtue, their movements are steady, their gaze unassuming. At that time, mountains had no paths or tunnels, marshes no boats or bridges. The myriad things flourished together, bound to their native lands. Birds and beasts gathered in flocks and herds, grasses and trees grew to their fullness. Thus, one could lead birds and beasts by tether and wander among them, or climb to peer into the nests of magpies and crows. In the age of utmost virtue, humans dwelt alongside birds and beasts, their kind mingling with the myriad things. How could one distinguish between noble and base? Alike in their lack of cunning, their virtue remained unbroken; alike in their lack of desire, they were called uncarved simplicity. In uncarved simplicity, the people’s nature was fulfilled.

Yet when the sage arose, he hobbled and strained for benevolence, tiptoed and stretched for righteousness—and the world began to doubt. Steady and solid, not hollow or frivolous. Obscure and unadorned, not bright or dazzling. *Biexie* (limping) and *qiji* (tiptoeing) describe forced effort, thus illustrating the contrivance of benevolence and righteousness. Indulgence became music, restraint became ritual—and the world began to divide. *Chanman* (indulgence) means excess, like overflowing waters. *Zhaipi* (restraint) means picking out and correcting what is deviant.

If unhewn simplicity is not destroyed, who would fashion sacrificial goblets? If white jade is not shattered, who would carve ceremonial tablets? If the Dao and virtue are not abandoned, why seek benevolence and righteousness? If nature and emotion remain unsevered, why employ ritual and music? If the five colors are not disordered, who would create patterned adornments? If the five tones are not confused, who would harmonize them with the six pitch-pipes? To mar simplicity into implements is the fault of the craftsman; to ruin the Dao and virtue for the sake of benevolence and righteousness is the error of the sage.

Now, returning to the horse as a metaphor, let us advance the argument further.

When dwelling on land, horses eat grass and drink water; when pleased, they entwine necks in mutual affection; when angered, they turn their backs to kick one another. In joy, they cross necks and nuzzle in harmony; in fury, they part their backs to strike with their hooves. For when horses kick, they always direct their feet backward—hence the phrase "turning their backs to kick." Thus, horses already possess this knowledge.

Yet when yokes and crossbars are imposed upon them—*heng* being the horizontal wood at the front of the carriage, *e* (the yoke) the device that restrains the horse’s neck—horses learn to feign armor’s edge, to sense the chokehold of city gates, to grow haughty and unruly, to deceive by accepting the bit, and to slip free of the reins. *Jie* means armor, for warhorses must wear it. *Yin* refers to city gates, which restrain treachery. *E* means to grasp, as city gates grasp and hold back villainy. *Zhiman* means arrogance and defiance. *Guixian* means feigning acceptance of the bit. *Qiepei* means stealing the reins beyond control. This describes how horses, familiar with human ways, foresee the signs of armor before donning it, anticipate the restraint of city gates before passing through, and thus become unmanageable—deceiving with the bit, slipping the reins—exhibiting such behavior.

Therefore, that horses possess this knowledge and can even become thieves is the fault of Bo Le.

In the age of the赫胥氏, people knew not what to do nor where to go; they played with food in their mouths and wandered with bellies full. The people had already attained this state. But when sages came, bending and shaping ritual and music to correct the forms of the world, dangling benevolence and righteousness like suspended objects to soothe the hearts of the world, people began to stretch on tiptoe for knowledge, to vie for profit, and could not be stopped. This, too, is the fault of the sages.

One who hangs an object makes others stretch to reach it.

*End of the Tenth Scroll of the Xunben Edition of the Nan Hua Zhen Jing*
*Beginning of the Eleventh Scroll of the Xunben Edition of the Nan Hua Zhen Jing*

Lujing Bamboo Peak’s Luo Miandao, as recorded by his disciple Peng Xiang, annotates:

**Outer Chapters: "Opening the Satchel"**

When one intends to pry open a satchel—*qu* (胠), meaning the armpit—it is as though reaching in from beneath the arm. Thieves who probe into bags to steal necessitate precautions: they must fasten cords and seals, *jianteng* (缄縢), the tools of binding and marking, and secure bolts and locks. *Jue* (鐍), pronounced as in *guxue* (古穴), refers to the bolt’s tongue; *jiong* (扃) means to latch. This is what the world calls "wisdom."

Yet when a great thief arrives, he hoists the chest, shoulders the satchel, and carries off the bag, fearing only that the cords, seals, bolts, and locks are not secure enough. Thus, what was once called wisdom is it not merely amassing spoils for the great thief?

Let us examine this: Is there any so-called wisdom in the world that does not accumulate for the great thief? Is there any so-called sageliness that does not guard for the great thief? How do we know this is so?

In ancient times, the cities and towns of Qi stood in sight of one another, the cries of chickens and dogs audible between them. Nets and snares spread wide, plows and hoes pierced deep—*lei* (耒) being the handle of the plow, *nou* (耨) the weeding tool, as described in the *Shuowen*: "a blade six inches long, a handle three feet, its edge two inches broad." Within a territory of over two thousand *li*, encompassing all within its four borders, the establishment of ancestral temples and altars of soil and grain, the governance of cities, villages, districts, and hamlets—was there ever a time these did not follow the models of the sages?

Yet Tian Chengzi slew the ruler of Qi in a single day and stole his state. What he stole was not merely the state—he seized along with it the sagely laws of wisdom. Thus, Tian Chengzi bore the name of a bandit, yet lived in the security of Yao and Shun. Small states dared not condemn him; great states dared not punish him. For twelve generations, he held Qi—was this not the theft of the state *and* its sagely laws to safeguard his bandit’s life?

Tian Chengzi is Chen Chengzi Heng. In the twenty-second year of Duke Zhuang’s reign in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, Chen killed its crown prince, Yu Kou. Gongzi Wan, styled Jingzhong, fearing disaster, fled to Qi and became Duke Huan’s minister of works, concealing his clan name as Tian, for Chen and Tian sound alike. Five generations later, Lizi Qi became a minister to Duke Jing, collecting taxes from the people with a small measure but distributing grain with a large one, thereby winning the people’s favor and seizing control of Qi’s governance. Finally, his descendant Chengzi Heng assassinated Duke Jian, enthroned Duke Ping, and received fiefs larger than the duke’s own, ruling through Xiangzi Ban. Zhuangzi writes this in the time of King Xuan, hence "eleven generations held Qi." Though Tian He had not yet usurped the state, the replacement of Jiang by Tian had already appeared in divination, so it is counted together.

Let us examine further: Is there any so-called supreme wisdom in the world that does not accumulate for the great thief? Is there any so-called supreme sageliness that does not guard for the great thief? How do we know this is so?

In former times, Long Feng was beheaded, Bi Gan was disemboweled, Xi Zhong was torn apart, and Wu Zixu was torn to shreds—*mi* (靡) meaning *xumi* (胥靡), where *xu* (胥) is "mutual" and *mi* (靡) is "to follow," a lighter punishment where offenders, clad in coarse cloth and bound with ropes, labored in chains. Though these four were virtuous, they could not escape execution.

Thus, the followers of Robber Zhi asked him: "Does theft also have a Way?" Zhi replied: "Where can one go without a Way? To divine the treasures hidden in a chamber—that is sageliness. To enter first—that is courage. To exit last—that is righteousness. To know what is possible and what is not—that is wisdom. To divide spoils equally—that is benevolence. Without these five, no one in the world has ever become a great thief."

From this, we see that the good cannot stand without the Way of the sages, nor can Zhi act without it. Since the world’s good people are few and the unvirtuous many, the sages benefit the world little but harm it much. Thus it is said:

When the lips are gone, the teeth grow cold;
When Lu’s wine was thin, Handan was besieged.

As the *Shiwen* explains: Handan was the capital of Zhao. King Xuan of Chu summoned the feudal lords, but Duke Gong of Lu arrived late with weak wine. Enraged, the king sought to humiliate him, but the duke refused, saying: "I am a descendant of the Duke of Zhou, elder among the feudal lords, practicing the rites and music of the Son of Heaven, with merit in the Zhou house. Though my wine was improper, to reproach its thinness is excessive." He departed without leave. The king, furious, sent troops with Qi to attack Lu. King Hui of Liang had long wished to strike Zhao but feared Chu, which was aiding Lu. Thus, Liang besieged Handan. Events follow one another—another account, from Xu Shen’s commentary on the *Huainanzi*: At a gathering of feudal lords, Lu presented wine to the King of Chu. Lu’s wine was thin, Zhao’s thick. The king’s wine steward demanded wine from Zhao, but Zhao refused. The steward, enraged, swapped Zhao’s thick wine for Lu’s thin one and presented it to the king, who then besieged Handan on the pretext of Zhao’s weak wine.

When sages arise, great thieves appear. Abandon the attack on sages, release the restraint on bandits, and the world will begin to be ordered. When rivers run dry, valleys empty; when hills are leveled, abysses fill. Once sages die, great thieves will not rise, and the world will be at peace without incident.

As long as sages do not die, great thieves will not cease. Though one may exalt sages to govern the world, this is but to enrich Robber Zhi. Fashion measures and bushels to quantify, and they will be stolen along with the measures; fashion scales and weights to weigh, and they will be stolen along with the scales; fashion tallies and seals to authenticate, and they will be stolen along with the tallies; fashion benevolence and righteousness to correct, and they will be stolen along with benevolence and righteousness.

How do we know this is so? Those who steal a belt-hook are executed, while those who steal a state become lords. Where the gates of lords stand, benevolence and righteousness abide. Are these not the theft of benevolence, righteousness, sageliness, and wisdom? Thus, in the wake of great thieves—following the example of Deng Xi—lords arise, seizing benevolence and righteousness along with the gains of measures, scales, tallies, and seals. Though offered the rewards of high rank, none can be induced; though threatened with the axe, none can be deterred. That such great profit for Robber Zhi cannot be restrained is the fault of the sages.

Thus it is said: "The fish must not leave the depths; the state’s sharpest tools must not be shown to others." Those sages are the state’s sharpest tools, not what should be displayed to the world. Therefore, sever sageliness and cast off wisdom, and great thieves will cease; pluck out jade and shatter pearls, and petty thieves will not arise; burn tallies and break seals, and the people will return to simplicity; smash the steelyard and snap the balance, and the people will know no contention. To "use simplicity to harmonize seals, use contention to harmonize scales"—here, the text plays with words. Exhaust and destroy the world’s sagely laws, and only then may the people be fit for discussion. Confound the six pitch-pipes, melt down the zithers and lutes, deafen the ears of Music-Master Kuang, and the world will begin to preserve its hearing. Erase patterns, scatter the five colors, blind the eyes of Li Zhu, and the world will begin to preserve its sight. Break the marking-line and discard the compass and square, sever the fingers of Craftsman Chui, and the world will begin to possess its skill. Thus it is said: "Great skill appears clumsy."

Cut short the conduct of Zeng and Shi, silence the mouths of Yang and Mo, cast aside benevolence and righteousness, and the world’s virtue will begin to merge in profound unity. When people preserve their sight, the world will not be dazzled; when they preserve their hearing, the world will not be burdened; when they preserve their knowledge, the world will not be deluded; when they preserve their virtue, the world will not be perverse. Those like Zeng, Shi, Yang, Mo, Music-Master Kuang, Craftsman Chui, and Li Zhu all set their virtue outward and thereby dazzle and confuse the world—useless to the law.

Do you alone not know of the age of perfect virtue? In ancient times, under the reigns of Rongcheng, Dating, Bohuang, Zhongyang, Lilu, Lixu, Xuanyuan, Hexu, Zunlu, Zhu Rong, Fuxi, and Shennong, the people knotted cords for use. They savored their food, admired their clothing, delighted in their customs, and rested content in their dwellings. Neighboring states were within sight, the sounds of chickens and dogs carried to one another, yet the people grew old and died without ever traveling between them. In such an age, perfect governance was already achieved.

Now, however, it has come to this: the people crane their necks and stand on tiptoe, saying, "In such-and-such a place there is a worthy," and carrying provisions, they rush toward him. They abandon their parents at home and forsake their lords’ service abroad. Footprints crisscross the borders of the states; carriage tracks entangle a thousand *li* beyond. This is the fault of those above who prize knowledge. If those above truly prize knowledge and lack the Way, the world will be thrown into great chaos.

How do we know this is so?
