When knowledge of bows, crossbows, nets, and snares multiplies, birds are thrown into confusion above; when knowledge of hooks, bait, traps, and weirs multiplies, fish are thrown into confusion in the water; when knowledge of fences, nets, and traps multiplies, beasts are thrown into confusion in the marshes; when knowledge of deceit, gradual poison, contention, sophistry, "hard and white," and "same and different" multiplies, the common folk are bewildered by disputation. (Here, the text follows the earlier pattern, presenting three examples: "deceit and gradual poison" refers to knowledge used to deceive, accumulating into poison; "hard and white" refers to obstinately insisting on whiteness; "same and different" refers to conflating difference into sameness—all terms found in the writings of Gongsun Long. "Contention" means competing for superiority; "sophistry" means shifting and evasive argument. Those who obstinately insist on whiteness and dispute it engage in contention, making others shift and evade. "Dissolving" means scattering; "dirt" refers to the grime of the body. "Dissolving dirt and sameness" means scattering sameness as if shedding grime. The first phrase speaks of knowledge, the next two of disputation.)

Thus, the world is repeatedly thrown into great chaos, and the blame lies in the love of knowledge. The world all knows to seek what it does not know yet does not know to seek what it already knows; all knows to condemn what it does not consider good yet does not know to condemn what it already considers good. Thus, great chaos arises. Above, it confounds the brightness of sun and moon; below, it scorches the essence of mountains and rivers; in between, it topples the operations of the four seasons. Even the tiniest wriggling creatures and the smallest sprouting plants lose their nature. How severe is the chaos wrought by the love of knowledge—this has been the case since the Three Dynasties and after!

Abandon the people of all kinds (here, "kind" is read in the rising tone, meaning categories—one type alone, without distinction or excellence) and delight instead in the flattery of the fawning; forsake the tranquil and effortless and delight instead in the restless scheming of the clever. Such restless scheming has already thrown the world into chaos.

*The Eleventh Scroll of the Circulating Edition of the True Classic of Southern Florescence is complete.*
*The Twelfth Scroll of the Circulating Edition of the True Classic of Southern Florescence.*

Lu Lingzhu of Bamboo Peak in Luling, recorded by disciple Luo Miandao and collated by Peng Xiang.

**Outer Chapters: "In You"**

We hear of "in-you-ing" the world, but never of governing the world. To "in" it is to fear the world may indulge its nature; to "you" it is to fear the world may alter its virtue. If the world neither indulges its nature nor alters its virtue, is there any need to govern it?

When Yao governed the world, he made it brim with joy—people delighted in their nature, yet this was not tranquility. When Jie governed the world, he made it languish in exhaustion—people suffered in their nature, yet this was not contentment. That which is neither tranquil nor content is not virtue; and nothing lacking virtue can endure long. The phrase "in-you" may have been current in those days, but today it reads strangely. As in the *Book of Songs*, where "you-mi" appears, "in" suggests letting things be as they are, while "you" means to allow. "You" is to be lenient. To "in-you" the world is to act without contrivance. To govern the world is to impose rites, music, punishments, and policies. If one "ins" and "yous" it, how can there be governance?

The exuberance of "brimming with joy" arises from a lack of "inning"; the exhaustion of "languishing" stems from a lack of "you-ing." When one is excessively joyful, one aids the yang; when excessively angry, one aids the yin. "Aids" means to strengthen. Joy belongs to yang; anger to yin. When yin and yang are balanced in the body, there is no illness. But if joy is excessive, it strengthens yang to dominance; if anger is excessive, it strengthens yin to dominance. When both yin and yang are over-aided, the four seasons fail to arrive, the harmony of cold and heat is disrupted, and the body is harmed in return. This analogy of the four seasons’ yin and yang illustrates the yin and yang within the human body.

If people’s joy and anger are misplaced, their dwellings unsettled, their thoughts restless, and their conduct inconsistent, then the world begins with the unruly and the fierce, and later produces the deeds of Robber Zhi and Zeng Shen. "Inconsistent conduct" means actions that start but fail to follow through with order. "Unruly and fierce" describes those who cannot be controlled.

Thus, even if the entire world were marshaled to reward the good, it would not suffice; even if the entire world were marshaled to punish the wicked, it would not be enough. The world is too vast for rewards and punishments to be adequate. Yet since the Three Dynasties, rulers have clamored endlessly over rewards and punishments—how could they have had leisure to preserve the natural state of their lives? This passage on rewards and punishments arises from the discussion of joy and anger.

Moreover, to delight in brightness is to indulge in form; to delight in keen hearing is to indulge in sound; to delight in benevolence is to disorder virtue; to delight in righteousness is to defy principle; to delight in ritual is to turn it into a craft; to delight in music is to turn it into licentiousness; to delight in sagacity is to turn it into artifice; to delight in knowledge is to turn it into fault-finding. How can the world then preserve the natural state of its life?

These eight may exist or not—either way, the world will not preserve the natural state of its life. But when these eight are present, they begin to entangle and ensnare the world in chaos. "Aid" means to assist, which is not natural. Ritual involves ornamentation, hence it becomes a craft; sagacity involves many skills, hence it becomes artifice; knowledge involves scrutinizing others’ faults, hence it is called fault-finding. "Entangle" describes a coiled, constricted state; "ensnare" suggests a flurry of meddling. Yet the world venerates and cherishes these things—how profoundly mistaken it is!

Is it merely a matter of passing them by? No, people purify themselves to discuss them, kneel to present them, and drum and sing to dance for them. What am I to make of such things? "Kneeling to present them" echoes Laozi’s "sitting to advance"—what am I to make of such people?

Thus, when the noble person is compelled to oversee the world, nothing is better than non-action. Only through non-action can the natural state of life be preserved. Therefore, one who risks their life for the world may be entrusted with it; one who cherishes their life for the world may be charged with it. But one who values and cherishes their life more than governing the world is truly fit to be entrusted with it.

Thus, if the noble person can refrain from loosening their five viscera or flaunting their intelligence, abiding like a corpse yet manifesting like a dragon, silent as an abyss yet thundering like a storm, moving with spirit so that Heaven follows, and acting with ease so that the myriad things rise and flourish—then why would I presume to govern the world? "Loosening" and "flaunting" both harm the self. "Abiding like a corpse" means sitting motionless; "manifesting like a dragon" means transforming like a dragon. "Silent as an abyss yet thundering like a storm" describes stillness that contains movement, not mere lifelessness. "The myriad things rise and flourish" means all things are nurtured within one’s generative power, like steam accumulating to cook food.

Cui Qu asked Lao Dan: "If one does not govern the world, how can the human heart be settled?" Lao Dan replied: "Be cautious—do not agitate the human heart. The human heart presses downward yet surges upward, rising and falling like a prisoner. It is pliant yet strong, sharp yet carved, scorching like fire, freezing like ice. In the blink of an eye, it can reach beyond the four seas. At rest, it is deep and still; in motion, it soars to Heaven. Proud and untamable, is it not the human heart alone?

This describes the human heart in extremes: when pressed, it shrinks and retreats; when spurred, it leaps and ascends. When pressed downward, though momentarily subdued like a prisoner, its indignation remains. When urged upward, if left unchecked, its fury can kill.

Soft yet able to be firm—this is the heart’s pliancy yielding to strength. Sharp yet carved and polished—this is the heart’s hardness made supple. All these characters refer to the heart. *Ji* means swift; *fu* means to traverse. *Qi* means movement. "Suspended to Heaven" describes the heart in motion, as if hung from the sky. *Fènjiāo* is an unstoppable force.

The Yellow Emperor first used benevolence and righteousness to agitate the human heart. Yao and Shun, in their devotion, wore their thighs smooth of down and their shins bare of hair, exhausting their bodies to nurture the world. They fretted over their five viscera to enact benevolence and righteousness, strained their blood and *qi* to establish laws and measures—yet still, they could not prevail. Yao banished Huan Dou to Mount Chong, executed the Three Miao at Sanwei, and exiled Gong Gong to Youdu—this was his failure to master the world. By the time of the Three Kings, the world was thrown into great turmoil. Below, there were Jie and Robber Zhi; above, Zeng and Shi; and the Confucians and Mohists all arose. Thus, joy and anger bred suspicion, the foolish and the wise deceived one another, the good and the bad reviled each other, and truth and falsehood mocked one another—so the world declined. Great virtue diverged, and human nature and destiny dissolved into chaos. The world prized cunning, and the people exhausted themselves. Then came axes and saws, inked cords and chisels, to carve and shape them. The world was bound in fetters, trembling with fear, tiptoeing as if shackled. The great disorder lay in agitating the human heart. Thus, the worthy hid in the caves of great mountains, while the lords of ten thousand chariots trembled in their ancestral halls.

In this age, the executed lie piled upon one another, the imprisoned press shoulder to shoulder, and the punished stretch as far as the eye can see—yet the Confucians and Mohists still stretch their toes and wave their arms in the midst of fetters. *Líqì* means standing on tiptoe; *rǎngbì* means raising one’s arms to argue—both hands and feet in motion. Alas! How shameless they are, yet feel no shame! I do not know whether the wisdom of sages does not become the interlocking beams of a prison frame, or whether benevolence and righteousness do not become the shackles and chisels of a mortise. How do we know that Zeng and Shi are not the arrows of Jie and Robber Zhi? Arrows and bows are tools for harm—this means Zeng and Shi are but instruments for Jie and Robber Zhi. Therefore it is said: "Abandon sagacity, discard knowledge, and the world will be greatly ordered."

The Yellow Emperor ruled as Son of Heaven for nineteen years, his commands obeyed throughout the world. Hearing that Master Guangcheng dwelt on Mount Kongtong—*Erya* says: "North of the Dipper’s handle lies Kongtong Mountain"; another account places it thirty *li* east of Yucheng in Liang—he went to see him, saying: "I have heard that you have attained the perfection of the supreme Dao. I venture to ask: what is the essence of the supreme Dao? I wish to gather the essence of Heaven and Earth to aid the five grains and nourish the people. I also wish to govern *yin* and *yang* to bring life to all beings. How may this be done?"

Master Guangcheng replied: "What you ask about is the substance of things; what you wish to govern is the remnant of things. What you inquire of is the fundamental essence of the Dao in things, while what you seek to control—*yin* and *yang*—is but their scattered residue. Since you began to govern the world, clouds gather before they amass into rain, plants wither before they yellow, and the light of the sun and moon grows dim. As for the petty minds of flatterers—how could they be worthy of discussing the supreme Dao?"

The Yellow Emperor withdrew, relinquished the world, built a solitary chamber, spread a mat of white grass, and lived in seclusion for three months. Then he returned to seek the master again. Master Guangcheng lay with his head to the south. The Yellow Emperor approached from downwind, crawling on his knees, bowed twice, touched his forehead to the ground, and asked: "I have heard that you have attained the perfection of the supreme Dao. I venture to ask: how may one cultivate the body to attain longevity?"

Master Guangcheng rose with a frown and said: "Excellent question!"

"Come, let me tell you of the supreme Dao.

The essence of the supreme Dao is profound and obscure;
its ultimate state is dim and silent.
See nothing, hear nothing, embrace the spirit in stillness—
the body will then right itself.
Be still, be pure; do not weary your form,
do not disturb your essence,
and you may attain long life.
Let the eyes see nothing, the ears hear nothing,
the heart know nothing—
your spirit will guard the body,
and the body will endure.
Guard your inner self, shut out the outer;
excessive knowledge brings ruin.

I have guided you to the great brightness above,
to the source of utmost yang;
I have led you through the gate of profound obscurity,
to the source of utmost yin.
*Suì* means to proceed and reach one’s destination.
The sources of yin and yang are the primordial Great Ultimate.

Heaven and earth have their offices,
yin and yang have their treasuries.
Guard your body with care,
and all things will flourish of themselves.
*Offices* are like government bureaus;
*treasuries* are like storehouses.
*Things* are what fill the bureaus and storehouses.

I preserve the One and dwell in harmony.
Thus have I cultivated my body for one thousand two hundred years,
yet my form has never declined."

The Yellow Emperor bowed twice, touched his forehead to the ground, and said:
"Master Guangcheng, you are what may be called heaven!"

Master Guangcheng said: "Come.
Let me tell you this:
those things are inexhaustible,
yet people all take them for finite;
those things are unfathomable,
yet people all take them for bounded.
Those who attain my Dao
rise above as emperors and below as kings;
those who lose my Dao
see light above but become earth below.
In life, they behold the light of sun and moon;
in death, they return to earth.
Now, the hundred flourishing things all arise from earth
and return to earth.
*The hundred flourishing things* refers to all that thrives.

Therefore, I shall leave you
and enter the gate of the boundless,
to advance into the wilderness of the infinite.
I shall share the radiance of the sun and moon,
I shall endure with heaven and earth.
When near me—ah, how inseparable!
When far from me—ah, how obscure!
Will all others perish,
while I alone endure?
*Mín* means the joining of threads;
*hūn* means profound darkness.
*When near me* refers to the realm one approaches—
the gate of the boundless, the wilderness of the infinite.
*When far from me* refers to the world one leaves—
that is, departing from you.
From now on, I fear that the immortal realm near me
will be as inseparable as joined threads,
while the mundane world far from me
will fade into profound obscurity, never to be seen again!
Will all those who have not attained the Dao perish,
while I alone endure?
The three *hū* express sorrow for the world
and lament the extinction of the Dao."

Cloud General was traveling east,
passing the branch of the *fúyáo* tree—
the *fúyáo* is a divine tree growing in the Eastern Sea—
when he happened upon Hong Meng.
Hong Meng, the primal qi of nature,
was slapping his thighs and leaping about in play.
When Cloud General saw him, he halted in startled confusion,
stood in solemn stillness, and asked:
"Venerable one, who are you?
What are you doing here?"

Hong Meng continued slapping his thighs and leaping without cease, then replied to Cloud General: "Wandering."

Cloud General said: "I wish to ask you something."

Hong Meng looked up at Cloud General and sighed: "Ah."

Cloud General said: "Heaven’s qi is disharmonious, earth’s qi is pent up, the six qi are unbalanced, and the four seasons are out of order. Now I wish to absorb the essence of the six qi to nurture all living things—how should I proceed?"

Hong Meng slapped his thighs, leapt, and shook his head, saying: "I do not know. I do not know."

Cloud General was thus unable to ask his question.

After another three years, traveling east again, he passed through the wilds of Song and once more encountered Hong Meng. Cloud General rejoiced greatly, hastening forward to say: "Has Heaven forgotten me? Has Heaven forgotten me?" He bowed twice, touching his head to the ground, and entreated Hong Meng for instruction.

Hong Meng said: "Drifting without aim, seeking nothing; reckless, knowing not where to go. The wanderer moves with restless steps, observing what is uncontrived. What do I know?"

Cloud General said: "I too thought myself reckless, yet the people follow wherever I go. I cannot be free of them, and now they imitate me. I wish to hear but a single word from you."

Hong Meng said: "To disrupt the warp of Heaven, to defy the nature of things—this thwarts the mysterious workings of Heaven. Beasts scatter from their herds, and birds cry out in the night; calamity befalls the grasses and trees, disaster strikes even the insects. Alas! This is the fault of those who govern men."

Cloud General said: "Then what am I to do?"
Hong Meng said: "Alas! How poisonous! Float away, return. Alas, how poisonous! I sigh for those who govern the world and leave behind such harm. Float away, return—Hong Meng wishes to depart in flight."

Cloud General said: "I have encountered a heavenly trial. I beg to hear but a single word."
Hong Meng said: "Alas! Cultivate the heart. Not 'nourish the heart' but 'heart-cultivation,' for the heart is cultivated through non-action. You need only abide in non-action, and things will transform of themselves. Cast off your form and body, expel your cleverness and wisdom, forget all categories and things. All categories—everything—must be forgotten with things. Merge into the vast primordial, unbind the heart and release the spirit, become vacant and without soul. The ten thousand things swirl in clouds, each returning to its root, each returning to its root yet unaware. This is what is meant by 'each thing possessing its own Supreme Polarity.' The 'swirling clouds' cannot be exhausted in a single word. In utter chaos, in primordial confusion, one passes one’s whole life without separation. If one claims to know it, one has already departed from it. It resides within the body, never once separated—only people cannot know it themselves. If one could know it, then it would be something external that could be left behind. Do not inquire into its name, for it has no name. Do not pry into its nature, for it has no nature. Things thus arise of themselves. This is what is meant by 'male and female unite their essence, and the ten thousand things are transformed and born.'"

Cloud General said: "Heaven has bestowed virtue upon me and shown me silence. Seeking it within myself, only now have I attained it. This is not found outside the self—turning inward to seek it, one finds it. This is what Laozi and Zhuangzi relied upon when they viewed heaven and earth, the chaff of human affairs, with such distant gaze. Yet those who inherit their teachings today merely engage in empty talk and sophistry, never grasping the reality."

He bowed twice, touching his head to the ground, rose, took his leave, and departed.

The common people all delight when others are like themselves and detest when others differ from themselves. Those who desire likeness and reject difference do so with the aim of standing out from the crowd. Yet those who aim to stand out from the crowd—have they ever truly stood out? Relying on the crowd to secure what they have heard, they are no better than the many. If someone differs from them, it is only because that person refuses to submit. Thus, they delight in those who are like themselves and detest those who differ, wishing all to submit and thereby surpass the crowd. Yet those who aim to stand out from the crowd—have they ever truly stood out? Only when all agree with them and no dissenting voices remain do their ears find peace. In this way, what they can do is far less than what the many can do. How have they ever stood out from the crowd?

Those who would rule others’ states grasp at the advantages of the Three Kings while blind to the perils. This is to gamble with others’ states. How many gambles do not end in the ruin of others’ states? The chance of preserving another’s state is less than one in ten thousand, while the chance of ruining it is more than ten thousand to one if even a single misstep is made. How lamentable that those who possess lands do not understand this.

To speak of governing a state is to speak of standing out from the crowd. Those who possess lands possess a great thing. Possessing a great thing, one cannot be possessed by things. Only by not being possessed by things can one master things. Understanding that those who master things are not things themselves—how could this apply only to governing the world’s people? Moving in and out of the Six Directions, roaming the Nine Provinces, coming and going alone—this is called "possessing alone." Those who possess alone are called "most noble."

A great thing is simply one great thing. "Mastering things" means dividing them by their kinds. "Not being possessed by things" means not being enslaved by things. The world is one undivided great whole—how can it be split into separate things? Only those who are not enslaved by things can govern its myriad details without being disturbed by their profusion. Thus it is said: "Not being possessed by things, one can master things." Realizing that those who master things are not things themselves—how could this apply only to governing the world without the trouble of trivialities? Those who attain the Way alone are most noble and thus able to stand out from the crowd.

The teaching of the great person is like a shadow following form, an echo answering sound. When questioned, they respond, exhausting what is in their heart, matching heaven’s measure. Abiding where there is no echo, moving where there is no direction, they lead you to wander and return, stirring and roaming without end. "Wander and return"—to go and come back. Leading you to go and return, the restless people roam without end. Moving in and out without sides, with the sun without beginning, they praise and discuss the body, merging into great unity without self. Without self, how could there be anything seen? Those who see are the noble ones of old; those who see nothing are friends of heaven and earth.

That which is lowly yet cannot be shunned is things. That which is humble yet cannot be ignored is the people. That which is hidden yet cannot be avoided is affairs. That which is coarse yet cannot be omitted is laws. That which is distant yet cannot be abandoned is righteousness. That which is close yet cannot be narrowed is benevolence. That which is measured yet cannot be unaccumulated is ritual. That which is centered yet cannot be unexalted is virtue. That which is one yet cannot be unchanging is the Way. That which extends yet cannot be unenacted is heaven.

Thus the sage observes heaven yet does not assist, completes virtue yet is not burdened, emerges from the Way yet does not scheme, meets benevolence yet does not rely on it, approaches righteousness yet does not accumulate it, responds to ritual yet does not conceal, engages with affairs yet does not refuse, equalizes laws yet does not disorder, relies on the people yet does not belittle them, follows things yet does not abandon them. Things are not worth acting upon, yet they cannot be left unacted upon.

Those who do not understand heaven are not pure in virtue. Those who do not comprehend the Way have no ground to stand upon. Those who do not understand the Way—how lamentable!

What is the Dao? There is the Dao of heaven and the Dao of man. That which is revered through non-action is the Dao of heaven; that which is burdened through action is the Dao of man. The ruler embodies the Dao of heaven; the minister embodies the Dao of man. The distance between the Dao of heaven and the Dao of man is vast—this cannot but be examined.

This chapter’s meaning is shallow. If one’s words falter, they will surely be like a dog’s tail appended to sable fur.

---

**Chapter Twelve of the *Xunben* Edition of the *True Classic of Southern Florescence* concludes here.**
*(Variant notes: #1 The received text reads ‘昔’ for ‘者’. #2#3 The received text reads ‘卓’ for ‘早’. #4 The received text reads ‘山’ for ‘上’.)*

**Chapter Thirteen of the *Xunben* Edition of the *True Classic of Southern Florescence***
*Recorded by Lu Lingzhufeng’s Luo Miandao, punctuated by his disciple Peng Xiang*

**Outer Chapters: Heaven and Earth**

Though heaven and earth are vast, their transformations are uniform; though the myriad things are numerous, their governance is one; though the people are many, their lord is singular. The lord originates in virtue and is completed by heaven. Thus it is said: The ancient rulers of the world practiced non-action—nothing more than the virtue of heaven.

Viewing words through the Dao, the lords of the world are rectified; viewing distinctions through the Dao, the righteousness of lord and minister is clarified; viewing abilities through the Dao, the offices of the world are well-ordered; viewing broadly through the Dao, the responses of the myriad things are complete.

Thus, that which pervades heaven and earth is virtue; that which courses through the myriad things is the Dao; that which governs people is affairs; that which possesses skill is technique. Technique is encompassed by affairs, affairs by righteousness, righteousness by virtue, virtue by the Dao, and the Dao by heaven. Thus it is said: Those of old who nurtured the world had no desires, yet the world was sufficient; practiced non-action, yet the myriad things were transformed; were profound and still, yet the hundred clans were settled.

The *Record* states: "Comprehending the One, all affairs are complete; without intent, one attains it, and even ghosts and spirits submit."

The Master said: *(Note: These two instances of "the Master said" are treated as separate chapters in the Cui edition, but when connected to the preceding text, they serve as corroborating quotations. If detached, they lack coherence. It is suspected that "the Master" here refers to Laozi. The preceding quotation from the *Record* is also Laozi’s, so this continues his words. Otherwise, it would be one whom Zhuangzi revered. The later passage where "the Master asked Lao Dan" clearly refers to Confucius.)*

The Dao—it covers and bears the myriad things, vast and boundless! The noble person cannot but hollow out their heart. *(Hollow out the heart of its emotions and desires. The following text elaborates Zhuangzi’s exposition.)*

Non-action as action is called heaven; non-action in speech is called virtue; loving people and benefiting things is called benevolence; unifying differences is called greatness; acting without eccentricity is called magnanimity; possessing myriad distinctions is called wealth.

Thus, holding to virtue is called discipline; the completion of virtue is called establishment; following the Dao is called fulfillment; not allowing things to wear down the will is called perfection. When the noble person is enlightened regarding these ten, they conceal their affairs in profound obscurity.

The greatness of the heart—how it surges forth, becoming the passage for the myriad things! Affairs are stored, yet the heart is not cramped; things depart, yet the heart does not linger.

Those who are thus store gold in the mountains and pearls in the abyss; they do not prize wealth or draw near to nobility and riches; they do not delight in long life nor grieve over early death; they are not honored by success nor shamed by poverty. They do not confine the world’s benefits to their private share, nor do they take ruling the world as their own exalted position. *(Exalted, yet their fundamental heart remains luminous as ever.)* All things are one storehouse; death and life share the same form.

The Master said: The Dao—profound is its abode, limpid is its clarity! Even metal and stone cannot remain silent. *(Metal and stone have voices, but without striking, they do not sound.)* Who among the myriad things can determine it?

*(Abode means stillness. 漻 (liú) describes the appearance of clear water. The fundamental nature of the Dao is like water, profound in its stillness, thus limpid in its clarity. It is also like metal and stone, which inherently possess their qualities—when struck, they resound. Extending this to water and metal, the principles of the myriad things are no different, yet people cannot discern and determine them.)*

Those of kingly virtue move plainly yet are ashamed to be entangled in affairs; they establish their root and origin, and their knowledge communicates with the divine. Thus their virtue is vast. *(Kingly virtue: their virtue suffices to rule the world. Plainly moving: as in the common saying, "plainly treading forward." Those of kingly virtue maintain a tranquil heart and regard entanglement in affairs as shameful. Yet by establishing their root and grasping the essential Dao, their wisdom naturally communicates with the divine, so their virtue is vast and suffices to rule the world.)*

When their heart emerges, things adorn it. Thus, form does not arise without the Dao; life does not become luminous without virtue. Preserving form and exhausting life, establishing virtue and illuminating the Dao—is this not the mark of one of kingly virtue?

Vast and boundless! Suddenly emerging, suddenly moving, and the myriad things follow—this is what is called one of kingly virtue! *(This further elaborates from the perspective of the human heart. When the heart emerges, things adorn it—this is what is meant by "human beings are tranquil at birth, this is the nature of heaven; stirred by things, they move, this is the desire of their nature." Our Confucians explain it plainly; Zhuangzi speaks with biting directness. Form does not arise without the Dao; life does not become luminous without virtue—Laozi and Zhuangzi’s conception of the Dao and virtue cannot be interpreted through our Confucian lens. 荡荡 (dàngdàng) describes a vastness that cannot be commanded. Only when one suddenly emerges and suddenly moves, with the myriad things following, does one truly embody kingly virtue.)*

Gazing into the obscure, listening to the soundless. Within obscurity, alone they see clarity; within silence, alone they hear harmony. *(Further, they are profound yet able to grasp things; divine yet able to be precise.)* Thus, in their interaction with the myriad things, they are utterly without substance yet supply what is sought; they roam freely and seize the essential, whether great or small, long or short, near or far.

From *"Gazing into the obscure"* onward speaks of the marvels of the Way. In their interaction with the myriad things, its responses are inexhaustibly transformative—utterly empty, devoid of any fixed form, yet able to supply all that is sought. At times, it roams freely, yet always finds its place of return. Now vast, it suddenly becomes small; now long, it suddenly turns short; now short, it suddenly extends far. Such is the one who embodies kingly virtue.

*(I suspect the preceding passage is an interpolated forgery, for it contains two instances of "the Master said," and the depth and shallowness of its meaning stand out starkly. It should be excised.)*

The Yellow Emperor roamed north of the Red Water and ascended the heights of Mount Kunlun, gazing southward. Upon his return, he lost his Dark Pearl. He sent Wisdom to seek it, but it was not found; he sent Li Zhu to seek it, but it was not found; he sent Chi Gou to seek it, but it was not found. Only when he sent Xiang Wang was it recovered.

The Yellow Emperor exclaimed: *"How extraordinary! Could it be that only Xiang Wang could retrieve it?"*

*(All this is allegory. The Dark Pearl is the Dao. "Wisdom" refers to the cunning; Li Zhu, also known as Li Lou, is the keen-sighted. Chi Gou represents those reviled as incompetent—opposites of the cunning. Xiang Wang, "Formless," is the opposite of the keen-sighted. The passage means that neither cleverness nor sharp vision suffices to grasp the Dao. Even the dull and shameless come close, yet still fall short; only by transcending all form can it be attained.)*

Yao’s teacher was Xu You; Xu You’s teacher was Nie Que; Nie Que’s teacher was Wang Ni; Wang Ni’s teacher was Pi Yi.

Yao asked Xu You: *"Could Nie Que be fit to match Heaven and become the Son of Heaven? I would rely on Wang Ni to assess him."*

Xu You replied: *"How perilous! The world would be in ruins. Nie Que’s nature is keen, wise, and swift—his abilities surpass others, and he even takes human governance as if it were Heaven’s mandate. With such innate gifts, if he were to receive the throne, his excesses would only grow. He scrutinizes prohibitions against transgression yet does not understand how transgressions arise. Could he match Heaven? He would ride roughshod over others, forgetting Heaven. If he matched Heaven, he would trample over people while remaining oblivious to Heaven itself. He would take one body and multiply it into countless forms; he would exalt cunning, his mind racing like fire; he would be driven by trivial affairs, bound by things, ever glancing about to meet their demands, adapting to every circumstance, yet never abiding in constancy. How could such a one match Heaven?"*

*(Nevertheless, though Nie Que is as described, one cannot generalize about people. Just as there are clans and ancestors—clans have their elders, and ancestors are revered above all—Nie Que might serve as a father to many clans, but not as the father of fathers. That is the role of the ancestor.)*

*"Order is the harbinger of chaos; facing north is the prelude to disaster; facing south is the seed of tyranny. Nie Que’s talents might make him a leader in a disordered age, yet he would not escape the calamity of serving as a minister below or the treachery of ruling as a sovereign above—like a capable minister in a well-ordered age who becomes a villain in times of chaos."*

Yao observed the land of Hua, where a border guard said: *"Ah! A sage!"* He offered blessings, wishing the sage long life.

Yao declined.

The guard wished him wealth.

Yao declined.

The guard wished him many sons.

Yao declined.

The guard said: *"Longevity, wealth, and many sons—these are what all people desire. Why do you alone refuse them?"*

Yao replied: *"Many sons bring many fears; wealth brings many affairs; longevity brings many humiliations. These three do not nurture virtue, so I decline."*

The guard said: *"At first, I took you for a sage, but now you are merely a gentleman. Heaven gives life to the people and assigns them duties. If you have many sons and assign them duties, what fear remains? If you are wealthy and distribute it, what affairs remain? The sage dwells like a quail and eats like a fledgling—unseen as a bird in flight. When the world is ordered, all things flourish together; when it is disordered, one cultivates virtue in seclusion. After a thousand years, weary of the world, one ascends as an immortal, riding the white clouds to the realm of the Emperor. With the three calamities averted and the body free from harm, what humiliation remains?"*

The guard departed, but Yao followed and asked: *"May I inquire further?"*

The guard said: *"Withdraw."*

When Yao ruled the world, Bo Cheng Zi Gao was enfeoffed as a lord. When Yao ceded the throne to Shun, and Shun to Yu, Bo Cheng Zi Gao resigned his lordship to till the fields. Yu went to see him and found him plowing in the wilds. Yu hastened to stand downwind and asked: *"In the past, when Yao ruled the world, you served as a lord. When Yao ceded the throne to Shun, and Shun to me, you resigned your lordship to plow. May I ask why?"*

Bo Cheng Zi Gao replied: *"In Yao’s time, there were no rewards, yet the people were encouraged; no punishments, yet the people stood in awe. Now you rely on rewards and punishments, yet the people grow ever less humane. Virtue has declined from this point; punishments have been established from this point. The chaos of later ages begins here."*

The Master said: "Why not fully align with the Way and let it flow freely? Do not obstruct my affairs."

Plowing diligently without pause—this is still abandonment. In the Great Beginning, there was Nonbeing; there was no Being, no name. From the One, all things arose, yet the One itself remained formless. That by which things are born is called *virtue*; before form emerged, all principles were already present, their order latent yet unbroken—this is called *ming* (fate). Stillness in motion gives rise to things; when things attain their inherent principles, this is called *form*. When form and body preserve spirit, each possessing its own pattern, this is called *nature*. Cultivating nature returns one to *virtue*; when *virtue* reaches its utmost, it merges with the primordial. This merging is emptiness; emptiness is vastness. The clamor of beaks falls silent; when beaks fall silent, they unite with Heaven and Earth. This union is seamless, like threads woven together—appearing foolish, appearing dull. This is called *mysterious virtue*, alignment with the *Great Compliance*.

If one asks whether the Great Beginning held Being or Nonbeing, the answer is: there was no Being, nor even this name. Yet within Nonbeing, the One arose—present, though not yet manifest. This is what is meant by "the Limitless that is the Great Ultimate." All things receive this to be born—this is called *virtue*. Before form emerged, all principles were complete, each with its own order, though still undifferentiated—this is called *ming* (fate). When stillness moves, things are born. Each thing attains its inherent principles—this is called *form*. When form and body preserve the primordial spirit, each possessing its own pattern, this is called *nature*—what is meant by "each thing containing a Great Ultimate." Cultivating nature returns one to *virtue*; when *virtue* reaches its peak, it merges with the formless beginning. This merging is emptiness; emptiness is boundlessness, beyond self and other. All the clamoring voices of the world become one. When beaks fall silent, they unite with Heaven and Earth. Seamless, like threads woven together—appearing foolish, appearing dull. *Mysterious virtue* is profound and wondrous; the *Great Compliance* ensures not a single being or thing is out of harmony.

Tradition holds that the Diagram of the Great Ultimate originated with recluses—this may well be so.

Confucius asked Lao Dan: "There is a man who practices the Way as if imitating it—affirming what others deny, denying what others affirm. A debater once said: 'Disjoin hardness and whiteness as if suspending them in the sky.' If this is so, can such a man be called a sage?"

Lao Dan replied: "This is but a clerk’s skill, a craftsman’s toil—wearying the body, startling the mind. The hound trained to catch raccoons ends up exhausted; the monkey’s agility lures it from the mountains. I tell you what you cannot hear and speak of what you cannot express: those with heads and tails but no heart or ears are legion; those with form and those without form or shape—all are ultimately nonexistent. Their movements and stillness, their life and death, their rise and fall—these are not their true essence. There is a Way of governance in man: forget things, forget Heaven, and it is called 'forgetting the self.' The one who forgets the self is said to enter Heaven."

"The Way of governance" refers to the Way one governs, as if imitating or dissecting it. "Suspended" means displayed; "sky" means the universe—visible as if hung in the open. The passage from "a clerk’s skill" to "from the mountains" has been explained earlier. "Heads and tails" means having beginnings and ends. "No heart" means no heart to listen; "no ears" means not hearing.

Confucius asked Lao Dan: "There is a man whose Way of dwelling resembles that of a sage—affirming what others deny, denying what others affirm. A skilled debater once said: 'Separate hardness and whiteness as if displaying them in the sky, clearly visible.' If this man is like that, can he be called a sage?"

Lao Dan replied: "How could such a one be a sage? Let me tell you what you cannot hear and what you cannot speak of: among what people hear, some remember things with beginnings and ends, some listen without heart, as if without ears. Among what they say, some describe many things with form, some speak of the formless and shapeless as if present before them—yet all of this is ultimately unreal, mere emptiness. Their discussions of movement and stillness, life and death, rise and fall are but deceptive words, not the true principle of how things are. Debate is so useless. There is another Way of governance in man: he forgets things, forgets Heaven. Heaven is the source of things; to forget both is to forget utterly. The world calls those who forget much 'self-forgetters.' Thus, this man is called one who forgets the self. The one who forgets the self is said to enter Heaven—meaning he becomes one with Heaven. The line 'There is a Way of governance in man' responds to the earlier 'a man who practices the Way as if imitating it,' making the meaning clear."

Jiang Lü Mian went to see Ji Che and said: "The Lord of Lu told me: 'I request your instruction.' I declined but was not permitted. Having already advised him, I do not know if it was fitting. Allow me to present it to you. I told the Lord of Lu: 'You must practice reverence and frugality, promote those who are publicly loyal without partiality—then who among the people would dare not be harmonious?'"

Ji Che laughed heartily and said: "If this is what you said, then compared to the virtue of emperors and kings, it is like a mantis raising its arms to block a chariot—it will surely fail. Moreover, if this is so, then he places himself in danger. His observation tower is crowded with things, and many will rush to follow his example."

Jiang Lü Mian started in alarm and said: "I am bewildered by your words. Nevertheless, I wish to hear the drift of your teaching."

Ji Che said:

When the Great Sage governs the world, he unsettles the people’s hearts, shaping them into teachings and transforming customs, eradicating their predatory impulses and fostering their solitary aspirations. It is as if their nature acts of itself, and the people know not how it comes to be. In this way, how could he be like a brother to Yao and Shun, who taught the people with deliberate methods? He desires unity in virtue, and the heart finds repose.

To dwell in lofty heights draws many to follow, and there is the peril of being unequal to the task. To unsettle the people’s hearts is to keep them from constraint. *Predatory impulses* are the inclinations to harm one another; *solitary aspirations* are the resolve of self-attainment. The phrase *"how could he be like a brother to Yao and Shun"* means that the Great Sage governs the world by letting things take their natural course, whereas Yao and Shun could not govern without leaving traces. Would the Great Sage deign to be a brother to Yao and Shun? *The heart finds repose* means the heart is at peace and still. When one shares virtue with the world without contrivance, the heart is at peace.

Zigong traveled south to Chu and, returning to Jin, passed by Hanyin. There he saw an old man preparing his garden beds, digging a tunnel to enter the well, and hoisting a jar to water the plants. He toiled laboriously, expending great effort for little result. Zigong said: "Here is a device that can irrigate a hundred beds in a day, requiring little effort yet yielding great results. Would you not wish to use it?" The gardener looked up and said: "What do you mean?"

Zigong said: "Carve wood into a machine, with the rear heavy and the front light. Drawing water as if pulling, swift as boiling water overflowing—this is called a well-swing."

The gardener flushed with anger, his face darkening as he laughed: "I have heard from my teacher that those who possess mechanical devices must engage in mechanical affairs, and those who engage in mechanical affairs must harbor a mechanical heart. When a mechanical heart lodges in the breast, purity and whiteness are incomplete—purity and whiteness being one’s original nature. If purity and whiteness are incomplete, the spirit’s vitality is unsettled. One whose spirit’s vitality is unsettled is not borne by the Dao. I do not fail to know of such things; I am ashamed to do them."

Zigong’s eyes dimmed with shame; he lowered his head and made no reply. After a while, the gardener said: "What are you doing here?"

"I am a disciple of Confucius."

The gardener said: "Are you not one of those who, with vast learning, presume to rival the sages, and with endless chatter, seek to overshadow the multitude? You strum a single string and sing mournful songs to peddle your reputation across the world! You are about to forget your spirit and qi, to ruin your body—how could you possibly govern yourself, let alone the world? Be on your way; do not hinder my work."

Zigong shrank back in dismay, his face pale. Overwhelmed with reverence, he felt ill at ease and walked thirty *li* before recovering. His disciples asked: "Who was that man just now? Why did you change countenance and lose composure, remaining unsettled for the rest of the day?"

Zigong replied: "At first, I thought there was only one man in the world—meaning Confucius—but now I know there is another. I have heard the Master say: 'In affairs, seek what is fitting; in achievement, seek completion; expend little effort yet yield great results—this is the Way of the sage.' Yet now I see it is not so. One who holds to the Dao has virtue complete; with virtue complete, the body is whole; with the body whole, the spirit is whole. With the spirit whole, this is the Way of the sage. Entrusting one’s life to the people, moving among them without knowing one’s destination—how boundless, how perfectly pure! Entrusting my life to the people, moving among them without knowing where I go—this is merely to dwell in harmony with them, forgetting all else. How boundless, how perfectly whole! One must forget the heart that pursues profit, skill, and cunning. Such a person does not turn toward what is not his will, nor act on what is not his heart. Though the world praises him, if it aligns with his principles, he remains unmoved; though the world condemns him, if it contradicts his principles, he remains untroubled. The world’s praise or blame neither benefits nor harms him—this is what we call a person of complete virtue. I am but a man tossed by wind and waves."

Returning to Lu, he reported this to Confucius. Confucius said: "That man practices the arts of Hundun. Hundun refers to the Great Ultimate. He knows one thing but not two, cultivates his inner self but does not attend to externals. One who is clear and enters simplicity, who practices non-action and returns to uncarved simplicity, who embraces the spirit and wanders among the common people—were you truly startled? As for the arts of Hundun, how could you or I comprehend them?"

Zhun Mang was traveling east to the Great Abyss when he encountered Yuan Feng on the shores of the Eastern Sea. Yuan Feng asked: "Where are you going?"

"To the Great Abyss."

"What will you do there?"

"The Great Abyss, as a thing, receives all without filling, draws from without exhausting. I will wander there."

Yuan Feng said: "Do you have no concern for the horizontal-eyed people? I wish to hear of sagely governance."

Zhun Mang said: "Sagely governance? Let me speak of what it entails. Offices are established without error, talents are promoted without mistake, all circumstances are seen clearly and actions taken accordingly. One acts, and the world is transformed. With a wave of the hand or a glance, the people of the four directions all come—this is called sagely governance. Sagely governance means establishing offices and enacting policies without error, promoting talents without mistake, fully perceiving human affairs and acting accordingly. One acts with such care that no misstep is feared. I have said that when one cultivates oneself, the world is transformed. Once self-cultivation is achieved, a wave of the hand or a glance brings all people to follow."

"I wish to hear of the virtuous person."

Yuan Feng, hearing Zhun Mang’s words, found sagely governance not the ultimate. He asked again: "I wish to hear of the virtuous person."

Zhun Mang said: "The virtuous person dwells without thought, moves without calculation, harbors no judgments of right or wrong, beauty or ugliness. Within the four seas, all rejoice in shared benefit, all find peace in shared provision. He is tender as a child toward its mother, yet carefree as one who wanders and loses the Way. Wealth and resources abound, yet he does not know their source; food and drink suffice, yet he does not know their origin—this is the bearing of the virtuous person."

"I wish to hear of the spirit person."

Zhun Mang said: "The supreme spirit rides the light, its form vanishing—this is called 'illuminating the vast.' After attaining the Dao, the spirit ascends and roams only in the realm of light. Looking down, its body is like withered wood or dung. This is called 'illuminating the vast,' meaning it shines through the boundless, no longer confined by form. When life is fully realized and emotions fully expressed, heaven and earth rejoice and all things dissolve. All things return to their true nature—this is called 'merging with the obscure.' To 'realize life fully' is to exhaust one’s destiny and express one’s emotions completely. As I have spoken of the spirit person’s state, so too one who exhausts the root of life and fully expresses emotions truly rejoices with heaven and earth, and all things vanish. All things return to their original nature, merging into the obscure and formless."

Menwugui and Chizhang Manji observed the armies of King Wu. Chizhang Manji said: "He does not measure up to the Yu clan."

Thus one avoids this calamity.

Menwugui said: "When the world is already well-governed, does it still require the Yu clan to govern it? Or does it wait until chaos arises before seeking order?"

Chizhang Manji replied: "That the world be well-governed is the very wish—why calculate whether it requires the Yu clan? If the world is well-governed, the wish is fulfilled; what need is there for the Yu clan? The Yu clan’s remedy for sores is like a bald man donning a wig or a sick man seeking a physician. A filial son, holding medicine to cure his compassionate father, wears a scorched expression—even the sage feels shame at this. King Wu’s armies certainly do not measure up to the Yu clan, yet even the Yu clan’s governance is no more than a physician using medicine to treat illness, not something arising from spontaneity.

In the age of utmost virtue, worthies are not exalted, abilities are not employed. The ruler is like the topmost branch—high above yet without intent, as the rulers of old were. The people are like wild deer: upright yet unaware of righteousness, loving yet unaware of benevolence, sincere yet unaware of loyalty, fitting yet unaware of trustworthiness, moving and employing one another without considering it a favor. Thus their actions leave no traces, their affairs no record.

The filial son does not flatter his parents, the loyal minister does not fawn upon his lord—this is the height of ministerial and filial excellence. When a parent’s words are affirmed and their conduct deemed good, the world calls the child unfilial; when a lord’s words are affirmed and their conduct deemed good, the world calls the minister disloyal. Yet how can one know this must be so? When the world’s so-called right is affirmed as right, and its so-called good is affirmed as good, it does not call such a one a flatterer. Is it then that the world’s standards are stricter toward kin and more reverent toward rulers?

The world deems loyal ministers and filial sons those who, when their lord or parent errs, admonish rather than flatter. Yet those who comply with and please their lord or parent are inevitably branded as flatterers, while those who comply with and please the world are not—thus one dares to admonish lord or parent but not the world. Are the world’s standards indeed stricter toward kin and more reverent toward rulers?

When others call one a guide, one bristles with anger; when called a flatterer, one flushes with indignation. Yet all one’s life one guides others, all one’s life one flatters others—gathering crowds with polished words and elaborate similes, so that beginning and end, root and branch never align. People refuse the name of guide or flatterer, yet their conduct is nothing but lifelong flattery. They mix metaphors, embellish speech, and seek to assemble followers, yet never recognize their own actions—how can one’s own beginning and end, root and branch, not stand in mutual accusation?

Draped in robes, adorned in colors, modulating one’s expression to ingratiate an age—yet never calling oneself a flatterer; joining others in debating right and wrong—yet never calling oneself one of the crowd: this is the height of folly.

This passage now turns to Shun: Shun draped his robes, and the world was governed; he displayed the five colors to adorn the five virtues, ruling with reverence from his southern-facing throne. Thus it is said: draped in robes, adorned in colors, modulating his expression. Shun thus pleased an entire age without calling himself a flatterer; he debated right and wrong with others without calling himself one of the crowd. This is the height of folly.

This speaks of Shun, yet the following text shifts to King Wu—seemingly a veiled critique, unnoticed by readers.

One who knows their own folly is not utterly foolish; one who knows their own confusion is not utterly confused."

Great confusion persists unsolved for a lifetime; great folly remains unenlightened to the end. Appending "folly" or adding "confusion" to a statement—such is the rhetorical style often seen in Zhuangzi’s writing.

When three travel together and one is confused, the destination may still be reached, for confusion is but slight; if two are confused, effort is spent in vain, for confusion prevails. Now that the whole world is confused, though I have my own aspirations, they cannot be fulfilled. Is this not lamentable?

This uses the journey as a metaphor: if three set out together toward the east, but one wishes to go west, confusion is but partial, and the east may still be reached. If two wish to go west, confusion prevails, and all labor is wasted. The analogy refers to King Wu’s campaign against Zhou, when eight hundred states pledged allegiance—thus the whole world was confused. "I" here is Chi Zhang Manji speaking of himself. *Qi xiang* means "desired direction." Though I have my own course, it cannot be realized—is this not lamentable? This deeply grieves the affair of King Wu.

Grand music does not enter the ears of the village; when the vulgar tunes *Zhe Yang* and *Huang Hua* are played, people cackle with delight. Thus lofty speech does not lodge in the hearts of the multitude; the profound is unspoken, and common speech prevails. If two earthen jars are struck as bells, confusion arises, and the true destination is lost.

Now that the whole world is confused, though I have my aspirations, how can they be awaited? This again uses music as a metaphor: ancient music does not please the common ear, but vulgar tunes bring joy. If two strike earthen jars for sound, people delight in the novelty and are led astray. The music of antiquity cannot spread—how much less so when the whole world is confused! Though I have my own course, how can it be realized? The repeated metaphors and reiterated words make the meaning all the more poignant.

Old commentaries, misled by the phrase "the destination," read this as a continuation of the previous passage and emend *fou zhong* to *chui zhong*, which is mistaken. Here, "the destination is lost" echoes the earlier "the destination may be reached." The former refers to a journey; this refers to the spread of music.

To know something is unattainable yet force it is yet another confusion. Therefore, it is best to let go and not pursue it. If not pursued, with whom should one share concern?

This subtly refers to those like Boyi: abandoning the matter and not examining it, one need not share others’ sorrows. Consider the leper who gives birth at midnight, hastily fetching a light to inspect the child, anxious lest it resemble himself. This illustrates one who need not share another’s concern: the leper fears his child will be like him, thus not wishing for sameness—so why share another’s sorrow?

A hundred-year-old tree, split into sacrificial vessels, adorned with blue and yellow patterns, its offcuts discarded in the ditch—compared to the vessel, the ditch-bound scrap is base, yet both have lost their original nature. Robber Zhi and the virtuous Zeng and Shi differ in conduct, yet their loss of nature is the same.

Moreover, there are five ways to lose one’s nature:
1. The five colors confuse the eyes, making them unclear;
2. The five sounds confuse the ears, making them dull;
3. The five odors choke the nose, causing the forehead to furrow;
4. The five flavors cloy the mouth, making it numb;
5. Desires and aversions agitate the heart, scattering the nature.

These five are all harms to life. Yet Yang Zhu and Mo Di, striving and self-satisfied, claim to have attained the Way—this is not what I call attainment. If distress can be called attainment, then the owl in a cage may also be said to have attained it.

Furthermore, desires and aversions, sounds and colors, barricade the inner self; ceremonial caps, kingfisher plumes, sashes, and long girdles constrain the outer. Internally hemmed in by barriers, externally bound by cords, one glories in being entangled yet deems it attainment. If so, then prisoners with bound arms and fingers, or tigers and leopards in cages, may also be said to have attained it.

*The True Classic of Southern Florescence* concludes here with Chapter Thirteen of the received text. Chapter Fourteen, "The Dao of Heaven," follows in the Outer Chapters.

*The True Classic of Southern Florescence*, following the received text, Chapter Fourteen: "The Dao of Heaven"
Recorded by Lu Lingzhu of Bamboo Peak, Luling; collated by disciple Peng Xiang

The Dao of Heaven operates without accumulation, thus all things are completed;
The Dao of the Sovereign operates without accumulation, thus the world submits;
The Dao of the Sage operates without accumulation, thus all within the seas obeys.
Without accumulation, there is no stagnation.

Those who are enlightened by Heaven, who penetrate the Sage, whose sixfold penetration and fourfold openness manifest the virtue of emperors and kings—when they act for themselves, they are obscurely quiescent, without exception.
Obscurely quiescent: all cleverness and brilliance are extinguished.
Sixfold penetration and fourfold openness: like the six directions and four quarters, all is thoroughly pervaded.

The stillness of the Sage is not because stillness is good, and thus he is still.
He does not take stillness as good and thereby impose it.
Nothing in the myriad things suffices to disturb his mind, thus he is still.
When water is still, it clearly mirrors beard and brows, its level serves as a standard, and the great craftsman takes it as his model.
*(The* Rites of Zhou*: "The artisan levels the ground with water.")*
If still water is so luminous, how much more so the spirit?
Is the Sage’s mind still?
It is the mirror of Heaven and Earth, the looking-glass of the myriad things.

Now, emptiness, stillness, tranquility, quiescence, and non-action—these are the level of Heaven and Earth, the perfection of the Way and its power.
Thus emperors, kings, and sages rest therein.
To rest is to halt.
At rest, one is empty; empty, then full—fullness is order.
Within emptiness there is fullness, naturally possessing sequence.
Empty, then still; still, then moving; moving, then attaining.
Within stillness there is movement; moving, there is nothing it does not attain.
Still, then non-action; in non-action, those who handle affairs bear their responsibility.
In non-action, those who manage affairs naturally assume their duty.
In non-action, one is carefree.
Carefree: sorrow and calamity cannot abide, and one’s years and lifespan are long.

Now, emptiness, stillness, tranquility, quiescence, and non-action—these are the root of the myriad things.
Illuminating this and facing south, one becomes Yao as sovereign;
Illuminating this and facing north, one becomes Shun as minister.
Practicing this in the superior position, it is the virtue of emperors, kings, and the Son of Heaven;
Practicing this in the inferior position, it is the Way of the obscure sage and the unadorned king.
Retiring with this to dwell in leisure, wandering among rivers and seas, mountains and forests, scholars submit;
Advancing with this to govern the age, then achievements are great, fame is illustrious, and the world is unified.

Still, one is a sage; moving, one is a king.
In non-action, one is revered; in simplicity and plainness, none in the world can rival their beauty.

Those who comprehend the virtue of Heaven and Earth—this is called the great root and great origin, harmonizing with Heaven.
This is how one harmonizes and regulates the world, harmonizing with others.
Harmonizing with others is called human joy;
Harmonizing with Heaven is called Heavenly joy.

Zhuangzi said: "My Master! My Master!"—that is, the Great Master—
"Who carves the myriad things yet is not cruel;
Whose beneficence extends to ten thousand generations yet is not benevolent;
Who is older than high antiquity yet is not long-lived;
Who covers and bears Heaven and Earth, who carves and adorns the forms of the multitude, yet is not skillful."
This is called Heavenly joy.

Thus it is said: One who knows Heavenly joy—
In life, they move with Heaven; in death, they transform with things.
Still, they share the virtue of yin; moving, they share the wave of yang.
Thus, one who knows Heavenly joy has no resentment from Heaven, no blame from others, no encumbrance from things, no reproach from spirits.

Thus it is said:
In movement, they are Heaven; in stillness, they are Earth.
With a single mind settled, they rule the world.
Their spirits do not haunt; their souls do not weary.
With a single mind settled, the myriad things submit.

Speaking of emptiness and stillness, extending them to Heaven and Earth, penetrating the myriad things—this is called Heavenly joy.
Heavenly joy is the Sage’s mind nurturing the world.

Now, the virtue of emperors and kings takes Heaven and Earth as its model, the Way and its power as its master, and non-action as its constant.
In non-action, one employs the world and has surplus;
In action, one is employed by the world and is insufficient.
Thus the ancients prized non-action.

When the superior practices non-action, the inferior also practices non-action—this is the inferior harmonizing in virtue with the superior.
When the inferior harmonizes in virtue with the superior, they do not serve as ministers.
When the inferior acts, the superior also acts—this is the superior harmonizing in the Way with the inferior.
When the superior harmonizes in the Way with the inferior, they do not serve as lords.
The superior must practice non-action and employ the world; the inferior must act and be employed by the world.
This is the unchanging Way.

Thus the ancients who ruled the world—
Though their knowledge encompassed Heaven and Earth, they did not scheme for themselves;
Though their eloquence adorned the myriad things, they did not delight in themselves;
Though their abilities exhausted the world within the seas, they did not act for themselves.
Heaven does not produce, yet the myriad things transform; Earth does not grow, yet the myriad things are nurtured; emperors and kings practice non-action, yet the world achieves merit.

Thus it is said: None is more divine than Heaven, none richer than Earth, none greater than emperors and kings.
Thus it is said: The virtue of emperors and kings matches Heaven and Earth.
This is the Way of riding Heaven and Earth, driving the myriad things, and employing the multitudes of people.

The root lies above, the branches below;
The essential lies with the ruler, the details with the ministers.
The deployment of the three armies and the five weapons—this is the branch of virtue;
Rewards and punishments, advantages and harms, the application of the five punishments—this is the branch of teaching;
Rites, laws, measures, and standards; forms, names, comparisons, and details—this is the branch of governance;
The sounds of bells and drums, the adornments of feathers and plumes—this is the branch of music;
Wailing and weeping, mourning garments, degrees of severity in attire—this is the branch of mourning.
These five branches require the operation of spirit and the movement of the mind’s art before they can be followed.

Branches and details—ancient people possessed them, but they are not what comes first.
The ruler leads, the minister follows; the father leads, the child follows; the elder brother leads, the younger brother follows; the elder leads, the younger follows; the man leads, the woman follows; the husband leads, the wife follows.
Now, precedence in honor and rank, sequence in before and after—these are the movements of Heaven and Earth. Thus the Sage takes them as his model.

Heaven is honored and Earth is humble—this is the proper station of the numinous;
Spring and summer come first, autumn and winter follow—this is the sequence of the four seasons;
The transformations and creations of the myriad things, their sprouting forms and configurations, their waxing and waning, their flowing changes—
Even Heaven and Earth, most numinous of all, possess this order of precedence and sequence, how much more so the Way of Man.
In ancestral temples, kinship is revered; in court, honor is revered; in village communities, seniority is revered; in affairs, worthiness is revered—this is the great order of the Dao.
To speak of the Dao while rejecting its order is not the Dao; to speak of the Dao while misrepresenting it is to grasp at nothing.
Thus the ancients who illuminated the great Dao first illuminated Heaven, then followed with virtue and power; once virtue and power were illuminated, benevolence and righteousness followed; once benevolence and righteousness were illuminated, the divisions of duty followed; once the divisions of duty were illuminated, forms and names followed; once forms and names were illuminated, the delegation of tasks followed; once the delegation of tasks was illuminated, investigation and examination followed (examination: pronounced *shěng*, meaning to trace origins and scrutinize forms);
Once investigation and examination were illuminated, right and wrong followed; once right and wrong were illuminated, rewards and punishments followed; once rewards and punishments were illuminated, the foolish and the wise were properly placed, the noble and the base occupied their stations, the benevolent and worthy, the unworthy and flawed, all accorded with their natures.
Abilities were necessarily distinguished, names necessarily followed.
With this, one served superiors, nurtured subordinates, governed things, and cultivated the self; wisdom and schemes were not employed, for all necessarily returned to Heaven.
This is called Great Peace, the ultimate in governance.
Thus the text says: "There are forms and names."
Forms and names—ancient people possessed them, but they are not what comes first.
Those of old who spoke of the great Dao underwent five transformations before forms and names could be established, nine transformations before rewards and punishments could be discussed.
From illuminating Heaven to establishing forms and names—five transformations; from illuminating Heaven to discussing rewards and punishments—nine transformations.
To abruptly speak of forms and names is to ignore their foundation; to abruptly speak of rewards and punishments is to ignore their beginning.
To speak of the Dao in reverse, to discourse on it while within it, is what men govern—how can they govern others?
To abruptly speak of forms, names, rewards, and punishments is to possess the tools of governance, not the Way of governance.
Such tools may be applied to the world, but they are insufficient to govern the world.
This is called a disputatious scholar, a man of partial understanding.
Rites, laws, measures, and degrees, the comparison and scrutiny of forms and names—ancient people possessed them.
These are how inferiors serve superiors, not how superiors nurture subiors.

Of old, Shun asked Yao: "How does the Son of Heaven employ his mind?"
Yao said: "I do not slight the bereft, I do not abandon the destitute, I grieve for the dead, I cherish the young and pity women—this is how I employ my mind."
Shun said: "Admirable indeed, yet it is not great."
Yao said: "Then what should it be?"
Shun said: "When the virtue of Heaven emerges in tranquility, the sun and moon shine and the four seasons proceed, like the constancy of day and night, like the movement of clouds and the falling of rain."
Yao said: "Then I am but entangled and troubled!" (Yao referred to himself.)
You, sir, align with Heaven; I align with men.
Now Heaven and Earth are what the ancients deemed greatest, what the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun all praised.
Thus those of old who ruled all under Heaven—what did they do? They simply modeled themselves on Heaven and Earth.
For Heaven and Earth are what the ancients deemed greatest.

(Here follows the passage from *Zhuangzi* where Confucius and Laozi converse:)
Confucius was storing books in the Western Zhou archives when Zilu counseled: "I have heard that the Keeper of the Archives in Zhou, a man named Lao Dan, has retired and lives at home. If you wish to store books, Master, why not seek his assistance?"
Confucius said: "Very well."
He went to see Laozi, who refused. Thereupon Confucius expounded the Twelve Classics to persuade him (one account says the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, *Music*, *Changes*, and *Spring and Autumn Annals*—the Six Classics—plus the Six *Weft* texts, totaling twelve; another says the *Changes*’ upper and lower canons plus the Ten Wings make twelve; another says the twelve ducal reigns of the *Spring and Autumn*. The accounts differ, and need not be rigidly adhered to).
Laozi interrupted his discourse, saying: "This is too prolix! I would hear the essentials."
Confucius said: "The essentials lie in benevolence and righteousness."
Laozi said: "May I ask: are benevolence and righteousness part of human nature?"
Confucius said: "Indeed. The noble man who lacks benevolence cannot be perfected; without righteousness, he cannot live. Benevolence and righteousness are the true nature of man—what more is there to be done?"
Laozi said: "May I ask: what are benevolence and righteousness?"
Confucius said: "A joyful heart in harmony with things, universal love without partiality—this is the essence of benevolence and righteousness."
Laozi said: "Hmm! Nearly the last word! (A remark on how such talk comes too late.)
Universal love—is this not impractical? Without partiality—is this not itself partiality? Sir, do you wish to ensure that the world does not lose its shepherds? Then Heaven and Earth already have their constancy, the sun and moon their brightness, the stars and constellations their ranks, birds and beasts their flocks, trees and plants their uprightness.
You, sir, should follow virtue in your conduct, proceed by guiding and leading—this is already perfection. Why then laboriously brandish benevolence and righteousness like one beating a drum to seek a lost child?
Ah! You, sir, confuse human nature!"
Shi Chengqi saw Laozi and asked: "I have heard that you, sir, are a sage. I did not hesitate to travel far to seek an audience, journeying a hundred stages with calloused feet, never daring to rest. Now I observe that you are no sage. You have leftover vegetables in rat burrows yet abandon your younger sister—this is not benevolent. Before you, food is not fully consumed, yet you hoard without limit." (Rat burrows contain leftover scraps, yet he cannot even nourish his sister; food is not exhausted before him, yet his hoarding knows no bounds—these are all unenlightened acts. The first sentence, "not benevolent," encompasses the following criticisms of abandoning his sister and hoarding, a rebuke based on worldly views, not an actual occurrence.)

Laozi remained silent, offering no response. The next day, Shi Chengqi sought him again and said: "Yesterday, I reproached you, but now my heart has truly retreated. *Retreated*—meaning withdrawn, no longer reproaching or criticizing. Why is this?" Laozi replied: "The man of cunning wisdom and divine sagacity—I once believed myself free of such traits. *Free*—meaning departed. In the past, when you called me an ox, you named me an ox; when you called me a horse, you named me a horse. If one truly possesses the reality, yet refuses the name others give, one invites calamity anew. I submit—always submit. I do not submit because submission is due. *Submit*—meaning to yield. My submission to others is but the constant presence of a yielding heart, not merely yielding to what is worthy of submission. Even when words are unworthy, I yield."

Shi Chengqi stepped aside like a wild goose avoiding its shadow, then advanced and asked how to cultivate the self. Laozi said: "Your countenance is haughty, your gaze piercing, your brow broad and towering, your mouth gaping wide, your demeanor rigid. You are like a horse reined in yet still straining to bolt. Though tethered, its mind ever races. To move is to restrain, to act is to trigger the crossbow’s mechanism. To observe is to scrutinize, to know is to perceive excess. All this betrays a lack of trustworthiness. Such a man at the borderlands would be called a thief."

Laozi continued: "As for the Dao, it is not exhausted in greatness nor neglected in smallness, thus all things are complete within it. Vast—so boundless it contains all; profound—so unfathomable it cannot be measured. Forms, virtue, benevolence, and righteousness are but the dregs of spirit. Who but the Perfected Man can stabilize them? The Perfected Man—though he possesses the world, how vast is this! Yet it does not burden him. Though the world seethes and struggles, he does not join it. He discerns the unreal and does not shift with profit. He exhausts the truth of things and guards their root. Thus, he transcends heaven and earth, forsakes the myriad things, and his spirit is never constrained. He penetrates the Dao, unites with virtue, sets aside benevolence and righteousness, and treats rites and music as guests—not as masters. The heart of the Perfected Man is thus settled."

This passage, in the manner of Laozi’s response to Shi Chengqi’s question:

The Dao prized by the world is but texts. Texts are merely words, and words have their value. What is valued in words is their meaning, and meaning has its source. Yet the source of meaning cannot be transmitted through words, though the world clings to transmitting words through texts. Though the world prizes them, they are not truly worthy—for what they prize is not what is truly prized. Thus, what can be seen are forms and colors; what can be heard are names and sounds. Alas! The world believes forms, colors, names, and sounds suffice to grasp the truth of things. Yet if forms, colors, names, and sounds truly do not suffice, then the wise do not speak, and those who speak are not wise. How, then, could the world recognize this?

Duke Huan was reading in the hall when Wheelwright Bian, carving a wheel below, set down his mallet and chisel, ascended, and asked: "May I dare ask what words Your Grace reads?" The Duke replied: "The words of a sage." The wheelwright said: "Is the sage still alive?"

Duke Huan said: "He is already dead."
Wheelwright Bian replied: "Then what Your Grace reads is but the dregs and husks of the ancients."

Duke Huan said: "I read books—how dare a wheelwright pass judgment? If you have an explanation, speak; if not, you shall die."
Wheelwright Bian said: "Your servant observes this through his own craft. When carving a wheel, if done too slowly, it is smooth but not firm; if done too swiftly, it is rough and does not fit. Neither too slow nor too swift—it is grasped by the hand and responds to the heart. The mouth cannot express it, yet a certain method resides therein. I cannot make my son understand it, nor can my son receive it from me. Thus, though I am seventy years old, I still carve wheels in my old age. The ancients and what they could not transmit are gone—then what Your Grace reads is but the dregs of the ancients."

*The Fourteenth Scroll of the Xunben Edition of the Nan Hua True Scripture* concludes.
*The Fifteenth Scroll of the Xunben Edition of the Nan Hua True Scripture*
Recorded by Lu Lingzhufeng’s Luo Miandao, collated by his disciple Peng Xiang.

**Outer Chapters: Heaven’s Revolutions**
Does Heaven revolve?

Does Earth abide in stillness?
Do the sun and moon contend for their positions?
The sun and moon share the ecliptic—thus it is said they contend for their positions.
Who governs this?
Who upholds this order?
Is it that some mechanism compels them, leaving them no choice?
Or do they revolve of themselves, unable to halt?
That which has a mechanism is as if bound by something.
That which cannot halt of itself is as if unbound by anything.
Do clouds form rain?
Or does rain form clouds?
Who bestows this abundance?
Who, idle and indulgent in pleasure, urges forth these words—clouds as abundance, rain as bestowal?
The wind rises from the north, then turns west, then east, its sound rising in wandering ascent.
Who exhales and inhales this?
Who, idle and unoccupied, stirs and scatters it thus?
The wind rises from the north, then moves west and east, ascending in wandering flight.
When it rises from the north, it naturally moves south—thus the south need not be named.
When it ascends, it naturally rises from below—thus the below need not be spoken.
May I ask why?
Wu Xian Rao replied: Come, let me tell you.

Heaven possesses the Six Ultimates and the Five Constants. If the emperor-kings align with them, there is order; if they oppose them, there is misfortune.
