The affairs of the Ninefold Luo bring governance to completion and virtue to fulfillment, overseeing the lands below, and all under heaven bear them—this is called the Supreme August. *Luo* is synonymous with *luo* (settlements), referring to the affairs of the nine provinces.

The Grand Steward Dang of Shang asked Zhuangzi about benevolence. Zhuangzi said: "Tigers and wolves are benevolent." Dang asked: "What do you mean?" Zhuangzi replied: "Father and son are affectionate—how is this not benevolence?" Dang said: "May I ask about perfect benevolence?" Zhuangzi said: "Perfect benevolence has no partiality." The Grand Steward said: "I have heard that without partiality there is no love, and without love there is no filial piety. Can one say that perfect benevolence is unfilial?" Zhuangzi said: "Not so. Perfect benevolence is lofty indeed—filial piety is hardly sufficient to describe it. This is not a statement that surpasses filial piety, but one that falls short of it. This is what is meant by perfect benevolence having no partiality. That which surpasses is contrived; that which falls short is natural.

Consider one traveling south to Ying: facing north, they do not see Mount Ming—why is this? It is because they are too far from it. *Mount Ming* is the name of a northern mountain. Just as one traveling far does not see Mount Ming, so too does perfect benevolence not see filial piety. Thus it is said: Reverent filial piety is easy; loving filial piety is difficult. Loving filial piety is easy; forgetting one’s kin is difficult. Forgetting one’s kin is easy; making one’s kin forget oneself is difficult. Making one’s kin forget oneself is easy; forgetting all under heaven is difficult. Forgetting all under heaven is easy; making all under heaven forget oneself is difficult.

The virtue of perfect benevolence leaves Yao and Shun behind without effort, its beneficence extending to ten thousand generations, yet none under heaven know it—how could it merely sigh and speak of benevolence and filial piety? Perfect benevolence is such that its virtue could be compared to Yao and Shun yet not pursued, its beneficence extending to ten thousand generations while none under heaven know it—how would it deign to sigh and boast of benevolence and filial piety?

Filial devotion, fraternal respect, benevolence, righteousness, loyalty, trustworthiness, integrity, and incorruptibility—these are all self-exertions that labor one’s virtue and are not worthy of praise. Thus it is said: The most noble casts aside state rank; the most wealthy casts aside state wealth; the greatest aspiration casts aside fame and reputation. Only thus does the Dao remain unchanging.

Beimen Cheng asked the Yellow Emperor: "When Your Majesty performed the music of *Xianchi* in the wilds of Dongting, I first heard it and was afraid, then heard it again and grew weary, and finally heard it and was moved. Adrift and silent, I could not find myself." *Adrift* describes the unsettled spirit; *silent*, the inability to speak. *Not finding oneself* means the heart could not be at ease. The Emperor said: "You were nearly correct. I performed it with human affairs, harmonized it with Heaven, conducted it with ritual and righteousness, and established it with the Great Purity.

The perfect music first responds to human affairs, accords with the principles of Heaven, is conducted with the Five Virtues, and answers to nature—only then does it regulate the four seasons and harmonize the myriad things. The four seasons arise in succession, and the myriad things follow their birth. Now flourishing, now declining, the patterns of civil and martial order unfold. Now clear, now turbid, yin and yang harmonize, their sounds flowing luminously. When hibernating creatures first stir, I startle them with thunder. Its end has no tail, its beginning no head. Now death, now life; now collapse, now rise—ever constant and inexhaustible, yet the One cannot be awaited.

Thus you were afraid. The four seasons and the myriad things speak to the spontaneity of creation. Flourishing and declining, civil and martial, clear and turbid, yin and yang—these describe their rhythms. *Their sounds flowing luminously* means their sounds flow smoothly and shine brightly. This line harmonizes the preceding rhyme, embodying the purity of following nature. When hibernating creatures first stir, I startle them with thunder. Its end has no tail, its beginning no head—it arises and ceases suddenly, like the unpredictability of thunder. Now death, now life; now collapse, now rise—ever cycling without end, yet the One cannot be awaited. Read this as a continuous passage: one death is followed by one life, one collapse by one rise, endlessly circulating, yet the One cannot be delayed. One death is followed by one life, one collapse by one rise, with no pause—how can it be awaited? Beimen Cheng, hearing the music thus, felt terror.

I then performed it with the harmony of yin and yang, illuminating it with the brightness of the sun and moon. Its sounds could be short or long, soft or hard, transforming uniformly without adhering to fixed patterns. In valleys, it filled valleys; in pits, it filled pits. Stopping up the gaps, I guarded my spirit, measuring by things. Its sounds were expansive, its name lofty and bright. Thus ghosts and spirits guarded their hidden realms, while the sun, moon, and stars followed their courses. I halted it at the bounded, flowed it into the boundless. You wish to ponder it but cannot know it; to gaze upon it but cannot see it; to pursue it but cannot reach it.

Suddenly standing on the Dao of the four voids, leaning against a withered catalpa and chanting: the eyes exhaust what they wish to see, strength is spent in what it wishes to pursue—I cannot attain it, so be it. The form fills the emptiness, even to the point of meandering. You meandered, thus grew weary." Continuing the preceding discussion of yin-yang harmony, I performed it with the harmony of yin and yang, illuminating it with the brightness of the sun and moon. Though harmonious, it had distinctions—this is what is called clarity. Its sounds could be short or long, soft or hard, transforming uniformly without adhering to fixed patterns—all embodying clarity. In valleys, it filled valleys; in pits, it filled pits—its sounds were all-pervading. *Stopping up the gaps* is like stopping the ears and covering the eyes. When my music was not performed, hearing and sight guarded their spirit, following the measure of things. The sounds received were great or small, the music’s tones expansive, its name lofty and bright.

The spirits and gods guard their hidden realms, the sun, moon, and stars follow their celestial paths—each without disorder. I halt it at the bounded, let it flow into the boundless. Sometimes halting, sometimes flowing, it has no constancy. Though you may strive to ponder it, you cannot know it; though you may gaze upon it, you cannot see it; though you may chase after it, you cannot reach it. Suddenly you stand lost amid the fourfold emptiness of the road, leaning against a withered catalpa, chanting—only to feel your vision exhausted, your strength spent. This is what is meant by "though you wish to follow it, there is no way to do so." Since it cannot be attained, all you perceive is its form and image filling the void. Thus, the heart slackens and grows weary of itself.

I then perform it with sounds of tirelessness, attuning it to the mandate of nature. Thus, it seems to blend and chase in profusion, like a forest of music without form; it spreads and unfurls without trailing, deep and dim without sound. It moves without direction, dwells in the obscure and unseen. Some call it death, some call it life; some call it substance, some call it glory. It flows and scatters, shifting without adhering to fixed sounds. The world doubts it and turns to the sages for verification. The sage is one who penetrates human nature and fulfills the mandate. The celestial mechanism does not unfold, yet the five offices are all complete—this is called the music of heaven. It speaks no words, yet the heart rejoices.

Thus, the Lord of Yan sang its praise:
*"Hear it, yet hear no sound;
See it, yet see no form.
It fills heaven and earth,
Envelops the six directions.
You wish to listen, yet find no point of contact—
Thus you are perplexed."*

Continuing from the preceding discussion of weariness, I perform it with sounds of tirelessness. What is meant by tirelessness? It is attuned to the natural mandate of heaven—emerging from spontaneity, like the flowing course of heaven’s decree. Thus, its sounds seem to mingle and pursue one another in a dense profusion, growing together like a forest, rejoicing without form—this is what is called *yiru* (resonant clarity). When it scatters and arises, it exhausts itself without lingering; when it is deep and dim, it is utterly silent. Its movement has no fixed direction, its dwelling is in the profound and obscure. Some measure it and call it death, some call it life; some call it substance, some call it glory. All one sees is its flowing, scattering, and shifting, without adhering to fixed sounds. Thus, the world doubts it and examines its principles, turning to the sages for inquiry. The sage is one who understands human nature and attains the mandate. This music cannot be made by anyone but a sage.

The term *mandate* here corresponds to the earlier "attuned to the mandate of nature." The celestial mechanism does not unfold—no artificial devices are employed. My offices are all complete; the governance of the five tones is fully established. The music of heaven is the spontaneous mandate itself. It speaks no words, yet the heart rejoices—its wonder cannot be captured in speech, yet the heart naturally awakens to joy.

The ode of the Lord of Yan is cited to reveal the marvel beyond sound. You seek to grasp its subtlety but find no point of contact—hence the perplexity.

Music begins with awe; awe gives rise to reverence. I follow with weariness; weariness gives rise to flight. It ends in confusion; confusion gives rise to foolishness. Foolishness gives rise to the Way, and the Way can be borne and journeyed with as one.

This concludes the three preceding sections, summarizing their ultimate return. Awe sharpens the spirit, as if haunted by ghosts and spirits—hence "reverence." Weariness exhausts the heart and strength, prompting the desire to abandon it—hence "flight." Confusion clouds knowledge—hence "foolishness." Once foolish, one may enter the Way; once in the Way, one may ride alongside it. Thus, the discussion of music leads to the Way.

This passage resembles the performance of music in the wilds of Dongting—seemingly without beginning or end, difficult to grasp at first glance, yet upon careful examination, it has its own order. Since Confucius spoke to the Grand Music Master, even Zhuangzi, who does not merely plagiarize old words, discusses music in this way to achieve its subtlety.

Confucius traveled west to Wei, and Yan Yuan asked Shi Jin (the Grand Music Master of Lu, named Jin): "What do you think of my Master’s conduct?"
Shi Jin replied: "Alas! Your Master is in dire straits."
Yan Yuan said: "Why?"
Shi Jin said: "Before a straw dog is displayed, it is kept in a fine basket, wrapped in embroidered cloth, and the priest fasts to prepare for its presentation. Once displayed, passersby trample its head and spine, and fuel-gatherers take it to burn. If one were to gather it again, place it in a fine basket, wrap it in embroidered cloth, and wander or rest beneath it, one would not only fail to dream but would surely suffer from *mi* (a condition where something enters the eye). Not only would dreams fail, but one would see omens and apparitions in the eyes. Now, your Master has taken the straw dogs of the former kings—already displayed—and leads his disciples to wander and rest beneath them. Thus, he was assaulted by the felling of a tree in Song, erased his traces in Wei, and was impoverished in Shang and Zhou—was this not like a dream? Originally, it was said one could not dream, yet now it is hastily declared, 'Was this not a dream?' Similarly, in the *Analects*, it was originally said one had not yet attained it, yet it is hastily declared, 'One fears attaining it.' He was besieged between Chen and Cai, went seven days without cooking a meal, and death and life became neighbors—was this not like *mi*? The people of Kuang mistook him for Yang Hu and besieged him, much like an apparition blinding the eyes.

The best way to travel on water is by boat, and the best way to travel on land is by cart. If, because a boat can travel on water, one seeks to push it on land, it will never move even a short distance in a lifetime. Are not past and present like water and land? Are not Zhou and Lu like boat and cart? Now, to seek to implement Zhou’s ways in Lu is like pushing a boat on land—laborious yet fruitless, and one’s person will surely suffer harm. He did not understand the transmission without fixed methods, which responds to things without exhaustion. Only by having no constant laws can one respond to the times without exhaustion."

Moreover, have you alone not observed the well-sweep? When pulled, it bows; when released, it rises. It is drawn by others, not drawing others. Thus, it bows and rises without offending anyone. Are not the rites, righteousness, laws, and standards of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors like the wild pear, pear, orange, and pomelo? Their flavors differ, yet all are pleasing to the mouth. Thus, rites, righteousness, laws, and standards are things that change with the times. Now, if one were to dress an ape in the robes of the Duke of Zhou, it would surely gnaw, tear, and rend them, casting them off entirely before feeling at ease—observe here the difference between past and present, between the Duke of Zhou and today. When Xi Shi was afflicted with heart pain and knit her brows in her village, the ugly women of her village saw it and thought it beautiful. Returning home, they too clutched their chests and knit their brows. The rich of the village, seeing this, firmly shut their doors and refused to go out; the poor, seeing it, took their wives and children and fled. They knew the beauty of knitting brows but did not know why knitting brows was beautiful. Alas, how confined you are!

Confucius, at the age of fifty-one, had still not heard the Dao. He then journeyed south to Pei to see Lao Dan. Lao Dan was a man of Chen, serving as minister in Ku County, which lay near Pei. Lao Dan said, “Have you come? I have heard that you are a worthy man of the north. Have you also attained the Dao?” Confucius replied, “I have not.” Lao Dan asked, “How have you sought it?” Confucius said, “I sought it in measures and numbers, yet after five years I had not attained it.” Lao Dan asked, “How else have you sought it?” Confucius said, “I sought it in yin and yang, yet after twelve years I had not attained it.” Lao Dan said, “Indeed. If the Dao could be offered, there would be none who would not offer it to their lord; if the Dao could be presented, there would be none who would not present it to their kin; if the Dao could be told to others, there would be none who would not tell it to their brothers; if the Dao could be given to others, there would be none who would not give it to their descendants. Yet this is not possible. Why? Because within, there is no master to abide, and without, there is no norm to proceed. That which issues from within is not received from without—thus the sage does not emerge. That which enters from without has no master within—thus the sage does not conceal. In one case, a person lacks a master within and cannot find where to abide. In another, there is no one without to affirm what is right, and thus one does not know how to proceed. Some may awaken to it spontaneously from within, yet without a teacher’s transmission, they refuse to receive instruction from without. Though the sage cannot cause this Dao to emerge from such a person, others may be guided by instruction from without, yet they abandon themselves and lack a master within. Though the sage cannot conceal this Dao within such a person, this is why the Dao cannot be sought.

Fame is a public vessel—one cannot take too much of it. Benevolence and righteousness are the travelers’ lodges of the former kings, where one may stay a single night but cannot dwell long. To be seen and held to many demands—if exposed and recognized, one will incur much blame and anger.

The perfected people of old borrowed the Way of benevolence and lodged in righteousness, roaming the wilds of carefree ease, feeding in the fields of simplicity, and standing in the gardens of non-transaction. Carefree ease is non-action; simplicity is easy sustenance; non-transaction is no expenditure. The ancients called this “roaming in the harvest of the real.” Those who take wealth as right cannot yield emolument; those who take prominence as right cannot yield fame.

Those who cling to power cannot share authority with others—grasping it brings trembling, relinquishing it brings sorrow, and they see nothing clearly, only gazing endlessly at what they cannot cease pursuing. These are the people punished by Heaven. Seeing nothing of the supreme truth of the real, they only peer ceaselessly at their own unending desires—these are the people punished by Heaven.

Resentment, gratitude, taking, giving, admonition, instruction, life, and death—these eight are the instruments of rectification. Only those who follow great transformation without obstruction can wield them. Thus it is said: "Rectification is rectitude." If one’s heart does not accept this, the Gate of Heaven remains closed. *Great transformation* means the great process of change, that is, Heaven. These eight are the tools for rectifying the world, but only by following the natural patterns of Heaven can one use them rightly: resent when resentment is due, show gratitude when gratitude is due, take when taking is due, give when giving is due, admonish when admonition is due, instruct when instruction is due, kill when killing is due, and spare when sparing is due. Only those who do not obscure these principles can wield them. Thus it is said: "Rectification is rectitude." If even a trace of self-interest intrudes, it is no longer rectitude. When the world’s people reject this principle and insist on imposing their private will, they remain utterly blind to the patterns of Heaven—like the Gate of Heaven never opening.

Confucius visited Lao Dan and spoke of benevolence and righteousness. Lao Dan said: "When chaff gets in your eyes, heaven and earth and the four directions seem to shift places. When mosquitoes and horseflies bite your skin, you cannot sleep all night. But benevolence and righteousness are far more grievous—they agitate the heart, and no disorder is greater. You would have the world not lose its simplicity, yet you yourself follow the wind and move, gathering virtue to stand tall. Why must you act so strenuously, like one carrying a drum in search of a lost child? Displaying lost righteousness to others is like striking a drum to find a missing child.

The egret does not bathe daily to stay white, nor the crow blacken itself daily to stay black. Black and white are their natural qualities, not worth debating. The spectacle of fame and reputation is not worth expanding. When a spring dries up, fish gather on dry land, puffing moisture at each other, moistening each other with foam—better to forget each other in rivers and lakes."

Confucius visited Lao Dan and returned, silent for three days. His disciples asked: "Master, when you saw Lao Dan, what correction did you seek?" Confucius said: "Only now have I seen the dragon. The dragon coalesces into ritual, disperses into pattern, rides the clouds and qi, and nourishes itself in yin and yang. My mouth gaped open, unable to close—what correction could I offer Lao Dan?"

Zigong said: "Then are there truly people who dwell like corpses yet manifest like dragons, thunder in voice yet plumb the depths in silence, moving like heaven and earth? Might I witness such a one?" He then spoke of Confucius’ reputation to Lao Dan. Lao Dan was sitting in the hall and responded faintly: "My years advance and depart—what warning do you bring me?"

Zigong said: "The governance of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors differed, yet their renown was the same. Why do you alone consider them not sages?"

Lao Dan said: "Young man, come closer. Why do you say they differed?" Zigong replied: "Yao passed the throne to Shun, Shun to Yu. Yu relied on labor, Tang on force; King Wen submitted to Zhou without daring to oppose, while King Wu opposed Zhou without submitting. Thus I say they differed."

Lao Dan said: "Young man, come closer, and I will tell you of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors’ governance of the world. The Yellow Emperor’s rule made the people’s hearts unified—when someone’s parent died and they did not weep, none criticized them. Yao’s rule made the people’s hearts affectionate—when someone shortened mourning for a parent, none criticized them. Shun’s rule made the people’s hearts competitive—pregnant women gave birth at ten months, children spoke at five months, and before they could even crawl, people began to distinguish themselves. Yu’s rule made the people’s hearts change—each had their own intentions, and weapons had their own justifications. Killing bandits was not considered killing people. Each made their own kind, and the world was thus divided. The world was greatly alarmed, and the Confucians and Mohists arose. Their beginnings had order, yet now even women speak of it—what can be said?

When someone shortened mourning for a parent, they reduced the five degrees of mourning from the highest. *People began to distinguish themselves* means that when the time came, it was natural; to do so prematurely was to impose human will on Heaven. *Weapons had their justifications* means that warfare became a matter of course. *Killing bandits was not considered killing* means that killing bandits was not yet killing—only killing in wars over land and cities was called killing. *Each made their own kind* means that originally, all under Heaven were one undifferentiated whole; now they divided into kinds, and this was called the world. *Their beginnings had order, yet now even women speak of it* means that the institution of marriage rites was meant to establish human order, yet now when women marry, the proper human relations are corrupted.

I tell you: the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors’ governance of the world was called *governance*, yet no disorder was greater. The institutions of the Three Sovereigns obscured the brightness of sun and moon above, estranged the essence of mountains and rivers below, and disrupted the operations of the four seasons in between. Their wisdom was as cruel as the sting of a scorpion’s tail or a venomous snake’s fang. None could preserve the naturalness of their lives, yet they still called themselves sages—is this not shameful? Truly, it is shameless."

Zigong stood uneasily, shifting his feet. Confucius said to Lao Dan: "I have studied the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, *Music*, *Changes*, and *Spring and Autumn*—the Six Classics—for a long time, and thought I thoroughly understood their principles. I have sought audience with seventy-two rulers, expounding the Way of the former kings and clarifying the traces of the Zhou and Shao, yet not one ruler took up my words."

"The application—how extreme it is! Is it truly so difficult to persuade rulers, or is the Dao so hard to illuminate?"

Lao Dan said, "Fortunate indeed that you have not encountered a ruler of a well-ordered age. The Six Classics are but the worn traces of the former kings. Traces are what the footwear leaves behind, yet are traces the footwear itself? When white egrets gaze at one another, their pupils do not move, yet the wind of transformation stirs; when the male cries upon the upwind, the female responds from downwind, and the wind of transformation stirs. Each becomes its own male or female, thus the wind of transformation arises. Nature cannot be altered, fate cannot be changed, time cannot be halted, and the Dao cannot be obstructed. If one attains the Dao, there is nothing that cannot be; if one loses it, there is nothing that can be."

Before three months had passed, Confucius sought audience again, saying, "I have grasped it! Crows and magpies nurture their young, fish coat their eggs with foam, and wasps transform grubs—each follows its own transformation. Yet when a younger brother is born, the elder weeps."

"For too long, I have not acted as others act. If I do not transform with others, how can I transform them?"

Lao Dan said, "Good. You have grasped it. Crows and magpies raising their chicks is the constant principle of things. Fish coating their eggs with foam need not be like crows and magpies. As for wasps carrying grubs and transforming them, this is yet again not like fish. Observing their transformations, which is real? Which is false? Yet when humans bear children, they cling desperately to kinship, unaware that love grows distorted with age and rank. Thus, an elder brother cannot find peace when a younger brother is born—even creatures are not so foolish. That single word 'weeps' is deeply poignant. One who does not transform with others does not follow their change. If I do not follow their transformation, how can I expect others to follow mine? How then can I transform them?"

**Outer Chapters: Autumn Floods**

When the autumn floods came, the hundred rivers poured into the Yellow River. The surging currents swelled so vast that between its banks and isles, oxen and horses could not be distinguished. The turbid flow obscured all—so distant were the shores that sight failed.

Then the River Lord rejoiced, exulting that all the world’s beauty was his alone. Following the current eastward, he reached the Northern Sea. Gazing east, he saw no end to the waters. Thereupon, the River Lord turned his face in dismay, looked toward Ruo with a sigh, and said:

"There is a rustic saying: *'Hearing a hundred truths, one thinks none surpasses oneself.'* That is I. I once heard men belittle Confucius’ learning and mock Boyi’s righteousness, and I did not believe them. Now that I behold the boundlessness of your realm, had I not come to your gate, I would have been in peril—forever ridiculed by those of great understanding."

The Lord of the Northern Sea replied: "A frog in a well cannot speak of the sea, for it is confined by its hollow. A summer insect cannot speak of ice, for it is bound by its season. A narrow scholar cannot speak of the Dao, for he is shackled by his teachings. Now that you have emerged from your banks and beheld the great sea, you know your own insignificance—only then can you be spoken with of great principles.

Of all the waters under heaven, none is greater than the sea. The ten thousand rivers flow into it, yet it never fills, though none knows when they cease. The Tail Pit drains it, yet it never empties, though none knows when it ends. Unchanged by spring or autumn, unmoved by flood or drought, its measure surpasses the flow of the Yangtze and Yellow River beyond all reckoning. Yet I have never prided myself on this, for I compare my form to heaven and earth, and receive my qi from yin and yang. I stand between heaven and earth like a pebble or twig upon a great mountain. Aware of my own smallness, how could I boast?

Consider the Four Seas within heaven and earth—are they not like a crevice in a marsh? Consider the Central States within the seas—are they not like a grain of millet in a granary? We number things by the tens of thousands, yet humanity is but one among them. All the people of the Nine Provinces, where grain is grown and boats and carts traverse, are but one among them. Compared to the ten thousand things, is this not like the tip of a hair upon a horse’s body?

The Five Emperors’ succession, the Three Kings’ strife, the benevolent men’s worries, the devoted scholars’ toils—all are encompassed herein. Boyi renounced it to gain fame; Confucius discoursed on it to display his erudition. Their self-importance—was it not like your former pride in your waters?"

The River Lord said, "Then may I esteem heaven and earth as great and the tip of a hair as small?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said, "No. Things have no fixed measure, time no ceasing, divisions no constancy, beginnings and ends no permanence. Thus, the man of great wisdom observes near and far, so that the small does not seem few, nor the great many—for he knows measure is boundless. He verifies past and present, so that the distant does not weary him, nor the near make him strain—for he knows time has no end. He examines fullness and emptiness, so that gain does not elate him, nor loss grieve him—for he knows divisions have no constancy. He understands the level path, so that life does not delight him, nor death afflict him—for he knows beginnings and ends have no permanence.

What a man knows is less than what he does not know; the time of his life is less than the time before his birth."

Seeking to exhaust the realm of the utmost greatness through that which is most minute—this is why one becomes confused and cannot attain self-realization.

From this perspective, how can one know that the tip of a hair suffices to determine the boundary of the most subtle, or that heaven and earth suffice to exhaust the realm of the utmost greatness?

The Lord of the River said, "All the debaters of the world say that the most refined has no form, and the greatest cannot be encompassed. Is this truly so?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea replied, "One who views the great from the perspective of the small cannot see it in full; one who views the small from the perspective of the great cannot discern it clearly. The refined is the subtlest of the small; the vast is the fullness of the great—thus they differ in their advantages. This is the nature of their conditions.

The refined and the coarse pertain to what has form; what has no form cannot be divided by numbers; what cannot be encompassed cannot be exhausted by numbers. What can be discussed in words is the coarse aspect of things; what can be grasped by intention is the refined aspect of things. What cannot be discussed in words, nor grasped by intention, does not pertain to the refined or the coarse.

Thus, the conduct of the great man: he does not act to harm others, nor does he boast of benevolence and kindness; he does not move for the sake of profit, nor does he despise menial servants; he does not contend over wealth and goods, nor does he pride himself on yielding; in affairs, he does not rely on others, nor does he boast of living by his own strength, nor does he despise the greedy and corrupt. His conduct differs from the common, yet he does not pride himself on being eccentric; his actions follow the multitude, yet he does not despise flatterers and sycophants. The world’s ranks and emoluments are insufficient to encourage him; disgrace and punishment are insufficient to shame him. He knows that right and wrong cannot be fixed as divisions, nor can the subtle and the great be determined as boundaries.

It is said: The man of the Dao does not strive for fame; the utmost virtue does not seek attainment; the great man has no self. This is the utmost of restrained destiny.

The conduct of the great man fundamentally does not harm others, nor does he consider benevolence and kindness as something to boast of. Fundamentally, he does not act for profit, nor does he despise menial servants—those who chase after gain. Fundamentally, he does not contend over wealth and goods, nor does he pride himself on his ability to yield. Fundamentally, in his affairs, he does not rely on others, nor does he consider living by his own strength as something to boast of. Nor does he despise the greedy and corrupt—those who do not hold to themselves but seek from others. His conduct fundamentally differs from worldly customs, yet he does not pride himself on being eccentric. His actions lie in following the multitude, and he does not despise flatterers and sycophants. He does not live by others’ strength—the following three phrases share a single meaning. His conduct differs from the common—the following four phrases share a single meaning. The utmost of restrained destiny is the extreme of restraint in one’s allotted portion, to the point of being indivisible and unassignable.

*Ni* refers to the tip of a hair. Yet even this has its order, like the bearing of heaven and earth—soundless and scentless, this is the utmost.

The Lord of the River said, "Beyond things, within things—where do distinctions of noble and base arise? Where do distinctions of small and great arise?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea replied, "Viewed through the Dao, things have no noble or base. Viewed through things themselves, each esteems itself and deems others base. Viewed through custom, noble and base do not lie within oneself.

Viewed through differentiation: if one magnifies what is great in each, then nothing in the world is not great; if one diminishes what is small in each, then nothing in the world is not small. Knowing that heaven and earth are but a grain of millet, and that the tip of a hair is a mountain—then the distinctions of measurement become apparent.

Viewed through function: if one affirms what each possesses, then nothing in the world does not possess; if one negates what each lacks, then nothing in the world does not lack. Knowing that east and west are opposites yet cannot be without each other—then the divisions of function are settled.

Viewed through inclination: if one affirms what each affirms, then nothing in the world is not affirmed; if one negates what each negates, then nothing in the world is not negated. Knowing that Yao and Jie each affirmed their own ways and negated the other—then the inclinations of conduct become apparent.

In ancient times, Yao and Shun yielded the throne and became emperors; Zhi and Kuai yielded the throne and perished. Tang and Wu fought and became kings; the Duke of Bai fought and was destroyed. From this, we see that the rites of contention and yielding, the conduct of Yao and Jie, noble and base have their times—these cannot be taken as constant.

A beam and a pillar are the same, yet one can batter down a city wall while the other cannot plug a hole—this speaks of differing implements. The fine steeds Qi Ji and Hua Liu can gallop a thousand *li* in a day, yet they cannot catch mice as well as a wildcat—this speaks of differing skills. The owl can seize fleas at night and discern the tip of a hair, yet in daylight it stares and cannot see a mountain—this speaks of differing natures.

Thus it is said: Should one take 'this' as the standard and reject 'not-this,' or take order as the standard and reject disorder? This is to fail to understand the principles of heaven and earth and the conditions of the myriad things. It is like taking heaven as the standard and rejecting earth, or taking yin as the standard and rejecting yang—its impossibility is clear. Yet if one still insists on speaking without ceasing, one is either foolish or deceitful.

The abdications of emperors and kings differed; the successions of the Three Dynasties differed. Those who go against their times and defy customs are called usurpers; those who accord with their times and follow customs are called righteous men.

Silent, O Lord of the River—how could you know the gate of noble and base, the house of small and great?"

The Lord of the River said, "Then what should I do? What should I not do? How should I determine what to accept or reject, what to pursue or abandon? What am I to do in the end?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "From the perspective of the Dao, what is noble and what is base? This is called 'reversed extension.' If one clings to rigid intentions, one greatly obstructs the Dao. What is few and what is many? This is called 'reciprocal bestowal.' If one acts with singular focus, one deviates from the Dao.

Majestic as a state with its ruler—it grants no private virtue. Reverent as a sacrifice with its altar—it confers no private blessing. Boundless as the four directions without end—it has no boundaries. Embracing the myriad things, who bears them up? This is called 'without fixed direction.'

The myriad things are equal; what is short and what is long? The Dao has no end or beginning; things have death and life, yet do not rely on their completion. Now empty, now full, they do not abide in their forms. Years cannot be lifted; time cannot be halted. Ebb and flow, fullness and emptiness—when one ends, another begins. This is how we speak of the great principles and discuss the patterns of the myriad things.

Things come into being as if galloping, as if racing. No movement without change, no moment without shift. What is to be done? What is not to be done? They will naturally transform of themselves.

'Extension' means to draw out and lengthen. From the perspective of the Dao, there is fundamentally no nobility or baseness. If one takes nobility and baseness as opposites and extends them, one knows not how many degrees they span. From the standpoint of dukes and ministers, dukes are noble and ministers base; from the standpoint of ministers and grand masters, ministers are noble and grand masters base. Thus it is said: without nobility, without baseness—this is called 'reversed extension.' If one clings to one’s intentions, insisting that this is noble and that is base, one obstructs the Dao.

'Reciprocal bestowal' is repayment. Those who repay others’ bestowals must calculate what is more and what is less. If one acts with singular focus, determining what is more and what is less to repay, one deviates from the Dao.

'Reverent' here is akin to Mencius’s 'contented.' 'Embracing the myriad things, who bears them up?' speaks of loving all things without perceiving the merit of their support.

The Lord of the River said: "Then what is noble about the Dao?"
The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "Those who know the Dao must comprehend its principles; those who comprehend its principles must understand its adaptability; those who understand its adaptability do not allow things to harm themselves.

The utmost in virtue—fire cannot burn them, water cannot drown them, cold and heat cannot harm them, birds and beasts cannot injure them. This does not mean they slight these things, but that they discern safety and danger, remain tranquil amid fortune and misfortune, are cautious in approach and retreat, and thus nothing can harm them. Thus it is said: Heaven is within, man is without; virtue resides in Heaven.

Know that human conduct is rooted in Heaven, positioned in attainment, and moves in accord with the turning of hooves—bending and stretching, returning to the essential and speaking of the ultimate."

The Lord of the River said: "What is Heaven? What is man?"
The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "Oxen and horses having four legs is called Heaven; bridling a horse’s head, piercing an ox’s nose is called man. Thus it is said: Do not let man obliterate Heaven, do not let the artificial obliterate fate, do not let gain be sacrificed for reputation. Guard this carefully and do not lose it—this is called returning to one’s true nature.

Kui pitied the centipede, the centipede pitied the snake, the snake pitied the wind, the wind pitied the eye, the eye pitied the mind.

Kui said to the centipede: 'I leap and stride with my single foot—nothing compares to me. Now you move with ten thousand feet—how do you manage?'
The centipede said: 'Not so. Have you not seen one who spits?'"

The spray scatters—some droplets large as pearls, some fine as mist, falling in countless profusion. Now I move by my Heavenly mechanism, yet know not how it comes to be.

The centipede said to the snake: "I walk with many feet, yet cannot match your footless stride—why is this?"
The snake replied: "How can one alter what the Heavenly mechanism sets in motion? What need have I for feet?"

The snake said to the wind: "I move by flexing my spine and ribs—there is something to it. Yet you rise in a great rush from the Northern Sea, rush into the Southern Sea, and seem as if you were nothing at all—why is this?"
The wind said: "Indeed. I rise in a great rush from the Northern Sea and enter the Southern Sea, yet a finger can overpower me, a toe can trample me. I, too, am overpowered by myself. Nevertheless, to snap great trees and topple mighty halls—only I can do this. Thus, to turn many small defeats into a great victory is the greatest victory of all. Only the sage can achieve this."

Confucius was traveling in Kuang when men of Song surrounded him in several rings, yet he continued playing his zither and singing without pause. Zilu entered and said: "Why is the Master so at ease?"
Confucius replied: "Come, let me tell you. I have long sought to avoid adversity, yet cannot escape it—this is fate. I have long sought success, yet cannot attain it—this is timing. In the age of Yao and Shun, there were no men in adversity, not because of their wisdom; in the age of Jie and Zhou, there were no men of success, not because of their folly: the circumstances of the times made it so. The fisherman who travels the waters without fear of dragons and serpents shows courage; the hunter who travels the land without fear of rhinoceroses and tigers shows courage; but to know that adversity is a matter of fate, that success comes with timing, and to face great peril without fear—this is the courage of the sage. You, Zilu, take your place. My fate is governed by something beyond me."

Not long after, armored soldiers advanced and declared: "We mistook you for Yang Hu, hence the siege. Now that we see our error, we beg leave to withdraw."

Gongsun Long questioned Wei Mou, saying: "In my youth, I studied the Way of the former kings; in maturity, I mastered the practice of benevolence and righteousness. I harmonized sameness and difference, separated hardness and whiteness; affirmed the untrue, negated the possible—baffling the wisdom of the hundred schools, exhausting the eloquence of all disputants. I took myself for one of perfect understanding. Yet now I hear the words of Zhuangzi, and feel only bewildered estrangement. Do I fall short in debate? Or do I simply lack his depth of insight? Now my beak hangs open—I dare ask: what is the method?"

Prince Mou leaned against his desk, sighed deeply, looked to the heavens, and laughed: "Have you never heard of the frog in the shallow well? It said to the turtle of the Eastern Sea: 'How joyful I am! I leap and prance upon the well’s railing, rest upon the broken bricks of its walls. When I plunge into the water, it supports my armpits and cradles my chin; when I trample the mud, it swallows my feet and ankles. I turn to look at the shrimp, crabs, and tadpoles—none can match me. Moreover, to command the waters of this single ravine and straddle the joys of this shallow well—what more could one desire? Why not come and see for yourself?'

The turtle of the Eastern Sea had not yet set its left foot inside when its right knee was already pinned. It retreated in hesitation and told the sea: 'A distance of a thousand *li* cannot convey its vastness; a height of a thousand *ren* cannot plumb its depths. In the time of Yu, nine years out of ten saw floods, yet the waters did not increase; in the time of Tang, seven years out of eight saw drought, yet the shores did not diminish. Unchanged by the passage of moments or ages, unmoved by the ebb and flow of quantity—this is the great joy of the Eastern Sea.'

When the frog in the shallow well heard this, it was struck dumb with astonishment, its gaze wandering in dismay, utterly lost. Now, if you cannot even discern the bounds of right and wrong, yet still wish to examine Zhuangzi’s words, it is like making a mosquito bear a mountain or a millipede race across a river—surely beyond your strength. And if you grasp not the subtleties of his discourse, yet seek only momentary advantage, are you not the frog in the shallow well?

Moreover, he treads the Yellow Springs and ascends the Great Vast, unbound by south or north, released in all four directions, submerged in the unfathomable; unbound by east or west, beginning in the dark and obscure, returning to the Great Thoroughfare. Yet you would measure him with narrow scrutiny, seek him with argument—is this not like gazing at the sky through a tube or pointing at the earth with an awl? Is it not petty? Begone!

Have you not heard of the young man from Shouling who went to Handan to learn its walk? Before mastering the state’s skill, he forgot his old gait and could only crawl home. If you do not leave now, you will forget your former ways and lose your craft."

Gongsun Long’s mouth gaped open, his tongue lifted and would not descend; he fled in haste.

Zhuangzi was fishing in the Pu River when the King of Chu sent two ministers to approach him first, saying: "I wish to trouble you with the affairs of my realm."

Zhuangzi held his rod and did not turn, saying: "I have heard that in Chu there is a sacred tortoise, dead for three thousand years. The king keeps it wrapped in silk and stored in a casket in his ancestral temple. Would this tortoise rather have died to leave its bones venerated, or lived to drag its tail through the mud?"

The two ministers said: "It would rather live and drag its tail through the mud."

Zhuangzi said: "Go away. I will drag my tail through the mud."

When Huizi was prime minister of Liang, Zhuangzi went to visit him. Someone told Huizi: "Zhuangzi is coming to replace you as minister." Huizi grew alarmed and searched the state for three days and three nights.

Zhuangzi went to see him and said: "In the south there is a bird called the *yuanchu*—do you know it? The *yuanchu* sets out from the Southern Sea and flies to the Northern Sea; it alights only on the *wutong* tree, eats only bamboo fruit, and drinks only sweet spring water. Then a *chi* hawk, clutching a rotten rat, saw the *yuanchu* pass overhead and looked up, crying: '*Ha!*' Now do you mean to frighten me with your state of Liang?"

Zhuangzi and Huizi strolled upon the bridge over the Hao River. Zhuangzi said: "The *tiao* fish swim leisurely—this is the joy of fish."

Huizi said: "You are not a fish—how do you know their joy?"

Zhuangzi said: "You are not me—how do you know I do not know the joy of fish?"

Huizi said: "I am not you, so of course I do not know you; but you are certainly not a fish, so your ignorance of the fish’s joy is complete."

Zhuangzi said: "Let us return to the beginning. When you said, 'How do you know the joy of fish?' you already knew I knew it, yet you asked me. I know it from here, above the Hao."

Huizi said: "As you put it, I am not you, so of course I do not know you. But you are certainly not a fish, so your ignorance of the fish’s joy is complete. That you and I are both human is evident. You and the fish are different kinds—your complete lack of mutual understanding is clear."

Zhuangzi said: "Let us return to the beginning. Originally, we spoke only of the joy of fish, but then came the debate over knowledge. Now, let us follow the original thread. When you said, 'How do you know the joy of fish?' you already knew I knew it, yet you asked me deliberately. Now, as for how I know the joy of fish—I know it from here, above the Hao. The fish advance in the river, and I wander above it. Our joys are linked, an unplanned harmony—like bathing in the Yi or dancing in the wind at the *wuyu*."

**Outer Chapters: Supreme Joy**

Is there in the world a supreme joy, or is there none? Are there means to preserve life, or are there none? What should one do now, and what rely upon? What avoid, and where abide? What pursue, and what forsake? What find joy in, and what detest?

What the world honors are wealth, nobility, long life, and goodness; what it delights in are bodily ease, rich flavors, fine garments, beautiful sights, and sweet sounds. What it despises are poverty, lowliness, early death, and wickedness; what it finds bitter are the body denied comfort, the mouth denied rich flavors, the form denied fine garments, the eyes denied beautiful sights, and the ears denied sweet sounds. If these are not attained, one is gripped by great sorrow and fear—how foolishly this treats the body!

The wealthy exhaust themselves with toil, amassing wealth they cannot fully use—how estranged this is from the body. The noble pass days and nights in ceaseless calculation, weighing good and ill—how distant this is from the body. As for human life, it is born with sorrow. The long-lived grow dull and muddled, enduring long sorrow without death—what agony this is! How far removed from the body!

The steadfast man is praised by the world for his goodness, yet it is not enough to preserve his life. Do I know whether goodness is truly good? Or truly not good? If we deem it good, it is not enough to preserve life; if we deem it not good, it is enough to preserve others. It is said: "When loyal remonstrance is unheeded, crouch low and follow—do not contend." Thus when Wu Zixu contended, his body was destroyed; had he not contended, his name would not have endured. Is there truly goodness, or is there none?

As for what the vulgar do and delight in, do I know whether their joy is truly joy? Or truly not joy? I observe that what the vulgar delight in is the clamor of the crowd, rushing together in a clanging throng as if they cannot help themselves. Yet all call it joy—do I find it joyful? Or do I not? I do not know whether it is truly joy or not. Is there truly joy, or is there none?

I take non-action as true joy, yet this is what the vulgar find most bitter. Thus it is said: "Supreme joy is no joy; supreme praise is no praise." The right and wrong of the world cannot truly be settled. Nevertheless, non-action can settle right and wrong. Supreme joy preserves life—only non-action comes close to this.

Let me try to speak of it: Heaven is clear through non-action; Earth is tranquil through non-action. Thus when the two non-actions combine, the myriad things are transformed. Vague and indistinct, from whence do they emerge? Indistinct and vague, without form do they appear? The ten thousand things, teeming and thriving, all grow from non-action. Thus it is said: "Heaven and Earth act through non-action, yet there is nothing they do not do." Who among men can attain non-action?

When Zhuangzi’s wife died, Huizi came to offer condolences. Zhuangzi was squatting with legs spread, drumming on a basin and singing. Huizi said: "To live with someone, raise a child to adulthood, and grow old together—if upon her death you do not weep, it would be enough. But to drum on a basin and sing—is this not excessive?"

Zhuangzi said: "Not so. When she first died, how could I alone not feel grief? Yet I examined her beginning and found she was originally without life. Not only without life, but originally without form; not only without form, but originally without *qi*. Amidst the vague and indistinct, there was transformation, and *qi* arose; the *qi* transformed and form arose; form transformed and life arose. Now it has transformed again into death. This is like the procession of the four seasons—spring, autumn, winter, summer. She now lies peacefully in the great chamber, and if I were to wail and weep after her, I would consider myself ignorant of fate. Thus I stopped."

Zhilishu and Gujieshu were observing the mound of Mingbo, the ruins of Kunlun, where the Yellow Emperor once dwelled. Suddenly, a willow sprouted from Gujieshu’s left elbow, and he was startled and dismayed. Zhilishu said: "Do you detest it?"

Gujieshu replied: "No—why should I detest it? Life is but a temporary borrowing. What borrows and gives rise to life is but dust and grime. Death and life are like day and night. Moreover, you and I observe transformation, and transformation has reached me—why should I detest it?"

Mingbo is a term for the dead, like the "Lord of the Dark" in the *Wen Xuan*. The mound of Mingbo is a burial ground, located in the ruins of Kunlun. Kunlun has five cities and twelve towers, the abode of immortals. The Yellow Emperor was an ancient ruler who attained the Dao and ascended as an immortal. These words express sorrowful reminiscence: this man dwells in the realm of immortals yet cannot, like the Yellow Emperor, cultivate immortality and thus faces death.

The willow refers to the funeral bier. In the *Tan Gong*, the Zhou people placed *sha* on the walls. The commentary states: "The wall is the *liu* covering." The *Zhengyi* says: "The wall that screens the bier is like a wall screening a house, hence the screening object is called a wall. The wall is the *liu*." The *Feng Ren* commentary: "*Liu* means gathering—the gathering of all adornments." Zhilishu and Gujieshu were observing among the mounds and ruins of the burial ground when, by the power of their thoughts, a willow for screening the bier suddenly sprouted from Gujieshu’s left elbow. An ominous sign—he was about to die. Thus he was startled and dismayed.

Zhilishu, though dismayed, asked Gujieshu: "Do you detest it?" Gujieshu replied: "No—why should I detest it? What has life is but a temporary borrowing. This is what is meant by the four great elements provisionally combining. Since it is but a borrowing that gives rise to life, it is no more than dust and grime gathering together."

"Why cling or struggle? Thus he spoke: life and death are but day and night—this is the constant principle of the Dao, not worth alarm or dread. Life is but a temporary borrowing, and in this discourse on human life and death, the character for 'life' (*sheng*) does not again adhere to the earlier instance—here, it refers to the 'growth' (*sheng*) on his left elbow. Moreover, you and I observe transformation, and transformation has now reached us. Transformation means death.

Mencius said: *'Among those who face transformation, let no soil cling to the skin.'* This means that you and I came to the burial grounds to observe others' deaths, yet witnessed this ominous sign—death was about to reach us. If all men must die, then I too must die. Why should I detest it?"

Some mistakenly take *liu* (柳) to mean the willow tree, but this misinterprets the passage’s meaning.

When Zhuangzi traveled to Chu, he saw an empty skull, bleached and still retaining form. He struck it with his horsewhip and questioned it: "Did you, sir, covet life and lose the Way, bringing this upon yourself? Or did you suffer the fall of a state or the execution of the axe? Or did you commit some shameful deed, bringing disgrace upon your parents, wife, and children? Or did you endure the miseries of cold and hunger? Or did your years simply come to this end?"

Having finished speaking, he took the skull as a pillow and lay down. At midnight, the skull appeared in a dream and said: "Your words resemble those of a sophist. All you have spoken of are the burdens of the living—death knows none of these. Would you hear the truth of death?"

Zhuangzi said: "Yes."

The skull replied: "In death, there is no ruler above nor minister below, nor the affairs of the four seasons. At ease, one takes heaven and earth as spring and autumn. Even the joy of a king facing south cannot surpass it."

Zhuangzi, unconvinced, said: "If I had the Controller of Fate restore your form, giving you bones, flesh, and skin, and returning you to your parents, wife, children, neighbors, and acquaintances—would you desire this?"

The skull frowned deeply and said: "How could I abandon the joy of a king facing south to return to the toils of the human world?"

When Yan Hui traveled east to Qi, Confucius wore a troubled expression. Zigong rose from his mat and asked: "May I dare ask why, when Yan Hui went east to Qi, you bear such a worried look?"

Confucius replied: "Excellent question. Long ago, Guanzi spoke words I deeply admired: *'A small sack cannot hold a large object; a short rope cannot draw from the deep.'* Such is the way of fate—it has its completion, and form has its proper place. Neither can be diminished or augmented. I fear that Yan Hui will speak to the Marquis of Qi of the Ways of Yao, Shun, and the Yellow Emperor, and further cite the words of Suiren and Shennong. The marquis will search within himself and find nothing, and finding nothing, he will be perplexed. Perplexity in a man leads to death.

Have you not heard the tale? Once, a seabird alighted in the outskirts of Lu. The Lord of Lu received it with ceremony, feasted it in the ancestral temple, performed the *Nine Shao* as music, and prepared a grand sacrifice as its meal. The bird, however, grew dazed and sorrowful, dared not eat a single morsel, nor drink a single cup, and died after three days. This was nourishing the bird as one nourishes oneself, not as a bird ought to be nourished. To nourish a bird as a bird, one should let it roost in deep forests, roam open plains, float upon rivers and lakes, feed on minnows and eels, follow its flock in flight, and rest at ease. It detests the clamor of human speech—why subject it to such clamor?

When the music of *Xianchi* and *Nine Shao* was played in the wilds of Dongting, birds heard it and flew away, beasts heard it and fled, fish heard it and dove deep, while men, upon hearing it, gathered to listen. Fish live in water and thrive, yet men drown in it. Their natures differ, and so do their likes and dislikes. Thus the sages of old did not impose a single standard of ability nor demand uniformity in affairs. Names were confined to reality, and principles were set according to what was fitting—this is called 'orderly coherence and sustained good fortune.'"

Liezi was traveling when he stopped to eat by the roadside. From the *Liezi*: The character *cong* (从) below has *zhe* (者), and below *zhi* (指) has *gu* (顾). He said to his disciples, "Bai Feng..."

Upon seeing a century-old skull, he plucked a stalk of mugwort and pointed at it, saying: "Only you and I know that you have never died, nor have I ever been born. Are you truly nourished? Am I truly joyful? Seeds contain *qi*—when they encounter water, they become *jue* (絶); at the border of water and soil, they become the raiment of frogs and clams. Born on mounds, they become *lingxi* (陵舄); when *lingxi* finds damp shade, it becomes *wuzu* (乌足); the root of *wuzu* becomes *qicao* (蛴螬), while its leaves become butterflies. Butterflies, in turn, transform into insects, born beneath the hearth, their form like a cast-off skin, named *quduo* (鸲掇). After a thousand days, *quduo* becomes a bird called *ganyugu* (乾余骨). The foam of *ganyugu* becomes *simi* (斯弥), and *simi* becomes *shixi* (食酰). *Yilu* (颐辂) arises from *shixi*, *huangkuang* (黄軦) from *jiuyou* (九猷), *maorui* (瞀芮) from rotten grubs, *yangxi* (羊奚) from withered bamboo, old bamboo gives birth to *qingning* (青宁), *qingning* to *cheng* (程), *cheng* to horses, horses to humans—and humans, in turn, return to the *mechanism*. All things emerge from the *mechanism* and return to the *mechanism*. In life, they depend on nourishment; in death, they bear no burden—yet still, we fear. If you have never died and I have never been born, how can we know that you are not nourished or that I am not joyful?

(For the passage beginning "Seeds contain *qi*," the meaning is not entirely clear; here, only what can be understood is excerpted. *Jue* (□) is the ancient form of *jue* (絶). When earth reaches water, it is cut off—yet even there, things are born. At the intersection of water and soil, they become the raiment of frogs and clams. The raiment of frogs and clams is *shuixi* (水舄), the aquatic variety—what the *Book of Songs* calls "gathering the *xu* (藚)," referring to frogs and clams, which use it as their covering. Born on mounds, it becomes *lingxi* (陵舄), what the *Book of Songs* calls *fuyi* (芣苡), commonly known as *cheqian* grass. The same thing takes different forms in water and on land. *Shixi* (食酰) is the midge. *Qingning* (青宁) is the bamboo-root grub. An old man once said he saw a worm over five inches long, with another inch or so still attached—was this not the bamboo-root grub before its transformation, the so-called *qingning*? While staying in Anxiang, I saw a white moth fly into a lamp, and suddenly, one after another appeared behind it—not from birth, but from empty transformation. I also saw in Dongting Lake a mountain called Mingshan, its peak crowned with Yu’s Temple and many households. Every late spring, *quchun* (□鹑) swarm the temple and homes, swept up with bamboo brooms and pickled into vinegar. Merchants pay in advance, and at harvest, some remain half-transformed, still resembling mice—this is what the *Monthly Ordinances* calls "field mice turning into *ru* (鴽)." Observing this, how can one doubt the boundless transformations of heaven and earth simply because they lie beyond our senses?

Horses give birth to humans, and humans return to the *mechanism*—how is this different from the Buddhist doctrine of reincarnation? Yet the Buddhist account is rigid: if one did evil in a past life, one may be reborn as a dog or horse, and only after exhausting one’s karma does one return to human form. What evidence supports this? Zhuangzi’s version is more fluid: all things emerge from the *mechanism* and return to the *mechanism*. The *mechanism* is where *qi* moves. To emerge from it is birth; to return to it is death. Between heaven and earth, there is only *yin* and *yang qi*, which generate the myriad things. In death, *yang qi* returns to heaven and *yin qi* to earth—yet this *qi* never leaves the realm of heaven and earth. Tomorrow, it will generate things anew, just as before, for it is merely *yin* and *yang qi* at work. One must not mistake a dead horse for a living person, or a dead person for a living horse.

From *Master Zhu’s Recorded Sayings*: Zhang Hengqu said, "When form dissolves, it returns to the origin to become human again. The thing we receive at birth, upon death, returns to the great origin. Then, from within, it is drawn out anew—this is most evident in human birth. If we followed the Buddhist account, heaven and earth would have to contain countless separate clusters of *qi*, each cycling through birth and death, entangled without end—how could this align with the workings of creation?"

From the *Brush Notes*: The people of Yanzhou still call tigers and leopards *cheng* (程), meaning "worm." *The Authentic Scripture of Southern Florescence*, following the original text of Chapter Seventeen (the character *zhu* (诸) was emended to *shi* (视) in common editions).

**From the Eighteenth Scroll of the *Authentic Scripture of Southern Florescence*, Following the Original Text**
*(Recorded by Lu Ling’s Bamboo Peak Scholar Luo Miandao; collated by his disciple Peng Xiang)*

**Outer Chapters: "Attaining Life"**

One who attains the essence of life does not strive for what is not essential to life.
*"Not essential"*—as in the *Analects*: "There is no need for this."
This speaks of such uselessness.

One who attains the essence of fate does not strive for what knowledge cannot alter.

To nourish the form, one must first attend to external things—yet there are cases where things are abundant while the form remains unnourished.
Life necessarily begins with form inseparable—yet there are cases where form remains intact while life perishes.
Life’s coming cannot be refused; its going cannot be halted.
How lamentable!

The world believes that nourishing the form suffices to preserve life—yet if nourishing the form truly does not suffice to preserve life, then what in this world is worth doing?
Though it may not suffice, one cannot but do it—such is the inescapable burden.
One cannot escape having form.

To escape the burden of form, nothing surpasses abandoning the world.
Abandoning the world means freedom from entanglements; freedom from entanglements means true equilibrium; true equilibrium means alternating renewal with that which gives life.
*That*—the Creator.
The Creator gives me life, which must end in death—yet if I can preserve this life, I alternate renewal with the Creator.
In such alternation lies the subtlest essence.

Why abandon affairs? Why forsake life?
Abandoning affairs spares the form from labor; forsaking life preserves the essence from depletion.
When form is whole and essence restored, one becomes one with Heaven.
Heaven and Earth are the parents of the myriad things:
United, they form a body; dispersed, they become a beginning.
When form and essence remain undiminished, this is called *shifting*.
Essence refined to its utmost returns to assist Heaven.
*Heaven*—here rhyming with *tian*.
*Shifting*—referring to the "alternation" above.
This means one can alternate with it.

Life originally possesses this essence, yet worldly affairs nearly exhaust it.
Now, by alternating renewal, one regains this essence—what gave me this life was Heaven.
If I, in turn, can preserve this life with Heaven, this is *assisting Heaven*.

Zi Liezi asked Guan Yin: "The perfected person walks underwater without obstruction, treads fire without heat, moves above the myriad things without fear. How does one reach such a state?"
Guan Yin replied: "This is the preservation of pure *qi*, not the realm of cunning, skill, or boldness. Sit, and I will tell you:
All things with appearance, form, sound, or color are merely *things*—how can one thing surpass another?
What suffices to transcend them? Only *formlessness*.
Formlessness is the origin of creation and the end of transformation.
One who attains this and exhausts it—how can mere things constrain them?
They dwell within the measure of restraint, conceal themselves in the traceless principle, and roam through the beginning and end of the myriad things.
Unifying their nature, nurturing their *qi*, harmonizing their virtue, they commune with the source of all things.
Such a one’s Heavenly endowment remains whole, their spirit unbroken—how could things intrude?

The drunken man who falls from a cart, though swiftly, does not die.
His bones and joints are the same as others’, yet the harm he suffers differs—his spirit remains whole.
He neither knows he is riding nor knows he has fallen; life and death, terror and dread do not enter his breast.
Thus, though he collides with things, he is not afraid.
If one preserved by wine can be thus, how much more one preserved by Heaven?
The sage hides within Heaven—thus nothing can harm them.

The avenger does not break the sword’s edge;
Even one with a vengeful heart does not resent a fallen tile—thus the world attains balance.
Therefore, there are no wars of conquest, no punishments of slaughter—this is the Way’s doing.
Do not open the Heavenly in man, but open the Heavenly of Heaven.
Opening Heaven’s Heaven gives birth to virtue; opening man’s Heaven harms life.
Do not reject one’s Heavenly nature, nor neglect the human—then the people approach their true essence."

Confucius traveled to Chu and, passing through a forest, saw a hunchbacked man catching cicadas as if plucking them.
Confucius said: "You are skillful—do you have a method?"
The man replied: "I do. After five or six months of stacking pellets without dropping them, I lose only a fraction; with three pellets, I lose one in ten; with five, it is as if plucking them.
When I steady my body, it is like a withered stump; when I extend my arm, it is like a dead branch.
Though Heaven and Earth are vast and the myriad things many, I am aware only of the cicada’s wings.
I do not turn aside, nor do I let the myriad things distract me from the cicada’s wings—how could I fail?"

Confucius turned to his disciples and said: "Where intent is undivided, it approaches the divine."
*(The Shu edition reads "approaches" (*yi*), and below it says: "The vessel’s divinity lies in its concentration (*ning*)—is this not the hunchbacked elder’s meaning?")*

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "Once, when I crossed the deep rapids of Shang, the ferryman handled the boat as if by magic. I asked him: ‘Can one learn to handle a boat?’ He said: ‘Yes. A skilled swimmer can learn quickly. As for those who dive and excel in the depths—even if they’ve never seen a boat, they can handle it immediately.’ I asked him, but he did not explain. May I ask what this means?"
Confucius said: "A skilled swimmer can learn quickly because they have forgotten the water. As for those who dive and excel in the depths—even if they’ve never seen a boat, they can handle it immediately because to them, deep pools are like hills, and a capsized boat is like a cart rolling backward.
Ten thousand dangers may lie before them, yet none enter their dwelling—where could they not be at ease?
One who stakes tiles in a game plays skillfully; one who stakes hooks hesitates; one who stakes gold grows confused.
Their skill is the same, yet when they fixate on the stake, they value externals.
All who value externals are clumsy within.
*(Here, the text shifts from the heart to the finger.)*"

Field Kai met Duke Wei of Zhou, who said: "I have heard that Zhu Shen studied the art of life-nurturing. You, sir, have traveled with Zhu Shen—what have you learned from him?"

Field Kai replied: "I merely wield broom and dustpan to tend the gates and courtyards. What could I have learned from the Master?"

Duke Wei said: "Do not decline, Master Tian. I wish to hear it."

Kai said: "I heard the Master say: 'One who nurtures life well is like a shepherd herding sheep—he watches those lagging behind and whips them.'"

Duke Wei asked: "What does this mean?"

Field Kai said: "In Lu there was Shan Bao, who dwelled in the cliffs and drank from streams, sharing no profit with the people. At seventy, his complexion remained like a child’s. Unluckily, he met a starving tiger, which killed and devoured him. There was also Zhang Yi, who frequented every high gate and thin curtain, running wherever he was called. At forty, he died of internal heat-illness. Bao nurtured his inner self, yet the tiger consumed his outer; Yi nurtured his outer self, yet illness attacked his inner. These two men both failed to whip the laggards.

When sheep have already moved forward, they need no whip. Only those falling behind require it—this illustrates that one who has already nurtured the inner need not labor further within, but should tend the outer; one who has already nurtured the outer need not labor further without, but should tend the inner.

Confucius said: 'Do not withdraw to hide, do not emerge to display, stand like firewood in the center.' If these three are attained, one’s name will surely reach its peak. 'Stand like firewood' means to stand like withered wood. 'Stand like firewood in the center' means neither to emerge nor withdraw.

Those who fear the roads—if one in ten is killed, fathers and sons, brothers and kin will warn one another, mustering armed retinues before daring to venture out. Is this not wisdom? Yet what people truly should fear lies in their bedding and at their meals, yet they fail to guard against it—this is their error.

The sacrificial officer, clad in dark robes, approaches the pen to address the pig, saying: 'Why do you hate death? I will fatten you for three months, purify you for ten days, fast for three, spread white couch-grass, and place your shoulder and rump upon the carved platter—would you accept this?' For the pig’s sake, he would say: 'Better to eat chaff and糟 and be left in the pen.' For his own sake, he would accept a life of noble carriages and caps, and a death upon painted shields and among heaped boughs. For the pig, he rejects it; for himself, he accepts it—how does he differ from the pig?

Painted shields bear the design of pheasant feathers. Heaped boughs are woven mats for gathering things.

Duke Huan was hunting in Fa Marsh, with Guan Zhong driving his chariot, when he saw a ghost. The Duke grasped Guan Zhong’s hand and asked: 'What do you see, Father Zhong?'

He replied: 'Your servant sees nothing.'"

Duke Huan returned, choked with rage and fell ill, repeatedly refusing to leave his chamber. Among the Qi scholars was one named Huangzi Gao’ao, who said: "The Duke has harmed himself; how could a ghost harm him? When the qi of pent-up fury scatters and fails to return, it leads to deficiency. If it rises but does not descend, it makes one prone to anger. If it sinks but does not ascend, it makes one prone to forgetfulness. If it departs the center and lodges in the heart, it causes illness."

Duke Huan asked: "Then do ghosts exist?"
He replied: "They do. In the depths lurks *Lü*. The hearth harbors *Ji*. In the dusty corners within doors, *Leiting* dwells. Beneath the northeast lies *Bei’e*, where *Xielong* and *Longyue* leap. Beneath the northwest, *Yiyang* resides. In waters abides *Wangxiang*; in mounds, *Shanxin*; in mountains, *Kui*; in the wilds, *Panghuang*; in marshes, *Weiyi*."

The Duke asked: "What is the form of *Weiyi* like?"
Huangzi said: "*Weiyi* is as large as a wheel hub, as long as a chariot shaft, clad in purple robes and a vermilion cap. As a creature, it is fearsome—when it hears the rumble of thunder-chariots, it clasps its head and stands upright. Whoever sees it is near to attaining hegemony."

Duke Huan burst into laughter and said: "This is what I saw!" Thereupon, he straightened his robes and cap, sat with Huangzi, and before the day was out, his illness had vanished.

---

Jingshengzi reared fighting cocks for the king. After ten days, the king asked: "Are the cocks ready?"
He replied: "Not yet. They are still hollow and arrogant, relying on their qi."
After another ten days, the king asked again.
He replied: "Not yet. They still react to shadows and echoes."
After another ten days, the king asked again.
He replied: "Not yet. They still glare fiercely and bristle with qi."
After another ten days, the king asked again.
He said: "Nearly so. Though other cocks may crow, these remain unchanged—from a distance, they seem like wooden cocks. Their virtue is complete. No other cock dares respond; they turn and flee."

Confucius observed the Lü Liang falls, where the water plunged from a height of thirty *ren*, its rapids surging for forty *li*, a place where even soft-shelled turtles, alligators, fish, and turtles could not swim. He saw a man swimming there and thought he must be in distress, seeking death. He sent his disciples to follow the current and rescue him. After several hundred paces, the man emerged, his hair unbound, walking and singing as he strolled beneath the embankment. Confucius followed and questioned him, saying: "I took you for a ghost, but upon closer inspection, you are a man. May I ask if there is a Dao to treading water?"

The man replied: "I have no Dao. I began with what was familiar, grew with my nature, and completed it through fate. I enter with the swirl and emerge with the surge, following the water’s Way without imposing my own will. This is how I tread it."

Confucius said: "What do you mean by beginning with what was familiar, growing with your nature, and completing it through fate?"

The man replied: "I was born on the hills and am at ease on the hills—this is what is familiar. I grew up in the water and am at ease in the water—this is my nature. I do not know why I am as I am, yet I am so—this is fate."

*Qi* (齐) is read as *navel*; as in the *Zuozhuan*, where "噬齐" is also written as "齐," referring to the swirling water’s entry point resembling a navel, while *gǔ* (汩) is where the water surges forth.

Ziqing carved wood into a *jù*, and when it was finished, those who saw it marveled as if it were the work of ghosts or spirits. The Lord of Lu saw it and asked him: "What technique did you use to create this?"

The craftsman replied: "I am but a workman—what technique could I possess? Yet there is one thing: when I set out to carve a *jù*, I never dare to waste my *qi*. I must fast in purity and still my mind. After three days of fasting, I no longer dare entertain thoughts of rewards, ranks, or emoluments. After five days, I no longer dare entertain thoughts of praise or blame, skill or clumsiness. After seven days, I suddenly forget I have limbs or a body. At that moment, there is no court or state. My skill becomes whole, and all external distractions dissolve. Only then do I enter the mountain forests to observe the natural forms of trees, waiting until the perfect *jù* appears before me—only then do I set my hand to it. Otherwise, I stop. Thus, I unite Heaven with Heaven. This is why the finished vessel seems the work of spirits."

*A jù* is the frame that holds bells and drums.

Dongye Ji presented his charioteering to Duke Zhuang. He advanced and retreated in perfect alignment with the marking line, turned left and right in perfect arcs as if drawn by a compass. The Duke believed no painted pattern could surpass it. He ordered Ji to drive a hundred intricate loops and return. Yan He encountered him and entered to say: "Ji’s horses will fail." The Duke kept this private, not wishing to make it known.

The master craftsman Chui could turn his hand to match compass and square, yet it was not by calculation. Soon after, the chariot indeed failed and returned. The Duke asked, "How did you know?" He replied: "His horses’ strength was exhausted, yet he still demanded more—thus I said they would fail."

To turn the hand and naturally match compass and square means to transform with things without the mind’s interference. Thus, his spirit-terrace remains unified and unshackled, forgetting the comfort of feet and sandals, the fit of waistband and sash, the rightness of knowledge and judgment, the mind’s ease. Neither inwardly altered nor outwardly swayed, he meets events with perfect accord. From the first step of ease, he never departs from ease—this is the ease of forgetting ease.

There was a man named Sun Xiu who came to Master Bian Qingzi’s door and exclaimed: "Though I dwell in my village, none call me unrefined; though I face peril, none call me cowardly. Yet when I till the fields, the years do not favor me; when I serve a lord, the age does not favor me. Cast out by my village, driven from my district—what offense have I given Heaven? Why has fate treated me thus?" Master Bian replied: "Have you not heard of the Perfect Man’s way of conduct? He forgets his liver and gall, leaves behind his ears and eyes, wanders in boundless vagueness beyond the dust and grime, roams freely in the work of no-occupation—this is called acting without reliance, leading without domination. Now you adorn your knowledge to startle the foolish, cultivate your person to expose others’ flaws, and stride forth as if bearing the sun and moon. You have preserved your bodily form intact, kept all nine orifices whole, and avoided midway afflictions like deafness, blindness, lameness, or deformity to be counted among men—you are already fortunate. Why then do you have leisure to complain of Heaven’s injustice? Begone!"

Sun Xiu departed, and Master Bian entered.

After a while, he sat gazing upward and sighed. A disciple asked: "Master, why do you sigh?"

Master Bian replied: "Just now, Sun Xiu came, and I spoke to him of the Perfect Man’s virtue. I fear he was startled and may fall into confusion."
