The disciple said: "Not so. If Sun Xiu’s words were right and yours wrong, he would not be confused by what is right. If Sun Xiu’s words were wrong and yours right, he was already confused when he came—what fault is that of yours?"

Master Bian said: "Not so. Once there was a bird that alighted in the outskirts of Lu. The Lord of Lu delighted in it, prepared a grand feast to entertain it, and performed the *Nine Shao* to please it. The bird grew anxious, sorrowful, and dizzy, and dared not eat or drink. This is called nourishing a bird with what nourishes oneself. As for nourishing a bird as a bird ought to be nourished, one should let it roost in deep forests, drift upon rivers and lakes, feed on whatever it encounters, and rest on level ground. Now Sun Xiu is a man of narrow understanding and scant knowledge. When I spoke to him of the Perfect Man’s virtue, it was like burdening a field mouse with chariots and horses, or delighting a sparrow with bells and drums—how could he not be startled?"*

*Kuan* means "small opening"; *qi* means "to open." *Kuanqi* describes one of limited vision.

*The Southern Florescence True Scripture, Following the Original Text, Chapter Eighteen, ends here.* #1 The character 是 was emended to 视 in accordance with the received text.

*The Southern Florescence True Scripture, Following the Original Text, Chapter Nineteen. Collated by Peng Xiang, disciple of Lu Lingzhufeng’s lineage under Luo Miandao.*

**Outer Chapters: The Mountain Tree**

When Master Zhuang was traveling through the mountains, he saw a great tree with luxuriant branches and leaves. A woodcutter stood beside it but did not fell it. Asking the reason, he was told, "It is of no use." Master Zhuang said, "This tree, by being useless, has lived out its natural span."

Emerging from the mountains, he lodged at the house of an old friend. The friend, delighted, ordered a servant to kill a goose and cook it. The servant asked, "One can caw, the other cannot— which should we kill?" The host replied, "Kill the one that cannot caw."

The next day, a disciple asked Master Zhuang, "Yesterday, the tree in the mountains lived out its natural span by being useless; today, the host’s goose died for being useless. Where will you, Master, take your stand?" Master Zhuang laughed and said, "I will stand between usefulness and uselessness. Yet between usefulness and uselessness—though it seems so—it is not so, and thus one is not free from entanglement. But if one rides the Dao and floats upon virtue, it is otherwise: neither praised nor blamed, now dragon, now serpent, transforming with the times, never clinging to one form. Rising and falling, taking harmony as measure, drifting upon the ancestor of the ten thousand things. Treating things as things yet not being treated as a thing by them—how then could one be entangled? This is the method of Shennong and the Yellow Emperor.

As for the nature of the ten thousand things and the transmission of human relations, it is not so. Union leads to separation, completion to ruin, sharpness to blunting, honor to slander, action to loss, worthiness to schemes, unworthiness to deceit. How can one be certain of anything? Alas! Remember this, disciples: it is only in the realm of Dao and virtue.

The worthy exhaust their minds scheming over affairs, while petty men turn to cunning and deceit.

Jiananyi of Shinan visited the Lord of Lu, who wore a troubled expression. Jiananyi asked, "My lord, why this troubled look?" The Lord of Lu replied, "I study the Way of the former kings, cultivate the undertakings of my predecessors; I revere ghosts and honor the worthy; I practice this without the slightest lapse. Yet I cannot escape misfortune—this is why I am troubled."

Jiananyi said, "My lord’s method of avoiding misfortune is shallow. The sleek fox and the patterned leopard dwell in the mountains and forests, crouch in caves and cliffs—this is stillness. They move by night and rest by day—this is vigilance. Though hungry and thirsty, in straitened circumstances, they still keep their distance upon the rivers and lakes while seeking food—this is steadfastness. Yet they cannot escape the calamity of nets and traps. What crime have they committed? Their skins bring disaster upon them. Is not the state of Lu your lordship’s skin? I wish you would carve away your form, shed your skin, wash your heart, cast off desire, and wander in the wilds where no man dwells."

*Xu* means "to assist"; *shu* means "to distance." Though hungry and thirsty, in straitened circumstances, they still keep their distance upon the rivers and lakes while seeking food—this means they remain in the mountains and forests, refusing to venture upon the rivers and lakes to seek food.

In the southern lands there is a city called the State of Establishing Virtue. Its people are simple and guileless, with few private interests and scant desires. They know how to work but not how to hoard; they give without seeking repayment. They do not know where duty leads, nor where ritual is headed.

The reckless and wayward tread the great square of the Dao. Their lives may be joyful, their deaths may be buried. I wish you would abandon your state, cast off custom, and walk in mutual support with the Way.

You say: "That Way is distant and perilous, with rivers and mountains in between. I have no boat or carriage—what then?"
Master South-of-Market replies: "Do not cling to form or pride, do not linger in one place, and take this as your carriage."

You say: "That Way is secluded and remote, with no one there—with whom shall I be neighbors? I have no provisions, no food—how can I reach it?"
Master South-of-Market replies: "Reduce your expenses, diminish your desires, and though you have no grain, you will find sufficiency. Cross the river and float upon the sea; gaze upon it and you will not see its shore. The farther you go, the less you will know its end. Those who see you off will turn back from the shore—from then on, you will be far away."

From the State of Establishing Virtue in the south to this point, later generations have interpolated these words, crude and shallow, surely not the authentic voice of Zhuangzi.

Thus, to possess others is a burden; to be possessed by others is a sorrow. Therefore, Yao neither possessed others nor was possessed by them. I wish to relieve you of your burdens, remove your sorrows, and wander alone with the Way in the boundless realm.

When a double-hulled boat crosses a river, an empty boat drifts into it—even a man of narrow heart would not grow angry. But if there were a man aboard, he would shout to steer it away. If the first shout goes unheard, he shouts again; if still unheard, he curses in anger. Before, he was not angry, but now he is—before, the boat was empty, but now it is full. If a man can empty himself and wander through the world, who can harm him?

When Beigong She levied taxes for Duke Ling of Wei to cast bells, he built an altar outside the outer gate of the capital. In three months, the upper and lower chimes were complete. Prince Qingji saw this and asked: "What method did you employ?"
She replied: "In the space of unity, I dared not impose any method. I have heard: after carving and polishing, return to simplicity. Vast and unknowing, drifting and hesitant, like plants growing wild and without intent—sending off the departing, welcoming the arriving. Let those who come not be barred, let those who go not be stopped. Follow the strong and unyielding, adapt to the crooked and compliant, and let them reach their own end. Thus, though taxes were levied day and night, not a hair was harmed—how much more so for those who tread the great path?"

The Way is one alone; if you act with purpose, you divide it into two. Thus, in the space of unity, I dared not impose any method.

Vast and unknowing, drifting and without intent, like plants growing wild—this is the appearance of having no mind. The strong and unyielding are those who do not follow; those who are crooked and compliant simply follow their nature. To "let them reach their own end" means to accept what comes of itself. "Not harmed" means not diminished. The "great path" is the great Way. If even tax collection can be so, how much more so governing the world by the great Way?

Confucius was besieged between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food. Taigong Ren went to console him, asking: "Are you near death?"
"Yes," Confucius replied.
"Do you hate death?"
"Yes."

Taigong Ren said: "Let me speak to you of the way of not dying. In the Eastern Sea there is a bird called *Yidai*. As a bird, it is slow and sluggish, appearing incapable. It flies only when supported, perches only when pressed. It does not dare to advance first or retreat last; it does not dare to eat before others, but must take the leftovers. Thus, it is never driven from the flock, and others never harm it—so it escapes misfortune. The straight tree is the first felled; the sweet well is the first drained. You, I fear, adorn your knowledge to startle the foolish, cultivate yourself to expose the corrupt, shining brightly like one who carries the sun and moon in procession—how could you escape harm?

Long ago, I heard from a man of great completion: 'Those who boast of themselves achieve no merit; those who achieve merit fall; those who achieve fame lose it. Who can relinquish merit and fame and return to the common people?' The Way flows but does not dwell in fame; it acts but does not claim a place. Pure and constant, it is like one who is mad, erasing traces and diminishing attachments, not seeking merit or fame. Thus, it demands nothing of others, and others demand nothing of it. The perfected person does not strive—why do you delight in this?"

Confucius said: "Excellent!" He then parted from his friends and disciples, fled to the great marsh, wore coarse cloth and furs, ate acorns and chestnuts, and entered among beasts without disturbing their herds, among birds without disrupting their flocks. Birds and beasts did not shun him—how much less would men?

Confucius asked Zisang Hu: "I was twice banished from Lu, had a tree felled in Song, was forced to erase my tracks in Wei, was impoverished in Shang and Zhou, and besieged between Chen and Cai. I have suffered these many misfortunes, my close friends growing more distant, my followers scattering—why is this?"

Zisang Hu replied: "Have you not heard of the escape of the man from Jia? Jia was a man who relied on others to flee. Lin Hui abandoned a jade disc worth a thousand gold pieces to carry a baby on his back and run. Someone asked: 'Was it for the sake of its value? The baby’s value is slight. Was it for the burden? The baby’s burden is great. Why abandon a jade disc worth a thousand gold pieces to carry a baby and run?' Lin Hui said: 'That was bound by profit; this is bound by heaven. Those bound by profit abandon each other in straitened circumstances, misfortune, and harm; those bound by heaven gather together in straitened circumstances, misfortune, and harm. The difference between gathering and abandoning is vast. Moreover, the friendship of a gentleman is as bland as water; the friendship of a petty man is as sweet as wine. The gentleman’s blandness brings closeness; the petty man’s sweetness brings estrangement. Those who come together without cause will part without cause.'"

Confucius said: "I respectfully receive your instruction." He then walked slowly and leisurely home, abandoned his studies, discarded his books, and his disciples no longer vied for his attention before him—yet their love for him grew deeper.

Another day, Zisang Hu said: "When Shun was about to die, he earnestly warned Yu: 'Be vigilant!'"

Shape should follow its natural course; emotions should follow their true nature. Following the natural course prevents separation; following true nature prevents exhaustion. Without separation or exhaustion, one does not seek adornment to match form. Thus, one does not depend on external things.

Zhenling Yu is a person’s name, and "you" refers to Shun.

Zhuangzi wore coarse cloth patched with hemp, tied his shoes with rough cords, and passed by King Wei. The king said: "Why is the Master so weary?" Zhuangzi replied: "It is poverty, not weariness. A scholar who possesses the Dao and virtue yet cannot put them into practice—that is weariness. Worn-out clothes and tattered shoes are poverty, not weariness. This is what is called not encountering the right time. Has Your Majesty never seen the leaping monkeys? When they find themselves among nanmu, catalpa, and camphor trees, they grasp the vines and swing through the branches, reigning supreme—even Yi or Feng Meng could not take aim at them. Yet when they find themselves among thorn bushes, brambles, and jujube thickets, they move cautiously, glancing about in fear, trembling with dread. This is not because their sinews and bones have suddenly stiffened, but because their position is unfavorable, and they cannot fully display their abilities. Now, living amid a benighted ruler and chaotic ministers, how can one avoid weariness? This is like the fate of Bi Gan, whose heart was cut out as proof!"

*Zhengxi* means to straighten one’s sash.

Confucius was stranded between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food. Leaning on a withered tree, he struck its dry branches and sang the airs of the *Yan* clan. There was the form of the music but not its precise structure, the sound but not its proper notes. The sound of the wood and the human voice blended like a plow turning the soil—loose and free—yet it resonated with the hearts of others. Yan Hui stood with folded hands, turning his gaze to observe. Fearing that Yan Hui might overreach in his admiration or grow sorrowful out of affection, Confucius said: "Hui, it is easy to accept what heaven diminishes, but difficult to accept what others bestow. There is no beginning that is not also an end—humanity and heaven are one. Who is it that sings now?"

Yan Hui said: "May I ask why it is easy to accept what heaven diminishes?" Confucius replied: "Hunger, thirst, cold, and heat—when one is trapped and cannot move—are the workings of heaven and earth, the outflow of the movement of things. To speak of going along with them is to say one moves with them. As a minister, one does not dare to depart from this. If even the way of serving a ruler is like this, how much more so the way of awaiting heaven? Hunger, thirst, cold, and heat—when one is trapped and cannot move—are the flowing of heaven and earth’s *qi*, the movement and release of the myriad things, which cannot be stopped. To wish to avoid this, one would have to halt the *qi* of heaven and earth—one can only go along with it. A minister regards his ruler as heaven and earth; he must simply follow."

"What do you mean by it being difficult to accept what others bestow?" Confucius said: "At the outset, one’s path opens in all four directions, and ranks and emoluments arrive without end. What others find beneficial is not one’s own—one’s fate lies beyond such things. When one first gains employment, there are no obstacles. Ranks and emoluments may be grand and not without appeal, but one exhausts oneself in service only to benefit others—what does it have to do with oneself? One’s fate lies beyond such things. The gentleman does not steal; the worthy does not pilfer. What would I take from them? Thus it is said: No bird is wiser than the swallow. Where its eyes should not linger, it does not glance; even if its eggs fall, it abandons them and flees. Though it fears humans, it nests among them—only for the sake of the altars of soil and grain.

*The swallow* is the swallow. *Does not glance* means it has no time. *Eggs* refers to its brood. *Altars of soil and grain* refers to the spring and autumn sacrifices to the gods of soil and grain. The swallow does not linger where it should not nest in human dwellings; even if its eggs fall, it abandons them without a backward glance. The reason is its fear of humans. Its nesting among humans only occurs during the sacrifices to the altars of soil and grain. Swallows come at the spring sacrifice and depart at the autumn sacrifice. The *Spring and Autumn Annals* states: 'Without bells and drums, it is called *stealth*.' This is merely a passing remark, yet commentators have made it needlessly abstruse and laughable—there are many such cases.

"What do you mean by 'there is no beginning that is not also an end'?" Confucius said: "When the myriad things transform, one does not know who succeeds them—how can one know their end? How can one know their beginning? One simply aligns with them and awaits."

"What does it mean for humans and Heaven to be one?" Confucius said: "Where there is humanity, there is Heaven; where there is Heaven, there is also Heaven. That humans cannot possess Heaven is their nature. The sage, at ease, embodies the passing and comes to an end. In all matters beyond human capacity, whatever humans accomplish is Heaven. And what Heaven accomplishes is also Heaven. That humans cannot possess themselves is simply the spontaneity of Heavenly nature. Only the sage is one with Heaven—at ease, embodying the passing, calmly allowing body and self to pass away together."

Zhuang Zhou wandered near the fence of Carved Mound and saw an unusual magpie coming from the south. Its wings spanned seven feet in breadth, its eyes measured an inch in circumference. It brushed against Zhou’s forehead in flight, descended, and alighted in a chestnut grove. Zhuang Zhou said: "What bird is this? Its wings are vast yet it does not fly far; its eyes are large yet it does not see clearly." He tucked up his robe, strode forward, took up his sling, and waited. He saw a cicada enjoying the fine shade and forgetting its own form. A mantis, seizing the opportunity, struck at it, seeing only its gain and forgetting its own shape. The unusual magpie, drawn by profit, seized the chance, seeing only the advantage and forgetting its true nature. Zhuang Zhou, startled, said: "Alas! Things indeed entangle one another—two kinds summon each other." He cast aside his sling and turned to flee, but the gamekeeper pursued him with shouts and curses.

Zhuang Zhou returned home and for three months did not step into the courtyard. Lan Ju approached and asked: "Master, why have you been so withdrawn lately?" Zhuang Zhou replied: "I guarded my form and forgot my self; I gazed upon muddy waters and became lost in clear depths. Moreover, I have heard from my master: 'Enter their customs, follow their customs.' Now I wandered in Carved Mound and forgot my self; the unusual magpie brushed my forehead, and I wandered in the chestnut grove and forgot my true nature. The gamekeeper of the chestnut grove took me for a disgrace—this is why I have withdrawn."

Yang Zi traveled to Song and lodged at an inn. The innkeeper had two concubines—one beautiful, the other ugly. The ugly one was honored, while the beautiful one was slighted. Yang Zi asked the reason, and the innkeeper’s servant replied: "The beautiful one is self-aware of her beauty, so I do not recognize her beauty; the ugly one is self-aware of her ugliness, so I do not recognize her ugliness." Yang Zi said: "Disciples, take note: act with virtue and abandon the conduct of self-regard—wherever you go, will you not be loved? One should simply practice virtuous deeds—then how could one fail to be loved by others?"

*The Nanhua True Classic, Following the Original Text, Chapter Nineteen Concludes*
*The Nanhua True Classic, Following the Original Text, Chapter Twenty*

Lúlíng’s Bamboo Peak, recorded by Luó Miǎndào, collated by disciple Péng Xiáng

**External Chapters: Tián Zǐfāng**

Tián Zǐfāng attended upon Marquis Wén of Wèi and repeatedly praised Xī Gōng. The Marquis said: "Xī Gōng—is he your teacher?" Zǐfāng replied: "No. He is but a villager of Wúzé. His praises were so apt that Wúzé could not but speak of him." The Marquis said: "Then do you have no teacher?" Zǐfāng said: "I do." The Marquis asked: "Who is your teacher?" Zǐfāng said: "Dōngguō Shùnzǐ." The Marquis said: "Then why have you never spoken of him?" Zǐfāng said: "His way of being is true. His appearance is human, yet his nature is of Heaven; he follows things while preserving his authenticity; he is pure yet embraces all. Though he bears a human form, he accords with Heaven’s spontaneity; though empty, he follows things while guarding his truth; though clear, he contains all things. When beings lack the Dao, he meets them with upright bearing to awaken them, dissolving their intentions. When confronted with perversity, he simply rectifies what lies within himself, and their resistance melts away. How could Wúzé be worthy of speaking of him?"

Zǐfāng departed. The Marquis, lost in thought, remained silent the whole day. Summoning the ministers who stood before him, he said: "Far indeed is the gentleman of perfect virtue! At first, I thought the words of sagely wisdom and the conduct of benevolence and righteousness were the ultimate. But when I heard of Zǐfāng’s teacher, my form dissolved and I had no desire to move; my mouth was sealed and I had no desire to speak. What I have learned is but a clay figurine. Truly, Wèi has been a burden to me."

Wēnbó Xuězǐ traveled to Qí and lodged in Lǔ. A man of Lǔ requested an audience with him. Wēnbó Xuězǐ said: "I cannot. I have heard that the gentlemen of the Central States are enlightened in ritual and righteousness but coarse in understanding the human heart. I do not wish to meet him." When he arrived in Qí and returned to lodge in Lǔ, the same man again requested an audience. Wēnbó Xuězǐ said: "Before, he sought to see me; now, he seeks to see me again—he must have some inspiration to stir me." He went out to meet the guest, then returned and sighed. The next day, he met the guest again and returned, sighing once more. His servant said: "Every time you meet this guest, you return and sigh. Why?" He replied: "I told you before: the people of the Central States are enlightened in ritual and righteousness but coarse in understanding the human heart. The one who saw me earlier advanced and retreated like a compass or square, moved with the composure of a dragon or tiger. When he admonished me, it was like a son; when he guided me, it was like a father. That is why I sighed."

Confucius met him and did not speak. Zǐlù said: "Master, you have long wished to see Wēnbó Xuězǐ. Now that you have met him, you do not speak. Why?" Confucius said: "In such a man, the Dao is present at a glance—how could words contain it? The Dao is present the moment one’s gaze falls upon him; there is no need for careful examination."

Yán Yuān asked Confucius: "When the Master walks, I walk; when the Master hurries, I hurry; when the Master runs, I run; when the Master dashes away, leaving no trace of dust, I can only stare blankly behind." Confucius said: "Yuān, what do you mean?" He replied: "When the Master walks, I walk; when the Master hurries, I hurry; when the Master debates, I debate; when the Master runs, I run; when the Master speaks of the Dao, I speak of the Dao. Yet when you dash away, leaving no trace of dust, I can only stare blankly behind. For the Master speaks without needing to establish trust, unites without needing to form bonds, and though without instruments, the people gather before you—yet I do not know why."

Confucius said: "Alas! How can you not examine this? There is no greater sorrow than the death of the heart, and the death of the body is secondary to it. The sun rises in the east and sets in the westernmost reaches; all things follow its pattern. Those with eyes and feet depend on it to complete their tasks. When it rises, they exist; when it sets, they perish. All things are thus: some depend on it to die, some depend on it to live. Once I received this completed form, I did not change but awaited its end. I move in response to things, day and night without pause, not knowing where it will end. My form was completed as if by the steaming of qi, and even knowing fate, I cannot plan for what lies ahead. I have followed this path daily. My whole life, I have extended my arm to you, yet you have lost it—is this not sorrowful? I grieve for you, Yuān. You cling to what I cling to. He has already exhausted it, yet you seek it as if it still exists—this is like searching for something in an abandoned marketplace. What I wear, you forget; what you wear, I also forget. Yet why should you worry? Though you forget the old me, there is something in me that cannot be forgotten."

"Staring blankly behind" means gazing straight ahead yet unable to catch up. "Though without instruments, the people gather before you" means that without tools to stir the people, they dance before you of their own accord. Yán Yuān did not understand why, so Confucius said: "Alas! How can you not examine this?" The reason he did not understand was that he had fixed his heart upon death. Thus, he used the sun as a metaphor: all things follow the sun’s rising and setting in their work and rest, and their life and death likewise depend on it. Confucius merely moved in response to things, flowing with them without cease, accepting his completed form without discussing fate, simply proceeding day by day. "As if by steaming" means as if formed by the steaming of qi. My whole life, I have been with you as if I had no conduct apart from the others, yet you have taken what I showed you daily as unattainable. This is like never having clasped arms, only extending one arm before losing it—how could it not be sorrowful? What you spoke of was merely the timely, what you saw of me was only what could be seen. "Clinging" means seeing.

He refers to the Master as "that one." That one has already attained perfect purity and emptiness, yet you seek in him what is substantial—like searching for a horse in a market stall. A market stall is indeed a place where horses are sold, but how could it always have horses?

The *Book of Songs* says, "In the central courtyard, there is a cry." The annotation explains: "The central courtyard is the path in the middle of the courtyard, a place for selling horses, with a path to facilitate their entry and exit." The *Book of Han* records: "West of the Jianzhang Palace, there was a central courtyard spanning dozens of *li*." Yang Xiong’s *Preface to the ‘Feathered Hunt’* states: "Sweet dew drips in the courtyard, sweet springs flow in the central path." Ban Gu’s *Rhapsody on the Western Capital* says: "Before the central courtyard, the great pool is dredged."

"Péi-fú" means to admire and follow. I admire your words, just as you admire mine—both should be utterly forgotten. Yet I fear Yan Yuan worries he will never attain this. Still, why should you worry? Within forgetting, there remains something unforgotten. What is forgotten is attachment; what remains unforgotten is the utmost truth. Though you forget my old teachings, if you gain something of your own, the unforgotten will still abide.

Confucius visited Lao Dan, who had just washed his hair and was letting it dry, standing still as if not human. Confucius withdrew and waited to the side. After a while, he saw him and said: "Was I deluded just now? Was it truly so? A moment ago, your form seemed like withered wood, as if you had abandoned things and stood apart from men."

Lao Dan said: "I let my heart wander in the beginning of things."

Confucius asked: "What do you mean?"

Lao Dan replied: "My heart was blocked and could not know; my mouth was shut and could not speak." (The character *bì* here is read as in "to ward off evil," pronounced *bìyě*. The mouth was as if sealed and could not speak.) "Yet I have tried to discuss with you what is to come. What is to come is what will be. Let me speak of the beginning of what will be: The utmost yin is solemn and still; the utmost yang blazes and surges. Solemn stillness emerges from heaven; blazing surging arises from earth. The two intermingle, harmonize, and things are born. Some serve as their pattern, yet no form is seen. They ebb and flow, fill and empty, now dark, now bright, changing day by day, month by month, acting without visible achievement. Life has its sprouting; death has its return. Beginning and end turn back without end, yet none know where they cease. If not this, then what is their source?"

Confucius said: "May I ask about wandering in this?"

Lao Dan said: "To attain this is the utmost beauty and utmost joy. To attain the utmost beauty and wander in the utmost joy—this is called the Perfect Man."

Confucius said: "I wish to hear the method."

Lao Dan replied: "Herbivorous beasts do not shun swamps; aquatic creatures do not avoid water. Small changes occur without losing their great constancy. Joy, anger, sorrow, and happiness do not enter the breast. The world is where the myriad things are one. If you attain this oneness and become the same, then your four limbs and hundred parts will be but dust and grime, and life and death, beginning and end will be like day and night—nothing can disturb them. How much less could gain and loss, fortune and misfortune affect them? Those who discard servitude do so as if discarding mud and mire, knowing the body is nobler than servitude. Nobility lies within me and does not lose itself in change. Moreover, the myriad transformations have no end—what is there to trouble the heart? Those who have attained the Way understand this. Only those who have personally realized the Way can comprehend it."

Confucius said: "Your virtue matches heaven and earth, yet you still borrow the utmost words to cultivate your heart. Of the noble men of old, who could escape this?"

(Here, *shuō* is read as *tuō*, meaning "to escape.")

Lao Dan said: "Not so. Water, when it flows, does nothing yet naturally displays its ability. The Perfect Man, in regard to virtue, does not cultivate it, yet things cannot depart from him. Like heaven’s natural height, earth’s natural depth, the sun and moon’s natural brightness—what cultivation is needed?"

Confucius left and told Yan Hui: "In the Way, I am like a vinegar fly. Without the Master’s uncovering of my blindness, I would not have known the great completeness of heaven and earth."

Zhuangzi visited Duke Ai of Lu. The Duke said: "In Lu, there are many Confucians, but few who practice your methods."

Zhuangzi said: "Lu has few Confucians."

The Duke said: "The whole state of Lu wears Confucian garb—how can you say there are few?"

Zhuangzi said: "I have heard that Confucians wear round caps to know the seasons, square shoes to know the earth’s forms, and jade pendants that sway to decide matters when they arise. Those who possess the Way are not necessarily those who wear its garb; those who wear its garb do not necessarily know the Way. If you truly do not believe this, why not issue a decree throughout the state: 'Those who lack this Way yet wear its garb shall be put to death.'"

The Duke issued the decree for five days, and no one in Lu dared wear Confucian garb. Only one man, dressed as a Confucian, stood at the palace gate. The Duke summoned him and questioned him about state affairs. His answers were inexhaustible, shifting a thousand ways without end.

Zhuangzi said: "With the whole state of Lu, there is only one Confucian—can this be called many?"

Baili Xi did not let rank and salary enter his heart, so when he fed cattle, the cattle grew fat. This made Duke Mu of Qin forget his lowly status and entrust him with governance. The lord of Yu did not let life and death enter his heart, so he could move others.

The Lord of Song Yuan was about to have paintings made. Many scribes arrived, bowed and stood, licked their brushes and mixed ink—half remained outside. One scribe arrived late, strolled in unhurriedly, bowed but did not stand, and went to his quarters. The Lord sent someone to observe him. He had taken off his clothes and was sitting cross-legged, bare-chested. The Lord said: "He is the true painter."

King Wen observed at Zang and saw an old man fishing—but his fishing was no fishing. Though he fished, he did not fish. He did not hold his fishing for what could be caught but fished constantly.

King Wen wished to promote the old fisherman and entrust him with governance, yet feared his great ministers and elders would not accept it. He wished to let the matter rest, yet could not bear the thought of the people being left without a protector. Thus, at dawn, he gathered his ministers and said: "Last night, I dreamed of a worthy man—dark of complexion, with a beard, riding a piebald horse with one red hoof. He called himself Yu and took charge of governance at Zang. Might this not bring relief to the people?"

The ministers, startled, said: "It was your late father, the former lord."

King Wen said: "Then let us divine his will."

The ministers replied: "The command of the former lord requires no further confirmation. What need is there for divination?"

Thereupon, they welcomed the old fisherman of Zang and entrusted him with governance. No laws were altered, no new decrees issued. After three years, King Wen surveyed the realm and found: the ranks of scholars had dissolved their factions and scattered, high officials no longer sought to distinguish themselves, and the grain measures dared not enter the four borders. When the ranks of scholars dissolved their factions and scattered, it showed they valued unity; when high officials no longer sought distinction, it showed they shared a common purpose; when grain measures dared not enter the four borders, it showed the feudal lords harbored no divided loyalties.

King Wen then made him Grand Preceptor and, facing north, asked: "Can governance thus extend to all under Heaven?"

The old fisherman of Zang remained silent and gave no answer, then vaguely declined and fled by night, never to be heard of again.

To "fish with a hook" is to angle for what can be caught—namely, fish. To "fish constantly" is to cast the line habitually without intent to catch. It is not to hold one’s fishing for what can be caught but simply to fish without ceasing.

The "former lord" in King Wen’s dream was his father. "No new decrees issued" means he never issued a single command. "Dissolved their factions" means they abandoned their established positions and no longer sought to stand apart from others. This account follows the precedent of Lü Wang, yet appears as a separate matter.

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "Was King Wen still not content? Why did he need to dream?"

Confucius said: "Silence. Say no more. King Wen had already perfected it—what need is there for criticism? He merely followed the momentary impulse. That momentary impulse meant: 'I am worth a hundred of you, or three of you.'"

Lie Yukou shot for Bohun Wuren, drawing the bow to its fullest and placing a cup of water on his elbow. When he loosed the arrow, it met the previous one, and as one arrow flew, another took its place on the string. At that moment, he resembled a wooden figure.

Bohun Wuren said: "This is the shooting of one who shoots, not the shooting of one who does not shoot. Let us ascend a high mountain, tread on perilous rocks, and stand above an abyss of a hundred fathoms. Then you may shoot."

Thereupon, Bohun Wuren climbed the high mountain, trod on perilous rocks, and stood above the abyss. Turning his back, he retreated until two-thirds of his feet hung over the edge, then beckoned Lie Yukou forward. Lie Yukou prostrated himself, sweat streaming to his heels.

Bohun Wuren said: "The perfected man gazes up to the blue sky, plunges down to the Yellow Springs, roams to the eight limits, yet his spirit and qi remain unchanged. Now you tremble with fear in your eyes—you are in grave danger."

"Met the previous arrow" means the arrow flew and struck the one before it. "As one arrow flew, another took its place" means that as one arrow was loosed, the next was already on the string. "Turning his back, he retreated until two-thirds of his feet hung over the edge" means he faced the mountain with his back to the abyss, retreating until two-thirds of his feet extended beyond the edge.

Shan Wu asked Sunshu Ao: "You have thrice served as Prime Minister without pride, and thrice been dismissed without sorrow. At first, I doubted you, but now I see your nostrils flare with ease—what is the secret of your heart?"

Sunshu Ao said: "How do I surpass others? I accept what comes without refusal and let go what departs without hindrance. I regard gain and loss as no concern of mine, and thus remain free of sorrow. How do I surpass others? Moreover, how do I know whether it lies with them or with me? Whether it lies with them and vanishes from me, or with me and vanishes from them? I am still hesitating, still looking around—what leisure have I to consider whether men are noble or base?"

"Nostrils flare with ease" describes his calm breathing as he lies at rest.

Confucius heard of this and said: "The true men of old could not be swayed by the wise, seduced by the beautiful, robbed by thieves, or befriended by Fuxi or the Yellow Emperor. Life and death are great matters, yet they remained unchanged—how much more so titles and emoluments! Such men’s spirit threads through the great mountains without obstruction, enters the deepest springs without getting wet, dwells in lowliness without weariness, and fills Heaven and Earth. The more they give to others, the more they possess themselves."

"Without obstruction" means without hindrance.

The King of Chu sat with the Lord of Fan. After a while, the King’s attendants announced three times that Fan had perished.

The Lord of Fan said: "Fan’s perishing does not diminish my existence. If Fan’s perishing does not diminish my existence, then Chu’s existence does not ensure its own existence. From this perspective, Fan has never perished, and Chu has never existed."

*The Twenty-First Scroll of the Southern Florescence True Classic (following the original text) ends here.*

*External Chapters: "Knowledge Wanders North"*
*The Twenty-First Scroll of the Southern Florescence True Classic (following the original text)*

Lu Lian of Bamboo Peak in Luling, as recorded by his disciple Peng Xiang, collated:

*External Chapters: "Knowledge Wanders North"*

Knowledge wandered north to the Dark Waters, ascended Hidden Peak’s Returning Cloud Mound, and there encountered Non-Action’s Discourse.

Knowledge said to Non-Action’s Discourse: "I wish to ask you: What thoughts, what reflections attain the Dao? What abiding, what practice settle into the Dao? What following, what path obtain the Dao?"

Three questions, yet Non-Action’s Discourse gave no answer. Not that he refused to answer—he did not know how to answer.

Knowledge, receiving no response, returned south of the White Waters, ascended Fox Tower, and there beheld Mad Qu. Knowledge posed the same questions to Mad Qu.

Mad Qu said: "Alas! I know the answers and would tell you, yet mid-speech I forget what I wished to say."

Knowledge, still unanswered, returned to the Imperial Palace and questioned the Yellow Emperor.

The Yellow Emperor said: "Without thought, without reflection—only then does one attain the Dao. Without abiding, without practice—only then does one settle into the Dao. Without following, without path—only then does one obtain the Dao."

Knowledge asked the Yellow Emperor: "You and I know these answers—pointing to the Yellow Emperor—yet those two—pointing to Non-Action’s Discourse and Mad Qu—do not. Which of you is correct?"

The Yellow Emperor said: "Non-Action’s Discourse is truly right; Mad Qu approaches it; you and I remain far from it. For those who know do not speak; those who speak do not know. Thus the sage enacts the teaching of no-words. The Dao cannot be attained; virtue cannot be reached. Benevolence may be practiced, righteousness may be diminished, ritual is mutual pretense. Therefore it is said: Lose the Dao, then virtue follows; lose virtue, then benevolence follows; lose benevolence, then righteousness follows; lose righteousness, then ritual follows. Ritual is the flower of the Dao and the beginning of chaos. Therefore it is said: Those who practice the Dao daily diminish, diminishing further and further, until they reach non-action. Through non-action, nothing is left undone. Now that you have already become entangled with things, to return to the root is no easy task. Is it not only the great person who finds it easy?

Life is the companion of death; death is the beginning of life. Who knows their boundary? Human life is the gathering of qi. Gathered, it becomes life; scattered, it becomes death. If death and life are companions, what cause have I for sorrow? Thus the ten thousand things are one. What they deem wondrous is called spirit-like; what they despise is called foul decay. Yet foul decay transforms into spirit-like, and spirit-like transforms into foul decay. Therefore it is said: The world is pervaded by a single qi. Thus the sage values unity."

Knowledge said to the Yellow Emperor: "I asked Non-Action’s Discourse, and he did not respond—not that he refused me, but that he did not know how to respond. I asked Mad Qu, and mid-speech he wished to tell me yet did not—not that he refused me, but that mid-speech he forgot. Now I ask you, and you know the answers. Why, then, are you not close to the truth?"

The Yellow Emperor said: "He is truly right because he does not know. This one approaches it because he forgets. You and I remain far because we know."

Mad Qu heard this and deemed the Yellow Emperor’s words wise.

Heaven and Earth possess great beauty yet do not speak; the four seasons follow bright patterns yet do not debate; the ten thousand things have completed principles yet do not explain. The sage traces Heaven and Earth’s beauty and comprehends the ten thousand things’ principles. Thus the perfected person does nothing; the great sage does not act—this is called observing Heaven and Earth.

Now, that numinous clarity and utmost essence accompany the hundred transformations. Things die and live, square and round—none know their root. Yet Pien and Pian, though alike, sustain the ten thousand things, which have endured since antiquity. The Six Directions are vast, yet nothing lies beyond their bounds; an autumn hair is small, yet depends on them to form its body. The world knows no constancy, yet all things rise and fall without ceasing. Yin and yang, the four seasons, move in their proper order. Obscurely, they seem absent yet remain; effortlessly, they take no form yet are spirit-like. The ten thousand things nurture and transform, unaware—this is called the root. Through it, one may observe Heaven.

That numinous clarity and utmost essence reside in the human heart. Yet people cannot observe as the sages do, so Heaven grants them that numinous clarity and utmost essence to accompany the hundred transformations. Thus things die and live, square and round—none know their root, yet the departed are gone, fluttering away without return. The principles of the ten thousand things have always endured; people simply fail to perceive them.

The Dao has no inside or outside, no small or great. The world is bound within the ebb and flow of growth and decline, ever-renewed and unchanging. The four seasons’ order moves without cease. Those who can be obscurely absent yet present, effortlessly formless yet spirit-like, nurturing the ten thousand things without awareness—only they know the root and may observe Heaven.

Nie Que asked the Way of Clothed-in-Skins. Clothed-in-Skins said: "Straighten your form, unify your gaze—Heaven’s harmony will arrive. Collect your knowledge, unify your measures—spirit will come to dwell. Virtue will adorn you; the Dao will reside within you. Your eyes will be bright as a newborn calf’s, seeking no reason for things."

Before the words were finished, Nie Que fell asleep. Clothed-in-Skins rejoiced greatly, departed singing, and said: "His form like withered bones, his heart like dead ashes—truly he knows reality, not clinging to the past. Dim and obscure, dark and indistinct, without mind, he cannot be schemed with. What manner of person is he?"

Shun asked Cheng: "Can the Dao be possessed?"

Cheng said: "Your body is not your own possession. How could you possess the Dao?"

Shun said: "If my body is not my own, who possesses it?"

Cheng said: "It is Heaven and Earth’s entrusted form. Life is not your own—it is Heaven and Earth’s entrusted harmony. Nature and destiny are not your own—they are Heaven and Earth’s entrusted compliance. Children and grandchildren are not your own—they are Heaven and Earth’s entrusted molting. Thus, when you walk, you know not where you go; when you abide, you know not what you hold; when you eat, you know not the taste."

The boundaries of Heaven and Earth are the expanses of yang qi—how could they be possessed?

Confucius asked Lao Dan: "Today, with leisure and tranquility, I venture to inquire about the supreme Dao."

Lao Dan said: "Purify your mind through fasting, cleanse your spirit, and shatter your knowledge. The Dao is profound and hard to express. Let me speak of its outlines: radiance emerges from obscurity, order arises from formlessness, spirit is born of the Dao, and form originates from essence. All things beget one another through form. Thus, creatures with nine orifices are born from the womb, while those with eight emerge from eggs. Its coming leaves no trace; its going has no boundary. Without gate or chamber, it pervades all four directions in boundless vastness. Those who attain this have strong limbs, penetrating thoughts, keen ears, and sharp eyes. Their minds do not toil, yet they respond to things without fixed method. Heaven cannot but be high, Earth cannot but be broad, sun and moon cannot but move, and all things cannot but flourish—this is the Dao.

Moreover, grasping it does not ensure understanding; debating it does not ensure wisdom. The sage has already settled this. As for that which gains nothing when augmented and loses nothing when diminished, this is what the sage preserves. Deep and still, it is like the sea; towering and vast, it returns to its beginning at the end. It measures and sustains the myriad things without exhaustion—this is the noble one’s Dao, yet it lies beyond them. All things turn to it for sustenance, yet it is never exhausted. This is the Dao.

There exists a being within the Central Kingdom, neither yin nor yang, dwelling between Heaven and Earth. For now, it is human, but it will return to its origin. Viewed from its root, life is but the shadow of things. Though some live long and others die young, how great is the difference? It is but a moment’s tale—why debate the right and wrong of Yao and Jie? The sage has already determined that one need not grasp or debate it. The sage’s teaching admits no addition or subtraction. Deep and still, it is like the sea; towering and vast, it returns to its beginning at the end—these words praise the sage’s Dao. To measure and sustain the myriad things without exhaustion—this is the noble one’s Dao, yet it lies beyond them. Those who strive to measure and sustain all things are merely pursuing externals. All things turn to it for sustenance, yet it is never exhausted. Is this the Dao? It is not sufficient to be called the Dao."

One in the Central States transcends beyond yin and yang, dwelling between heaven and earth merely as a transient human, soon to return to their origin. That origin is the Great Void. Viewed from the Great Void, human life is but a fleeting shadow. As the *Record of Rites* annotates: "醷 is plum juice; 喑 means long-fermented." Though the juice ferments long, how much time can it truly hold? Those who live many days are called long-lived; those who live few, short-lived. Thus, all that世人 contend over is but a moment’s tale—why debate the right and wrong of Yao and Jie?

Fruits and gourds have their principles; though human relationships are complex, they serve to order ranks. The sage encounters them without resistance, passes them without clinging. Harmonizing and responding is virtue; coinciding and responding is the Dao. From this arise emperors and kings.

Though fruits and gourds are humble things, their branches, flowers, and fruits still follow principles. The relationships of ruler and minister, father and son, husband and wife, friends—though seemingly intricate—serve to order ranks. The sage dwells among these human relationships, meeting them without resistance, never abandoning them; passing them without clinging, never lingering over them. Harmonizing with them and responding is how virtue is enacted; coinciding with them and responding is how the Dao is enacted. The rise of emperors and kings is no different.

Human life between heaven and earth is like a white colt passing through a gap—gone in an instant. Bursting forth, nothing fails to emerge; flowing smoothly, nothing fails to return. Transformed, one is born; transformed again, one dies. Living things mourn this; humankind grieves it. Loosening the bow-case of heaven, casting off the bonds of fate—tumultuous, winding, the soul departs, and the body follows. Thus, the great return.

The formless taking form, form dissolving into the formless—this is what all people know, yet it is not what the perfected strive for. This is what the multitude debate. Those who attain it do not debate; those who debate do not attain it. Clear insight finds no encounter; disputation is not as good as silence. The Dao cannot be heard; hearing is not as good as blocking the ears. This is called the Great Attainment.

Tumultuous, winding—these describe the loosening of the bow-case and casting off of bonds. "The formless taking form" means arising from nothing; "form dissolving into the formless" means returning to nothing. *值* means "to encounter." The essence of the Great Dao is that clear insight cannot encounter it—thus, disputation is not as good as silence.

Dongguo Zi asked Zhuangzi: "Where does the so-called Dao reside?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is nowhere absent."
Dongguo Zi said: "Specify, and then it may be acceptable."
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the ants."
Dongguo Zi said: "Why so low?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the weeds."
Dongguo Zi said: "Why even lower?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the tiles."
Dongguo Zi said: "Why still lower?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in excrement and urine."
Dongguo Zi did not respond.
Zhuangzi said: "Your question fundamentally misses the mark. When Zheng and Huo asked the market inspector about weighing a pig by stepping on it, the lower they went, the clearer the condition became. *期* means to specify. *质* means the root. Your question was vague and did not reach the root, so my answer was thus. Zheng and Huo refer to the *Rites of Drinking and Archery*, where there are officers called *Sizheng* and *Sihuo*. The market inspector weighs pigs by stepping on them to gauge their weight. Though *Sizheng*, *Sihuo*, and the market inspector have different duties, they are all involved in the same affair. Stepping on the pig—from its upper body to its lower—each step reveals more about its fatness or leanness. The common saying that a pig’s thighs being even indicates its fatness is an example. The more one descends, the more evident the condition becomes—thus, the Dao may be observed. You must not insist on certainty; nothing escapes it.

The supreme Dao is like this; so too is the great discourse. *Zhou* (all-encompassing), *bian* (pervasive), and *xian* (complete)—these are different names for the same reality, their meaning one. Have you ever wandered together in the Palace of Nothingness, merging in discussion without end? Have you ever practiced non-action together? Serene and still, vast and clear, harmonious and at ease? My will is emptied—wherever I go, I do not know my destination; departing and returning, I do not know where I rest. I have come and gone yet do not know my end, roaming through the Gate of Vastness. Great wisdom enters and does not know its limit.

*莫* means "not so"; *必* means "certain." This aligns with the *Analects*: "No predisposition, no prejudice." The above states the Dao is nowhere absent, so here it says: you must neither insist nor deny—simply follow the natural course of things without resistance. This is what the supreme Dao is like; so too is the great discourse. *Zhou*, *bian*, and *xian*—though different names, their reality is one, encompassing all things. In this way, all is affirmed; otherwise, all is negated—thus, no contention arises.

*尝* means "to try." Have you ever wandered together in the Palace of Nothingness, merging in discussion without end? *寥* means "empty." "My will is emptied"—my mind is void. Though I may go somewhere, it is as if I do not go, and I do not know my destination. Though I depart and return, I do not know where I rest. Though I have come and gone, I do not know I have done so—thus, I do not know my end. *Feng hong* is the gate of emptiness. The *Heavenly Questions* asks: "Feng-yi’s image—how can it be known?" The *Huainanzi* says: "Before heaven and earth took form, feng-feng and yi-yi roamed." *Feng hong* means roaming through the gate of emptiness—great wisdom enters this gate and does not know its limit.

The one that gives rise to things has no boundary with things, yet things have boundaries—this is what is called the boundary of things. The boundary of what has no boundary is the boundary that is not a boundary. It is said that fullness and emptiness, growth and decline—what is full and empty is not full and empty, what grows and declines is not growth and decline, what is root and branch is not root and branch, what accumulates and disperses is not accumulation and dispersal.

The above further elaborates the meaning of not knowing its end or limit. *Wu wu* (the first *wu*): to enumerate and count things by name. *Suo wei* ("what is called") refers to what the multitude calls it. The second *wei* ("it is said") is the same as *bi* ("that"), also pointing to "that" which is called. To name and count things is inexhaustible, having no limit with them; what has a limit is merely what the multitude calls the boundary of things. In truth, it has no boundary, yet they call it a boundary—thus, it is the boundary of what has no boundary. What they call a boundary is, in truth, no boundary—thus, it is the boundary that is not a boundary.

Ji Hegan studied with the Divine Farmer under Old Dragon Ji. The Divine Farmer, leaning on his armrest, closed the door and dozed at midday. At noon, Ji Hegan pushed open the door and entered, saying: "Old Dragon is dead." The Divine Farmer, still leaning on his armrest, grasped his staff and rose, then suddenly let it fall with a clatter and laughed, saying: "Heaven knew my perversity, sloth, and arrogance—so pleased with myself—so it cast me aside and let him die. Alas! I have no one to inspire my wild words, and so he has died!"

Yan Gang, hearing of this, said: "Those who embody the Dao are what the noble ones of the world depend upon. Now, in the Dao, he had not even attained a ten-thousandth part of the tip of a hair, yet still knew to conceal his wild words before dying—how much more so for those who truly embody the Dao? What is seen has no form; what is heard has no sound. In human discourse, this is called the obscure and dark—thus, to discourse on the Dao is not the Dao."

*Pai hu*: to push open the door. *Pao ran*: the sound of the staff being let fall.

The term "Heaven" here refers to Old Dragon Ji. "I have no one to inspire my wild words, and so he has died" means that much of what I spoke was reckless and ignorant. While Old Dragon Ji lived, my questions could be examined and corrected. Now that he has died, I have no one to inspire my wild words, and so I too shall fade into oblivion and die.

"Now, in the Dao, he had not even attained a ten-thousandth part of the tip of a hair" refers to Shen Nong. "Concealing his wild words" means having nothing to inspire them.

"In human discourse, this is called the obscure and dark" means that those who discuss the Dao describe it as obscure and dark because it has no form and no sound.

Thereupon, Tai Qing asked Wu Qiong: "Do you know the Dao?"
Wu Qiong said: "I do not know."
He then asked Wu Wei, who said: "I know the Dao."
Tai Qing said: "In your knowing of the Dao, are there principles?"
Wu Wei said: "There are."
Tai Qing said: "What are these principles?"
Wu Wei said: "I know the Dao can be noble, can be base, can be bound, can be dispersed—these are the principles by which I know the Dao."

Tai Qing then asked Wu Shi: "If this is so, then between Wu Qiong’s not-knowing and Wu Wei’s knowing, which is right and which is wrong?"
Wu Shi said: "Not-knowing is profound; knowing is shallow. Not-knowing is internal; knowing is external."

Thereupon, Tai Qing sighed and said: "Is not-knowing then knowing? Is knowing then not-knowing? Who knows the knowing of not-knowing?"

Wu Shi said: "The Dao cannot be heard; what is heard is not the Dao. The Dao cannot be seen; what is seen is not the Dao. The Dao cannot be spoken; what is spoken is not the Dao. Do you know that which gives form to the formless? The Dao should not be named."

Wu Shi said: "One who answers when asked about the Dao does not know the Dao. Even the one who asks about the Dao has not heard the Dao. The Dao admits no questions; questions admit no answers. To ask what cannot be asked is to ask into the inscrutable. To answer what cannot be answered is to lack inwardness. Responding to the inscrutable with what lacks inwardness—such a one outwardly does not observe the cosmos, inwardly does not know the Great Beginning. Thus, he does not pass beyond Kunlun, nor roam the Great Void."

Guang Yao asked Wu You: "Sir, do you exist? Or do you not exist?"
Guang Yao, receiving no answer, gazed intently at Wu You’s appearance: it was empty and hollow. He looked at him all day but saw nothing; he listened but heard nothing; he reached out but grasped nothing.

Guang Yao said: "This is perfection! Who could attain this? You can be without being, but you have not yet attained non-being. How could one reach this state of non-existence?"

The master blacksmith of Da Ma, who forged belt hooks, was eighty years old yet never missed a hair’s breadth in his work. Da Ma asked: "Is your skill innate, or do you follow a Way?"

The smith replied: "Your servant has a discipline. At twenty, I took delight in forging belt hooks. I saw nothing else in the world—nothing but hooks claimed my attention. Thus, what I employ borrows from what I do not employ, and so I long attain its use. How much more so for one who employs nothing without employing it? What thing does not draw upon this?"

Da Ma was the Grand Marshal. The "belt-hook forger" was a smith who hammered clasps.

Ran Qiu asked Confucius: "Can one know what existed before Heaven and Earth?"
Confucius said: "Yes. The past is as the present."

Ran Qiu, unsatisfied, withdrew. The next day, he returned and said: "Yesterday, I asked whether one could know what existed before Heaven and Earth. You said, ‘Yes. The past is as the present.’ Yesterday, I understood clearly; today, I am confused. May I ask what this means?"

Confucius said: "Yesterday’s clarity came because your spirit first received it. Today’s confusion arises because you now seek it with what is not spirit. There is no past, no present, no beginning, no end. Could there be descendants before there were descendants?"

Ran Qiu had no reply. Confucius said: "Enough. No answer is needed. Do not use life to give life to death, nor use death to give death to life. Do life and death depend on something? All share a single body. Is there something that existed before Heaven and Earth? That which gives rise to things is not itself a thing. Things cannot emerge before that which gives rise to them—just as there is no thing without self. The sage’s love for others never ends, for it draws from this very principle.

The spirit is the luminous clarity of the human heart. Yesterday’s clarity came when the heart’s spirit first grasped it. Today’s confusion arises when this heart is suddenly obscured, and what is not spirit intrudes. Heaven and Earth have no past or present, no beginning or end. Your question about what existed before Heaven and Earth is like asking whether there could be descendants before there were descendants. ‘No answer is needed’ means Ran Qiu’s question does not require an answer—I have spoken for you.

Life arises of itself; it does not use life to give life to death. Death arises of itself; it does not use death to give death to life. Life and death do not depend on anything—they are simply one body in life, one body in death. How could there be something that existed before Heaven and Earth? That which bursts forth and is called ‘the giving rise to things’ is not itself a thing. Before birth, where was this body? Things cannot emerge before that which gives rise to them. It is as if something gives rise to them, and if something gives rise to them, then the Way of ceaseless arising has no end. The sage’s love for others never ends, for it draws from the principle of ceaseless arising."

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "I once heard you say, ‘Do not send off, do not welcome.’ May I ask about its wandering?"

Confucius said: "The ancients transformed outwardly but remained untransformed inwardly. People today transform inwardly but remain untransformed outwardly. One who transforms with things is one who does not transform. How can one be at peace with transformation or non-transformation? How can one be at peace and yet grind against them? One must inevitably be worn away until little remains.

The parks of Xi Wei, the gardens of the Yellow Emperor, the halls of Yu, the chambers of Tang and Wu—these were the domains of rulers. The followers of Confucians and Mohists took them as masters, and so they contended with right and wrong. How much more so for people today? The sage dwells among things without harming them. One who does not harm things cannot be harmed by them. Only one who harms nothing can send off and welcome others.

One who transforms outwardly but remains untransformed inwardly responds to things without the heart following them. One who transforms inwardly but remains untransformed outwardly has no settled heart and is buffeted by affairs. To transform with things is outward transformation. To remain untransformed is inward non-transformation. What is this ‘peace’? What is transformation? What is non-transformation? How can one be at peace and yet grind against them? One will inevitably be consumed until little remains.

From Xi Wei, the Yellow Emperor, Yu, Tang, and Wu down to the Confucians and Mohists, all contended with right and wrong, wearing each other away. How much more so for people today? Contention is more grinding than mere friction. Parks are narrower than gardens. Chambers are deeper than halls. The further one goes, the worse it becomes."

The character "harm" (傷) directly corresponds to "grind" (磨) and "wear away" (). Mountains and forests, marshes and wilds—do they bring me joy? Joy is not yet complete when sorrow follows. The coming of sorrow and joy, I cannot resist; their going, I cannot halt. How lamentable! The world’s people take things as mere inns. They know what they encounter but not what they do not. They know what they can do but not what they cannot. Those without knowledge or ability are indeed what people cannot avoid. Yet striving to avoid what cannot be avoided—is this not also lamentable?

Ultimate words transcend words; ultimate actions transcend actions. To equalize knowledge is to make what is known shallow.

*The Twenty-First Scroll of the Xunben Edition of the Nan Hua Zhen Jing* concludes here.¹

*The Twenty-Second Scroll of the Xunben Edition of the Nan Hua Zhen Jing*

**Miscellaneous Chapters: Gengsang Chu**

Among the servants of Lao Dan was one named Gengsang Chu, who had partially grasped the Master’s Dao. He went north to dwell in the mountains of Weilei. Those among his retainers who were cunningly clever departed from him; those among his concubines who were ostentatiously kind withdrew.

Those who were dull-witted remained to dwell with him; those who were restless and overbearing he employed as servants. After three years, Weilei flourished abundantly. The people of Weilei said to one another: “When Master Gengsang first arrived, we regarded him with surprise. Now, when we reckon our days, we find them insufficient, but when we reckon our years, we find them abundant. He must be a sage! Why do you not join together to honor him as a spirit, to offer him sacrifices of millet and grain?”

When Gengsang Chu heard this, he turned southward, displeased. His disciples found this strange. Gengsang Chu said: “Disciples, why do you find me strange? When spring’s qi arises, the hundred grasses grow; when autumn arrives in due season, the myriad treasures ripen. Is it not the Dao’s unfolding that makes spring and autumn thus? The Perfect One, I have heard, dwells like a corpse in a walled chamber, while the common people roam wildly, not knowing where to turn. Now these petty folk of Weilei secretly wish to place me among the sacrificial vessels of the thousand worthies—am I to be one who is displayed like a marker? This is why I cannot let go of Master Lao Dan’s words. The Perfect One dwells like a corpse in a walled chamber, while the common people roam wildly, not knowing where to go—this means that when the Perfect One conceals himself, people do not know his name and thus wander without direction. A marker is made by raising wood so that all may see it. ‘Cannot let go of Master Lao Dan’s words’ means that the Way of Lao Dan takes non-action as its essence, yet people still cannot forget him; this is why I cannot let go of his words.”

His disciples said: “Not so. In a ditch of ordinary size, great fish have no room to turn their bodies, while eels and loaches rule there; on a hillock of a few fathoms, great beasts have no place to conceal themselves, while foxes and badgers thrive. Moreover, to honor the worthy and empower the capable, to put goodness and benefit first—this has been so since the time of Yao and Shun, let alone the people of Weilei. Master, you should listen to them.”

Gengsang Chu said: “Come here, young ones.”

The beast that fits in a carriage, once armored and parted from the mountain, cannot escape the snares of nets; the fish that swallows a boat, once beached and parted from the water, is tormented even by ants. Thus birds and beasts do not tire of heights, fish and turtles do not tire of depths. Those who preserve their bodily form and life conceal themselves, and nothing suffices for them but the deepest obscurity.

Moreover, as for those two—Yao and Shun—what are they worth extolling? In their disputations, they would recklessly breach walls to plant mugwort and thistles, comb their hair strand by strand, count grains of rice to cook them—how could such petty fussing ever save the world? Promote the worthy, and the people contend; gather the clever, and the people steal. These measures do not enrich the people. The people’s pursuit of profit is relentless: sons kill fathers, ministers kill rulers; in broad daylight they rob, at midday they plunder.

I tell you: the root of great disorder arose between Yao and Shun, and its branches endure a thousand generations hence. There will surely be men who devour one another.

Nanrong Chu sat bolt upright and said: “At my age, I am already old—how can I entrust myself to a practice that might reach such understanding?”

Gengsang Chu said: “Preserve your form, embrace your life, and let not your thoughts race restlessly. If you do this for three years, you may attain this understanding.”

Nanrong Chu said: “Eyes and form—I do not see their difference, yet the blind cannot see themselves; ears and form—I do not see their difference, yet the deaf cannot hear themselves; heart and form—I do not see their difference, yet the mad cannot grasp themselves. Form and form should open to one another, yet things somehow come between them. We seek each other but cannot find each other. Now you tell me: ‘Preserve your form, embrace your life, let not your thoughts race restlessly.’ I strain to hear the Way, but it reaches only my ears.”

Gengsang Chu said: “My words are exhausted. It is said: the fleeing bee cannot transform the hornworm, the Yue chicken cannot hatch the swan’s egg—only the Lu chicken can. Chickens are all chickens, their virtue no different. What distinguishes them is ability or inability, for their talents are inherently great or small. My talent is small and cannot transform you. Why not go south to see Laozi?”

Gengsang Chu taught Nanrong Chu to preserve his form, but Nanrong Chu was unconvinced: “Let me speak of form—eyes, ears, heart—all are part of the same body, no different in kind. Yet the blind cannot see themselves, the deaf cannot hear themselves, the mad cannot grasp themselves. When form is bestowed, it should open freely, yet things come between, so that though we seek each other, we cannot find each other. Thus form cannot be preserved whole.” Though Nanrong Chu strained to hear Gengsang Chu’s Way, it reached only his ears—his eyes saw nothing, his heart grasped nothing.

Gengsang Chu then drew an analogy: “The common saying captures it: the fleeing bee cannot transform the hornworm, the Yue chicken cannot hatch the swan’s egg. This illustrates that my talent is too small to transform you, so I send you to see Lao Dan.”

Nanrong Chu packed provisions and, after seven days and nights, arrived at Laozi’s abode. Laozi said: “Have you come from the land of Chu?”

Nanrong Chu said: “Yes.”

Laozi said: “Why have you brought so many people with you?”

Nanrong Chu turned fearfully to look behind him.

Laozi said: “Do you not understand what I mean?”

Nanrong Chu bowed his head in shame, then looked up and laughed, saying: “Now I have forgotten my answer and so lost my question.”

Laozi said: “What do you mean?”

Nanrong Chu said: “If I am ignorant, people call me a fool—like Zhu, who was unworthy, so that later ages call the unperceptive ‘Zhu the fool.’ If I am wise, it only brings sorrow to my body. Without benevolence, I harm others; with benevolence, I bring sorrow upon myself. Without righteousness, I injure others; with righteousness, I bring sorrow upon myself. How can I escape this? These three sayings are my afflictions. I wish to ask you about them through Chu.”

Laozi said: “Just now, I saw it in the space between your brows—I understood you then. Now you speak and confirm it. You are like one who has lost his parents, raising a pole to search the seas—you are a lost man, as if something is missing. Bewildered, you seek to return to your nature but have no way in. Pitiable indeed!”

Nanrong Chu asked to enter and rest in his lodging. He summoned what he loved and cast off what he hated. After ten days of sorrow, he saw Laozi again.

Laozi said: “You have cleansed yourself—why are you still so troubled? Yet within, you still brim with what is foul. What is bound on the outside cannot be grasped if one clings too tightly—it must be released from within. What is entangled within cannot be grasped if one forces it—it must be released from without. Those bound both inside and out cannot be held by virtue and power—how much less those who act without the Way?”

“Troubled” here means “distracted.” “Brim” means “overflowing.” You have cultivated and cleansed yourself, appearing composed, but this is only external. Within, you still flow with what is foul.

Here the term "韄" signifies binding objects with leather straps to restrain them. "犍" refers to the bolt of a door that locks it shut. When what is restrained externally becomes too numerous to grasp, it will turn to internal resistance and closure. When what is restrained internally becomes chaotic and ungraspable, it will turn to external resistance and closure. With such afflictions both inside and out, even one possessed of virtue and power will be unable to maintain equilibrium—how much less those who merely imitate the Way in their conduct?

Nanrong Chu said: “When a villager falls ill, his neighbors come to his gate. If the patient can describe his symptoms, yet does not understand the cause of his illness, he remains as one who has not truly fallen ill.” The latter two instances of "illness" mean that if one can thoroughly explain the cause of the affliction, though the illness may be severe, it is as if it were not severe at all. “My hearing of the great Dao is like taking medicine that only worsens the disease. I wish only to learn the classic of preserving life.”

Laozi said: “The classic of preserving life—can you embrace the One? Can you not lose it? Can you know good and ill fortune without divination? Can you come to rest? Can you cease? Can you abandon seeking in others and turn to seeking within yourself? Can you be carefree? Can you be uncontrived? Can you be like a child? A child may wail all day without hoarseness—for harmony is at its utmost. He may clench his fists all day without cramping—for he shares in the power. He may gaze all day without blinking—for his attention is not fixed on externals. He moves without knowing where he goes, dwells without knowing what he does, flowing with things and riding their waves. This is the classic of preserving life.”

Nanrong Chu said: “Is this then the virtue of the perfected one?” “This is what is called the thawing of ice and the melting of frost. The perfected one shares sustenance with others upon the earth and rejoices with them under heaven. He does not entangle himself with the gains and harms of men and things, nor does he conspire with others in strangeness, scheming, or affairs. He comes and goes with ease, uncontrived. This is the classic of preserving life.”

“Then is this perfection?” “A child acts without knowing what he does, moves without knowing where he goes. His body is like the branch of a withered tree, his heart like dead ashes. In such a state, misfortune does not arrive, nor does fortune come. With neither misfortune nor fortune, how can there be human calamity?”

Nanrong Chu doubted whether Laozi’s words on the classic of preserving life were indeed the virtue of the perfected one. Laozi said: “Not so. This is what is called the thawing of ice and the melting of frost. When ice and frost first dissolve, they have not yet fully melted—this is not yet perfection. The perfected one shares sustenance with others upon the earth, comes and goes uncontrived—all this describes the state of the perfected. Yet what was spoken earlier is merely the classic of preserving life.”

Then is this the ultimate state?” Nanrong Chu asked, “Is this the utmost of the Way of preserving life?”

Laozi replied: “It is not yet the ultimate. What I have told you is merely this: one who embodies the childlike can be free from misfortune and fortune. When the cosmos is at peace and settled, it radiates the light of Heaven. One who radiates the light of Heaven—people see such a person, and those who cultivate themselves may thereby attain constancy. Those who attain constancy are abandoned by others yet aided by Heaven. Those abandoned by others are called the people of Heaven; those aided by Heaven are called the sons of Heaven.

The learner learns what cannot be learned. The doer does what cannot be done. The debater debates what cannot be debated. To know when to stop at what cannot be known—this is the ultimate. If there is any who does not conform to this, the great balance of Heaven will undo him.

When the heart’s cosmos is at peace and settled, it naturally radiates the light of Heaven—this is what is called ‘settled life gives rise to wisdom.’ Those who see one radiating the light of Heaven may think it a human ability, yet they do not know it arises from the heart’s cosmos being at peace and settled—this is Heaven. There are those who cannot achieve this settled state of the heart’s cosmos and must rely on cultivation; even so, they cannot radiate the light of Heaven. They may only attain constancy. Those who attain constancy, though abandoned by others, are surely aided by Heaven. Those abandoned by others remain without rank and are the people of Heaven; those aided by Heaven hold rank and are the sons of Heaven.

Yet these are not the ultimate. The ultimate must utterly cast aside cleverness, knowledge, and deliberation, as if utterly incapable—only then is it the ultimate. Those who cannot achieve this are undone by the great balance of Heaven; it is not that Heaven does not aid them.

The great balance is my own great balance. Prepare things to nurture form; store what is unforeseen to establish the heart; maintain reverence within to reach without. If, despite this, myriad evils arrive, they are all of Heaven, not of man—they cannot disturb one’s accomplished virtue, nor can wayward matters enter the numinous terrace. The numinous terrace has something it holds, yet one does not know what it holds, nor can it be held.

*Nurture* means to cultivate; *store* means to preserve; *establish* means to stand. Externally, one prepares things to nurture form; internally, one constantly preserves unforeseen defenses to establish the heart. One maintains reverence within to reach without. If misfortune arrives thus, it is all the decree of Heaven, not summoned by human affairs—it cannot disturb my accomplished virtue, nor can wayward matters enter my heart. Though the heart has something it holds, others do not know what it holds. There is that which cannot be held—this means that which cannot be grasped.

Hong Mai’s *Rongzhai Random Jottings* records Hong Qingxiang’s explanation, which is merely a Confucian interpretation: “If one does not see the sincerity within oneself manifest, every manifestation will be inappropriate. Though one’s deeds have entered a situation, one cannot let them go, and each time makes further errors. Doing evil in the open, others may punish it; doing evil in secret, ghosts may punish it. Only when one is clear about others and clear about ghosts can one act alone.”

This reverses the earlier text. Only when one has not yet achieved sincerity within does every manifestation miss the mark. Though one clearly knows it is insincere, one’s deeds have already entered the situation and cannot be removed. Unable to correct the error, one instead compounds it, thus incurring the calamities of others and ghosts—one cannot speak of wayward matters here.

Those within their allotted bounds act without seeking fame; those beyond their allotted bounds act with expectations of vast expenditure. Those who act without seeking fame, though obscure, have a radiance; those who aspire to vast expenditure are but merchants. People see them as towering, yet they are hollow. Those who exhaust themselves in competition with things find things occupying their place; those who merely accommodate things cannot even contain themselves—how can they contain others? Those who cannot contain others have no intimacy; those with no intimacy are forsaken by all.

*Within their allotted bounds* means within one’s proper role. Those who seek within their proper role do not pursue fame. Those who seek beyond their proper role aspire to vast and lavish expenditure. The *Doctrine of the Mean* says, “The Way of the noble person is vast yet hidden.” Those who act without seeking fame, though obscure, have a radiance in their use. Those who aspire to vast expenditure seek to dazzle and sell themselves in the world, like merchants.
