Or like one who stands on tiptoe—people see them as towering and grand, yet in truth they are not. Those who go beyond their bounds exhaust themselves in competition with things, and things instead occupy their place. Those within their bounds accommodate things without being burdened, and do not even know they have a self—how could they know others? Without others, even the closest kin are absent; without intimacy, all people are absent. This is the meaning of standing apart from the world.

No weapon is more reckless than the will; even *Mo Ye* is inferior. No enemy is greater than yin and yang—there is no escape from them between Heaven and Earth. It is not that yin and yang harm one; the heart causes it.

The Way flows through one’s allotted bounds—its completion is its destruction. What is detestable about allotted bounds is their duality; allotment exists for preparation. Why detest preparation? Because it implies something to prepare for. Thus, to go forth and not return is to see one’s ghost. To go forth and gain is called gaining death. To perish yet have substance—this is one kind of ghost. Using what has form to model what is formless—this is settled.

Originally, there was no completion or destruction; people divided themselves into two. Thus, the Way flows through one’s allotted bounds. What is detestable about allotment? How does one allot it? If distant, it must reach the extreme. Completion involves a hundred kinds of schemes to achieve success; destruction involves complete abandonment, letting ruin take its course.

*Preparation* means extremity. Why detest extremity? Because once extreme, it is like one who goes forth and does not return. One only sees them pursuing external things, gradually worn away, like a ghost bent and unable to rise. Yet the world believes that going forth brings gain—this is nothing but the way of gaining death.”

The Way of gaining death extinguishes this life. Yet one believes it possesses true substance—how does this differ from a ghost? Using what has form to model what is formless is to use a person to model a ghost. What is "settled" cannot return to being human.

To go forth without root, to enter without aperture—possessing substance yet without location; having length yet without root or tip, where root and tip are the same. To emerge yet have no aperture is to possess substance. Possessing substance yet without location is space; having length yet without root or tip is time. There is life, there is death; there is going forth, there is entering. Entering and emerging without seeing its form—this is called the Gate of Heaven. The Gate of Heaven is Non-Being. The myriad things emerge from Non-Being. Being cannot use Being to create Being; it must emerge from Non-Being, and even Non-Being is not Non-Being. The sage conceals himself therein.

The so-called Way emerges without showing its root, enters without showing its aperture, possesses substance yet does not reveal its location. *Piao* is the same as *biao*, meaning "tip." It has length yet does not show its root or tip. Though it emerges without root and enters without aperture, it still possesses substance. It is not empty words—possessing substance yet without location, like the four directions and above and below of space, which have no fixed place. Having length yet without root or tip, like the past and present of time, which have no beginning or end. Life and death, going forth and entering—none reveal their form; this is called the Gate of Heaven. The Gate of Heaven is emptiness and Non-Being. The myriad things emerge from Non-Being. All Being cannot use Being to create Being; it must emerge from Non-Being, and even Non-Being is not Non-Being. The sage cherishes and conceals this Way.

The ancients, their knowledge reached a certain point. To what point? Some believed there had never been anything—this is the ultimate, the utmost, beyond which nothing can be added. Those next believed there were things, taking life as loss and death as return—thus they divided themselves. They already split life and death into two paths. Those next said: first there was Non-Being, then there was life, and life swiftly turned to death. They took Non-Being as the head, life as the body, and death as the tail. Who knows the one who guards the unity of Being, Non-Being, death, and life? I befriend such a one.

Though these three differ, they belong to the same clan. The Zhaos and Jings were distinguished by office; the Jia clan was distinguished by fief. They were not the same. *Dai* means official duty; *feng* means enfeoffment. Though these three differ, they are like the Zhaos, Jings, and Jias—all noble clans of Chu—some distinguished by office, some by fief, yet differing in this alone.

There is life, and the five flavors congeal into blackness—*yan* (black) and *xian* (salty) are pronounced similarly—scattered, it is called "shifting this." Once one speaks of "shifting this," it is no longer what was spoken of. Yet it remains unknowable. The sacrificial meat of the *La* festival includes the tripe—*pi* (pronounced *pihai*, the third stomach of a cow)—which can be separated but not truly divided. One who inspects a house must circle the ancestral temple and bedchambers, then proceed to the resting quarters. This illustrates "shifting this." *Yan* is the blackness at the bottom of a cauldron. *Piran* means "scattered." *Yi shi* means the "this" that shifts and does not remain fixed. *La* is the name of the sacrifice held on the third *xu* day after the winter solstice. *Pi* is the omasum (the third stomach of a cow). *Hai* refers to the hair and flesh of the toes. *Yan* is the chamber for rest. What people take as "this" is like the specks at the bottom of a cauldron—scattered and shifting, never fixed. Thus it is called "shifting this."

To speak of "shifting this" is fundamentally baseless, yet there are indeed unknowable things. For example, in the *La* sacrifice, the tripe and toe flesh are divided on the platter—this can be separated—but as parts of a single sacrificial animal, they cannot be truly divided. Or when inspecting a house, one must circle the ancestral temple and bedchambers to call it complete, yet one must also reach the resting quarters. To observe one aspect, one must divide to unite; to observe another, one must unite to divide—this is unknowable. From this perspective, where is there a true "this"? Thus the saying of "shifting this."

Let us speak of "shifting this": taking life as the root, knowledge as the teacher, and thereby riding upon right and wrong. Truly believing in names and realities, taking oneself as the essence, making others take it as one’s integrity—even dying to uphold this integrity. Those who do so use action as wisdom and inaction as foolishness; thoroughness as fame and poverty as disgrace. "Shifting this" is the way of people today—like the cicada and the owl, they are the same in their sameness.

The above speaks of "there is life, and the five flavors congeal into blackness," scattered, called "shifting this." This "shifting this" refers to people who take life as fundamental, valuing it too heavily. Following knowledge, they generate right and wrong; believing in names and realities, they know only themselves. Establishing integrity, they would even die without hesitation. Such people are moved by success and failure, poverty and prosperity. Thus, "shifting this" is the way of the common people today. How do they differ from the cicada and the owl in their narrow views? People are the same as these creatures—thus it is said they are "the same in their sameness."

Trampling a stranger’s foot in the marketplace, one apologizes profusely; trampling a brother’s foot, one merely soothes him; trampling a parent’s foot, one does not even do that. Thus it is said: the utmost ritual does not involve others; the utmost righteousness does not treat things as things; the utmost knowledge does not scheme; the utmost benevolence has no partiality; the utmost trust repels weapons without need for metal.

*Jian* means "to tread upon." Accidentally stepping on a stranger’s foot in the marketplace, one apologizes excessively for the lack of restraint. Stepping on a brother’s foot, one merely soothes him. For parents, not even soothing is necessary. *Bi* means "to repel." The utmost trust does not require metal or jewels as collateral. It unbinds the knots of ambition, resolves the errors of the heart, removes the burdens of virtue, and opens the blockages of the Way.

The six of noble rank, wealth, prominence, majesty, fame, and profit stir the ambitions of the will; the six of countenance, movement, complexion, principle, qi, and intent distort the heart; the six of aversion, desire, joy, anger, sorrow, and delight burden virtue; the six of advancing, retreating, taking, giving, knowledge, and ability obstruct the Way. When these four sets of six do not agitate the breast, one is upright; upright, then still; still, then luminous; luminous, then empty; empty, then non-action and yet nothing is not done.

The passages in the text that are thus verbose and impertinent may well be omitted.

The Dao is the reverence of virtue; life is the radiance of virtue; nature is the essence of life. *Qin* means "reverence," implying restraint. The Dao precedes virtue, hence it is said: the Dao is the reverence of virtue. With virtue, one nourishes the self, hence it is said: life is the radiance of virtue. Nature arises together with life, hence it is said: nature is the essence of life.

When nature moves, it is called *wei*; the artificiality of *wei* is called loss. *Zhi* means "to engage"; *zhi* means "to deliberate." The knowledge of what the wise do not know is like a sidelong glance. Movement through necessity is called virtue; movement without self is called governance. Names may oppose one another, yet realities align.

Yi excelled at hitting the subtle mark yet was clumsy at preventing others from praising him; the sage excels at Heaven yet is clumsy at humanity. One who excels at Heaven and is *liang* (good) at humanity may yet be capable of humanity. Only the complete person can do so. Only the insect-like can be insect-like; only the insect-like can be Heaven-like. The complete person detests Heaven, detests the Heaven of others, let alone the Heaven of oneself in relation to others.

The sage excels at uniting with Heaven yet is clumsy at preventing others from praising him. Only the complete person can excel at Heaven and also at humanity—yet how could they boast of this? If the insect-like can be insect-like and also Heaven-like, then being human-like and Heaven-like is not enough to boast of. Thus the complete person detests the reputation of being Heaven-like, and moreover detests the Heaven of others, let alone one’s own Heaven in relation to others.

When a single sparrow approaches Yi, Yi must capture it—such is his might. If the world is made into a cage, then no sparrow can escape. Thus Tang used a kitchen servant to enclose Yi Yin, and Duke Mu of Qin used five sheepskins to enclose Bai Li Xi. Therefore, there is nothing that cannot be captured if one uses what they love as the cage.

The hunchback discards adornment, indifferent to external praise or blame. The convict climbs high without fear, having abandoned life and death. The amputee limps along, his form already incomplete, unconcerned with others’ praise or blame. The convict climbs high without fear, for among prisoners, life and death are held lightly. How can one who is swayed by external glory, disgrace, likes, or dislikes be caged?

Responding without offering and forgetting others—by forgetting others, one thereby becomes a Heavenly person. Thus, one who is revered yet feels no joy, or insulted yet feels no anger, is one who harmonizes with Heavenly harmony. Anger expressed without anger means anger arises from non-anger; action performed as non-action means action arises from non-action. To still the mind, calm the qi; to spiritually attune, follow the heart. In action, to be fitting, align with necessity. The category of necessity is the Way of the sage.

*Fu* means "to respond." *Xie* is the same as *zhe*, meaning "soft speech." In responding, one speaks softly and subtly, as if compelled, never forcing words upon others—so much so that one forgets others. This sentence should be read in three parts.

*Nanhua Zhenjing Xunben*, Scroll Twenty-Two, concludes.

#1 The character *zhe* 者 has been added according to the common edition.

*Nanhua Zhenjing Xunben*, Scroll Twenty-Three
Recorded by Lu Ling Zhu Feng’s Luo Miandao
Collated by Disciple Peng Xiang

**Miscellaneous Chapters: Xu Wugui**

Xu Wugui, through the introduction of Nü Shang, was granted an audience with Marquis Wu of Wei.
*Shiwen* notes: Xu Wugui was a recluse from Minshan in Wei.
Li [Yun] says: Wugui and Nü Shang were both favored courtiers of Wei.

Marquis Wu greeted him, saying: “Master, you must be weary—exhausted by the toils of mountain and forest, and thus you have come to see me.”
Xu Wugui replied: “It is I who am wearied by you, my lord. What weariness have you for me? If you indulge your desires and prolong your likes and dislikes, the true nature of your life will sicken. If you suppress your desires and cast off your likes and dislikes, your ears and eyes will sicken. I would weary you, my lord—what weariness have you for me?”

Marquis Wu, appearing aloof and self-exalted, made no reply.
After a moment, Xu Wugui continued: “Let me tell you how I judge dogs. Those of low quality seize their fill and stop—such is the virtue of a wildcat. Those of middling quality gaze as if toward the sun. Those of the highest quality seem as though they have forgotten their own singularity. Dogs are meant for hunting. The low-quality ones capture only enough to fill their bellies and then cease—this is mere wildcat talent. *De* here means ‘capacity.’ The middling ones, with lofty aspirations and far-reaching vision, like one gazing at the sun in the sky, disregard small prey before them—surely superior to the lower sort. Yet the highest quality ones consider hunting itself beneath them. Though hunting is the dog’s sole purpose, the finest among them lose sight of their specialization, transcending the common dog entirely.”

“My judgment of dogs is not as refined as my judgment of horses. When I judge horses: the straight align with the marking cord, the curved with the hook, the square with the carpenter’s square, the round with the compass. This means they respond to the reins in perfect accord with these measures. Such are the horses of a state—yet they do not compare to the horses of the world. The horses of the world possess a perfected capacity, appearing anxious yet absent, as though they have lost their singularity. *Anxious yet absent*—as if dazed and forgetful. The horse’s singular purpose is to run. When it forgets its specialized task, it emerges as a steed beyond the ordinary. Such horses surpass all bounds, leaving no trace—transcending dust, their destination unknown. *Chaoyi* means ‘to surpass.’ *Juechen* means their hooves do not touch the earth, raising no dust.”

Marquis Wu was greatly pleased and laughed.
When Xu Wugui departed, Nü Shang asked: “Master, how did you alone succeed in delighting my lord? When I attempt to persuade him, whether I expound broadly with the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music*, or present the subtleties of the *Golden Tallies* and *Six Strategies*—the *Golden Tablets* and *Six Strategies* being chapters from the Zhou *Book of War*, the *Six Strategies* of the Grand Duke comprising the *Civil*, *Martial*, *Tiger*, *Leopard*, *Dragon*, and *Hound*—those who have served him with great merit are beyond counting, yet my lord has never so much as parted his lips. How did you delight him?”

“Did you truly delight my lord to such an extent?”
Xu Wugui replied: “I merely told him how I judge dogs and horses.”
Nü Shang said: “Is that all?”
Xu Wugui said: “Have you not heard of the exiles of Yue? When they have been away from their state for several days, they rejoice at seeing those they know. When they have been away for a month, they rejoice at seeing those they once saw within the state. After a full year, they rejoice even at seeing those who merely resemble people—does this not show that the longer one is parted from others, the deeper one’s longing grows? Now, those who flee into the emptiness of the wilds, where brambles and thorns block the paths of weasels and badgers, stumble through the void, and when they hear the sound of human footsteps—*qiong*—they rejoice. How much more would they rejoice at the voices and coughs of brothers and kin at their side? For too long, has no one offered the words of a true person at my lord’s side?”

*[Annotations within the text:]*
*Zhu* means “to block.” *Liang* means “to stumble.” *Wei* means “to dwell.” The phrase describes hastening through an empty valley. *Qiongran* is the sound of footsteps. This illustrates how, for so long, no one has spoken essential words to Marquis Wu, so that even hearing talk of dogs and horses brings him joy.

When Xu Wugui met with Marquis Wu, the marquis said: “Master, you dwell in the mountains and forests, eating acorns and chestnuts, spurning scallions and leeks, and have kept your distance from me for so long. Are you now aged? Do you crave the taste of wine and meat? Or do you believe I possess the good fortune of the altars of soil and grain?”
Xu Wugui replied: “I, Wugui, was born poor and lowly and have never dared to partake of your lordship’s wine and meat. I have come to offer you service.”
The marquis said: “What service? How do you intend to serve me?”

Xu Wugui said: "I come to relieve the strain upon your lordship’s spirit and form."
Marquis Wu said: "What do you mean?"
Xu Wugui replied: "The nurturance of Heaven and Earth is one. Ascending heights does not make one tall; dwelling low does not make one short. Yet you alone, as lord of ten thousand chariots, afflict the people of your state to nourish your ears, eyes, nose, and mouth—while the spirit does not consent to this. The spirit delights in harmony and detests perversity. Perversity is sickness, and thus I come to relieve it. Why does your lordship alone suffer from this sickness?"

Marquis Wu then asked what weighed upon his thoughts and caused him distress.
Marquis Wu said: "I have long wished to meet you, Master. I desire to cherish the people and practice righteousness by laying down arms—would this be possible?"
Xu Wugui said: "It would not. To cherish the people is the beginning of harming them; to practice righteousness by laying down arms is the root of raising arms. If you persist in this, it will likely not succeed. All perfected beauty is a baleful vessel. Though you act with benevolence and righteousness, you will verge on hypocrisy. Form inevitably begets form; once traces appear, further traces are added. Success inevitably invites attack; change inevitably brings external conflict. Once this heart stirs, it contends with external things.

You must not array your cranes in battle formation between the towers of the *Liqiao*, nor station infantry and cavalry in the ancestral temples of *Zitan*. The *Helie* is a battle formation, like the *Yuli*. The *Liqiao* is a tower’s name. *Tu* means foot soldiers; *ji*, cavalry. *Zitan* refers to the grounds of ancestral sacrifices. This is to say: desires and strife within the heart are like spears and halberds within a chamber.

Do not store up resistance in your gains, for with gain comes loss, and adversity is already concealed within fortune. Do not seek to overcome others with skill, nor with strategy, nor with warfare. Those who slaughter the people’s soldiers and seize their lands to nourish their own selfishness and spirit—how can one know which side is right? Where does victory lie?

If you cannot desist, then as the saying goes—even if Mencius could not stop, there is still one path: cultivate sincerity within your breast to respond to the feelings of Heaven and Earth, and do not provoke conflict. The people’s deaths will have ceased; what need will you then have for laying down arms?"

The Yellow Emperor, seeking to meet the Great Vastness at Mount Jucí, had Fangming as his driver and Changyu as his outrider. Zhangruo and Xipeng rode ahead of the horses, while Kunhun and Guji followed in the rear carriage. When they reached the wilds of Xiangcheng, the Seven Sages all lost their way and had no one to ask for directions. They happened upon a boy herding horses and asked him the way. "Do you know Mount Jucí?" they said. "I do," he replied. "Do you know where the Great Vastness resides?"

The boy replied, "I do."
The Yellow Emperor said, "How extraordinary, little boy! Not only do you know Mount Jucí, but you also know where the Great Vastness resides. May I ask how to govern the world?"
The boy said, "Governing the world is just like this—what more is there to do? When I was young, I roamed freely within the Six Directions. The boy is not truly a boy, but merely appears youthful. I once suffered from a sudden affliction of dim-sightedness, and an elder instructed me: 'Ride the chariot of the sun and wander the wilds of Xiangcheng.' Because of my dim-sightedness, he taught me to ride the sun’s chariot so that I could see by following its light. Now that my illness has slightly improved, I am about to roam beyond the Six Directions. Governing the world is just like this—what more is there to do?"
The Yellow Emperor said, "Governing the world is indeed not your concern. Nevertheless, may I ask how to govern it?"
The boy declined to answer. The Yellow Emperor asked again.
The boy said, "What difference is there between governing the world and herding horses? It is merely a matter of removing what harms the horses."
The Yellow Emperor bowed twice, touching his forehead to the ground, and departed, calling him "Heavenly Master."

A scholar finds no joy without the changes of thought and contemplation; an eloquent speaker finds no joy without the order of discourse and persuasion.

The scrutinizing scholar finds no joy without matters of critique and inquiry—all are confined by things. *Ling* means to override; *ping* means to question. They override and question every matter.

Those who summon the age serve in court; those of middling ability take pride in office; those of physical strength boast of hardships; the brave rush into peril; warriors delight in battle; recluses cling to fame; legalists expand governance; ritualists revere decorum; the benevolent and righteous value connections. *Zhaoshi* means taking the world as one’s own task, as if stirring it; *xing chao* means standing in court; *su ming* means leaving a name; *gui ji* means prizing social ties.

Farmers without the work of clearing weeds grow estranged; merchants without the bustle of markets grow estranged; commoners with daily tasks are diligent; artisans with skill in tools are bold. *Bi* means unity—without it, they lose their livelihood and scatter. *Quan* means exerting effort; *zhuang* means acting with courage.

Wealth unaccumulated troubles the greedy; power unconsolidated saddens the boastful—those who love grandeur and crave influence delight in change. When the times afford use, they cannot remain inactive. All these follow the seasons in accord, not altering with things.

They exhaust their bodies and natures, sinking into the myriad things, never returning—how lamentable! *Wu* means affairs; *tu* means kind. Those fond of power and change take joy in inconstancy. One should meet such times with equanimity; when opportunity arises, one cannot remain inactive, yet need not act like the greedy or boastful. Those who understand this principle align with the seasons without shifting with things. Yet people today exhaust their bodies and natures, sinking into the myriad things unawares, living out their lives without return—how pitiable!

Zhuangzi said: "An archer who hits the mark without aiming is called a master shot—would it be right to say all under heaven are Yi?" Huizi said: "Yes." Zhuangzi said: "If there is no universal rightness, and each takes their own rightness as right, would it be right to say all under heaven are Yao?" Huizi said: "Yes." Zhuangzi said: "Then the Confucians, Mohists, Yangists, and Bingists—four schools—and you make five. Which is truly right? Or is it like Lu Ju? His disciple said: ‘I have grasped the Master’s Way! I can heat a cauldron in winter and make ice in summer!’ Lu Ju said: ‘This merely summons yang with yang and yin with yin—it is not what I call the Way. Let me show you my Way.’ Thereupon, he tuned two zithers, placing one in the hall and one in the chamber. When he plucked the *gong* note on one, the *gong* note on the other sounded; when he plucked the *jue* note, the *jue* note responded—their pitches were in harmony. Then he altered one string so it no longer fit the five notes. When he plucked it, all twenty-five strings vibrated, yet the sound was not distinct, for it had become the ruler of tones. Is this not like that?

An archer must aim at the target to hit it—this is called a master shot. If one hits by chance without aiming, would it be right to say all under heaven are Yi? This illustrates that if there is no universal rightness, and each takes their own rightness as right, would it be right to say all under heaven are Yao?

Heating a cauldron in winter means making fire without flame in cold weather; making ice in summer means creating ice without cold in heat. Though these seem marvelous, they merely follow winter’s yang to summon yang for fire, or summer’s yin to summon yin for ice—nothing extraordinary.

*Fei* means to place. He set one zither in the hall and one in the chamber. Plucking the *gong* note on one caused the *gong* note on the other to sound; plucking the *jue* note caused the *jue* note to respond. This seems marvelous, yet it occurs because their pitches harmonize—nothing extraordinary. Like when Cao Shaokui, a master of tones, visited a monk in Luoyang whose chime would sound day and night of its own accord. The monk, deeming it strange, fell ill. Shaokui, a friend, came to inquire. The monk explained, and just then, striking a bell caused the chime to sound again. Shaokui laughed: "Prepare a feast tomorrow, and I will rid you of this." The monk complied. After eating, Shaokui took a file from his sleeve, struck the chime several times, and the sound ceased. The monk pressed him for the reason. He said: "The chime’s pitch matched the bell’s, so they resonated." The monk rejoiced, and his illness was cured.

Another example: Li Sizhen heard a carriage bell’s echo underground. Digging, he found a bell—such things happen."

The term *dang* (当) means "to govern." As the *Xue Ji* states: "When a drum is struck without aligning with the pitch of the five tones, or when a single string is altered apart from the five notes, yet the drum is sounded without a governing tone, all twenty-five strings will resonate. Though this one string initially differs in no way, it becomes the master of all sounds. Thus, when struck, the other strings obey its command."

The sixty-four modes all arise from the *huangzhong* gong, for gong is the sovereign and thus commands the other pitches. This is but the natural principle—what is there to find strange? If such is the case, then Yuhuizi’s self-assurance resembles that of Lu Ju.

Huizi said: "Now, the Confucians, Mohists, Yangists, and Bingists all engage me in debate, clashing words and overpowering voices, yet they have never proven me wrong—what do you make of that?" Huizi replied: "Zhuangzi says I stand with the Confucians, Mohists, Yangists, and Bingists as five, yet who is correct? The four cannot refute me, so I am right, and they are wrong. What of this argument?"

Zhuangzi said: "A man of Qi sent his son to Song to serve as a gatekeeper, dismissing him because his body was imperfect. Yet though his form was flawed, he was still his own son—how could he bear to cast him out? Consider this: when seeking a tripod or bell, one binds and secures it for fear of damage. How does this compare to abandoning one’s child? Or when seeking a servant for the hall path, one keeps him within the domain—how does this differ from exiling a son abroad? In such ways, the principle of kinship is neglected. People are blinded by distinctions of closeness and distance, nobility and baseness, near and far, unaware of their own delusion. This illustrates how Huizi recognizes the errors of the four schools yet fails to see his own."

The man of Chu who lodged aboard a boat and served as a gatekeeper in exile once fought with a boatman in the dead of night, though he had not yet reached shore—enough to sow resentment. *Li* (离) means "to reach," and *cen* (岑) refers to a mountain bank. The man of Chu, dwelling on a boat and limping as a gatekeeper in a foreign land, quarreled with the boatman at night when no one was present. He did not consider that before reaching shore, such conflict would only breed enmity. This further illustrates that contention not only blinds one to reason but also invites disaster.

Zhuangzi, passing by Huizi’s grave while attending a funeral, turned to his followers and said: "A man of Ying had plaster as thin as a fly’s wing on the tip of his nose. He called upon Craftsman Shi to carve it away. Craftsman Shi swung his axe like the wind, heeding the task and striking, removing all the plaster without harming the nose. The man of Ying stood unmoved. When the Lord of Song heard of this, he summoned Craftsman Shi and said, 'Try it for me.' Craftsman Shi replied, 'Your servant once could do this. However, my partner is gone. The one who stood still was my partner. The one who struck was my tool. He has been dead for a long time. Since the Master’s passing, I have had no partner, no one to speak with.'"

When Guan Zhong fell ill, Duke Huan asked him: "Your illness has grown severe. If it worsens, to whom should I entrust the state?" Guan Zhong asked: "Whom does the Duke wish to appoint?" The Duke said: "Bao Shuya." Guan Zhong replied: "He will not do."

Guan Zhong was a man of spotless integrity, a true gentleman. Toward those who fell short of his standards, he would not align himself; once he heard of another’s fault, he would remember it for life. If placed in charge of the state, above, he would oppose and thwart his lord; below, he would defy the people. It would not be long before he offended the ruler." The Duke said, "Then who is suitable?" Guan Zhong replied, "If you must choose, then Xi Peng will do. His character is such that he forgets those above and neglects those below. He feels ashamed that he does not measure up to the Yellow Emperor, yet he pities those who fall short of himself. To distribute virtue among others is the mark of a sage; to distribute wealth among others is the mark of a worthy. To rule others with one’s worthiness has never won their hearts; to humble oneself before others with one’s worthiness has never failed to win their hearts. In governing the state, there are things he does not hear; in managing his household, there are things he does not see. If you must choose, then Xi Peng will do."

The King of Wu was sailing on the river when he ascended the Mountain of Monkeys. The troop of monkeys, seeing him, scattered in fear, fleeing into the dense thickets. One monkey, however, lingered—twisting and turning, it plucked and scratched, swinging and climbing with such skill that it caught the king’s eye. The king shot at it, but the monkey deftly caught the arrow in midair. The king then ordered his attendants to hasten their shots, and the monkey, clutching the arrow, died. The king turned to his companion, Yan Buyi, and said: "This monkey flaunted its skill, relied on its agility, and thus behaved arrogantly toward me—only to meet this fate. Take heed!" Alas.

Do not let your countenance make you arrogant toward others! Upon returning, Yan Buyi took Dong Wu as his teacher, to temper his bearing, renouncing pleasure and ostentation. Three years later, the people of the state praised him.

Nanbo Zimo sat leaning on his armrest, gazing upward and sighing. Yan Chengzi entered and said: "Master, you are the rarest of beings. Can one truly make the body like withered wood, the heart like dead ashes?"

He replied: "Once I dwelt at the mouth of a mountain cave. At that time, when Tian He caught sight of me, the multitudes of Qi thrice congratulated him—congratulating him on having found worth. I had to first relinquish it, and thus they knew it; I had to first offer it for sale, and thus they bought it. Had I not possessed it, how could they have known it? Had I not sold it, how could they have purchased it? Alas! I grieve for those who lose themselves; I grieve, too, for those who grieve for others; and I grieve yet again for those who grieve over the grief of others. From then on, the distance grows daily. From this point onward, the sorrow extends endlessly."

When Confucius traveled to Chu, the King of Chu offered him a toast. Sunshu Ao stood holding the goblet, while Shinan Yiliao received the wine and made an offering, saying: "Men of old—here, their words end." He praised Confucius, declaring him incomparable to men of today, and begged him to speak.

Confucius said: "I have heard of the wordless word, yet never before have I spoken it. Here, now, I shall speak: Shinan Yiliao juggled his balls and resolved the conflict between two families; Sunshu Ao slept soundly, feather fan in hand, and the people of Ying laid down their weapons. Would that I had a beak three feet long!"

Sunshu Ao was the son of Wei Jia, named Ai Lie, and served as Prime Minister under King Zhuang of Chu—before Confucius’ time. Shinan Yiliao was skilled at juggling balls and bells, keeping eight in the air while one remained in his hand. When Chu and Song went to war, Yiliao bared his chest and performed his juggling before the army, halting the battle and securing victory. This, too, occurred after Confucius’ death. It is but an allegory.

Confucius spoke of two men who resolved conflict and stilled weapons through non-action—then why should I speak at all? If words were of use, I would wish for a beak three feet long; if words were of no use, such a beak would be useless. That is what they call the Way beyond the Way, and this is what they call the eloquence beyond words. "They" refers to the two men; "this" refers to Confucius. Thus, virtue encompasses the unity of the Dao, and words cease where knowledge knows no more—this is perfection. The unity of the Dao cannot be matched by virtue; what knowledge cannot know cannot be expressed by eloquence. To bear the names of Confucian or Mohist is already perilous.

Thus, the sea does not refuse the eastward flow—such is its boundlessness. The sage embraces heaven and earth, bestowing blessings upon the world, yet none know his name. Therefore, in life he holds no rank, in death he bears no posthumous title; no wealth is amassed, no fame is established—this is the Great Man. A dog is not deemed good for its fine barking, nor a man for his fine words—how much less so for greatness. To be great is not enough to be called great, and how much less so for virtue. Yet nothing is more complete than heaven and earth. What do they seek to be so complete? One who knows completeness seeks nothing, loses nothing, discards nothing, and does not exchange the self for things. Turning inward without exhaustion, following antiquity without friction—this is the sincerity of the Great Man.

Ziqi had eight sons; he lined them up before him and summoned Jiufang Yin, saying: "Examine my sons for me and tell me which is most auspicious." Jiufang Yin replied: "Kun is the most auspicious." Ziqi started in surprise and joy: "In what way?" He said: "Kun will share the ruler’s table and feast until the end of his days." Ziqi’s face fell, and tears welled up: "How could my son come to such an extreme?" Jiufang Yin said: "To share the ruler’s table brings blessings to three clans—how much more to his parents. Now, sir, you hear this and weep—this is to reject fortune. The son is auspicious, but the father is not." Ziqi said: "Yin, how could you know this? Is Kun truly auspicious? His fate is bound to meat and wine, entering only through nose and mouth—how could you know its origin? I have never herded, yet a ewe was born in the inner chamber; I have never hunted, yet a quail hatched in the hearth. I have never tended sheep, never hunted, yet these creatures appeared in my home as if born in the inner chamber or hearth. This echoes the *Book of Songs*: *'Without hunting, without stalking, why do I see quails hanging in your courtyard?'* Yet if one does not marvel at such things, what is there to wonder at?

Those with whom I wander wander through heaven and earth; with them I roam in joy beneath the sky, with them I feast upon the earth. I do not involve myself in their affairs, do not join their schemes, do not share their marvels. With them I ride the sincerity of heaven and earth, untouched by things, and drift along without being bound to their concerns. Now, if this is so, is there any recompense from the world? Wherever strange omens appear, strange conduct must follow. Alas, this is not the fault of my son or me—it is heaven’s doing. This is why I weep."

Before long, Kun was sent to Yan, where bandits seized him on the road. Selling him whole would be difficult, but maiming him would make it easy. So they cut off his feet and sold him in Qi, where he ended up in the marketplace of Qu Gong—yet he still ate meat and lived out his days.

Nie Que met Xu You and asked: "Where are you going?" He replied: "I am fleeing from Yao." "Why?" "Yao is so earnestly benevolent that I fear he will become a laughingstock to the world. Future generations may even turn to cannibalism. The people are not hard to gather: love them and they draw near, benefit them and they come, praise them and they are encouraged, bring them what they hate and they scatter. Love and benefit arise from benevolence and righteousness, but those who discard benevolence and righteousness are few, while those who profit from them are many. The practice of benevolence and righteousness lacks sincerity and is but a tool for the greedy and rapacious. Thus, for one man to decide what benefits the world is like a fleeting glance—momentary and unreliable. Yao knows that worthy men benefit the world but does not see that they also harm it. Only those beyond worthiness understand this."

There are those who are *nuan shu*, those who are *ru xu*, and those who are *quan lou*. The *nuan shu* learn the words of a single master, warming themselves with self-satisfaction, delighting in their own beauty, and believing themselves complete—yet they do not know that nothing has truly begun. This is what is called *nuan shu*. The *ru xu* are like lice on a pig: they choose sparse bristles, thinking it a vast palace; they nestle in the crooks of hooves, between teats and thighs, believing it a safe and comfortable dwelling. They do not know that one day the butcher will roll up his sleeves, spread straw, and light the fire—then they and the pig will burn together. They advance and retreat within their narrow domain—this is what is called *ru xu*. At ease, they cannot extricate themselves. *Ru* means stagnation; *xu* means waiting.

The *quan lou* is like Shun. Mutton does not long for ants, but ants long for mutton—because of its rank odor. Shun had a rank character, and the people delighted in him. He moved three times, and each time his settlement became a city; by the time he reached Deng’s ruins, he had a hundred thousand households. Yao, hearing of Shun’s worthiness, raised him from barren land, saying: "I hope to receive his blessings." Shun was raised from barren land, his years advanced, his senses dulled, yet he could not return to rest—this is what is called *quan lou*. Bent and hunched, unable to stand upright. Thus, the spirit-man detests the multitude’s approach, for when the multitude comes, they do not harmonize, and when they do not harmonize, no benefit arises.

Thus, he has no extreme attachments, no extreme estrangements, embracing virtue and warming harmony to align with the world—this is called the Perfected Person.

To ants, he abandons knowledge; to fish, he attains strategy; to sheep, he abandons intent.

With eyes, he sees eyes; with ears, he hears ears; with heart, he returns to heart.

Such a one is level as a plumb line in stillness, and in change, follows the course. Above, it speaks of Shun gaining the multitude yet being unable to return to rest. Thus, it says the spirit-man detests the multitude’s approach, for when the multitude comes, each has likes and dislikes, and when they do not harmonize, strife arises, bringing no benefit. Therefore, one who holds no partiality in relations, embraces the Dao and warmth, aligning with the world—this is called the Perfected Person.

As for the earlier saying that ants long for mutton, ants possess knowledge, so they cannot be without desire. Sheep cannot be without intent, so they cannot be without rankness. The Perfected Person, however, to ants abandons knowledge, to sheep abandons intent. The insertion of "to fish, he attains strategy" follows naturally from the earlier discourse on the joy of fish in the Hao River.

"With eyes, he sees eyes; with ears, he hears ears; with heart, he returns to heart"—these are no ordinary words. Without personally entering this state, one cannot know its levelness. "Level as a plumb line" describes the state of absolute stillness, where the line is straight and without crookedness. "In change, follows the course" describes the state of responding to things. "Follows" means that in the interplay of events, one responds with compliance, not being disturbed by them.

The Perfected Person of old awaited all with Heaven, not imposing human will upon Heaven. Heaven is spontaneity; humanity is contrivance. The Perfected Person of old: in gaining, it is life; in losing, it is death; in gaining, it is death; in losing, it is life—like medicine. In truth, *dong quai*, platycodon, chicken gizzard, and *shi ling* are each sovereign in their time—how can they all be named?

Goujian, with three thousand armored men, perched on Mount Kuaiji, yet only Zhong knew how to preserve what was lost, and only Zhong did not know how sorrow would come to his own body. The Perfected Person of old entrusted gain and loss to spontaneity, recognizing that gain must entail loss, and loss must entail gain. One thing’s birth means another’s death; one thing’s death means another’s birth. They ever depend upon one another.

Taking medicine as an analogy: *dong quai* and platycodon, chicken gizzard, and *shi ling* serve as sovereign, minister, assistant, and envoy in turn—where is constancy in gain or loss? Again, taking the affair of Yue as proof: Minister Zhong knew that after Yue’s fall it could be preserved, yet did not realize that in turn it would cost his own life—both are cases of gain and loss without constancy.

Thus it is said: The owl’s eyes have their proper use; the crane’s legs have their proper measure—severing them brings sorrow. From here, five *gu* ("thus") clauses further expound the meaning:

The owl’s eyes are bright at night and dim by day, each suited to its time. The crane’s legs are long or short as circumstance demands, each fitted to its measure. If severed with a blade, their life is harmed. The point is simply to follow their spontaneity.

Thus it is said: When wind passes, the river is diminished; when the sun passes, the river is diminished. Yet if only wind and sun were to guard the river together, the river would deem itself never disturbed—it relies on its source to flow onward. This passage returns to a more intimate point: it does not teach one to forcibly dismiss things as they arise. Rather, one must possess an inner constancy, like water with its source, to achieve this.

Thus water guards earth with certainty; a shadow guards its person with certainty; things guard their counterparts with certainty. This principle means that root and branch ever guard one another without separation—like water guarding earth, a shadow guarding its person, things guarding their counterparts—fixed and unchanging.

Thus the eye’s reliance on clarity is perilous; the ear’s reliance on acuity is perilous; the heart’s pursuit of externals is perilous. Whatever the faculty, its reliance on its storehouse is perilous. Once peril is formed, it cannot be altered. Disaster’s growth is swift and dense; its reversal depends on gradual effort; its fruition requires time. Yet people treasure these as their own jewels—is this not lamentable? Thus nations fall and peoples are slaughtered without end, yet none inquire into this.

Again, the three *dai* ("perilous") terms invert the earlier three *shen* ("certain") terms: heart, ears, and eyes—if they chase externals, they cannot remain fixed and become perilous. Expanding further, whatever one is capable of becomes harmful; the storehouse encompasses all. Once peril is formed, it cannot be altered, and disaster’s growth accelerates. To reverse peril into safety, disaster into fortune, one must follow the gradual path. Following is the work of incremental progress; even those who rigidly overcome themselves must wait long before speaking of goodness—for goodness is exceedingly difficult.

Yet the world indulges the desires of ears, eyes, and the heart’s cleverness as if they were treasures. In small matters, this brings ruin to the self; in great matters, it brings down nations and slaughters peoples—its calamity never ceases. Few indeed understand this inquiry.

Thus the foot’s contact with the ground is limited, yet it relies on what it does not tread to walk broadly. Human knowledge is scant, yet it relies on what it does not know to comprehend Heaven’s design.

To know the Great Unity, the Great Yin, the Great Eye, the Great Balance, the Great Square, the Great Faith, the Great Constancy—this is perfection. The Great Unity unites them; the Great Yin dissolves them; the Great Eye observes them; the Great Balance harmonizes them; the Great Square embodies them; the Great Faith verifies them; the Great Constancy sustains them.

There is a consummation of Heaven; there is a luminous following; there is a pivot in darkness; there is a beginning that has its counterpart. Thus, to dissolve them seems like not dissolving; to know them seems like not knowing. Only by not knowing can one come to know.

The ground the foot treads is minimal, yet what it does not tread is essential for walking. What knowledge grasps is minimal, yet what it does not grasp is the greater part. Knowing the Great Unity, the Great Yin, the Great Eye, the Great Balance, the Great Square, and the Great Faith may seem to encompass all, yet there remains an inexhaustible Heaven. One may believe they follow only what they ought, yet there is always something overseeing. One may think all is obscure, yet there is one who holds the pivot. One may think this is the beginning, yet there is always a counterpart—this counterpart then becomes the new beginning.

Following the earlier statement that the Great Yin dissolves, this ultimate inquiry makes dissolution seem like non-dissolution, knowledge like non-knowledge. Only by not knowing can one truly know.

This inquiry cannot be bounded, yet it cannot be boundless. It is slippery yet substantial; ancient and modern do not replace it, nor can it be diminished—can this not be called the great *yanghuo*? Why then remain perplexed? To resolve confusion with non-confusion and return to non-confusion is the highest non-confusion.

The earlier passage spoke of "not knowing to inquire," so here the character for "inquiry" is brought forward to conclude. If one inquires into this Dao, it has no bounds yet is never without bounds. What is spoken may seem like the debates of *hua* and *jian* (firmness and whiteness), yet here there is substance. All things undergo change, yet this remains unchanged and undiminished.

*Yanghuo*: Xu Shen’s commentary on the *Huainanzi* states it is a term for vast numbers. The explanation is still unclear. *Yang* means to raise or proclaim; *huo* means to turn the hand repeatedly. It refers to raising and answering questions while gesturing with the hands. These two characters likely reflect contemporary colloquial usage—even today, Chan masters ascend the seat to expound the Dharma in this manner. Though the Dao’s discourse may seem divergent, it is in fact real—is this not a great proclamation? Why not inquire into this instead of remaining deluded? If one can resolve another’s confusion with one’s own non-confusion, enabling them to return to non-confusion, then perhaps all may be free from confusion. Even without an inquirer, one still hopes for someone to awaken—this is how those among the immortals direct their minds.

The twenty-third scroll of the *Nanhua True Scripture: Following the Original Text* concludes.
The twenty-fourth scroll of the *Nanhua True Scripture: Following the Original Text*
Recorded by Luoling Zhu Feng’s Luo Miandao, collated by his disciple Peng Xiang

**Miscellaneous Chapters: Zeyang**

Zeyang traveled in Chu. Yi Jie spoke of him to the king, but the king did not summon him. Yi Jie returned.

A man of Peng, surnamed Peng Yang, saw King Guo and said: “Why does the Master not speak of me to the king?”
King Guo replied: “I am not equal to Gong Yuxiu.”
Peng Yang said: “What does Gong Yuxiu do?”
The king said: “In winter, he retreats alone to the river to catch turtles; in summer, he rests in the mountain thickets. If someone passes by and asks, he says: ‘This is my home.’”

Peng Yang was eager for advancement, so the king used the words of a recluse to make him reflect. “If even Yi Jie is incapable, how much more so am I? I am not even Yi Jie’s equal.”

Now, Yi Jie’s character was such that he lacked virtue but possessed cunning. He did not commit himself but used his connections to mystify his dealings, firmly entangled in the realm of wealth and honor. Such men do not aid others with virtue but instead consume them. Like one freezing who borrows clothes in spring, or one sweltering who turns to winter’s cold wind—so too is the King of Chu.

The King of Chu’s bearing was imposing and stern. In punishing offenses, he was as relentless as a tiger. Without a man of true virtue to correct him, who could sway him?

Thus, when the sage is in adversity, he makes his family forget their poverty; when he prospers, he makes kings and lords forget their ranks and emoluments, transforming them to humility. Toward things, he delights in harmony; toward people, he rejoices in their communion while preserving himself.

Thus, sometimes without speaking, he harmonizes others; standing beside them, he transforms them—like the natural bond between father and son. When he returns to his dwelling, he remains at ease in his conduct. His influence on others’ hearts is so distant that he says: “Wait for Gong Yuxiu.”

One who does not commit himself but mystifies his dealings humbles himself to follow others, leaving them unable to fathom his motives. The freezing man, upon meeting spring, finds clothing; the sweltering man, upon meeting a cold wind, reverts to winter. This illustrates that though the King of Chu is steeped in the pursuit of profit and desire, with proper guidance, he is easily swayed.

“Flatterers” refers to men like Yi Jie; “true virtue” refers to men like Gong Yuxiu. To subdue flatterers, one uses flattery; to subdue the upright, one uses the upright Way. “Transforming to humility” means becoming humble. “Standing beside others and transforming them” means standing with them and fostering the intimacy of father and son.

“Ah, that man!”—whether concealing or acting, he remains unmoved by external concerns, far removed from the common heart of profit and desire. So distant is he.

This is the meaning of “This is my home.”

The above speaks of Gong Yuxiu.

As for Yi Jie, he could not reach the point of mutual aid and dissolution—thus the persuasion of Yi Jie. As for the freezing man, who can sway him once warmth arrives? Thus the persuasion of the King of Chu. Therefore, the sage descends below, and again speaks of following Gong Yuxiu above.

The sage comprehends the entangled, encompassing all as one body, yet unaware of why it is so—this is nature. Returning to the root, reviving the mandate, and moving with Heaven as teacher, others then follow and name him thus.

Anxious in knowledge, yet his actions seldom align with the moment—how can he find stillness?

"Entangled" refers to the knotted intricacies of affairs and principles, which only the sage can fully grasp. Permeating the entire body without exception, yet unaware of its source—this is what is meant by "nature," its tranquility. Returning to the root, reviving the mandate, its movement—all stirring and arising—accords with Heaven. Others then follow and name him a sage.

Anxious in knowledge, yet his actions seldom align with the moment—this is the meaning of "though man’s years do not reach a hundred, he bears the cares of a thousand." One born beautiful, others offer him a mirror; if uninformed, he does not know his beauty surpasses others. Whether he knows or does not know, whether he hears or does not hear, his joy remains unceasing, and others’ admiration for him is likewise endless—this is nature.

The sage’s love for others: others bestow the name upon him, yet if uninformed, they do not know of his love. Whether they know or do not know, whether they hear or do not hear, his love for others remains unceasing, and their contentment in him is likewise endless—this uses man to illustrate the sage.

An old state, an old capital—gazing upon them brings boundless delight. Even if hills, grasses, and trees fill nine-tenths of the view, the delight remains; how much more when seeing what is seen and hearing what is heard, like a ten-*ren* tower hung with the music of the masses.

Ran Xiangshi grasped the center of the ring, following completion without beginning or end, without moment or interval. Daily transforming with things, he alone remains unchanging.

"Boundless delight" means joy. "Filling" means convergence. "The music of the masses" refers to the interludes of *sheng* and *yong*. "The center of the ring" is the empty space within. Gazing upon an old state or capital, none fail to rejoice; even if grasses and trees fill nine-tenths, the joy persists—how much more when seeing what is seen and hearing what is heard, like a ten-*ren* tower hung with the music of the masses? Who would not see, who would not hear?

Like Ran Xiangshi, who grasped the principle of emptiness within, daily transforming with things yet never truly changing.

He never abandons it.

How could he ever abandon it? "Abandon" means to cast aside. How could he ever elevate or set aside affairs without engaging with them?

Those who follow Heaven yet fail to attain it follow both Heaven and things to their doom. In their conduct of affairs, what can be done? The sage has never begun with Heaven, never begun with man, never begun with beginnings, never begun with things. Moving with the world without ceasing, what he practices is complete yet never stagnant. In his convergence with it, what can be done?

Tang found his charioteer, and Menyin Dengheng served as his mentor. Following the teacher without constraint, he attained the principle of following completion. In governing, he oversaw the names of names; in applying the law, he achieved dual insight.

Confucius exhausted his thoughts to serve as his mentor. The House of Rongcheng said: "Remove the days, and there are no years; no inner, no outer." Heaven is spontaneity. If one insists on discarding laws to imitate others, this is not spontaneity. Those in the world who are not at peace with their nature sacrifice themselves to things. In their conduct of affairs, what can be done? This speaks of their inability to align with the Dao in their actions.

The sage, then, transcends even the absence of Heaven. Moreover, he causes kings and lords to forget rank and emolument, transforming them to humility. In relation to things, he delights in their communion; in relation to people, he rejoices in their connection while preserving himself.

Thus, some may remain silent yet harmonize others with a single utterance; standing alongside others, they transform them to the fitting bond of father and son.

In their return to stillness, they unify the scope of their influence. As for their effect upon the minds of outsiders, it is so distant that one says: "Await Gongyue Xiu." Those who do not assert themselves to spiritually engage with others bend themselves to follow others, yet none can fathom their underlying intent.

The frozen, upon encountering spring, become as if clad in garments; the parched, upon meeting a cool breeze, revert to winter’s chill. This illustrates that even if King Chu is sunk in the depths of profit and desire, with proper guidance, he too may easily be led to follow.

"Flatterers" refers to the likes of Yi Jie; "those of upright virtue" refers to the likes of Gongyue Xiu. One subdues flatterers by bending to their flattery, while one subdues the upright by means of the upright Way.

"Transformed to humility" means transformed into humility and submission. "Standing alongside others and transforming them" refers to the fitting bond of father and son—standing alongside others, one transforms them into the intimacy of father and son.

"Ah, such a one!" is an exclamation of admiration. Whether concealing or employing their abilities, they remain unmoved by external concerns, free from the common person’s mind of profit and desire—so distant is this from the ordinary.

This is but a provisional dwelling.

The above speaks of Gongyue Xiu.

As for Yi Jie, who could not reach the point of mutual aid and dissolution, it now speaks of Yi Jie. As for one frozen in rigidity, who could bend him? This speaks of the King of Chu.

Thus, the sage, by descending, again follows from Gongyue Xiu above.

The sage comprehends the entangled and obscure, penetrating all as one body, yet unaware of why it is so—this is nature. Returning to the root and reviving the mandate, moving with Heaven as teacher, others then follow and name him thus.

Anxious in knowledge, yet his actions seldom align with the moment—when they come to rest, what then?

Entangled and obscure, only the sage can fully comprehend such matters. Permeating the whole body without exception, yet unaware of why it is so—this is what is meant by nature in its quiescence. Returning to the root and reviving the mandate, in its movement, all stirring and arising accords with Heaven. Others then follow and name him a sage.

Anxious in knowledge, yet his actions seldom align with the moment—this is like the saying that though man’s life does not span a century, he often harbors cares of a thousand years.

One born beautiful, others offer him a mirror; if not told, he does not know his beauty surpasses others. Whether knowing or not knowing, whether hearing or not hearing, his joy remains unceasing, and others’ fondness for him likewise knows no end—this is nature.

The sage’s love for others, others bestow upon him the name; if not told, he does not know his love for others. Whether knowing or not knowing, whether hearing or not hearing, his love for others remains unceasing, and others’ contentment in him likewise knows no end—this uses man to illustrate the sage.

An old state, an old capital—gazing upon them brings unbounded delight. Though hills and mounds, grasses and trees, may fill and block nine-tenths of it, still one feels unbounded delight. How much more when seeing what is seen and hearing what is heard, like a towering terrace suspended amid the throng of musicians?

Ran Xiangshi grasped the center of the ring, following completion without beginning or end, without moment or duration. Daily transforming with things, he alone remains untransformed.

Unbounded delight is joy. *Min* means convergence. *Zhòngjiān* refers to the interludes of sheng and yong. The *center of the ring* is the empty space within.

Gazing upon an old state or capital, none fail to rejoice. Though grasses and trees converge to block nine-tenths, still one rejoices—how much more when seeing what is seen and hearing what is heard, like a ten-*ren* terrace suspended amid the multitude of musicians? Like Ran Xiangshi, who grasped the principle of its empty center, daily transforming with things yet never transformed.

Would he ever abandon it?

How could he ever abandon it? *"Hé"* means "how," and *"shě"* means "to discard." How could he ever ascend to set aside affairs and not engage with them?

Those who model themselves on Heaven yet fail to attain it—both Heaven and things become their sacrifices. In their conduct of affairs, what can be done? The sage has never begun with Heaven, never begun with man, never begun with beginnings, never begun with things. Moving in step with the world without cessation, their actions are complete yet never excessive. In their convergence with the Way, what can be done?

Tang found his charioteer, and Menyin Dengheng served as his mentor. Following the teacher without constraint, he attained the principle of following completion. In governing, he oversaw the names of names, and through the law of surplus, both were made manifest. Confucius exhausted his thoughts to serve as his mentor.

The House of Rongcheng said: "Remove the days, and there are no years; no inner, no outer." Heaven is spontaneity. If one must depart from following it, it is no longer spontaneity. Yet the world contains those who are not at peace with their spontaneity, sacrificing themselves to things. In their conduct of affairs, what can be done? Their actions cannot align with the Dao.

The sage, however, even dispenses with Heaven—what then remains of man, of beginnings, of things? Though moving in step with the world, it does not hinder or obstruct. Though responding to affairs and engaging with things, their actions are thorough yet never sink into excess. In their convergence with the Dao, what can be done? It is the spontaneity of alignment with the Way.

*"Liǎng ruò zhī hé"* refers to the two perspectives. Menyin Dengheng is sometimes said to be Yi Yin. When Tang took Menyin Dengheng as his teacher, he was not confined by rules, succeeding wherever he dwelled. Yet what he achieved was no more than overseeing Tang’s reputation, ensuring Tang’s renown in the world. But reputation is an external surplus beyond one’s true lot—what benefit does it bring to the fundamental? It merely allows others to see both ruler and minister in a favorable light. Yet Confucius and his followers exhaust their thoughts, believing they have transmitted this to the lords of their age.

Later, the words of the House of Rongcheng are cited: "The reason a year is called a year is because it is named by the accumulation of three hundred and sixty days. Remove the days, and there is no year. If one can eliminate it from the subtlest stirrings of thought, then there is no burden of external names—no inner, thus no outer."

When Wei Ying made a pact with Marquis Tian Mou, the latter betrayed it. Wei Ying grew enraged and was about to send an assassin. Xishou, hearing of this, found it shameful and said: "You are a lord of ten thousand chariots, yet you would resort to the ruse of a commoner. Let me receive two hundred thousand armored troops and attack him for you. I will capture his people, seize his cattle and horses, and make his lord’s back burn with inner heat. Then I will take his state. When Ji flees, I will strike his back and break his spine."

Jizi, hearing this, found it shameful and said: "To build a wall ten *ren* high, only to tear it down once it reaches ten *ren*—this is the toil of convict laborers. Now, for seven years, no war has arisen. This is the foundation of your rule. Xian is a troublemaker; do not listen to him."

Huazi, hearing this, found it disgraceful and said: "Those who speak well of attacking Qi are troublemakers; those who speak well of not attacking are also troublemakers. Those who say that both attacking and not attacking are troublemakers—*they* are troublemakers as well."

The lord said: "Then what should be done?"
He replied: "Seek the Way, and that is all."

Huizi, hearing this, went to see Dai Jinren. Jinren said: "There is a creature called the snail—do you know it?"
The lord said: "Yes."
Jinren said: "On the left horn of the snail is a state called the House of Chu; on the right horn is a state called the House of Man. They constantly fight over territory, leaving tens of thousands of corpses. After pursuing the routed army for fifteen days, they return."
The lord said: "Alas! Is this an empty tale?"

He said: "Allow your servant to make this real for you. Does Your Majesty imagine the four directions and the realms above and below to have limits?"
The lord said: "They are without limit."
He replied: "To know the mind roams in the boundless, yet to return to the states that lie within reach—do they not seem as if they exist, as if they do not?
The lord said: "So it is."
He continued: "Within the reachable lies Wei; within Wei lies Liang; within Liang lies the king. Does the king differ from the House of Man?"
The lord said: "There is no difference."
The guest departed, and the lord was left in a daze, as if something had been lost.

When the guest had gone, Huizi said: "Blowing through a pipe produces a deep hum; blowing across a sword’s pommel yields but a faint whistle. Yao and Shun are those whom men praise. Yet to speak of Yao and Shun before Dai Jinren is like that faint whistle.

When Confucius traveled to Chu, he lodged at the thatched hut of Ant Mound. His neighbor had a servant and his wife climb to the roof. Zilu said: 'What are those bustling figures doing?'
Zhongni said: 'They are the attendants of a sage. He buries himself among the people, conceals himself at the margins. His voice fades, yet his aspiration knows no bounds. Though his mouth may speak, his heart never truly speaks. He goes against the world, yet his mind disdains to follow it—such is the one submerged in the land.'
Is this not the Market South’s Yi Liao?"

Zilu requested to go and summon him. Confucius said: "Let it be. He knows that I am aware of him, knows that I am traveling to Chu, and takes me for one who would surely have the King of Chu summon him. Moreover, he takes me for a flatterer. If that is so, then even hearing the words of a sycophant would shame him—how much more to see him in person? What would he remain for?" Zilu went to look, and his dwelling was empty.

Ant Mound’s brew-seller was one who sold brew at Ant Mound. *Ascending the ridge* means climbing to the roof beam to gaze afar. *Bustling* means a crowd. *Attendant of a sage* refers to a follower of a sage. *Margins* means borders. *Submerged on land* means dwelling on level ground yet sinking into obscurity, akin to one who hides in the marketplace. "Is this not Yi Liao of Market South?" Yi Liao likewise concealed himself in Market South, unknown to others. *What would he remain for* means he would surely flee.

The Warden of Changwu asked Zilao: "When governing, do not be reckless; when ordering the people, do not be careless. Formerly, when I cultivated grain, plowing recklessly, the harvest repaid me in kind; weeding carelessly, the harvest repaid me likewise. The following year, I changed my ways, deepened the plowing, and tended it with care. The grain flourished and multiplied, and I was sated for the whole year."

Zhuangzi, hearing this, said: "Nowadays, when men cultivate their bodies and order their minds, many resemble what the Warden calls fleeing from Heaven, departing from nature, extinguishing their emotions, losing their spirit, and acting by convention. Thus, those who recklessly treat their nature breed the shoots of desire and aversion, which become the thorns of nature—reeds and rushes. At first sprouting, they seem to support our form, but soon they uproot our nature. When desire and aversion first sprout, one believes they can support our form, only to immediately uproot and disorder our nature. *First* and *soon* echo one another—how laughable to read *reeds and rushes first sprout* as a single phrase! All at once, they burst forth in uncontrolled discharge, erupting as boils, scabies, and sores, with internal heat and oily urine. Such is the consequence of acting by convention."

*Thorns* means perverse growths. *Soon* means immediately. *Uproot* means to tear out. *Oily urine* refers to urine containing fatty discharge—all disorders of nature.

Bo Ju studied under Lao Dan and said: "I beg to wander through the world." Lao Dan said: "Enough."

The world remains thus. Bo Ju requested again, and Lao Dan said: "Where will you begin?" He replied: "I will begin in Qi."

There he saw a condemned man—a criminal prisoner. He pushed forward to honor him, and urged him to rise from his emaciation and distress. *Pushed* means to esteem and uphold. *Urged him* means to rouse him from weakness and suffering. Removing his court robes, he covered the man with them, wailed to Heaven, and wept, saying: "Alas! Alas! The world suffers great calamity, and you alone have been the first to encounter it."

He declared: "Do not steal. Do not kill." (Both *steal* and *kill* read in the departing tone.) "Only when honor and disgrace are established does one see what brings suffering; only when wealth and property are amassed does one see what sparks strife. Now, you establish what brings people suffering and amass what sparks their strife, exhausting their bodies with unending hardship. Could one hope to avoid reaching this state?"

The rulers of old held that success lay with the people, while failure lay with themselves; that uprightness lay with the people, while crookedness lay with themselves. Thus, if even a single person lost their proper form, they would withdraw and take responsibility. Today, it is not so: they conceal their actions to fool the ignorant, magnify difficulties to blame the fearful, impose heavy burdens to punish the incapable, and distance their paths to ensure punishment never reaches them. When the people know their strength is exhausted, they resort to deception to continue. With deception arising daily, how can scholars and commoners not also deceive?

When strength is insufficient, one resorts to pretense; when knowledge is insufficient, one resorts to deceit; when resources are insufficient, one resorts to theft. For the acts of theft and robbery, whom can one justly blame?

When Qu Boyu reached sixty, he transformed sixty times, never failing to begin with what he deemed right, only to end by condemning it as wrong. How could he know that what he now calls right is not the wrong of fifty-nine transformations past?

All things have life, yet none see their root; all things emerge, yet none see their gate. People all honor the knowledge of what they know, but none know to rely on the knowledge of what they do not know before attaining true understanding—can this not be called a great delusion?

Enough! Enough!

Yet there is no escape from this. Is this what is meant by "ran" and "ping-ran"? Both "yu" and "hu" are particles of doubt. This is to say, Qu Boyu believed he knew the errors of his forty-nine years, yet this cannot be called true knowledge.

Confucius questioned the Grand Historian Da□, Bo Changqian, and Xi Wei, saying: "Duke Ling of Wei indulged in wine and revelry, neglecting the governance of his state; he hunted with weapons, failing to respond to the diplomatic exchanges of the feudal lords. What made him Duke *Ling*?"

Da Qiang replied: "It is *because* of this." The first *shi* (是) means "this"; the second *shi* (是) means "what people deem right." He was posthumously named *Ling* based on what people deemed right.

Bo Changqian said: "Duke Ling had three wives, with whom he bathed promiscuously. Yet when Historian Qiao presented the ceremonial jade, he grasped the silk and supported him with reverence. Though his arrogance was so extreme, his deference to the worthy was so solemn—this is why he was called Duke *Ling*."

Xi Wei said: "When Duke Ling died, divination for burial in the ancestral tomb was inauspicious; divination for burial at Shaqu was auspicious. After digging several fathoms, they found a stone outer coffin. Washing and inspecting it, they saw an inscription: *'Do not rely on his son.'* Duke Ling seized and buried it. That he was called *Ling* was long determined. How could these two men understand it?"

*Tuan* (抟) means "to grasp"; *fu yi* (扶翼) means "to have others assist him." *"Do not rely on his son"* means his son was untrustworthy.

Little Knowledge asked Great Master Diao: "What is meant by 'village words'?"

Great Master Diao replied: "A village combines ten clans and a hundred families to form its customs, merging differences into unity and dispersing unity into differences. Now, if you point to the hundred parts of a horse and cannot obtain the horse, yet the horse stands before you, it is by assembling its hundred parts that you call it a horse. Thus, hills accumulate the lowly to become high; rivers converge waters to become great; the great person unites all to become impartial. Therefore, what enters from without has a master but is not clung to; what emerges from within has a norm but is not resisted. The four seasons have distinct qi, yet Heaven does not bestow favor, and so the year is completed. The five offices have distinct duties, yet the ruler does not act for private ends, and so there is no fame. Without fame, there is non-action; with non-action, there is nothing not done. Time has endings and beginnings; the world has changes and transformations. Fortune and misfortune are substantial, yet what seems adverse may be fitting, and what is pursued may differ in outcome. Those who seek to correct may err. Compared to a great marsh, all materials find their measure; observed in a great mountain, trees and stones share the same ground. This is what is meant by 'village words.'"

"The great person unites all to become impartial. Thus, what enters from without has a public Dao as its master and is not clung to; what emerges from within has a public Dao to align with and is not resisted." *"Does not bestow favor"* means not treating it as a personal favor. *"Time has endings and beginnings; the world has changes and transformations"*—one cannot cling to uniformity. *"Substantial"* means real. Fortune for the good and misfortune for the excessive are both real principles. What seems adverse may be fitting—like the old man who lost his horse, which may not have been misfortune. *"Those who pursue may differ in outcome; those who seek to correct may err"*—people each follow their private inclinations, like faces differing, and in seeking to correct, they may instead go astray.

Little Knowledge said: "Then is this sufficient to be called the Dao?"

Great Master Diao said: "No. Now, the number of things is not limited to ten thousand, yet we call them 'the ten thousand things' because we use the term for the greatest number to refer to them. Thus, Heaven and Earth are the greatest of forms; yin and yang are the greatest of qi; the Dao is their impartiality. To use their greatness to name and refer to them is acceptable, but once a name exists, how can it be compared? If you argue thus, it is like comparing dogs and horses—how far it falls short!"

"Once the name of the Dao exists, how can it be compared to other things? If you compare it to other things, it is like using dogs and horses as analogies—it grows ever more distant."

Little Knowledge said: "Within the four directions, encompassing the six directions, where do the myriad things arise?"

Great Master Diao said: "Yin and yang illuminate and cover each other, govern and succeed one another; the four seasons replace, give birth to, and destroy one another. Desires and aversions, departures and approaches arise in turn. Male and female unite in pairs, and thus there is constant existence. Safety and danger alternate; fortune and misfortune give rise to one another; urgency and leisure rub against each other; gathering and scattering form completion. These are the names and realities that can be recorded, the essences that can be noted. Following the sequence of their principles, the turning of their movements, exhaustion returns to the beginning, and endings return to the start—this is what things possess, what words exhaust, what knowledge reaches, nothing more than the limits of things. The person who perceives the Dao does not follow what is abandoned, nor trace what arises—this is where discussion ceases."

*"Arise in turn"* means rising prominently. *"Unite in pairs"* means separating and joining. *"Essences that can be noted"* means recording the subtle and refined. *"What words exhaust, what knowledge reaches"*—nothing more than the limits of things. Only the person who perceives the Dao does not follow the endings of things nor trace their beginnings, returning them to nothingness. This is why debate ceases.

Little Knowledge said: "Ji Zhen’s 'non-action' and Jie Zi’s 'someone causes it'—which of these two theories accords with reality, and which is biased in its reasoning?"

Great Master Diao said: "Cocks crow and dogs bark—this is what people know. Even with great wisdom, one cannot describe in words the transformations they undergo, nor anticipate in thought what they will do. Analyzing this, the essence reaches the unparalleled, the greatness reaches the unbounded. 'Someone causes it' and 'non-action' cannot escape from things and ultimately err. *Non-action* means there is no one who acts."

Mencius said: "That which acts though nothing acts is Heaven." The "someone causes it" theory holds that something causes things to be so. Mencius said: "Action is caused by something." Thus the two doctrines differ in this way. *Si* (斯) means "to split apart." As the *Odes* says: "With an axe one splits it; splitting and analyzing it, the subtle reaches the unparalleled, the great reaches the unbounded." What the *Odes* calls "fur still has its patterns" is surpassed by "the workings of high Heaven, without sound, without scent"—this is the ultimate. The theories of "someone causes it" and "non-action" cannot escape from things and ultimately remain flawed. "Someone causes it" is substantive; "non-action" is empty. That which has name and substance dwells among things; that which has no name and no substance resides in the emptiness of things. What can be spoken or conceived—speaking of it only increases its remoteness. What has not yet arisen cannot be feared; what has already perished cannot be obstructed. Life and death are not distant, yet their principle cannot be seen. "Someone causes it" and "non-action" are but suppositions of doubt. Observing their root, their going is without end; seeking their end, their coming is without cease. Without end, without cease—speaking of it is nothingness, sharing the principle of things. "Someone causes it" and "non-action" are the root of speech, beginning and ending with things. The Dao cannot be possessed; what is possessed cannot be without it.
