The name "Dao" is but a borrowed vehicle for its movement. "Someone causes it" and "non-action" are but partial aspects of things—how could they encompass the great square? If words suffice, then speaking all day exhausts the Dao; if words do not suffice, then speaking all day exhausts things. The Dao is the ultimate of things; speech and silence cannot contain it. Neither speech nor silence—discourse has its limits. It cannot be feared, nor can it be forbidden.

*The Nanhua True Scripture, Following the Root*, Scroll 24 ends.
*The Nanhua True Scripture, Following the Root*, Scroll 25
Recorded by Lu Ling’s Bamboo Peak, Luo Miandao; collated by disciple Peng Xiang.

**Miscellaneous Chapters: External Things**

External things cannot be relied upon—thus Longfeng was executed, Bi Gan slain, Jizi feigned madness, Elai put to death, and Jie and Zhou perished. No ruler fails to desire loyalty from his ministers, yet loyalty does not always earn trust. Thus Wu Yun was cast into the river, Chang Hong died in Shu, his blood concealed, and after three years transformed into jade. No parent fails to desire filial piety from their children, yet filial piety does not always bring love. Thus Xiaoji grieved and Zeng Shen mourned.

When wood rubs against wood, it ignites; when metal is held to fire, it melts. When yin and yang clash in their courses, heaven and earth convulse in great turmoil. *Gai* (絯) means "to bind"—thus thunder and lightning arise, fire emerges within water, and the great locust tree is consumed. Thunder and lightning are fire within water. Fire from thunder can burn wood, yet only the locust is mentioned, for it is the wood used to kindle fire.

When one is deeply troubled yet finds no escape, *chen* (螴) and *dun* (蜳) cannot take form. The heart hangs suspended between heaven and earth, stifled and restless, drowned and knotted. When profit and harm grind against one another, the fire they generate is extreme. The multitude burns away their harmony, and even the moon, inherently yin, cannot withstand the fire’s ascent. Thus the Way collapses utterly.

This speaks again of the fire within the human heart—how one may be deeply troubled yet find no escape, as in the earlier examples of wood rubbing wood or metal held to fire, yet ultimately unable to flee. For the heart cannot forget profit and harm. *Chen* and *dun* describe insects stirring from hibernation yet still torpid. Affairs fail to come to fruition in this way. When things do not succeed, the heart hangs suspended between heaven and earth, oppressed and agitated, submerged and bound. With these afflictions, profit and harm grind together, and the heart’s fire grows ever more intense—not merely a fleeting flame like fire in water. The multitude, burning away the harmony of their nature, turns even the moon, which belongs to yin, into fire, unable to endure its excess. The subtlety of heaven’s principle cannot withstand the blazing of human desire, and so the Way crumbles and is lost.

Zhuang Zhou was poor, so he went to borrow grain from the Marquis of the River. The Marquis replied, "Very well."

"I shall soon receive the gold from my fief—may I lend you three hundred pieces? Would that be acceptable?"

Zhuang Zhou’s face flushed with anger as he replied: "Yesterday, as I came, I heard a voice calling from the middle of the road. I turned to look and saw a carp in the rut of a cart wheel. I asked, 'Little carp, what brings you here?' It answered, 'I am a wave-minister of the Eastern Sea. Might you spare a dou or sheng of water to keep me alive?' I said, 'Very well. I am about to journey south to visit the kings of Wu and Yue. I shall divert the waters of the Western River to bring you home—would that do?' The carp’s face darkened with rage. 'I have lost the water that was my constant companion, my accustomed home. I have nowhere to go. A dou or sheng would be enough to keep me alive—just that much. Yet you speak as you do! You would do better to seek me now in the dried-fish market.'"

Master Ren cast a great hook with a thick black line, baited with fifty castrated oxen, and crouched on Mount Kuaiji, casting his rod into the Eastern Sea. Day after day he fished, but for a year he caught nothing. Then a great fish took the bait, dragging the massive hook down in a plunge, thrashing its fins until white waves towered like mountains. The sea churned, its roar shaking the earth for a thousand li, as though gods and demons had been unleashed. When Master Ren landed the fish, he butchered and dried it. From the Zhe River eastward and Cangwu northward, none went unsatisfied by its flesh.

Yet later generations—those who weigh talent and debate men’s worth, those who recite and parrot what is already settled—all gaped in astonishment and spread the tale. But those who brandish their poles with thin lines, scurry to irrigation ditches, and watch over minnows and carp—how hard it is for them to catch a great fish! Those who adorn petty words to seek high office are as far from true attainment as the sky from the earth. Thus, one who has never heard of Master Ren’s way of life is far indeed from being fit to guide the world.

The Confucians rob graves with *Odes* and *Documents* in hand. A great Confucian calls down from above: "Dawn breaks in the east—how goes the work?" A lesser Confucian replies, "The skirt and jacket are not yet removed, but there is a pearl in the mouth." The *Ode* says: *Green grows the wheat, / upon the mound it springs. / In life he gave no alms— / why hold a pearl in death?* They seize the corpse’s temples, press its cheeks, and with a metal mallet pry open the jaw, carefully working from the side to split the flesh without damaging the pearl in the mouth.

They even compose poems about grave-robbing Confucians, from *"Green grows the wheat"* to *"Do no harm to the pearl in the river."* If one takes these as poems of only four lines, the rest must be the great Confucian’s instructions.

A disciple of Lao Lai was gathering firewood when he encountered Confucius. Returning, he reported: "There is a man over there—tall above, narrow below, slightly hunched, ears set back. His gaze seems fixed on the four seas. I do not know whose son he is." Lao Lai said, "That is Qiu. Summon him." When Confucius arrived, Lao Lai said, "Qiu, cast off your bodily pride and your cultivated knowledge—then you may be called a noble man." Confucius bowed and withdrew, his expression troubled. He asked, "May I advance in my work?" Lao Lai replied, "To bear the wound of a single generation and yet act heedless of ten thousand generations’ peril—is this not stubborn poverty? Or have you simply failed to consider the consequences? To curry favor with delight is to court lifelong disgrace—this is but the conduct of the common crowd. Men lead one another by reputation, bind one another with obscurity. Rather than praise Yao and condemn Jie, it would be better to forget both and close the door on such judgments. To turn back is to invite harm; to act is to risk error. The sage hesitates to initiate affairs, yet each endeavor succeeds. Why, then, do you persist in your pride? Tall above’ means a long upper body; ‘narrow below’ means slender legs—said to be three inches shorter than Yu’s. ‘Slightly hunched’ means a slightly curved back. ‘Ears set back’ means they are not visible when viewed from the front. ‘Bodily pride’ is self-importance; ‘cultivated knowledge’ is polishing one’s appearance to seek recognition. ‘Heedless’ means acting without regard. ‘Heedless of ten thousand generations’ peril’ means acting as though one’s choices have no lasting consequences. ‘Stubborn’ is a turn of phrase: *Are you so stubbornly poor as to do this? Or have you simply failed to think it through?* ‘To curry favor with delight’ means flattering others to win their affection, unaware that this invites calamity for generations. Thus it is said: *To curry favor with delight is to court disaster.* Though you may see this as delight, it is lifelong disgrace—nothing more than the conduct of the common crowd, advancing no further. Men lead one another by reputation, bind one another with obscurity—nothing more."

Concealment is obscurity. Confucius often praised Yao and condemned Jie, so his teaching went: *Rather than laud Yao and denounce Jie, better to forget both praise and blame—indeed, to dispense with praise altogether.* To act contrary to this principle is to invite harm; to act recklessly is to invite perversity.

The sage undertakes affairs with hesitation, as though compelled, and thus invariably succeeds. *Carrying out* means *acting.* How could it be otherwise? If one persists in such pride to the end, this is a warning against such persistence.

Duke Yuan of Song dreamed in the dead of night of a man with disheveled hair peering through the side gate. Beside the gate, he said: *I come from Zailu’s deep abyss, where I am known as the Luminous One. I serve as the emissary of the Clear River to the River Lord. The fisherman Yu Qiu caught me by surprise.* The duke awoke and had the dream interpreted. The diviner said: *This is a spirit-turtle.* The duke asked: *Is there a fisherman named Yu Qiu?* His attendants replied: *There is.* The duke said: *Summon Yu Qiu to court.*

The next day, Yu Qiu appeared. The duke asked: *What did you catch?* He replied: *My net snared a white turtle, five feet in circumference.* The duke said: *Present your turtle.* When the turtle arrived, the duke wavered—first wanting to kill it, then to spare it. Unsure, he consulted the oracle, which said: *Slay the turtle for divination—it will be auspicious.* So they split the turtle open, drilling seventy-two times without a single miscast.

Confucius remarked: *The spirit-turtle could appear in Duke Yuan’s dream yet could not escape Yu Qiu’s net. It could endure seventy-two divinations without error yet could not avoid the disaster of having its entrails carved out. Thus, even wisdom has its limits, and spirit its inadequacies. Though one may possess supreme knowledge, it is best to consult the many.*

Fish do not fear nets but fear pelicans. Discard petty cleverness, and great wisdom becomes clear; abandon contrived goodness, and true goodness arises. A newborn babe, though untaught by any master, learns to speak—simply by dwelling among those who can. Even with supreme knowledge, one does not compare to consulting the many. Fish fear pelicans, not nets—this is the fish’s ignorance. They know the pelican can harm them, yet the net comes from where they least expect. Little do they realize that the pelican’s harm is small, while the net’s is great.

When a person discards petty cleverness, great wisdom becomes clear; when one ceases to pride oneself on goodness, true goodness arises naturally. Like a babe who learns speech without a master—simply by dwelling among speakers. Even the wise should consult the many.

Hui Shi said to Zhuangzi: *Your words are useless.* Zhuangzi replied: *Only when one understands uselessness can we speak of use. The earth is vast and great, yet what man uses is only enough to stand on. But if you dug beneath your feet to the Yellow Springs, would the ground still be of use?* Hui Shi said: *It would be useless.* Zhuangzi said: *Then the usefulness of the useless is clear.*

*To place the feet* means *to set them down.* *To sink* means *to fall through.* Zhuangzi said: *If a man can wander freely, how could he not wander? If a man cannot wander, how could he wander at all?* Those with *drifting aspirations* and *decisive actions*—alas, are these not beyond the capacity of the supremely wise and profoundly virtuous? They plunge headlong without turning back, rush like fire without a glance behind. Though they may serve as ruler and minister for a time, when the age changes, they will no longer hold sway over one another. Thus it is said: *The perfected person does not linger in their actions.*

To honor the past and disparage the present is the way of scholars. If we observe the present age through the lens of the Xuwei clan, who could avoid being swept along? Only the perfected person can wander through the world without bias, adapting to others without losing themselves. They do not study others’ teachings, yet align with their intent without opposition.

*To wander* means to float freely between heaven and earth, moving with the world without conflict. Is there one who can wander yet chooses not to? Or one who cannot wander yet does? Some can, some cannot. The following speaks of those who *drift* and *sever ties*—those who cannot wander—while the perfected person can.

*Drifting aspirations* means indulging desires without restraint, forgetting to return. *Decisive actions* means acting with absolute resolve, beyond persuasion. Alas, these are not the province of the supremely wise and profoundly virtuous. For when wisdom is perfected, one does not drift; when virtue is profound, one does not sever ties. Such people, like one teetering on the edge of a precipice, do not retreat; like fire spreading unchecked, they do not look back. Though they may submit as ministers for a time, when the age changes, they no longer hold authority—all distinctions of rank vanish.

Having spoken of what is not good, let us speak of what is. Consider the ancient sage-kings like the Xuwei clan: in this present age, people follow the current, honoring their own rulers and no longer mentioning the Xuwei. Only the perfected person can bend yet wander through the world without bias or eccentricity. Though they adapt to others, they do not lose themselves. They do not study others’ teachings, yet align with their intent without opposition—like the debates of the Confucians and Mohists, following nature as though no division exists.

Eyes that penetrate are called *bright*; ears that penetrate are called *keen*; noses that penetrate are called *sensitive*; mouths that penetrate are called *discerning*; minds that penetrate are called *wise*; wisdom that penetrates is called *virtue.* The Dao does not tolerate blockage—blockage leads to choking, and if choking persists, it leads to trampling, from which a multitude of harms arise.

Living things rely on breath, yet if it is not abundant, it is not heaven’s fault. Heaven bores through them day and night without cease, yet man clogs their openings. *Sensitive* means *responsive.* *Penetrate* means *unobstructed.* The ears’ unobstructedness is *keenness*; the eyes’, *brightness*; the nose’s unobstructedness allows it to sense qi, causing the head to *quiver.* *Choking* means *obstructing the throat.* *Trampling* means *being trodden upon*—like feet unsteady in walking, as though trampled by others.

The three yin meridians of the foot all originate from the toes and ascend along the throat—is this not so? Qi flows through the channels and collaterals; thus, if choking persists unchecked, it transforms into foot ailments. Once foot ailments arise, a multitude of harms follow—not merely choking and trampling.

This illustrates that the Dao must not be obstructed; obstruction breeds afflictions of this kind. *Abundant* means *flourishing*. It is further said that sentient beings rely on the free circulation of breath to sustain this body. If their breath is not flourishing, it is not heaven’s doing. Heaven bores through human orifices, and qi circulates day and night without cease. Yet man himself clogs his own apertures. *Descend* means *to sink downward*. The breath within the human body all rises from below; if it descends without ascending, death ensues. This analogy of breath serves to illustrate the heart.

The *bao* (womb/bladder) has layered chambers; the character *gen* (艮) lacks a dot, signifying that the heart possesses *heavenly wandering*. If a chamber has no emptiness, then mother and daughter-in-law quarrel within; if the heart has no heavenly wandering, then the six senses contend. The great forests and towering mountains seem virtuous to men only because their spirits are unequal to them.

Virtue overflows into fame, and fame overflows into ostentation. Schemes deepen through contention; barriers arise from clinging to office. Affairs are settled by the consensus of the many. In spring’s timely rains, grasses and trees burst forth in growth, and only then do the tools of cultivation—hoes and plows—begin their work. Yet more than half of the plants that are *inverted* (planted upside down) thrive unknowingly.

*Chonglang* refers to layered thresholds, like the empty spaces within the *bao*. The *bao* has layered thresholds, while the heart possesses heavenly wandering. If a chamber has no emptiness, then mother and daughter-in-law are pressed together before one’s eyes. For when a chamber has empty space, mother and daughter-in-law may dwell in ease. If the heart lacks heavenly wandering, the six senses vie for dominance. Yet if the heart has a place for natural wandering, do passions and desires not contend?

Likewise, when men behold great forests and mountains and deem them virtuous, it is because their minds are narrow and their spirits cannot match them. If one’s spirit were sufficient to meet them, why would one praise them?

Virtue resides within, yet fame causes it to overflow; fame itself is colorless, yet ostentation causes it to overflow. *Overflow* is like water spilling beyond its bounds. Schemes, though originally without deep calculation, grow more entrenched through the desire to entice others. *Chai* (barriers) follows the meaning of *chai zha* (palisades) from the *Nei Tian Di* chapter. It refers to the fortified posts where officials stand guard, erecting palisades for defense. Palisades do not naturally arise in official precincts; affairs are not inherently fixed but are resolved by what the multitude deems fitting—all depart from the natural.

For example, in the season of spring rains, grasses and trees naturally flourish, yet only then do men sharpen their hoes and plows, harming growth and contravening nature. *Inverted* means *reversed*. Stillness can remedy illness; *zi* (目病, eye ailments) can ease aging; tranquility can halt haste. Yet these are the concerns of the toiling, not the idle who have never inquired; they are what startle the world, yet the spirit-like man has never inquired; they are what startle the state, yet the worthy man has never inquired.

In Yanmen, when a man’s kin died, he was appointed an official for his skill in mourning. Half his fellow villagers followed his example and mourned themselves to death. *Yanmen* was the name of a village. *Official* here means *leader*.

When Yao offered the world to Xu You, Xu You fled; when Tang offered it to Wu Guang, Wu Guang grew angry. When Ji Ta heard of this, he led his disciples to crouch by the Kuai River, and the feudal lords came to offer condolences. Three years later, Shen Tu Di drowned himself in the Yellow River.

The *quan* (basket trap) is for catching fish, yet once the fish is caught, the trap is forgotten; the *ti* (snare) is for catching hares, yet once the hare is caught, the snare is forgotten; words are for conveying meaning, yet once the meaning is grasped, the words are forgotten. Where might I find a man who has forgotten words, that I might speak with him?

*The Twenty-Fifth Scroll of the Circulating Edition of the Southern Florescence True Scripture Concludes*
*The Twenty-Sixth Scroll of the Circulating Edition of the Southern Florescence True Scripture*

Lulu Bamboo Peak’s Luo Miandao, as recorded by his disciple Peng Xiang, collates and punctuates:

*Miscellaneous Chapters: Parables*

Parables make up nine-tenths, weighty words seven-tenths, and goblet words emerge daily, harmonized by the Heavenly Boundary.

A *parable* is speech by borrowed authority.
*Weighty words* are speech spoken with solemnity.
*Goblet words* are speech like wine poured in celebration.

"Nine-tenths" and "seven-tenths" mean that nine or seven parts out of ten are so.
"Emerge daily" means that such words arise day after day, not merely nine or seven parts out of ten.

Parables, nine-tenths of all, rely on external discourse.
A loving father does not act as matchmaker for his own son.
If the father praises him, it is not as convincing as if someone else did.
It is not my fault—it is the fault of others.
When something aligns with oneself, one responds; when it does not, one resists.
What aligns with oneself is deemed right; what differs is deemed wrong.

A loving father does not act as matchmaker for his son because he cannot recommend or advance him.
A father cannot praise his own son; he must rely on others to praise him.
This shows that words spoken by oneself are not trusted; only when borrowed from others do people begin to believe.
Yet this borrowing is not my fault—it is the fault of others.
People only favor what aligns with themselves and dislike what differs.
Thus, my words will not be believed, leaving no choice but to borrow authority.

Weighty words, seven-tenths, serve to express one’s own speech.
These are the words of elders, whose years come first.
Yet if one lacks the warp and weft of beginnings and ends, merely counting years to claim elder status, this is not true precedence.
If one cannot surpass others, one lacks the Way of humanity.
One who lacks the Way of humanity is called a "worn-out person."

Weighty words are meant to end disputes, and such solemn speech must come from those of mature experience.
People respect those called elders, yet true elders are not merely those whose years come first.
If one’s years come first but one’s words lack structure and coherence, merely relying on advanced age to claim seniority, this is not true precedence.
What makes one surpass others is the ability to fulfill the Way of humanity.
If one cannot fulfill the Way of humanity, then despite long years, one is but a long-lasting, useless person.

Goblet words emerge daily, harmonized by the Heavenly Boundary, flowing and extending to exhaust the years.
Without speech, all is equal.
Equality and speech are not equal; speech and equality are not equal.
Thus it is said: "Speak no words."
To speak of "no words" is to speak all one’s life yet never have spoken;
To never speak all one’s life is to never have failed to speak.

There is a source from which something is permissible, and a source from which it is not;
There is a source from which something is so, and a source from which it is not.

Whence comes "so"? "So" is so in its being so.
Whence comes "not so"? "Not so" is not so in its being not so.
Whence comes "permissible"? "Permissible" is permissible in its being permissible.
Whence comes "impermissible"? "Impermissible" is impermissible in its being impermissible.

Things inherently have that which makes them so; things inherently have that which makes them permissible.
Yet there is nothing that is not so, nothing that is not permissible.
Without goblet words emerging daily, harmonized by the Heavenly Boundary, who could attain such enduring constancy?

All things are seeds, succeeding one another in different forms, beginning and end like a ring—none can grasp their order. This is called the Heavenly Equality.
The Heavenly Equality is the Heavenly Boundary.

Those who have already seen the "Equalizing Things" chapter need no further redundant explanation.

Without speech, things naturally equalize; with speech, right and wrong arise in confusion, and equality is lost.
This is why equality and speech, speech and equality, are all unequal.

"Speak no words"—to speak of "no words" is speech without words, without any verbal expression.
It is like music without sound.
If one speaks all one’s life yet fails to transform others, such speech is as if non-existent—this is to never have spoken.
If one never speaks all one’s life yet others are naturally transformed, then though one does not speak, it is as if one has taught them—this is to never have failed to speak.

Is it permissible or not? Is it so or not so?

Though all things have their origins, how can I claim to know them?
I but affirm what is affirmed, approve what is approved—nothing more.
Things inherently possess their own "thusness" and "rightness," their own "approval."
I too deem nothing not thus, nothing not permissible.

It is not that the goblet words emerge daily, harmonizing with Heaven’s Boundary—how else could they endure without contention?
The character "enduring" (久) corresponds to "daily emergence" (日出).

Expanding further: all things under Heaven share a single root, born of the Supreme Ultimate, the two qi, and the Five Phases—only their forms differ.
In truth, this principle and this qi alternate through expansion and contraction, waxing and waning, succeeding one another in an endless cycle without discernible order.
This is what is called Heaven’s Equality.
"Equality" means sameness.
Why insist on disputing right and wrong?

Heaven’s Equality is precisely Heaven’s Boundary, for its initial signs were already thus.
Zhuangzi said to Huizi: "Confucius lived sixty years, and in sixty years he transformed. What he once deemed right, he later deemed wrong. Who knows whether what he now calls right is not what he deemed wrong at fifty-nine?"
Huizi replied: "Confucius toiled his will and pursued knowledge."
Zhuangzi said: "Confucius has renounced such things, though he never spoke of it.
Confucius said: 'One receives one’s endowment from the Great Root and restores one’s numinous essence to life. When one sings, it accords with the pitch-pipes; when one speaks, it accords with the law. Profit and righteousness are set before one, yet likes and dislikes, right and wrong merely subdue people’s mouths. To make people submit in their hearts and dare not raise a contrary word, to establish a standard and settle the world’s settled—enough, enough! How could I ever reach such a one?'"

"Pursuing knowledge" means laboring in the pursuit of knowledge.
"Endowment" is as Mencius said: "Heaven’s bestowed endowment."
"The Great Root" is the Great Origin.
"Restoring the numinous" means the human heart is empty and numinous, yet obscured by material desires—restoring it returns it to its original state.
"Contrary" (蘁) means opposition.
"Settling the world’s settled" means the world has its own settled principles; one merely settles what is already settled.

When Huizi heard Zhuangzi say Confucius transformed at sixty, he thought Confucius merely toiled his will and pursued knowledge, failing to achieve true transformation.
Zhuangzi said: "Not so. Confucius said: 'One receives one’s nature from the Origin and returns to one’s empty numinosity. In life, when one sings, it accords with the pitch-pipes; when one speaks, it accords with the law. Profit and righteousness are set before one, yet one knows only likes and dislikes, right and wrong—this is the work of the Confucians, merely subduing people’s mouths. As for making people submit in their hearts and dare not oppose, this alone is the Way of the Perfected Person.' He then praised him, saying: 'Is the Way limited to this? Is it limited to this? How could I ever reach such a Perfected Person?'"
All from "enough, enough" onward are Confucius’ words.

Master Zeng served twice, and his heart transformed twice. He said: "When I first served to provide for my parents, three *fu* brought me joy; later, when I served, three thousand *zhong* were not enough, and my heart grieved."
A disciple asked Confucius: "Can Zeng Shen be said to have no attachments?"
Confucius said: "He already has attachments. How could one without attachments feel sorrow?"
To him, three *fu* and three thousand *zhong* were like watching sparrows, mosquitoes, and gnats pass before him.
"Attachments" (縣) means entanglements.

"To say he had 'no entanglements in his transgressions' means that Zeng Shen’s service to his parents could be called utmost filial piety, with no fault to be found. Confucius said: 'His transgressions are already exposed. How could one without fault be moved to sorrow by grief? To him, three *fu* and three thousand *zhong* were like sparrows, mosquitoes, and gnats passing before him—what merit is there to speak of! Three thousand *zhong* were as sparrows, three *fu* as mosquitoes and gnats.'

Yan Chengzi You said to Dongguo Ziqi: 'Since I heard your teachings, in the first year I became rustic; in the second, I followed; in the third, I penetrated; in the fourth, things came to me; in the fifth, they arrived; in the sixth, spirits entered; in the seventh, Heaven was completed; in the eighth, I knew neither death nor life; in the ninth, I attained the Great Mystery.
In the first year, I returned to simplicity; in the second, I knew where to enter; in the third, nothing was beyond my reach; in the fourth, all things served me; in the fifth, they came as I summoned; in the sixth, spirits and gods drew near; in the seventh, I became one with Heaven; in the eighth, neither life nor death could move my heart; in the ninth, the divine mystery was unfathomable.'

Life (生, pronounced as *qù* in *yǒuwéi*) is death. The passage exhorts Lord Gong that death has its causes, while life is yang and has no cause. Is this truly so? Where does one go? Where does one not go?

*Quàn* (勸) means to exhort. *Gōng* (公) refers to the deceased. *Zì* (自) means 'cause'—the living act with purpose, while the dead are exhorted thus: Lord Gong believed death must have a cause, such as illness or execution, all brought about by human agency, whereas life arises naturally from yang qi, not by human effort. Is this teaching truly so? Where should one follow? Where should one not?"

Heaven has its celestial reckonings, earth has its human □—where should I seek them? None know where it ends; how then can there be no fate? None know where it begins; how then can there be fate? When there is resonance, how can there be no spirits? When there is no resonance, how can there be spirits?

Those who discourse on Heaven speak of the laws of celestial reckonings. As for Earth, there are the traces of human investigation □. Are these truly sufficient to exhaust the principles of Heaven and Earth? Where should I seek them?

Humans cannot be certain of their ultimate ends—wealth or poverty, nobility or baseness, long life or early death—how can one be sure there is a governing power? How can one say there is no fate? Yet at the moment of bestowal, who truly enacts it? How can one say there is fate? When good fortune befalls the virtuous and calamity the wicked, there is resonance—how can one say there are no spirits? Yet when Yan [Hui] dies young and Zhi [the Robber] lives long, there is no resonance—how can one say there are spirits? These are all words meant to awaken the deceased.

The crowd of *wangliang* spirits asked the Shadow: "Before, you bowed your head; now, you raise it. Before, your hair was bound; now, it is loose. Before, you sat; now, you stand. Before, you walked; now, you stop. Why is this?"

The Shadow replied: "Why do you, old one, ask such trifling questions? I act, yet I know not why. I am like the cicada’s molted husk, the snake’s sloughed skin—seemingly so, yet not so. Fire and sunlight are my gathering; yin and night are my succession. That—do I depend on it? How much more so those who depend on what depends! When it comes, I come with it; when it goes, I go with it. When it is strong and yang, I am strong and yang with it. What question is there in strong yang?"

*Kuo* means bound hair. *Sou* is a term for an old man. *Shao* means "slightly." *Bi* refers to form. *Kuang hu* is used for emphasis.

"Strong yang" refers to qi of the yang that is not upright.

The relationship between *wangliang* and shadow is like that of an old man to another old man. When two old men meet in this world, how much time do they have to question one another? It is but a trifling moment. Why must you ask even slightly?

"I" refers to the shadow speaking of itself. Though I possess this shadow, I know not why it is so. Thus, one might say the shadow is born of form, like the cicada’s husk or the snake’s sloughed skin—this explanation seems so, yet is not. The husk still belongs to the cicada; the sloughed skin still belongs to the snake. But the shadow? When fire and sunlight shine upon it, it gathers. When yin and night descend, it departs. Without fire or sunlight, even with form, there can be no shadow. From this, it is clear that form, though it may give rise to my shadow, must depend on something. How much more so *wangliang*, which relies on what depends! When form comes and goes, I come and go with it. When form is strong and yang, I am strong and yang with it. Yet strong yang is not the true essence of blood and qi, and the shadow that follows it to manifest form is likewise unreal—what question is there to ask?

This passage repeats earlier words yet surpasses them in subtlety, refining the old man’s analogy. The depth of this elder’s mind is boundless; one cannot say it falls short of the Inner Chapters.

Yang Ziju journeyed south to Pei, while Lao Dan traveled west to Qin. They met by chance at the outskirts of Liang. Midway, Laozi looked up to the heavens and sighed, saying: "At first, I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot." Yang Ziju made no reply. Upon reaching their lodging, he brought water for washing, a towel and comb, removed his shoes outside the door, and advanced on his knees, saying: "Earlier, your disciple wished to ask you, Master, but you were walking without pause, so I dared not. Now that you have paused, I beg to know my fault."

Laozi said: "Your haughty and overbearing demeanor—with whom could you dwell? The purest white appears tainted; the greatest virtue seems insufficient."

Yang Ziju’s expression changed abruptly, and he said: "I humbly receive your instruction."

On his departure, the innkeeper and his household came to greet him. The inn’s master held the mat, his wife the towel and comb; the guests stepped aside from their seats, and the cook left the hearth. On his return, the innkeeper vied with him for a seat.

Having heard Laozi’s words, Yang Ziju deeply restrained and humbled himself, withdrawing into obscurity, so that others no longer scrutinized him.

Now, following Su Shi of the Eastern Slope’s interpretation, I have removed the four chapters *Yielding the Throne*, *Robber Zhi*, *Discoursing on the Sword*, and *The Old Fisherman*, and appended *Lie Yukou’s* encounter with Bohun Wuren in Qi while returning mid-journey, merging them into a single parable.

Su Shi’s *Record of the Zhuangzi Shrine* states: "One may doubt whether *Robber Zhi* and *The Old Fisherman* truly slander Confucius, while *Yielding the Throne* and *Discoursing on the Sword* are shallow and far from the Dao. Upon repeated reading, I discerned their allegorical intent, concluding with: 'Yang Ziju traveled west to Qin and met Laozi. On his departure, the innkeeper and his household came to greet him—the master held the mat, his wife the towel and comb; the guests stepped aside from their seats, and the cook left the hearth. On his return, they vied with him for a seat.' Removing the four chapters *Yielding the Throne*, *Discoursing on the Sword*, *The Old Fisherman*, and *Robber Zhi*, I merged this with *Lie Yukou’s* return mid-journey to Qi, saying: 'I was startled. I dined at ten broth-sellers’ stalls, and five served me first.' Then I understood and laughed: 'This was indeed one chapter.' Those who fail to grasp Zhuangzi’s words to the end merely splice them into his text."

Lie Yukou was traveling to Qi when he turned back midway and encountered Bohun Wuren. Bohun Wuren asked: "Why have you returned?"

He replied: "I was startled."

"Startled by what?"

"I once dined at ten broth-sellers’ stalls, and five served me first."

Bohun Wuren said: "If so, why be startled?"

He replied: "When inner sincerity is unresolved, outward demeanor gleams with false brilliance, overawing others’ hearts, causing them to slight the venerable and esteemed and to focus on their concerns. 'Inner sincerity unresolved' means one cannot attain emptiness within. 'Outward demeanor gleams with false brilliance' means one cannot rest in tracelessness. Outwardly, one may spy and craft a radiant display, thus becoming an object of scrutiny. 'To slight the venerable and esteemed' means one does not inquire whether others possess the Dao but merely honors them for their age and seniority. *Ni* means 'fermented' or 'aged.' 'Their concerns' refer to abandoning one’s natural state to engage in external deference, which is deemed wearisome. The broth-sellers merely peddle food and surplus goods; their profit is meager, their influence slight. Yet even they act thus—how much more so the lord of ten thousand chariots? He labors for the state and exhausts his knowledge in affairs. They will entrust me with tasks and demand results. That is why I was startled."

Bohun Wuren said: "Well observed! Now that you have settled yourself, others will protect you."

Shortly after, he went to visit, and the shoes outside the door filled the entrance. "Protect you" means others will gather to shelter him. The *Annals of Han* records that Xiao and Cao feared crossing the city to protect the High Ancestor—its meaning is the same.

Bohun Wuren stood facing north, firmly planting his staff and pressing it against his chin until his skin wrinkled. After standing for a while, he departed without a word. The attendant informed Liezi, who rushed barefoot, clutching his shoes, and reached the gate, saying: "Master, since you have come, have you no remedy to offer?"

He replied: "Enough! I already told you: 'Others will protect you'—and now they do. It is not that you can make others protect you, nor can you prevent them from doing so. What use is there in this?"

The marvels of foreknowing arise from responsiveness. There must be a stimulus, and when shaken, one’s fundamental nature emerges. *Yu* means "not yet." *Cai* may also be written as *xing* (nature). The responsiveness to what has not yet come to pass is what gives rise to the extraordinary. The verification of the multitude lies in the certainty of some stimulus, which then stirs one’s inherent nature. Yet this too is meaningless.

Among those who wander with you, none will tell you. Even those who keep your company will not inform you of this. Those who speak petty words are all poison to others. Petty words are clever, insinuating speech. When such petty words exist, they become harm to others. None awaken, none comprehend—why question one another? What is there to ask?

The cunning toil and the wise worry; the incapable seek nothing. They eat their fill and roam freely, drifting like an untethered boat, empty and wandering. There was a man of Zheng named Huan who recited and murmured in the land of Qiu. After just three years, Huan became a Confucian. His influence moistened the land for nine *li*, and his beneficence extended to his three clans, even prompting his younger brother to study Mohism.

The Confucians and Mohists debated, and their father sided with Mo Di. After ten years, Huan took his own life. In a dream, his father said to him: "The one who made your son a Mohist was I. Why did you never once regard him favorably? Now I have become the fruit of the cypress on the grave." The Creator’s repayment to man does not repay the man himself but repays the Heaven within him—that which made him so. Yet man, believing himself to possess something that sets him apart from others, thereby demeans his kin.

The people of Qi who drew water from the well fought and grappled with one another. Thus it is said: All in the world today are like Huan. From this, even those with virtue find the Creator’s repayment unknowable—how much more so for those with the Dao? The ancients called this "the punishment of fleeing from Heaven."

Murmuring refers to recitation. "Just three years" means that in just three years, his Confucian studies were complete. "The river moistens nine *li*" is a metaphor for his beneficence extending to his three clans, meaning his official salary as a Confucian brought blessings to his lineage. "Prompting his brother to study Mohism" means he supported his brother in learning Mo Di’s teachings. *He* means "why." *He hu* is an emphatic expression meaning "why ever." In the dream, he told his father: "The one who led your son to study Mohism was I. Yet you never once looked upon him with favor. Now I have died and become the fruit of the cypress and cedar on the grave."

Zhuangzi cited this story and concluded: The Creator’s repayment to man does not stoop to repaying what the man has done but often repays what he could not do. Huan, however, demanded repayment for his actions—this was his error. The Creator truly made his brother a Mohist, yet Huan resented it. By demeaning his kin, he was like the people of Qi who, when drawing water from the well, fought and grappled over something that belonged to no one. Those who seize and drink merely fight among themselves—this is ignorance of Heaven. All people today are like Huan.

From this, it follows that even those with virtue find the Creator’s repayment unfathomable—how much more so for those with the Dao? Those with the Dao entrust everything to nature. The ancients called Huan’s kind "those who flee from the punishment of Heaven."

The sage finds peace in what is naturally peaceful and does not seek peace in what is not; the multitude seeks peace in what is not peaceful and does not find peace in what is. These few lines suffice to convey Huan’s lack of peace.

Zhuangzi said: To know the Dao is easy; to refrain from speaking of it is hard. To know and not speak is to follow Heaven. To know and speak is to follow man. The ancients followed Heaven, not man.

Zhu Pingman studied dragon-slaying from Zhi Liyi, exhausting a thousand pieces of gold from his family. After three years, his skill was perfected, yet there was no use for his dragon-slaying craft—this too was following man.

The sage takes what must not be as what must not be, thus having no weapons. The multitude takes what must not be as what must be, thus having many weapons. Yielding to weapons, they act with ulterior motives. Weapons, when relied upon, lead to ruin—this is not following Heaven but man.

The petty man’s knowledge does not extend beyond bribes and memoranda. He exhausts his spirit in trivial and shallow matters, yet wishes to universally save the Dao and things, to unify form and emptiness. Those like this are lost in the cosmos, burdened by form and unaware of the Great Beginning.

"To universally save" refers to the Dao and things; "to unify" refers to form and emptiness. It is like saying "to achieve perfect balance." "Burdened by form and unaware of the Great Beginning" means being shackled by form and ignorant of the Great Beginning.

The perfected person returns their spirit to the beginningless and delights in deep slumber in the land of Nothing Whatever. Like water flowing without form, they release and disperse into the Great Purity. Alas! You take knowledge to reside in the tip of a hair yet do not know the great tranquility. The perfected person is like water, without intent. "Knowledge in the tip of a hair" is petty knowledge.

There was a man of Song named Cao Shang who was sent by the King of Song as an envoy to Qin. On his departure, he obtained several carriages. The king was pleased with him and added a hundred more carriages. Upon returning to Song, he saw Zhuangzi and said: "Living in a cramped alley, impoverished and weaving sandals, with a withered neck and sallow face—this was my shortcoming. But to awaken a ruler of ten thousand chariots and follow him with a hundred carriages—this is my strength."

Zhuangzi said: The King of Qin had an illness and summoned physicians. Those who lanced boils and drained abscesses received one carriage; those who licked his hemorrhoids received five carriages. The lower the treatment, the more carriages they received. Did you perhaps treat his hemorrhoids? Why else would you receive so many carriages? Be on your way!

Duke Ai of Lu asked Yan He, saying: "I take Zhongni as the pillar of the state—will the kingdom then recover?"

Yan He replied: "Perilous indeed! It teeters on the brink.

"Zhongni adorns feathers to make patterns—patterns, pronounced *hua*, meaning ornate embellishments—adorns feathers to create such ornamentation. He busies himself with florid rhetoric, taking branch for root, spinning fragmented theories. He hardens his nature to display it to the people, yet does not know his own lack of sincerity. He receives it in the heart and governs it with the spirit—how could this suffice to lead the people? Man is born receiving this heart, with the spirit as its natural master. Yet Zhongni forcibly seeks to control it—how could this suffice to govern the people? *Fu* refers to Zhongni.

"Those people—you and I—ought to let them be, guided only by our bearing. Even if there are errors, it would suffice. Now you cause the people to abandon substance and learn falsity—this is no way to lead them. *Bi* refers to the people; *ru* to you, Duke; *yu* to myself, Yan He. *Yi* means direction, as in a nod or gesture. *Wu* means error. The people should be left to forget themselves; you and I need only guide them with our bearing—why impose governance? Though there may be some mistakes, it is better than forcing them into falsity.

"Considering the future, it would be better to cease. The state is difficult to govern. What is bestowed upon men yet not forgotten is not the natural dispensation of Heaven. If you take Zhongni as the pillar, you plan for future generations—better to desist, for the kingdom is hard to rule. What is given to men yet cannot be forgotten is not a natural bestowal.

"Merchants are not ranked among the gentry. Though circumstances may occasionally place them there, the spirit does not accept it. The heart does not wish to rank them so—*chi* means to rank. Yan He’s displeasure with Zhongni is thus.

"External punishments are meted out by metal and wood; internal punishments arise from movement and transgression. When petty men incur external punishments, metal and wood interrogate them; when they incur internal punishments, yin and yang consume them. Only the true person can escape both inner and outer punishments." Here Yan He expounds the Way of the true person, drawing a clear distinction from Zhongni.

Confucius said: "The human heart is more treacherous than mountains and rivers, harder to know than Heaven. Heaven has its seasons—spring, autumn, winter, summer—and its fixed times of day and night. But man has a thick façade and deep emotions. Thus, some appear honest yet seek gain; some seem incapable yet possess talent; some are compliant yet sharp, or stubborn yet yielding; some are slow yet severe. *Hu* is read as *han*, *gan*—though firm, they are circuitous; though unhurried, they are harsh. Therefore, those who rush to righteousness as if parched may flee from it as if burned."

Thus, the noble person tests them from afar to observe their loyalty, tests them at close hand to observe their reverence, burdens them with tasks to observe their ability, suddenly questions them to observe their knowledge, sets urgent deadlines to observe their trustworthiness, entrusts them with wealth to observe their benevolence, warns them of danger to observe their integrity, intoxicates them with wine to observe their restraint, and mingles them in company to observe their demeanor. When all nine tests are complete, the unworthy are revealed.

Zheng Kaofu, upon his first appointment, bent low; upon his second, stooped; upon his third, bowed deeply, and crept along the wall—who would dare transgress? But such a man as this, upon his first appointment, puffed up with pride; upon his second, danced atop his carriage; upon his third, called his elders by name. Who could compare to Tang Xu? Zheng Kaofu’s conduct is recorded in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*. "Such a man" refers to the common sort. "Danced atop his carriage" means he danced with arms outstretched while riding. "Called his elders by name" means he addressed his seniors familiarly. Tang Xu refers to Xu You of the era of Emperor Yao. Xu You declined the empire and would not accept it—how could he be arrogant on account of rank? Who could truly match him?

No greater thief exists than virtue held with intent, and intent with an eye—once it has an eye, it turns inward; turning inward, it is undone. Five baleful virtues exist, and the central virtue is chief among them.

What is the central virtue? The central virtue is one who takes self-satisfaction and reviles those who do not act as they do. The desires of the ear, eye, nose, mouth, and heart—these five are all baleful virtues, yet the heart’s desire, being central, is the chief of baleful virtues. Whoever takes self-satisfaction and reviles others for not doing as they do—this is baleful virtue.

Poverty has eight extremes; success has three certainties. The two—poverty and success—are expounded below. "Certainties" means inevitabilities.

The body has six receptacles. Beauty, whiskers, height, stature, strength, splendor, courage, and boldness—these eight surpass others, yet by these one is impoverished. (Beauty and whiskers, height and stature are one; strength and splendor, courage and boldness are one. When Viscount Xiang of Zhao deemed a man with fine whiskers and great stature worthy, or one skilled in archery and charioteering worthy, these too were two matters.) Combining the six receptacles into eight, one is impoverished by what surpasses others—this further explains the eight extremes of poverty.

Compliance, ease, and humility—these three all lead to success. Compliance means following the natural course; ease means acting with self-possession; humility means falling short of others through timidity and fear. These three do not seek success, yet success comes of itself—this explains the three certainties of success.

Wisdom that extends outward brings resentment when boldness stirs; benevolence and righteousness bring reproach. Only one who grasps the essence of life acts with towering self-assurance. One who grasps knowledge, though not reaching this, may still resemble it. One who grasps the great mandate of Heaven moves in accord with it; one who grasps the lesser mandate, though unable to match this, may still accept what befalls them. What is called success does not lie in acting outwardly.

A man who had an audience with the King of Song was granted ten carriages. With these ten carriages, he grew arrogant toward Zhuangzi. Zhuangzi said: By the river lived a poor family who wove rushes to eat. Their son dived into the depths and obtained a pearl worth a thousand gold pieces. His father said to him: Bring a stone to smash it! For a pearl worth a thousand gold pieces must lie in the ninefold abyss beneath the chin of a black dragon. That you could obtain it means you encountered its sleep. Had the black dragon awakened, what trace of you would remain?

Now the depths of the state of Song are not merely a ninefold abyss; the King of Song is not merely a black dragon. That you could obtain the carriages means you encountered his sleep. Had the King of Song awakened, you would be reduced to dust.

Arrogance means haughty condescension, treating others as children. Weaving rushes means crafting implements from grass. "What trace would remain?" means the dragon would devour him, leaving nothing.

Someone sent an envoy to Zhuangzi. Zhuangzi replied to the messenger: Have you seen the sacrificial ox? It is clothed in embroidered silk and fed with fine grain. Yet when it is led into the great temple, though it might wish to be a lone calf, can it be so?

As Zhuangzi neared death, his disciples wished to give him a lavish burial. Zhuangzi said: I take heaven and earth as my inner and outer coffins, the sun and moon as my paired jades, the stars as my pearls, and the ten thousand things as my funeral gifts. Are my burial furnishings not complete? What could be added to this?

His disciples said: We fear the crows and kites will eat you.

Zhuangzi said: Above, I would be eaten by crows and kites; below, by ants and mole crickets. To take from one to give to the other—how partial is that!

To level with the uneven is to fail in leveling; to verify with the unverifiable is to fail in verification. Only the enlightened act, and the spiritually attuned verify. That the enlightened cannot surpass the spiritually attuned has long been so. Yet fools rely on what they see to impose upon others—this external effort is truly lamentable.

*Verification* means proof.

*Following the Original Text of the Southern Florescence True Scripture*, Scroll 26
(Note: The character "门" in the original was emended to "问" following the received text.)
(Note: The character "汝" was added following the received text.)

*Following the Original Text of the Southern Florescence True Scripture*, Scroll 27
Recorded by Luo Miandao of Luling Bamboo Peak; collated by disciple Peng Xiang.

The world abounds with those who practice the arts of governance—each deeming their methods unsurpassable. These are the *arts of branches*, where each clings to what they possess, believing none can exceed them.

What was called the *Dao-arts* in antiquity—the arts of the Way—transcends these branch-arts. Where, then, does it reside? It is nowhere absent. How does the spirit descend? How does clarity emerge? The sage is born of it, the king is perfected by it—all trace back to the One, the Great Ultimate.

Unsevered from the Source, one is a *Heavenly Person*; unsevered from essence, a *Spiritual Person*; unsevered from truth, a *Perfected Person*. Taking Heaven as their source, virtue as their root, and the Dao as their gate—this encompasses the Heavenly, Spiritual, and Perfected Persons.

Manifesting through transformation, one is a *sage*. Taking benevolence as grace, righteousness as principle, ritual as conduct, and music as harmony—this defines the sage. Radiant with compassion, one is a *noble person*. Taking law as division, names as standards, deeds as proof, and scrutiny as judgment—counting one, two, three, four—officials rank themselves thus. Taking duty as constant, sustenance as primary, fostering growth, storing provisions, caring for the aged, weak, orphaned, and widowed—this is the governance of the people. This describes the ranking of noble persons.

From the Heavenly Person to the noble person, the Dao-arts of antiquity are fully encompassed. Yet what follows laments the divergence of past and present.

The ancients were complete: they matched the spirit’s brightness, harmonized with Heaven and Earth, nurtured the myriad things, brought concord to the world, and extended blessings to the people. They understood the fundamental principles and adhered to their applications, their influence pervading all—great and small, refined and coarse—with no place untouched.

Where their clarity was bound to measures and degrees, remnants of old laws and transmitted histories still endure. In the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music*, scholars of Zou and Lu, and the gentry, often elucidate them. The *Odes* convey intent, the *Documents* convey affairs, the *Rites* convey conduct, the *Music* conveys harmony, the *Changes* convey yin and yang, and the *Spring and Autumn Annals* convey names and duties. These principles, scattered across the world and established in the Central States, are sometimes praised and expounded by the Hundred Schools.

This describes the Dao-arts of antiquity, encompassing Fuxi, Shennong, the Yellow Emperor, Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Kings Wen and Wu, the Zhou, and Confucius. Yet when the world fell into great disorder, worthies and sages lost their clarity, and the Dao and virtue diverged. The world grasped at partial insights, each prizing their own. Like the ear, eye, nose, and mouth—each has its clarity but cannot communicate—so too the Hundred Schools’ arts each have their strengths and occasional uses. Yet they are incomplete, partial—scholars of a single corner.

They dissect the beauty of Heaven and Earth, analyze the principles of the myriad things, and examine the wholeness of the ancients. Few can fully embody the beauty of Heaven and Earth or match the countenance of the spirit’s brightness. Thus, the Way of inner sageliness and outer kingship dimmed and remained unexpressed, and the world’s people each pursued their desires, crafting their own methods. How lamentable!

The Hundred Schools departed and did not return—they will never reunite. Later scholars, unfortunate to have missed the purity of Heaven and Earth and the greatness of the ancients, saw the Dao-arts torn asunder by the world.

The world’s great disorder stems from partial insights—each grasping one aspect and delighting in their own bias. Like the ear, eye, nose, and mouth, each has its knowledge but cannot communicate. The Hundred Schools’ arts each have their strengths and occasional uses, yet they cling to partiality. Few, in examining the ancients’ wholeness, can fully embody the beauty of Heaven and Earth or match the spirit’s countenance. Each crafts their own methods—how lamentable!

Not indulging in later ages, not lavishing on the myriad things, not flaunting measures and degrees, but using the carpenter’s line to correct themselves and prepare for the world’s urgencies: this was a path within the ancient Dao-arts. Mo Di and Qin Guli heard its teachings and rejoiced in them. They overemphasized it, yet followed it to excess.

They composed the *Against Music*, calling it *Frugality*; they forbade singing in life and mourning in death. Such was the ancient Dao-arts, yet Mo Di’s followers alone delighted in these teachings, carrying them to extremes.

Those who correct themselves with the carpenter’s line do so to an extreme degree. Their insistence on neither extravagance nor ostentation, neither excess nor display, is itself an extreme of compliance. In composing *Against Music*, they declare all their undertakings to be devoid of joy. Mozi advocated universal love and mutual benefit while opposing strife, and his Way forbade anger. He was diligent in study and broad in learning, yet neither aligned with the ancients nor diverged from the common crowd—his path neither matched the ancient kings’ nor differed from the multitude’s. He condemned the rites and music of antiquity: the Yellow Emperor had *Xianchi*, Yao had *Dazhang*, Shun had *Dashao*, Yu had *Daxia*, Tang had *Dahu*, King Wen had the music of the *Biyong* academy, and King Wu and the Duke of Zhou composed *Wu*—these were the music of old. In ancient funeral rites, noble and base had their proper forms, high and low their distinctions. The Son of Heaven was buried in seven layers of inner and outer coffins, the feudal lords in five, ministers in three, and scholars in two—these were the rites of old. Yet Mozi alone forbade singing in life and mourning attire in death, prescribing only a paulownia coffin three inches thick with no outer casket as the model. Teaching others thus, he showed no love for them; practicing it himself, he showed no love for himself. Though his Way had not yet failed, instructing others in meagerness was hardly a way to love them. Yet in his own conduct, he embraced meagerness, surely showing no love for himself. Thus, none could fault him, and his Way did not collapse.

Nevertheless, to sing yet condemn singing, to weep yet condemn weeping, to rejoice yet condemn rejoicing—is this truly fitting? Though what follows are Zhuangzi’s critical remarks: to refrain from singing when one ought to sing, to refrain from weeping when one ought to weep, to refrain from joy when one ought to rejoice—does this accord with human nature? His life was one of toil, his death one of meagerness; his Way was stark and unadorned, causing others sorrow and grief. His conduct was difficult to emulate. I fear it cannot be the Way of the sage, for it runs counter to the hearts of all under Heaven. The world cannot endure it. Though Mozi alone could bear it, what could he do for all under Heaven? Estranged from the world, he was far from the kingship of old. This is how he diverged from the ancient kings.

Mozi spoke of the Way, saying: "In ancient times, Yu dammed the floods, dredged the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers, and opened the roads to the four barbarians and the nine provinces. There were three hundred great rivers, three thousand tributaries, and countless smaller streams. Yu personally wielded the basket and spade, the tools for carrying food and clothing, and dredged the myriad rivers of the world. His calves bore no flesh, his shins no hair; he bathed in harsh rains, combed through fierce winds, and established the ten thousand states. Yu was a great sage, yet he wearied his body for all under Heaven to such an extent. Thus, later Mohists wore coarse garments of fur and hemp, bound their feet in rough sandals, labored day and night without rest, and took self-imposed hardship as their ultimate principle, saying: 'Without this, one cannot follow Yu’s Way and is unworthy of being called a Mohist.'"

Xiangli Qin’s disciples, such as Xiangli (a surname), Qin (a given name), and the followers of the Five Lords (the five ranks of feudal lords, as in the *Zuozhuan*),...

The southern Mohists—Ku Huo, Ji Chi, Deng Lingzi, and their ilk—all recited the *Mo Jing* yet diverged in their interpretations, calling themselves "separate Mohists" of distinct lineages. They engaged in debates over the "hard and white" and "same and different," responding to one another with asymmetrical, unorthodox arguments—neither clashing nor contradicting, yet aligning in their discourse. They revered the *Juzi* as sages, each aspiring to serve as their proxy, hoping to become their successors. This dispute remains unresolved to this day.

The *Juzi*—akin to "great ones"—were chosen from the most eminent of their faction as sages. To serve as their proxy (*shi*, "master") was to become the lineage’s standard-bearer. Yet while some believed in this, others did not, and the debate has never been settled. Mo Di and Qin Guli’s intentions were sound, but their methods were flawed. They would have later Mohists vie in self-imposed hardship, boasting of calves without flesh and shins without hair. This is the height of chaos, the nadir of order—where disorder abounds and governance is scarce. Nevertheless, Mozi was truly one who loved the world and sought its betterment. Even if one were to seek such a figure in vain, they would persist, undeterred by emaciation—a true talent indeed. Unburdened by convention, unadorned by material things, uncompromising with others, and unyielding to the crowd, he desired the world’s peace to preserve the lives of the people. His own sustenance and that of others were to be met in full measure, and no more—thus baring his heart to make its purity known.

Among the ancient arts of the Dao, some resided in this approach. Song Xing and Yin Wen, hearing of its influence, were delighted. They crafted the "Mount Hua cap" as their emblem—its shape flat above and below, symbolizing balance. They engaged with all things by first distinguishing right from wrong, for bias obscures judgment. They spoke of the heart’s capacity, naming it "the heart’s conduct." Through harmony, they sought to unite the world, proposing to establish it as the guiding principle. To describe the heart’s capacity was to depict its form; to name it was to define its function. *Er* (cooking to tenderness) was likened to fostering harmony, as one might prepare a feast to bring joy, thereby pacifying the realm. They urged that this principle be made the master of all.

Unmoved by insult, they sought to quell strife and end warfare, rescuing the people from conflict. With this, they traversed the world, persuading rulers above and instructing the masses below. Though the world rejected them, they persisted, clamoring without cease—thus, though reviled above and below, they forced their presence. Yet their concern for others was vast, while their self-regard was minimal. They declared: "Five *sheng* of rice suffice. The master may fear hunger, but though disciples starve, they never forget the world, laboring day and night without rest. They say: 'We must surely bring life to all!'"

Would they truly take pride in being saviors of the world? They declared: "Five *sheng* of rice suffice." Yet the master feared hunger, and disciples, though starving, followed without respite, day and night. They said: "We must surely bring life to the people!" Would they take pride in those saviors of the world who do not give their all?

They said: "The noble person is not overly scrupulous, nor does he lend himself to things. To deem something of no benefit to the world is to make clear that it is not as good as oneself." They took the prohibition of attack and cessation of warfare as external matters, and the restraint of passions and desires as internal. Whether great or small, refined or coarse, their conduct extended precisely to this point and no further. Though their learning differed in scale and depth, their conduct sufficed to reach this end. Thus, their words held no distinction of great or small, high or deep.

Impartial yet not partisan, level and without bias, detached and without fixed intent, they followed things without duality, unburdened by deliberation, unguided by cunning. Toward things, they made no selection, but went along with them. "Level" means even and unobstructed. "Detached and without fixed intent" means meeting affairs with decisive action, without preconceived notions. "Following things without duality" means responding to matters without divided intentions. For instance, if one were engaged in a task and another generated a separate intention, this would be a sign of contrivance.

Among the ancient arts of the Dao, there were those who practiced this way. Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao heard of its influence and delighted in it. They took the equalization of the myriad things as their foremost principle, saying: "Heaven can cover them but cannot bear them; earth can bear them but cannot cover them; the great Dao can encompass them but cannot discriminate among them. Know that the myriad things all have what is acceptable and what is not."

Thus they said: "Selection is not comprehensive; teaching does not reach all; only the Dao leaves nothing out." Therefore, Shen Dao abandoned knowledge and relinquished the self, acting only out of necessity. He was unencumbered by things, taking this as the principle of the Dao. He said: "To know yet feign ignorance is to approach knowledge only to be harmed by its proximity." If one makes selections, they will not be comprehensive; if one uses this as teaching, it will not reach all. Only by returning to the Dao can nothing be lost.

"Unencumbered" (*ling*) means clarity and coolness; "detached" (*tai*) means purification and cleansing. To be "unencumbered and detached by things" is to act with ease in all affairs. "To know yet feign ignorance" means that though one knows, one acts as if one does not. Both "approach" (*bo*) and "proximity" (*lin*) imply slightness—if one has even a slight knowledge, one will inevitably suffer slight harm.

Peng Meng, Tian Pian, Shen Dao, Hui Shi, and Deng Xi were all contemporaries of King Xuan of Qi, residing at Jixia. Their learning was rooted in the Huang-Lao tradition. (See *Yin Wenzi* and *Xunzi*.)

They mocked the world’s esteem for worthies, indulging in unrestrained conduct without adherence to standards, and denounced the world’s great sages.

"Chui" (椎, pronounced like *chui* in "hammer") and "pai" (拍) imply bending; "wan" (輐, pronounced *zhí* in "straight tube" and *duàn* in "sever") implies brevity—both adapt to things without contention. To "hammer" is to strike, to "sever" is to cut, yet both yield without resistance. Abandoning right and wrong, one may thus avoid entanglement.

Without reliance on wisdom or foresight, unaware of what lies ahead or behind, one stands towering and alone. Pushing others to turn back before proceeding, dragging one’s steps before advancing—like the swirling return of a whirlwind, the circling of a feather, the grinding of a millstone in its groove—one remains whole without fault, moving or still without transgression, never incurring blame.

The whirlwind’s return, the feather’s circling—both suggest yielding. The millstone’s groove, too, turns and emerges without resistance. Why is this so? For things devoid of knowledge bear no burden of self-assertion, no encumbrance of cleverness; their movement and stillness never depart from principle, and thus they pass their lives without praise.

Thus it is said: "Attain the state of things without knowledge, and the worthies and sages become superfluous." A clod of earth does not lose the Dao.

The heroic and eminent mocked him, saying: "Shen Dao’s Way is not the conduct of the living but the principle of the dead." They found it merely strange.

A clod, devoid of knowledge, does not lose the Dao. Yet the heroic and eminent laughed among themselves: "What Shen Dao practices is not the way of the living but the principle of the dead." They could only marvel in bewilderment.

Tian Pian was the same, having studied under Peng Meng and received the teaching of "no-teaching." Peng Meng’s master said: "The ancient Dao reaches the point where nothing is affirmed, nothing denied." Its manner was utterly still (*kuòrán*), beyond all description.

Yet even so, he could not escape the fate of being "cut off like a turtle" (*yuán duàn*). What he called the Dao was not the Dao, and what he spoke as right was not free from wrong.

Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao did not understand the Dao. Nevertheless, in broad terms, they had once glimpsed something of it.

"Tian Pian was the same" means he was like Shen Dao. Peng Meng was Tian Pian’s teacher. Tian Pian studied under Peng Meng and received the teaching of "no words." Peng Meng’s master had said: "The ancient Dao reaches the point where nothing is affirmed, nothing denied." Its manner was so obscure (*lùwǎngrán*) as to be inexpressible, and thus Peng Meng, too, remained silent.

Yet when Tian Pian returned from Peng Meng’s house, after some time, people no longer gathered to watch—though a few still came, like fish breaking the surface in scattered succession. Though his words did not astonish, some still recognized him.

From this, we may conclude: what Tian Pian called the Dao was not the Dao, and even if his words were right, they were not free from wrong.

Zhuangzi assessed them, saying: "Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao cannot be credited with understanding the Dao, yet viewed broadly, they had once heard something of it."

Zhuangzi, taking Lao Dan as one who understood the Dao, thus compared them to this passage. "Taking the root as essential, the material as coarse; regarding accumulation as insufficiency, one dwells in serene solitude with the spirit luminaries."

Among the ancient arts of the Dao, there were those who embodied this. Guan Yin and Lao Dan heard of their manner and delighted in it. They established it upon the constancy of "non-being" (*wúyǒu*) and centered it in the Great One (*Tàiyī*). They took gentleness, weakness, and humility as its outward form, and emptiness, non-destruction of the myriad things as its substance.

Guan Yin said: "Within oneself, hold no fixed position, and the forms of things will manifest of themselves. In movement, be like water; in stillness, like a mirror; in response, like an echo. Fading like vanishing, silent like purity—harmonizing with those who are alike, giving life to those who attain it. Never preceding others, always following them."

Lao Dan said: "Know the masculine, keep to the feminine, and be the world’s stream. Know the bright, keep to the obscure, and be the world’s valley." (Obscurity here means darkness; streams and valleys are empty yet capacious places—metaphors for the Mysterious Female (*Xuán Pìn*).)

"While others seek to be first, I alone take the rear," he said. "I bear the world’s filth. While others seek fullness, I alone take emptiness. Without hoarding, I have abundance—abundance in tranquility. In conducting myself, I am unhurried and unburdened, acting without contrivance and laughing at others’ cleverness.

While others seek fortune, I alone seek to bend and preserve wholeness, saying: 'Let me merely avoid blame.' Taking depth as my root, frugality as my guiding principle, I say: 'What is rigid will break; what is sharp will be blunted.' Always yielding to things, never cutting others down—this may be called the ultimate attainment.

Ah, Guan Yin and Lao Dan! Were they not the vast and true persons of antiquity? Silent and formless, their transformations without constancy—do they belong to death or life? Do they stand with heaven and earth?"

Where does the spirit’s radiance go? Vast and boundless—whither does it drift? Sudden and fleeting—where does it alight? All things are gathered within it, yet none can claim it as their home.

Among the ancient arts of the Dao, there was one that dwelt herein. Zhuang Zhou heard its wind and delighted in it. With meandering discourse, wild and unbounded words, and speech without beginning or end, he gave free rein to his thoughts without constraint, refusing to be confined by partial views—meaning he would not be limited to a single perspective. He saw the world as sunk in turbidity, unfit for solemn speech or direct instruction. Thus, he used *zhi*-words to weave expansive tales, *zhong*-words to affirm truth, and *yu*-words to broaden his reach. Alone, he roamed with the spirit of heaven and earth, yet did not scorn or detach from the myriad things. He did not dispute right or wrong, but dwelt among the common world.

Though his writings were grand and wondrous, their flow was harmonious and unmarred. Though his words were uneven and strange, they were striking to behold. Zhuangzi himself marveled at his own prose. His fullness could not be contained. Above, he wandered with the Creator; below, he befriended those beyond life and death, without beginning or end. In his grasp of the root, he was vast and expansive, profound and unrestrained. In his reverence for the source, he attained perfect harmony and ascended to the heights—this source being the unbroken lineage of the Dao.

Yet though he responded to transformation and dissolved the constraints of things, his principles were inexhaustible, his insights unchanging. Vague and obscure, they remained unfathomed.

Zhuangzi followed the teachings of Lao Dan. Since Lao Dan had already been praised as a vast and true person, what more could Zhuangzi add? Thus, the final passage merely addresses his writings.

Hui Shi was a man of many methods—contemporary with Zhuangzi as minister of Liang. While earlier passages spoke of the arts of the Dao, here alone they speak of "many methods," referring to the arts of disputation. His writings filled five carts; his doctrines were contradictory and discordant; his words did not strike the mark.

In his *Discourse on Things*, he said: "That which is greatest, having no outside, is called the Great One; that which is smallest, having no inside, is called the Small One. What has no thickness cannot be accumulated, yet it may extend a thousand *li*. The sky and earth are level; mountains and marshes are flat. The sun is at its zenith even as it declines; things are born even as they die. If great things are the same while small things differ, this is called the lesser sameness and difference. If all things are entirely the same or entirely different, this is called the greater sameness and difference."

His words did not align with reason, yet he proceeded to ponder the nature of things. He said: "What has no thickness cannot be accumulated, yet its greatness may span a thousand *li*—for from the infinitesimal, accumulation may reach vastness. As for the sun’s decline, it may be observed aslant. The sky and earth, mountains and marshes—all share in the sun’s ascent and descent. The birth and death of things are likewise united in sameness. If great things are alike while small things differ, this is the lesser sameness and difference. If all things are entirely the same or entirely different, this is the greater sameness and difference."

"The south is both boundless and bounded. Today I go to Yue, yet I arrived yesterday. Linked rings can be unlinked. I know the center of the world—it lies north of Yan and south of Yue. Embracing all things with universal love, heaven and earth become one body."

Hui Shi took this as his great principle, surveying the world to enlighten its debaters. The debaters of the world rejoiced in his company.

Those who dwell in the north know nothing of the south’s geography and deem it boundless—yet in truth, it has limits. Though one may set out for Yue today, upon arrival, it is as if one had come yesterday. The center of the world is unknowable—yet the people of Yan and Yue each take their own land as the center, so the north of Yan and the south of Yue may also be deemed the center. To embrace all things with universal love is to make heaven and earth one body with oneself.

Eggs have feathers. A chicken has three feet. Ying possesses all under heaven. A dog can be a sheep. Horses lay eggs. A tadpole has a tail.

Fire is not hot. Mountains speak. Wheels do not touch the ground. Eyes do not see. The finger does not reach, and what reaches does not persist. A tortoise is longer than a snake. The carpenter’s square is not square; the compass cannot draw a circle. The socket does not encircle the tenon. The shadow of a flying bird never moves. The swiftness of an arrow’s flight includes moments of neither motion nor rest. A dog is not a hound. A yellow horse and a black ox make three. A white dog is black. A lone colt has never had a mother. If you take half of a one-foot staff each day, it will never be exhausted for ten thousand generations.

Debaters used these propositions to engage with Hui Shi, and their exchanges continued endlessly. This passage records several such paradoxes of the time.

Feathers grow within the egg—hence, "eggs have feathers." A pheasant has two feet, but the intent behind its movement counts as a third—hence, "a chicken has three feet." Ying was originally a state ruled by a marquis, yet it was called a kingdom—hence, "Ying possesses all under heaven." The names of large and small sheep are assigned by people; if one first names a dog as a sheep, then people will call it a sheep. Horses are certainly born from the womb, yet since a foal is covered in hair at birth, how is it different from being born from an egg? Not only do creatures have tails—observe the characters for "hook" (钩), "ding" (丁), and "child" (子), all of which resemble tails. Xunzi also said, "Hooks have bristles; eggs have feathers"—these are difficult claims to uphold. Yet Deng Xi and Hui Shi could defend them. The commentary explains: "Hooks having bristles" is akin to "a tadpole having a tail," for the curved part of *ding* (丁) forms a hook, and bristles resemble tails.

Fire is hot, yet in winter it is not. Mountains are still, yet an echo in an empty valley makes them "speak." A perfectly round wheel does not touch the ground. The *Kao Gong Ji* states of wheelwrights: "When inspecting a wheel, one desires it to be so smooth that no part stands out—this is the essence of roundness." Eyes may be obstructed and thus fail to see; a finger may miss its target and thus fail to reach. Even a straight path cannot be fully traversed.
