Snakes are long, yet tortoises are short—but since tortoises can foretell fortune, they are "longer" than snakes. The carpenter’s square is a tool for making right angles, yet its own form is not perfectly square. The compass is a tool for drawing circles, yet its own form cannot create a perfect circle. The socket is not designed to encircle the tenon—rather, the tenon fits into it. Though the shadow of a flying bird appears to move, it merely clings to the bird’s form and does not separate from it—hence, it "never moves." Though an arrow flies swiftly, if it is not loosed, it does not move; once loosed, it cannot be stopped—thus, its swiftness lies in the archer, not the arrow itself.

A dog and a hound are the same, yet one with dewclaws is called a *hound*, while one without is merely a *dog* and cannot be called a hound. A horse is one, yet there is also the category of "yellow"—that makes two. If the horse is inherently yellow, and people call it so, does that not make three? The same applies to a black ox. A black dog cannot become white, but a white dog can be dyed black—thus, one may say a dog can be called a "black colt." Yet to say a "lone colt" implies it never had a mother—is this acceptable? A one-foot staff is not long, yet if you use half of it today and half of the remainder tomorrow, repeating this endlessly, it will never be exhausted for ten thousand generations.

Debaters of the time used such arguments to engage with Hui Shi, their exchanges continuing without end. Huan Tuan, Gongsun Long, and their ilk—these sophists—distorted people’s minds and altered their intentions. They could triumph in verbal sparring but could not win over hearts; such was the confinement of the debaters. Hui Shi daily matched wits with others, setting himself apart from the world’s debaters with his strangeness—this was his root. Yet in his oral disputations, he considered himself the most worthy, declaring: "Are not heaven and earth grand? I, Hui, possess a heroic spirit but lack the method." The confinement of the debaters refers to how none could escape the bounds of their own arguments. Ordinary debaters are common people; the world’s debaters transcend the ordinary, like those called the "worthies of the world."

The root here means foundation. Hui Shi daily engaged in debate with ordinary people, but when he suddenly confronted Huan Tuan and Gongsun Long, he was pitted against the world’s debaters—was this not strange? His position was like the tangled roots of a tree, difficult to uproot. Yet in his speech, he boasted: "Is my strength not like that of heaven and earth? How could others surpass heaven and earth?" Such was his delusion. Though Hui Shi harbored a competitive spirit, he lacked the means to prevail. He began with many methods but ended with none—thus, he had neither method nor principle.

In the south there was a man of extraordinary talent named Huang Liao, who asked why heaven and earth do not collapse, and the causes of wind, rain, thunder, and lightning. Hui Shi did not decline to answer nor pause to reflect but expounded on all things without end. Though his explanations were exhaustive, he still deemed them insufficient and added ever more bizarre claims, taking contrarian positions as truth and seeking fame through victory—thus, he found no harmony with others. Weak in virtue yet strong in disputation, his path was tortuous. "Weak in virtue" means deficient in moral power; "strong in disputation" means excessive in argumentation. *Tu* means path; *yu* means winding. Hui Shi’s way was circuitous and crooked.

Viewing Hui Shi’s abilities through the Dao of heaven and earth, he was like the labor of a single mosquito or gnat—what use was he to the world? Even if one’s partial theories were sound, to claim they were the most esteemed Dao would be perilous. Hui Shi could not find peace in this, scattering his efforts across all things without satisfaction, seeking only fame as a skilled debater. He could not settle into unity but instead sought to encompass all things, flaunting his ability.

Alas! Hui Shi’s talents, though expansive, could not attain even the smallest gain. Chasing after the myriad things without return—this is like exhausting echoes for sound, like form racing alongside shadow. How lamentable!

Hui Shi said, “Heaven and earth are indeed grand!”—thus this passage speaks of the Way of heaven and earth. But how could Hui Shi truly comprehend it? In the closing section of his work, Zhuangzi thoroughly rebukes Hui Shi, making clear his disdain for the strategists of the Warring States. The final chapter recounts the arts of the Way: first the Heavenly Person, the Spirit Person, and the Perfect Person; then the Sage and the Noble Person. Only after the arts of the Way fragmented in later ages did the various schools diverge. Hui Shi’s methods are placed last of all.

Zhuangzi ranked himself after Lao Dan, never daring to claim superiority over the Six Classics. Having read thus far, could there be any chapters remaining? *The True Classic of Southern Florescence* concludes with this, the twenty-seventh scroll. *The True Classic of Southern Florescence*, twenty-eighth scroll. Recorded by Luo Miandao of Zhufeng in Luling; collated and punctuated by his disciple Peng Xiang. *Rejecting the False, Cultivating Sincerity*.

Cultivating Sincerity and Restoring Nature are shallow and clumsy. The four chapters beginning with *Yielding the Throne* are coarse and harsh. These are not the authentic words of Zhuangzi; they are rejected and appended to the end of the scroll.

Those who *cultivate sincerity* exalt conduct, distancing themselves from the world and defying custom, their lofty discourses filled with resentment and reproach—this is but obstinacy. Such are the preferences of recluses in the mountains and valleys, men who are not of this world, those who delight in withering away or plunging into the abyss. Those who speak of benevolence and righteousness, loyalty and trust, reverence and frugality, deference and yielding—this is but refinement. Such are the preferences of gentlemen in peaceful times, teachers and admonishers, those who roam and dwell in the love of learning. Those who speak of great achievements, establishing great renown, honoring ruler and subject, rectifying high and low—this is but governance. Such are the preferences of courtiers, men who exalt their lord and strengthen the state, those who seek merit and annexation. Those who retreat to marshes and wilds, dwelling in seclusion, fishing and living idly—this is but inaction. Such are the preferences of men of rivers and seas, those who shun the world, those who delight in leisure. Those who practice breath control, exhaling the old and inhaling the new, imitating the bear’s climbing and the bird’s stretching—this is but longevity. Such are the preferences of practitioners of Daoist arts, those who nurture the body, those who seek the long life of Peng Zu.

Yet there are those who attain loftiness without cultivating sincerity, refinement without benevolence and righteousness, governance without merit and fame, seclusion without rivers and seas, longevity without Daoist arts. They forget nothing, yet possess all. Serene and boundless, all beauties follow them. This is the Way of Heaven and Earth, the virtue of the Sage.

Thus it is said: Tranquility, stillness, emptiness, and non-action—these are the equilibrium of Heaven and Earth and the essence of the Way and virtue. Thus it is said: When the Sage rests in ease, he is level and yielding. Being level and yielding, he is tranquil. Tranquil and level, cares and troubles cannot enter, perverse qi cannot assail. Thus his virtue is whole and his spirit unharmed.

Thus it is said: The Sage’s life is the movement of Heaven; his death is the transformation of things. At rest, he shares the virtue of yin; in motion, he moves with the waves of yang. He does not precede fortune nor initiate misfortune. He responds only after being stirred, moves only when pressed, rises only when compelled. He discards knowledge and precedent, following the principle of Heaven. No heavenly disasters, no burdens from things, no human reproach, no blame from spirits. His life is like floating; his death is like repose. He does not ponder nor scheme. Radiant yet not dazzling, trustworthy yet not bound by expectation. In sleep, he does not dream; awake, he has no worries. His spirit is pure and unadulterated; his soul does not weary. Emptiness and tranquility—only thus does he accord with the virtue of Heaven.

Thus it is said: Sorrow and joy are perversions of virtue; delight and anger are excesses of the Way; likes and dislikes are lapses of virtue. Therefore, a heart without sorrow or joy is the perfection of virtue; unity without care is the perfection of stillness; nothing to oppose is the perfection of emptiness; nothing to resist is the perfection of purity.

Thus it is said: When the body labors without rest, it breaks down; when essence is used without cease, it wearies; weariness leads to exhaustion. The nature of water is such that when unadulterated, it is clear; when undisturbed, it is level. If pent up and unmoving, it cannot remain clear—this is the image of Heavenly virtue.

Thus it is said: Pure and unadulterated, still and unchanging, serene and non-active, moving in accord with Heaven’s course—this is the Way of nurturing the spirit. As for the sword of Gan and Yue, though cased and treasured, it is never used—this is the height of preciousness. When the spirit flows unimpeded in all four directions, reaching everywhere, above touching Heaven, below coiling upon Earth, transforming and nurturing the myriad things, beyond all image—its name is *Uniting with the Emperor*. The Way of purity and simplicity guards only the spirit. Guarding without loss, it unites with the spirit. The essence of unity penetrates, harmonizing with Heavenly order.

A rustic saying goes: The common man values profit; the incorruptible man values fame; the worthy man esteems will; the Sage treasures essence. Thus, *simplicity* means having nothing mixed with it; *purity* means not harming the spirit. One who embodies purity and simplicity is called a *True Person*.

*Restoring Nature*
Those who *restore nature* through vulgar learning seek to return to their original state. (Some versions mistakenly add an extra "vulgar" due to scribal repetition.) Those who muddle their desires with vulgar thoughts to attain clarity are called the benighted. The ancients who cultivated the Way nurtured knowledge with tranquility. To live without employing knowledge is called nurturing tranquility with knowledge. Knowledge and tranquility nurture each other, and harmony and principle emerge from one’s nature.

Virtue is harmony; the Way is principle. Virtue that embraces all is benevolence; the Way that orders all is righteousness. Righteousness made manifest and things drawn close is loyalty. Centered, pure, and returning to genuine feeling is joy. Trust in conduct, accommodating form, and following pattern is propriety. When propriety and joy spread everywhere, the world falls into chaos. When the upright are obscured and virtue is veiled, virtue does not overreach. Overreaching causes things to lose their nature.

In ancient times, people dwelt in primordial chaos, attaining tranquility with their age. In that era, yin and yang were harmonious and still, ghosts and spirits did not disturb, the four seasons kept their rhythm, the myriad things were unharmed, and the multitudes did not die young. Though people possessed knowledge, they had no use for it—this was called *Supreme Unity*. In that time, none acted, yet all was constantly spontaneous.

When virtue declined, Suiren and Fuxi began to rule the world, and thus compliance was no longer unity. When virtue declined further, Shennong and the Yellow Emperor began to rule the world, and thus peace was no longer compliance. When virtue declined yet further, Yao and Shun began to rule the world, initiating the currents of governance and transformation. Integrity dissolved and simplicity scattered; they departed from the Way to pursue goodness, risked virtue to act. Then they abandoned nature and followed the heart. Heart matched heart in recognition, yet knowledge was insufficient to settle the world. Thus they adorned it with culture and augmented it with erudition.

Culture obliterated substance, erudition drowned the heart—only then did the people first fall into confusion and delusion, unable to return to their nature and feelings and restore their origin.

From this we see: the world lost the Way, the Way lost the world—the world and the Way mutually destroyed each other. How could the person of the Way arise in the world? How could the world arise in the Way? If the Way cannot arise in the world, and the world cannot arise in the Way, even if the sage does not dwell in the mountains and forests, his virtue is concealed. Yet concealment is not self-concealment.

What the ancients called "recluses" did not crouch in hiding to avoid being seen, did not seal their words to prevent their utterance, did not bury their knowledge to withhold its expression—it was the great error of the times that compelled them. Had their time and destiny aligned with the flourishing of the world, they would have returned to unity without trace. Had their time and destiny been utterly at odds with the world, they would have deeply rooted themselves in tranquility and awaited their end. This is the Way of preserving the self.

The ancients who preserved themselves did not adorn knowledge with eloquence, did not exhaust the world with knowledge, did not exhaust virtue with knowledge. Serene in their place, they returned to their nature—what more was there to do? The Way is not fulfilled in petty conduct, virtue is not fulfilled in petty understanding. Petty understanding harms virtue, petty conduct harms the Way. Thus it is said: rectify the self and that is all.

To delight in wholeness is called "attaining one’s aspiration." What the ancients called "attaining one’s aspiration" did not refer to carriages and caps of office. It meant nothing could augment their joy. What is now called "attaining one’s aspiration" refers to carriages and caps of office. Carriages and caps of office are not one’s nature and destiny—they are transient things that come by chance, a temporary lodging. Lodged in them, their coming cannot be fenced in, their going cannot be halted. Thus one does not indulge the heart for carriages and caps, nor follow the vulgar in poverty and distress. Their joy is the same in both, and so they are without sorrow. Now, if the lodging departs, they are no longer joyful. From this we see: though they may rejoice, they are never without distraction.

Thus it is said:

Those who lose themselves in things and forfeit their nature to custom are called "the people of inverted priorities."

*Yielding the Throne*
Yao offered the world to Xu You, but Xu You declined. He then offered it to Zizhou Zhipu, who said: "That I might be Son of Heaven is acceptable. However, I happen to suffer from a deep-seated melancholy, and am presently treating it. I have no leisure to govern the world." The world is of utmost weight, yet he would not let it harm his life—how much less would he do so for other things? Only one who does not take the world as his own concern may be entrusted with the world.

Shun offered the world to Zizhou Zhibo, who replied: "I happen to suffer from a deep-seated melancholy, and am presently treating it. I have no leisure to govern the world." Thus the world is a great vessel, yet he would not exchange his life for it. This is how those who embody the Way differ from the vulgar.

Shun offered the world to Shan Juan. (Today, on Mount Cang in the south of Changde Prefecture, Wuling County, there stands the Altar of Shan Juan. During the He-Harmonizing reign of the Song, he was posthumously honored as "Master of Reclusive Loftiness." The prefect Li Dao composed an inscription, and near the altar his tomb remains.) Shan Juan said: "I stand in the midst of the cosmos. In winter I wear furs, in summer I wear coarse or fine hemp. In spring I plow and sow, and my body is sufficient for labor; in autumn I reap and gather, and my person is sufficient for rest and sustenance. I rise with the sun and work, I rest with the sun and sleep, wandering carefree between heaven and earth, my heart and mind content. Why would I take the world for myself? Alas, you do not understand me!" He refused the offer. Then he departed and entered the deep mountains, and none knew his whereabouts.

Shun offered the world to his friend, the farmer of Shihu. The farmer of Shihu said: "How earnest is the lord’s conduct! A man who conserves his strength!" He deemed Shun’s virtue not yet perfected. Thereupon, he and his wife shouldered their burdens, led their child, and entered the sea, never to return.

King Tai of the Zhou dwelled in Bin, and the Di tribes attacked him. He offered them furs and silks, but they refused; he offered them dogs and horses, but they refused; he offered them pearls and jade, but they refused. What the Di sought was land. King Tai said: "To dwell with a man’s elder brother and kill his younger brother, to dwell with a man’s father and kill his son—I cannot bear it. All of you, do your best to remain here. What difference is there between being my subjects and being subjects of the Di? Moreover, I have heard: one must not harm what is nurtured by what is used for nurturing." Thereupon, he took up his staff and departed. The people followed in a continuous stream.

Thus he established a state beneath Mount Qi. Indeed, King Tai of Danfu may be said to have known how to honor life. One who honors life, though rich and noble, does not harm the body with what sustains it; though poor and lowly, does not burden the form with profit. Yet men of this age who occupy high office and exalted rank all fear losing them. Seeing gain, they lightly cast away their lives—how can this not be delusion?

The people of Yue murdered their ruler for three generations. Prince Sou, troubled by this, fled into the cinnabar caves, and Yue was left without a king. When they could not find Prince Sou, they pursued him into the cinnabar caves. Prince Sou refused to emerge, so the people of Yue smoked him out with mugwort and bore him forth in the royal carriage. Prince Sou grasped the reins and mounted the chariot, then looked to heaven and cried: "Rulership! Rulership! Can you truly not spare me?" Prince Sou did not despise being king—he despised the calamity that comes with kingship. Such a man may be said not to harm his life for the sake of a state. This is why the people of Yue desired him as their ruler.

Han and Wei contended over invaded lands. Master Hua Zi visited Marquis Zhao of Wei, who wore a troubled expression. Master Hua Zi said: "Suppose now that the world inscribed a pledge before you, its words saying: 'If your left hand seizes it, your right hand will be maimed; if your right hand seizes it, your left hand will be maimed. Yet whoever seizes it shall possess the world.' Would you seize it?"

Marquis Zhao said: "I would not seize it."

Master Hua Zi said: "Excellent. From this we see that both arms are weightier than the world, and the body is weightier than both arms. How much lighter than the world is Han! And how much lighter than Han is what you now contend over! Yet you grieve and harm your life with sorrow for what you cannot obtain."

Marquis Zhao said: "Well said."

The many who have instructed me are legion, yet never have I heard such words before. Master Hua Zi may truly be said to know the weight of things.

When the Lord of Lu heard that Yan He was a man who had attained the Way, he dispatched an envoy bearing gifts to precede his visit. Yan He dwelt in a humble lane, clad in coarse cloth, and fed his own oxen. When the envoy of the Lord of Lu arrived, Yan He himself received him.

The envoy asked: "Is this the house of Yan Wei?"

Yan He replied: "This is my house."

The envoy presented the gifts.

Yan He responded: "I fear that if I mishear and accept, I may bring blame upon the envoy. It would be better to verify."

The envoy returned, confirmed the matter, and came again to seek him, but Yan He could not be found. Thus it was with Yan He—he truly despised wealth and honor.

Therefore it is said: The essence of the Way is for the cultivation of the self; its remnants may be used to order the state; its dregs may be applied to govern the world. From this we see that the achievements of emperors and kings are but the leavings of sages, not the means by which one perfects the self and nourishes life. Yet the gentlemen of this vulgar age often endanger their bodies and cast aside their lives in pursuit of things—is this not lamentable?

Whenever the sage acts, he must examine both his purpose and his means. Consider this: if a man were to use the pearl of Marquis Sui to shoot at a sparrow a thousand fathoms high, the world would surely laugh at him. Why? Because what he employs is weighty, while what he seeks is light. How much more so is life itself, which far exceeds the pearl of Marquis Sui in value!

When Master Lie Zi was poor, his countenance bore the hue of hunger. A guest mentioned this to Zi Yang of Zheng, saying: "Lie Yukou is a man of the Way. That he dwells in your state yet suffers poverty—does this not suggest that you, my lord, fail to esteem scholars?"

Zi Yang of Zheng immediately ordered an official to send him grain. When Master Lie Zi saw the envoy, he bowed twice and declined. The envoy departed, and upon entering his home, Master Lie Zi’s wife struck her breast and said: "I have heard that the wife and children of a man of the Way all enjoy ease and happiness. Yet now you bear the look of hunger, and when the lord sent you food, you refused it. Is this not fate?"

Master Lie Zi laughed and said to her: "The lord does not know me personally; he sent me grain on the word of another. When he seeks to punish me, he will likewise do so on the word of another. This is why I did not accept."

In the end, the people indeed rose in rebellion and killed Zi Yang.

King Zhao of Chu lost his kingdom, and Tuyang Shuo fled in the king’s retinue. When King Zhao regained his kingdom and sought to reward those who had followed him, he came to Tuyang Shuo. Tuyang Shuo said: "When Your Majesty lost the kingdom, I lost my butcher’s stall. Now that Your Majesty has regained the kingdom, I have regained my butcher’s stall. My rank and emolument are already restored—what reward is there to give?"

The king said: "Insist that he accept."

Tuyang Shuo replied: "When Your Majesty lost the kingdom, it was not my fault, so I dared not suffer punishment. Now that Your Majesty has regained the kingdom, it is not my merit, so I dare not claim reward."

The king said: "Let him be received in audience."

Tuyang Shuo said: "According to the laws of Chu, only those who have rendered great service and received heavy reward may be granted an audience. My wisdom was not sufficient to preserve the kingdom, nor was my courage sufficient to die in battle against the enemy. When the Wu army entered Ying, I fled from danger and avoided the foe—I did not follow Your Majesty by design. Now if Your Majesty wishes to set aside the law and break the covenant to receive me, this is not what I would have the world hear of me."

The king said to Marshal Zi Qi: "Tuyang Shuo’s station is lowly, yet his principles are lofty. You shall extend to him the trust of the Three Standards of Honor."

Tuyang Shuo said: "The rank of the Three Standards—I know it is nobler than a butcher’s stall. The emolument of ten thousand bushels—I know it is richer than a butcher’s profit. Yet how could I covet rank and emolument and thereby cause my lord to bear the name of reckless bestowal? I dare not accept. I wish only to return to my butcher’s stall." And so he would not receive it.

Yuan Xian dwelt in Lu. His dwelling was a walled hut thatched with green grass, its gate of woven brambles incomplete, its door pivot made of mulberry wood, its windows of jars. Two rooms were stopped with coarse cloth. The roof leaked, the floor was damp. Yet he sat upright and played the zither.

Zi Gong arrived in a grand carriage, clad in an inner robe of dark blue and an outer robe of white, his towering chariot too wide for the lane. He came to see Yuan Xian.

Yuan Xian, wearing a hat of rushes and hempen sandals, leaning on a staff of autumn millet, answered the door. Zi Gong said: "Alas! Master, what ails you?"

Yuan Xian replied: "I have heard that to lack wealth is called poverty, but to study and be unable to practice is called affliction. Now I am poor, not afflicted."

Zi Gong stepped back, his face flushed with shame.

Yuan Xian laughed and said: "To act for the sake of worldly approval, to befriend for the sake of cliques, to study for others, to teach for oneself, to cloak oneself in benevolence and righteousness, to adorn oneself with carriages and horses—these are things I cannot bear to do."

Zengzi dwelt in Wei. His padded robe had no outer garment, his complexion was sallow and emaciated, his hands and feet calloused. For three days he did not kindle a fire, for ten years he did not make new clothes. When he straightened his cap, its strings broke; when he grasped his lapel, his elbows showed; when he put on his shoes, their heels split. Yet he dragged his hempen robe and sang the *Odes of Shang*, his voice filling heaven and earth, as if issuing from metal and stone. The Son of Heaven could not make him a subject, the feudal lords could not make him a friend. Thus, he who cultivates the will forgets the body; he who cultivates the body forgets profit; he who attains the Way forgets the mind.

Confucius said to Yan Hui: "Hui, come. Your family is poor and your station low—why do you not enter office?"

Yan Hui replied: "I do not wish to enter office. Outside the outer wall I have fifty *mu* of fields, enough to provide gruel and porridge. Within the outer wall I have ten *mu* of fields, enough to provide silk and hemp. Playing the zither is enough to amuse myself. What I have learned of the Master’s Way is enough to bring me joy."

Yan Hui did not wish to enter office. Confucius’s countenance changed with solemnity, and he said: "Excellent, Hui’s resolve! I have heard it said: he who knows contentment does not burden himself with profit; he who examines and attains self-mastery fears not loss; he who cultivates conduct within feels no shame without rank. I have recited these words for a long time, but only now, through Hui, do I truly see them—this is my own gain."

Prince Mou of Zhongshan said to Zhanzi: "Though my body dwells by rivers and lakes, my heart remains beneath the palace gates of Wei—what is to be done?" Zhanzi replied: "Value life. When life is valued, profit becomes light." Prince Mou of Zhongshan said: "Though I know this, I cannot overcome myself." Zhanzi said: "If you cannot overcome yourself, then follow your inclinations—what harm is there to the spirit? But if you cannot overcome yourself yet force yourself not to follow, this is called double injury. A doubly injured man belongs not to the long-lived."

Wei Mou was the son of a lord of ten thousand chariots, yet his retreat to cliffs and caves was harder to achieve than for a commoner. Though he had not yet attained the Way, he could be said to have grasped its intent.

Confucius was beset between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food, subsisting on a thin gruel of wild greens without grain. His complexion was deeply worn, yet he still plucked the zither and sang within his chamber. As Yan Hui picked vegetables, Zilu and Zigong said to one another: "Our Master was twice driven from Lu, his traces erased in Wei, his tree felled in Song, impoverished in Shang and Zhou, and besieged between Chen and Cai. Those who would kill him bear no guilt; those who would trample him face no prohibition. Yet he still plucks the zither and sings, never ceasing his music—can a gentleman be so shameless?" Yan Hui had no reply and went in to tell Confucius.

Confucius set aside his zither and sighed deeply: "You and Ci are petty men. Summon them, and I will speak to them." Zilu and Zigong entered.

Zilu said: "In such circumstances, one may truly be called destitute."
Confucius replied: "What words are these? The gentleman who is at one with the Dao is called 'at one'; he who is cut off from the Dao is called 'destitute.' Now I embrace the Dao of benevolence and righteousness yet encounter the calamities of a disordered age—how can this be called destitution? Thus, when I look within, I am not cut off from the Dao; when I face adversity, I do not lose my virtue. When the cold arrives and frost and snow descend, then I know the flourishing of the pine and cypress. The straits of Chen and Cai—are they not my good fortune?"

Confucius then calmly returned to his zither and played and sang, while Zilu, grasping his shield, danced vigorously. Zigong said: "I did not know the height of heaven or the depth of earth. Those of old who attained the Dao found joy in adversity and joy in prosperity—what they rejoiced in was not adversity or prosperity. When the virtue of the Dao is present here, then adversity and prosperity become as the sequence of cold and heat, wind and rain."

Thus, Xu You took delight by the Ying River, and Gong Bo found contentment on Gong Peak. (Sima Qian notes: Gong Bo’s name was He. He cultivated his conduct and esteemed worthy men. During the troubles of King Li of Zhou, when the Son of Heaven’s line was broken, the feudal lords all requested that he become the new king. He ruled for fourteen years, but a great drought came, and houses burned. Divination was performed at Taiyang. The oracle said: "King Li is the ancestral culprit; the Duke of Shao then established King Xuan." Gong Bo returned to his ancestral line, roaming carefree and content on the heights of Mount Gong. Yet this account may lack foundation. The *Records* state: "The Duke of Zhou and the Duke of Shao administered the government together, which was called the Gonghe Regency.")

Shun wished to cede the world to his friend Beiren Wuze. Beiren Wuze said: "How strange is the ruler’s conduct! Dwelling in the furrows of the fields, yet wandering at Yao’s gate. Not content with that, he now seeks to sully me with his disgraceful conduct. I am ashamed to see him." Thereupon he cast himself into the Qingling Abyss.

When Tang was about to attack Jie, he consulted Bian Sui. Bian Sui said: "This is not my affair." Tang asked: "Who would be suitable?" He replied: "I do not know." Tang then consulted Mao Guang. Mao Guang said: "This is not my affair." Tang asked: "Who would be suitable?" He replied: "I do not know." Tang said: "What of Yi Yin?" Mao Guang said: "He is strong and enduring, able to bear disgrace—I know nothing beyond that." Thereupon Tang consulted with Yi Yin and attacked Jie, defeating him. He then offered the throne to Bian Sui. Bian Sui declined, saying: "When the ruler consulted me about attacking Jie, he must have regarded me as a villain. Now that he has defeated Jie and offers me the throne, he must regard me as greedy. I was born in a disordered age, and a man without the Dao has twice sought to sully me with his disgraceful conduct—I cannot bear to hear of it again." He then cast himself into the Zhou River and died.

Tang next offered the throne to Mao Guang, saying: "The wise plan, the martial execute, the benevolent reside—this is the Way of antiquity. Why do you not take the throne?" Mao Guang declined, saying: "To depose a ruler is not righteous; to kill the people is not benevolent; to let others face danger while I enjoy the benefits is not upright. I have heard it said: 'One does not accept emoluments that are not righteous; in an age without the Dao, one does not tread its soil—how much less accept its honors?' I cannot bear to see this any longer." He then carried a stone and drowned himself in the Lu River.

In the rise of the Zhou, there were two knights dwelling in Guzhu, named Boyi and Shuqi. The two said to one another: "We have heard there is a man in the west who seems to possess the Dao. Let us go and observe." When they reached Qiyang, King Wu heard of them and sent Shu Dan to meet them.

They swore an oath, saying: "We shall grant you two ranks of wealth and appoint you to one rank of office." They slaughtered an animal and buried it as a sacrificial pledge. The two looked at each other and laughed, saying: "Ah, how strange! This is not what we call the Dao.

In the days when Shennong ruled all under Heaven, sacrifices were performed with utmost reverence, yet no blessings were sought. In his dealings with others, loyalty and sincerity governed all, yet nothing was demanded in return. Joy in governance was governance itself; joy in order was order itself. He did not build himself up by others’ ruin, nor exalt himself by others’ lowliness, nor profit from the times he encountered.

Now the Zhou, seeing the disorder of Yin, has hastily seized power. They scheme above and traffic in bribes below; they rely on military force to maintain their might, slaughter animals to swear oaths as pledges of trust, parade their conduct to please the masses, and resort to killing and warfare to seize advantage. This is but replacing one form of violence with another.

We have heard that the knights of old, when encountering an age of order, did not shirk their duty; when facing an age of chaos, they did not cling to mere survival. Now all under Heaven is benighted, and the virtue of Zhou has declined. To align ourselves with Zhou would only sully our persons—better to avoid it and preserve the purity of our conduct."

The two then traveled north to Mount Shouyang, where they starved to death.

As for Boyi and Shuqi, when it came to wealth and honor, if it could be avoided, they would never rely on lofty integrity or perverse conduct to indulge their own wills while refusing to serve the world. Such was the steadfastness of these two knights.

*The Twenty-Eighth Scroll of the Nan Hua Zhen Jing, Following the Original Text, Concludes*

**The Twenty-Ninth Scroll of the Nan Hua Zhen Jing, Following the Original Text**

*Collated by Peng Xiang, Disciple of Lu Ling Zhu Feng Luo Mian Dao*

**Miscellaneous Chapters: Robber Zhi**

The following account resembles those found in the *Strategies of Lu* and the *Records of the Grand Historian*, likely composed by Chu Shaosun.

Confucius was a friend of Liuxia Ji. Liuxia Ji’s younger brother was named Robber Zhi.

Robber Zhi commanded nine thousand followers, rampaging across the world and terrorizing the feudal lords. He broke into homes, drove off people’s cattle and horses, seized their wives and daughters, coveted gain while forgetting kinship, showing no regard for parents or brothers, nor offering sacrifices to his ancestors. Wherever he passed, great states fortified their cities, small states took refuge in their strongholds, and the common people suffered greatly.

Confucius said to Liuxia Ji: "As a father, one must instruct his son; as an elder brother, one must teach his younger brother. If a father cannot instruct his son, or an elder brother cannot teach his younger brother, then the bond of father and son, elder and younger brother, is worthless. Now, sir, you are a man of talent in this age, yet your brother is Robber Zhi, a scourge upon the world, and you cannot teach him. I am secretly ashamed for you. Allow me to go and reason with him."

Liuxia Ji replied: "You say that a father must instruct his son, and an elder brother must teach his younger brother. But if a son does not heed his father’s instruction, or a younger brother does not accept his elder brother’s teaching, what can even your eloquence accomplish? Moreover, Zhi’s nature is such that his mind surges like a spring, his will gusts like the wind. He is strong enough to resist enemies, eloquent enough to gloss over wrongs. He delights when his heart is obeyed, rages when it is opposed, and is easily provoked by words. You must not go!"

Confucius did not listen. Yan Hui drove the carriage, and Zigong stood to the right as they went to see Robber Zhi.

Robber Zhi was then resting his troops on the southern slope of Mount Tai, devouring the liver of a man. Confucius descended from the carriage and approached, addressing the herald: "I, Kong Qiu of Lu, have heard of the general’s lofty virtue and humbly bow twice to the herald."

The herald went in to announce him. Robber Zhi, upon hearing this, flew into a rage. His eyes blazed like stars, his hair bristled to his cap, and he said: "Is this not that crafty, deceitful man Kong Qiu of Lu? Tell him this: You fabricate words and invent phrases, falsely invoking Wen and Wu. You wear a cap of dead branches and gird yourself with the ribs of a dead ox. You spout endless, twisted arguments, eating without plowing, clothing yourself without weaving. You flap your lips and wag your tongue, stirring up right and wrong to delude the rulers of the world. You lead the scholars of the world astray, making them abandon their roots to falsely practice filial piety and brotherly love, all in hopes of seizing fiefs and riches. Your crimes are grave and heavy indeed! Flee at once! Or I shall add your liver to my midday meal."

Confucius sent word again: "I am fortunate to be acquainted with Ji and wish to gaze upon the hem of your robe."

The herald relayed this. Robber Zhi said: "Let him come forward."

Confucius hurried forward, stepped back from his mat, and bowed twice to Robber Zhi. Robber Zhi was enraged. He stretched out his legs, gripped his sword, glared fiercely, and roared like a nursing tiger: "Come forward, Qiu! If your words please me, you shall live; if they oppose me, you shall die."

Confucius said: "I have heard that there are three virtues under Heaven: To be born and grow tall, surpassingly handsome, so that young and old, noble and base, all delight in seeing you—this is the highest virtue. To comprehend the workings of Heaven and Earth and distinguish all things—this is the middle virtue. To be brave, fierce, and resolute, gathering troops and leading armies—this is the lowest virtue. Whoever possesses even one of these virtues is fit to face south and declare himself sovereign. Now, general, you possess all three: your body stands eight feet and two inches tall, your face and eyes radiant, your lips like gleaming cinnabar, your teeth like aligned shells, your voice attuned to the Yellow Bell. Yet you are called Robber Zhi—I am secretly ashamed for you that you do not claim what is rightfully yours. If you are willing to heed me, I shall go south to Wu and Yue, north to Qi and Lu, east to Song and Wei, west to Jin and Chu, and have a great city of several hundred *li* built for you, establishing a domain of hundreds of thousands of households. I shall exalt you as a feudal lord, renewing the world with you, disbanding your troops, gathering and nurturing your brothers, and together offering sacrifices to your ancestors. This is the conduct of a sage and a man of talent, the wish of all under Heaven."

Robber Zhi flew into a rage and said: "Come forward, Qiu! Those who can be swayed by profit or admonished by words are but the foolish and vulgar masses. Now, to be tall and handsome, so that all who see you are pleased—this is the legacy of my parents. Even if you do not praise me, do I not know this myself? Moreover, I have heard that those who flatter others to their faces also slander them behind their backs. Now you speak to me of great cities and multitudes of people—you seek to lure me with profit and treat me like a tame beast. How can this last? The greatest of cities is still smaller than the world. Yao and Shun ruled all under Heaven, yet their descendants had not even a place to drive a nail. Tang and Wu became Sons of Heaven, yet their lines were later extinguished. Was this not because their gains were too great?

Furthermore, I have heard that in ancient times, beasts were many and people few, so the people dwelled in nests to avoid them. By day they gathered acorns and chestnuts, by night they roosted in trees—thus they were called the people of the Nest Builder. In ancient times, people did not know clothing. In summer they piled up firewood, in winter they warmed themselves by it—thus they were called the people who knew how to live. In the age of Shennong, they lay at ease and rose carefree. People knew their mothers but not their fathers, living among the deer, plowing to eat, weaving to clothe themselves, with no thought of harming one another."

This was the height of utmost virtue. Yet even the Yellow Emperor could not fully realize virtue, battling Chiyou on the fields of Zhuolu, where blood flowed for a hundred li. Yao and Shun arose and established their ministers. Tang exiled his lord, and King Wu killed Zhou. From then on, the strong oppressed the weak, and the many tyrannized the few. Since the time of Tang and Wu, all have been followers of disorder.

Now you, sir, cultivate the Way of Wen and Wu, grasp the world’s debates, and teach later generations. Wearing fine robes and sashes, you speak with artful words and feign virtuous conduct to delude the lords of the world, all to seek wealth and honor. No thief is greater than you—why does the world not call you *Thief Qiu* instead of calling me *Robber Zhi*?

You used honeyed words to persuade Zilu to follow you. You made Zilu remove his warrior’s cap, lay aside his long sword, and receive your teachings. The world all says: *Confucius can halt violence and forbid wrongdoing.* Yet in the end, Zilu sought to kill the lord of Wei but failed, and his body was minced upon the eastern gate of Wei. This was because your teaching was insufficient.

Do you still claim to be a man of talent and a sage? You were twice driven from Lu, your traces erased in Wei, impoverished in Qi, besieged in Chen and Cai, and found no refuge under heaven. You taught Zilu to meet such a fate—unable to secure his own safety above or benefit others below—how can your Way be worthy of esteem?

Of those the world exalts, none surpasses the Yellow Emperor. Yet even he could not preserve perfect virtue, fighting on the fields of Zhuolu where blood flowed for a hundred li. Yao was not kind, Shun was not filial, Yu was crippled, Tang exiled his lord, King Wu attacked Zhou, and King Wen was imprisoned at Youli. These six men are those the world reveres. Yet if we examine them closely, all were deluded by profit, forcing themselves against their true nature. Their conduct is truly shameful.

The world calls worthy men like Boyi and Shuqi. Boyi and Shuqi renounced the rulership of Gubuzhu, starved to death on Mount Shouyang, and their flesh went unburied. Bao Jiao adorned his conduct to condemn the age and died embracing a tree. Shentu Di remonstrated but was not heeded, so he carried a stone and drowned himself in the river, becoming food for fish and turtles. Jie Zitui was utterly loyal, cutting flesh from his thigh to feed Duke Wen. Later, the duke betrayed him, so Zitui left in anger and burned to death holding a tree. Weisheng arranged to meet a woman beneath a bridge; when she did not come and the waters rose, he refused to leave and drowned holding the bridge pillar. These six men differ not from dismembered dogs, drowned pigs, or beggars clutching gourds—all abandoned their names, scorned death, and failed to cherish their fundamental nature or nurture their lives.

The world calls loyal ministers like Prince Bigan and Wu Zixu. Zixu was sunk in the river, and Bigan had his heart cut out. These two are called loyal ministers, yet in the end, the world laughs at them. From a higher perspective, even Zixu and Bigan are not worthy of esteem.

As for what you, Qiu, tell me: if you speak of ghostly matters, I cannot know them. But if you speak of human affairs, it is no more than this—all things I have already heard and understood.

Now I tell you of human nature: the eye desires to see beauty, the ear desires to hear sound, the mouth desires to savor flavor, and the will and qi desire fulfillment. Of men, the longest-lived reach a hundred years, the average eighty, the shortest sixty—yet when we subtract days lost to illness, frailty, mourning, death, and sorrow, the moments when one opens one’s mouth to laugh amount to no more than four or five days in a month.

Heaven and earth are without end, but man’s death comes at its appointed time. Clutching the instrument of this appointed time, one entrusts oneself to the boundless expanse—suddenly, it is no different from a fine steed galloping past a crack in the wall. Those who cannot delight their will and nurture their lives are all men who have not mastered the Way.

All that you, Qiu, have said is what I reject. Go quickly, return home, and speak no more of it. Your Way is but a frenzied, babbling, deceitful, and hollow sham—it cannot preserve the true. What is there to discuss?

Confucius bowed twice, hurried out, mounted his carriage, and grasped the reins—three times they slipped from his hands. His eyes were dazed and sightless, his complexion ashen like the dead. He leaned against the crossbar, head bowed, unable to catch his breath. Returning to the eastern gate of Lu, he chanced upon Liuxia Ji.

Liuxia Ji said, “You have been absent these past days, and your carriage bears the marks of travel. Might you have gone to see Zhi?”

Confucius looked up to the heavens and sighed, “Yes.”

Liuxia Ji asked, “Did Zhi perhaps offend you as I warned?”

Confucius replied, “Yes. I am one who, having no illness, yet cauterizes himself. I rushed to stroke the tiger’s head and braid its whiskers—nearly did I not escape the tiger’s jaws!”

Zizhang asked Mang Goude, “Why not act with virtue? Without virtuous action, one is not trusted; without trust, one is not employed; without employment, one gains no benefit. Thus, when one considers reputation and calculates profit, righteousness and truth are what matter. If one abandons reputation and profit and turns inward to the heart, then can a scholar go even a single day without virtuous action?”

Mang Goude replied, “The shameless grow rich, and the deceitful rise to prominence. The greatest of reputation and profit lie in shamelessness and deceit. Thus, when one considers reputation and calculates profit, trust and truth are what matter. If one abandons reputation and profit and turns inward to the heart, then does not the scholar’s action embrace the Heavenly?”

Zizhang said, “In ancient times, Jie and Zhou were honored as Sons of Heaven and possessed all under heaven. Yet if one were to tell Zang Ju, ‘Your conduct is like Jie and Zhou,’ his face would flush with shame, and he would bristle with resentment—such is the low regard of petty men. Confucius and Mo Di lived in poverty as commoners. Yet if one were to tell a prime minister, ‘Your conduct is like Confucius and Mo Di,’ his expression would change, and he would claim insufficiency—such is the true esteem of the scholar. Thus, to be a Son of Heaven does not necessarily confer honor, nor does poverty as a commoner necessarily confer disgrace. The distinction between honor and disgrace lies in the beauty or baseness of one’s conduct.”

Mang Goude said, “Petty thieves are imprisoned, but great thieves become lords. At the gates of lords, righteous scholars abide. In former times, Duke Huan of Qi killed his elder brother and took his sister-in-law, yet Guan Zhong served as his minister. Tian Chengzi murdered his lord and usurped the state, yet Confucius accepted his gifts.”

The judgment deems them base, the conduct degrades them—thus the true nature of word and deed wars within the breast. Is this not perverse?

Thus the *Book* says: “Who is vile? Who is noble? The accomplished stand at the head, the unaccomplished at the tail.”

Zizhang said: “If you do not act, kinship and estrangement will lack order, noble and base will lack righteousness, elder and younger will lack sequence. How then shall the Five Norms and Six Positions be distinguished?”

Mang Goude said: “Yao executed his eldest son, Shun exiled his younger brother—where is the order in kinship? Tang banished Jie, King Wu slew Zhou—where is the righteousness in noble and base? King Ji was the rightful heir, yet the Duke of Zhou killed his elder brother—where is the sequence in elder and younger? The Confucians preach hypocritical words, the Mohists advocate universal love—how then shall the Five Norms and Six Positions be distinguished?

Moreover, you rectify for the sake of fame, I rectify for the sake of profit. In truth, fame and profit neither accord with principle nor reflect the Dao. Daily I dispute with you before Wu Yue, saying: ‘The petty man dies for wealth, the noble man dies for fame—their motives for altering their nature differ, yet in abandoning what they do to pursue what they do not, they are one and the same.’

Thus it is said: Do not be a petty man, lest you die in rebellion against Heaven. Do not be a noble man, but follow Heaven’s principle. Whether crooked or straight, align with the celestial pole. Observe the four directions, ebb and flow with the times. Whether right or wrong, grasp the revolving mechanism. Stand alone in intent, wander with the Dao. Act without contrivance, establish no fixed righteousness, lest you lose what you seek. Pursue no wealth, achieve no fame, lest you abandon Heaven.

Bigan was disemboweled, Zixu had his eyes gouged out—such are the calamities of loyalty. Zhigong testified against his father, Weisheng drowned holding to his word—such are the perils of trust. Baozi stood until he withered, Shenzi refused to defend himself—such are the harms of integrity. Confucius did not see his mother, Kuangzi did not see his father—such are the failures of righteousness.

From the traditions of antiquity to the discourses of later ages, those esteemed as scholars rectify their words and align their deeds, yet still suffer misfortune and encounter disaster.”

Wu Zu asked Zhi He: “No man in the end fails to pursue fame and seek profit. The wealthy draw men to them; those drawn defer to them; those who defer honor them. To be honored and deferred is the Way to long life, bodily ease, and joy of heart. Yet you alone show no such intent—is your wisdom insufficient? Or do you know but lack the strength to act? Or do you deliberately reject the true path?”

Zhi He said: “These men, born in the same age and dwelling in the same village, regard those who transcend custom and surpass the world as lacking any guiding principle. Thus they survey the ages, discerning right and wrong. They conform to the俗 and transform with the世, abandoning what is most weighty, discarding what is most honorable, all for the sake of their pursuits. How can their discourse on the Way to long life, bodily ease, and joy of heart not be far from the mark?

The agony of sorrow, the peace of contentment—these are not reflected in the body. The terror of alarm, the joy of delight—these are not mirrored in the heart. They know what they do but not why they do it. Thus, though they be honored as Son of Heaven and possess all under Heaven, they cannot escape misfortune.”

Wu Zu said: “Wealth, to man, is without disadvantage. It exhausts beauty and exhausts power, reaching what the perfected cannot attain, what the worthy cannot equal. It wields the courage and strength of the heroic as its might, commands the wisdom and schemes of others as its insight, and appropriates the virtue of others as its own excellence. Though not enthroned, it is revered as a lord and father.

Moreover, the allure of sound and color, flavor and power—these delight the heart without need of learning, comfort the body without need of imitation. Desires and aversions, approach and avoidance—these require no teacher, for they are man’s nature. Though all under Heaven condemn me, who can renounce them?”

Zhi He said: “The actions of the wise move in accord with the people, not transgressing their measure. Thus, content, they do not contend; lacking nothing, they do not seek. Those who lack seek, contending in all directions yet not deeming themselves greedy. Those who have surplus yield, relinquishing all under Heaven yet not deeming themselves incorrupt. The truth of incorruptibility and greed lies not in external compulsion but in reflecting on one’s own measure.

Though enthroned as Son of Heaven, they do not use their nobility to domineer. Though possessing all under Heaven, they do not use their wealth to toy with others. Calculating the harm, considering the consequences, they deem it injurious to their nature, and thus decline and do not accept—not to seek fame and reputation.

Yao and Shun ruled as emperors and were revered, not to benevolently govern all under Heaven, but to avoid harming life with beauty. Shanjuan and Xu You declined the throne, not out of empty humility, but to avoid harming themselves with affairs. All sought what benefited them and declined what harmed them. Though all under Heaven calls them worthy, they did not act to cultivate fame and reputation.”

Wu Zu said: “If one must cling to fame, endure bodily hardship, forgo sweetness, and restrain nourishment to preserve life, one is no better than a chronic invalid, long afflicted yet not dead.”

Zhi He said: “Equilibrium is fortune, excess is harm—of all things, none is not so, and wealth is the most extreme. Now, the rich man’s ears are filled with the sounds of bells, drums, pipes, and flutes; his mouth savors the taste of fine meats and rich wines, stirring his mind and causing him to neglect his affairs—can this be called disorder? Engulfed in arrogance, he labors like one bearing a heavy burden uphill—can this be called suffering? Coveting wealth, he seeks consolation; craving power, he exhausts himself. At rest, he is drowned in indolence; in comfort, he is swollen with pride—can this be called sickness? Driven by the desire for wealth and the pursuit of profit, he is deafened as though his ears were walled up, yet does not avoid it; he is haughty yet does not desist—can this be called disgrace? Wealth accumulates yet goes unused; he clings to it without release, his heart filled with sorrow and bitterness, ever seeking more without cease—can this be called sorrow?”

Within, he fears the threats of extortion and demands; without, he dreads the harm of bandits and thieves. His inner chambers are fortified with towers and bars, and he dares not walk alone outside—can this be called fear? These six afflictions are the greatest harms under heaven, yet all are overlooked and left unexamined. When disaster strikes, he exhausts his nature and depletes his wealth in vain, unable even to restore a single day of peace. Thus, when viewed by name, they are unseen; when sought for profit, they are unattainable. Yet men strain their minds and break their bodies in contention for these—is this not delusion?

*The Southern Florescence True Classic, Following the Root, Scroll Twenty-Nine Concludes*
*The Southern Florescence True Classic, Following the Root, Scroll Thirty*

**Miscellaneous Chapters: Discourse on Swords**

Long ago, King Wen of Zhao delighted in swords. Over three thousand swordsmen thronged his gates as guests, clashing before him day and night. Each year, more than a hundred perished or were wounded, yet his passion knew no end. After three years of this, the state declined, and the feudal lords plotted against him.

The crown prince, Kui, grew anxious and offered a reward to his attendants: “Whoever can persuade the king to abandon his fondness for swordsmen shall receive a thousand gold pieces.” His attendants replied, “Zhuangzi is the one for this.”

The prince sent envoys bearing a thousand gold pieces to present to Zhuangzi. Zhuangzi declined the gift and accompanied the messengers to see the prince. “What instruction does the prince have for me,” he asked, “that he offers me a thousand gold pieces?” The prince replied, “I have heard that you are enlightened and sagely. I humbly present this gold to honor your attendants. If you refuse it, how dare I speak further?”

Zhuangzi said, “I have heard that the prince wishes to employ me to end the king’s obsession. If I persuade the king but displease him, or fail to satisfy the prince, I shall be executed—what use would gold be to me then? But if I please the king and satisfy the prince, what in Zhao could I not obtain?”

The prince said, “Indeed. My father sees only swordsmen.”

Zhuangzi replied, “Very well. I am skilled in the sword.”

The prince said, “But the swordsmen my father favors all have unkempt hair, bristling sideburns, drooping caps, tassels of coarse silk, short jackets, glaring eyes, and speak with difficulty. The king delights in them. If you appear before him in scholar’s robes, the outcome will be most unfavorable.”

Zhuangzi said, “Allow me to prepare a swordsman’s attire.” After three days, he had his sword attire ready and was presented to the prince, who then accompanied him to see the king.

The king drew his naked blade and waited. Zhuangzi entered the palace hall without haste, and when he saw the king, he did not bow. The king asked, “What instruction do you bring, that the prince has gone before me?”

Zhuangzi replied, “I have heard that Your Majesty delights in swords, so I have come to present myself with my sword.”

The king asked, “What can your sword do to restrain others?”

Zhuangzi answered, “My sword cuts down one man every ten paces, and its course is unobstructed for a thousand *li*.”

The king was greatly pleased and said, “You would be invincible under heaven!”

Zhuangzi replied, “The way of the sword is to present emptiness, offer advantage, strike after the opponent moves, and arrive first. I would like to demonstrate.”

The king said, “Rest now, return to your lodging, and await my command. I shall arrange a contest and summon you.”

The king then tested his swordsmen for seven days, during which over sixty were killed or wounded. He selected five or six and had them stand with their swords in the palace courtyard before summoning Zhuangzi.

The king said, “Today, let us see you contend with these men.”

Zhuangzi replied, “I have long awaited this.”

The king asked, “What length of sword do you wield?”

Zhuangzi said, “Any length will do, but I possess three swords, and I shall use whichever Your Majesty prefers. Allow me to describe them first, then demonstrate.”

The king said, “I wish to hear of the three swords.”

Zhuangzi replied, “There is the sword of the Son of Heaven, the sword of the feudal lords, and the sword of the commoner.”

The king asked, “What is the sword of the Son of Heaven like?”

Zhuangzi said, “The sword of the Son of Heaven has its edge in Yanxi and Shicheng, its guard in Mount Tai of Qi, its spine in Jin and Wei, its hilt in Zhou and Song, and its sides in Han and Wei. It is wrapped in the four barbarian tribes, bound by the four seasons, encircled by the Bohai Sea, and girded by Mount Heng. It is governed by the five phases, judged by punishment and virtue, opened by yin and yang, sustained by spring and summer, and wielded in autumn and winter. When this sword is thrust forward, nothing precedes it; when raised, nothing is above it; when pressed down, nothing is beneath it; when swung, nothing stands beside it. Above, it severs floating clouds; below, it cuts through the earth’s foundations. When this sword is employed, the feudal lords are brought to order, and all under heaven submit. This is the sword of the Son of Heaven.”

King Wen, bewildered and lost in thought, asked, “What is the sword of the feudal lords like?”

Zhuangzi replied, “The sword of the feudal lords has its edge in the wisdom and courage of knights, its guard in the purity and integrity of scholars, its spine in the worthiness and goodness of gentlemen, its hilt in the loyalty and sagacity of sages, and its sides in the boldness and heroism of warriors. When this sword is thrust forward, nothing precedes it; when raised, nothing is above it; when pressed down, nothing is beneath it; when swung, nothing stands beside it. Above, it follows the roundness of heaven, aligning with the three luminaries; below, it follows the squareness of earth, aligning with the four seasons.”

The sword of the feudal lords harmonizes the people’s will and pacifies the four quarters. When this sword is wielded, it strikes like thunder, and within the four borders, none fail to submit and heed the ruler’s command. Such is the sword of the feudal lords.

The king asked, “What is the sword of the common people like?”

Zhuangzi replied, “The sword of the common people: unkempt hair, bristling sideburns, a drooping cap, a frayed tassel, a short-backed robe, glaring eyes, and quarrelsome speech. They clash before one another, severing heads and necks above, piercing livers and lungs below. This sword of the common people differs not from cockfighting—once their lives are spent, they serve no purpose in the affairs of state. Now, though Your Majesty holds the position of the Son of Heaven, you favor the sword of the common people. Your servant humbly deems this unworthy of you.”

The king then led Zhuangzi to ascend the hall. The royal chef presented the meal, and the king circled the table thrice.

Zhuangzi said, “May Your Majesty sit composed and settle your qi. The matter of the sword has now been concluded.”

Thereafter, King Wen did not leave his palace for three months, and the swordsmen all perished where they stood.

---

Confucius wandered in the forest of black curtains and rested upon the Apricot Terrace.

The disciples read their books while Confucius strummed the zither and sang. Halfway through the melody, an old fisherman disembarked and approached. His beard and brows were snowy white, his hair unbound, his sleeves swaying as he walked up from the marshland. He halted at the edge of the shore, his left hand resting on his knee, his right hand supporting his chin, listening intently. When the song ended, he beckoned to Zilu and Zigong, who both came forward.

The stranger pointed at Confucius and asked, “What is that man doing?”

Zilu replied, “He is a gentleman of Lu.”

The stranger asked about his lineage.

Zilu answered, “His family name is Kong.”

The stranger asked, “And what does this Kong family practice?”

Zilu did not respond, but Zigong answered, “The Master Kong cultivates loyalty and trustworthiness in his nature, embodies benevolence and righteousness in his conduct, refines ritual and music, and discerns human relationships. Above, he is devoted to the rulers of the age; below, he transforms the common people, all for the benefit of the world. This is what the Kong family practices.”

The stranger asked further, “Is he a lord with lands?”

Zigong said, “No.”

“Is he an advisor to kings and princes?”

Zigong said, “No.”

The stranger laughed and turned to leave, saying, “Benevolent, to be sure—but I fear he will not escape harm to himself. He wearies his mind and wears out his body, endangering his true nature. Alas!”

Far indeed has he strayed from the Dao.

Zigong returned and reported to Confucius. Confucius pushed aside his zither and rose, saying, “He must be a sage!” Then he descended to seek him out. By the marsh’s edge, the stranger was just then staff in hand, drawing his boat toward shore. Glancing back, he saw Confucius, turned around, and stood facing him. Confucius retreated, bowed twice, and advanced.

The stranger asked, “What do you seek?”

Confucius replied, “A short while ago, sir left with words unfinished. I, unworthy as I am, did not grasp their meaning. I have waited humbly in your wake, hoping to hear even the sound of your cough or spittle, that I might complete my understanding.”

The stranger exclaimed, “Ah! How earnest is your love of learning!”

Confucius bowed twice and rose, saying, “From my youth I have cultivated learning, and now at sixty-nine, I have yet to hear the highest teaching. How dare I not remain open in heart?”

The stranger said, “Like attracts like, and like sounds resonate—this is the principle of Heaven. Let me set forth what I possess and expound upon what you pursue. What you pursue pertains to human affairs. When the Son of Heaven, the feudal lords, the great officers, and the common people—these four—each rectifies himself, governance is at its finest. When these four abandon their proper stations, no disorder could be greater. When officials attend to their duties and each person concerns himself with his own affairs, none oversteps his bounds. Thus, fields lie fallow and homes stand exposed, clothing and food are insufficient, taxes and levies go uncollected, wives and concubines are at odds, and elders and juniors lack proper order—these are the worries of the common people. Inability to fulfill one’s role, official duties left undone, conduct that is not pure and clear, subordinates who are negligent and idle, achievements unrecognized, ranks and emoluments unsecured—these are the worries of the great officers. The court lacks loyal ministers, the state is benighted and disordered, artisans lack skill, tribute is unworthy, seasonal offerings lag behind others, and one does not follow the Son of Heaven—these are the worries of the feudal lords. Yin and yang are out of harmony, cold and heat arrive untimely, harming the myriad things; feudal lords act with violence and disorder, seizing and attacking one another, ravaging the people; ritual and music lack moderation, resources are exhausted, human relationships are unadorned, and the hundred clans are licentious and chaotic—these are the worries of the Son of Heaven’s ministers.

Now, you possess neither the authority of the Son of Heaven, the feudal lords, or their ministers above, nor the office of a great officer with its duties below, yet you presume to refine ritual and music, select human relationships, and transform the common people. Is this not overreaching in affairs?

Moreover, people have eight flaws, and affairs have four perils—these cannot be left unexamined. To engage in affairs not one’s own is called *meddling*; to advance where none pays heed is called *flattery*; to speak in accord with another’s wishes is called *sycophancy*; to speak without discerning right or wrong is called *obsequiousness*; to delight in speaking of others’ faults is called *slander*; to sever friendships and estrange kin is called *treachery*; to praise falsely and deceive in order to ruin others is called *depravity*; to fail to distinguish good from bad, to accommodate both sides, and to stealthily seize what one desires is called *perfidy*. These eight flaws bring chaos to others externally and harm to oneself internally. The noble person does not befriend such, nor does the enlightened ruler employ them.

As for the four perils: to delight in managing great affairs, altering and changing constants in pursuit of merit and fame is called *presumptuousness*; to monopolize knowledge, arrogate affairs, encroach upon others, and act on one’s own is called *greed*; to see one’s faults yet not change, and to hear admonition yet grow worse is called *obduracy*; to approve only what accords with oneself and disapprove even what is good if it differs from oneself is called *arrogance*. These are the four perils. Only when one has cast off the eight flaws and refrains from the four perils can one be taught.

Confucius sighed deeply, bowed twice, and rose, saying, “I was twice driven from Lu, had my traces erased in Wei, was made to fell a tree in Song, and was besieged between Chen and Cai. I do not know what errors I committed to incur these four slanders—why is this?”

The stranger’s expression turned somber as he said, “How difficult it is for you to awaken! There was a man who feared his shadow and loathed his footprints, and so ran to escape them. The more he lifted his feet, the more footprints he made; the faster he ran, the more his shadow clung to him. Believing he was still too slow, he ran without cease until he collapsed and died. He did not know that by standing in the shade he could rest his shadow, and by remaining still he could still his footprints—how foolish he was!

You scrutinize the space between benevolence and righteousness, examine the boundaries of sameness and difference, observe the changes of activity and stillness, adjust the measure of giving and receiving, order the passions of like and dislike, and harmonize the moderation of joy and anger—yet you nearly fail to escape harm. If you carefully cultivate your own person, guard your true nature, and return things to others, you will have no encumbrances. Now, instead of cultivating your own person, you seek from others—is this not misplaced?”

Confucius sighed deeply and asked, “May I ask what is meant by *true*?”

The stranger said, “*True* is the utmost of essence and sincerity. Without essence and sincerity, one cannot move others. Thus, one who forces tears may seem sorrowful but is not mournful; one who forces anger may seem stern but is not awe-inspiring; one who forces affection may smile but is not harmonious. True sorrow is silent yet mournful; true anger is unexpressed yet awe-inspiring; true affection is unsmiling yet harmonious. When truth resides within, the spirit moves without. This is why truth is prized.

Applied to human principles: in serving parents, it is filial piety; in serving rulers, it is loyalty; in drinking wine, it is joy; in mourning, it is sorrow. In all things, integrity must be primary: in achievement, merit is primary; in drinking wine, joy is primary; in mourning, sorrow is primary; in serving parents, suitability is primary. When merit is achieved, no trace of it remains; in serving parents with suitability, no account of the means is kept; in drinking wine with joy, no selection of vessels is made; in mourning with sorrow, no inquiry into ritual is had.

Ritual is what the common customs devise; truth is what one receives from Heaven, naturally and unalterably. Thus, the sage models himself on Heaven and values truth, not being bound by custom. The foolish do the opposite: they cannot model themselves on Heaven and are anxious about others; they do not know the value of truth and, in their mediocrity, are swayed by custom. Thus, they are never sufficient. How lamentable! You were early immersed in human artifice and only now hear of the great Dao.”

Confucius bowed twice and rose, saying: “Today, I, Qiu, have had the good fortune of an encounter—truly as if favored by Heaven. You, Master, have not disdained to treat me as a servant and instruct me in person. Dare I ask where you dwell? I would follow and receive your teaching, that I might complete my study of the great Dao.”

The stranger replied: “I have heard this: those who can be led may be guided to the subtle Dao; those who cannot be led do not comprehend the Way. Take care not to guide them, lest you bring harm upon yourself. Strive on, for I leave you now.” With that, he poled his boat away, gliding along the reeds.

Yan Yuan turned the carriage, and Zilu handed the reins, but Confucius did not look back. Only when the water’s ripples stilled and the sound of the oar vanished did he dare to board. Zilu stepped beside the carriage and asked: “I, You, have long served you, yet never have I seen you meet another with such reverence. Kings of ten thousand chariots, lords of a thousand, when they meet you, do not fail to observe proper courtesy, yet you still bear a bearing of dignified reserve. Now this old fisherman, leaning on his staff and standing defiantly, and you bowed deeply, bending like a chime stone, responding with words and bows—was this not excessive? The disciples all find it strange. What has this fisherman done to deserve such treatment?”

Confucius leaned on the crossbar and sighed, saying: “Truly, You, you are difficult to transform. Though you have been steeped in ritual and righteousness for some time, your coarse and rustic heart remains unchanged to this day. Come closer, and I will tell you: to meet an elder without reverence is to lack ritual; to meet a worthy without respect is to lack benevolence. If he were not a perfected person, he could not humble himself before others. If one does not humble oneself with utmost sincerity, one cannot attain the truth, and thus one’s person suffers lasting harm. Alas! There is no greater misfortune for a person than the lack of benevolence—and you alone cling to it!

Moreover, the Dao is that by which all things proceed. All beings that lose it die; those that attain it live. In affairs, to go against it is to fail; to follow it is to succeed. Thus, wherever the Dao resides, the sage honors it. Now this old fisherman possesses the Dao—how could I dare not revere him?”

*The Southern Florescence Scripture, following the original text, concludes its thirtieth scroll.*
